Reunited at Last.\r\n\r\n  \xc2\xb7 CONCLUSION.\xe2\x80\x94Out with the Invader.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER I.\xe2\x80\x94_Squatter Darrell Reviews the Past._\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo be guided by good advice, is to profit by the wisdom of others; to\r\nbe guided by experience, is to profit by wisdom of our own,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs.\r\nDarrell to her husband, in her own sweet, winning way, as they sat alone\r\nin the sitting room of their Alameda farm house, having their last talk\r\nthat evening, while she darned his stockings and sewed buttons on his\r\nshirts. The children (so-called, though the majority were grown up) had\r\nall retired for the night. Mr. and Mrs. Darrell sat up later, having\r\nmuch to talk about, as he would leave next day for Southern California,\r\nintending to locate\xe2\x80\x94somewhere in a desirable neighborhood\xe2\x80\x94a homestead\r\nclaim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTherefore,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Mrs. Darrell, seeing that her husband smoked his\r\npipe in silence, adding no observations to her own, \xe2\x80\x9clet us this time be\r\nguided by our own past history, William\xe2\x80\x94our experience. In other words,\r\nlet us be wise, my husband.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy way of variety, you mean,\xe2\x80\x9d said he smiling. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is, as far as I am\r\nconcerned, because I own, frankly, that had I been guided by your\r\nadvice\xe2\x80\x94your wisdom\xe2\x80\x94we would be much better off to-day. You have a right\r\nto reproach me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not wish to do anything of the kind. I think reproaches seldom do\r\ngood.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo use in crying over spilt milk, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is not my idea, either. On the contrary, if by \xe2\x80\x98_milk_\xe2\x80\x99 it is\r\nmeant all or any earthly good whatever, it is the \xe2\x80\x98_spilt milk_\xe2\x80\x99 that we\r\nshould lament. There is no reason to cry for the milk that has not been\r\nwasted, the good that is not lost. So let us cry for the _spilt milk_,\r\nby all means, if by doing so we learn how to avoid spilling any more.\r\nLet us cry for the _spilt milk_, and remember how, and where, and when,\r\nand why, we spilt it. Much wisdom is learnt through tears, but none by\r\nforgetting our lessons.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut how can a man learn when he is born a fool?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly an idiot is, truly speaking, a born fool; a fool to such a degree\r\nthat he cannot act wisely if he will. It is only when _perversity_ is\r\nadded to foolishness, that a being\xe2\x80\x94not an idiot\xe2\x80\x94is utterly a fool. To\r\npersist in acting wrongfully, that is the real folly. To reject good\r\ncounsel, either of one\xe2\x80\x99s own good thoughts or the good thoughts of\r\nothers. But to act foolishly by deciding hastily, by lack of mature\r\nreflection, that I should only call a foolish mistake. So, then, if we\r\nhave been foolish, let us at least utilize our foolishness by drawing\r\nfrom it lessons of wisdom for the future. We cannot conscientiously\r\nplead that we are born fools when we see our errors.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Darrell smilingly bowed, and with a voice much softer than his usual\r\nstentorian tones, said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI understand, little wife, but I fear that my streak of perversity is a\r\nbroad one, and has solely been the bane of my life; it has a fatality\r\naccompanying it. I have often seen the right way to act, and yet I have\r\ngone with my eyes wide open to do the wrong thing. And this, too, not\r\nmeaning to do harm to any one, nor wishing to be malicious or mean. I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know what power impelled me. But if you will forgive my past\r\nwickedness, I\xe2\x80\x99ll try to do better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t say that. Don\xe2\x80\x99t speak of your wickedness, for real wickedness is\r\nperversity. You have acted wrongly at times, when you have misapplied\r\nyour rights and the rights of others, but you have not intentionally\r\ndone wrong. You are not perverse; don\xe2\x80\x99t say that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn a few days it will be twenty-four years since we crossed the plains\r\nwith our three babies, in our caravan of four wagons, followed by our\r\nfine horses and choice Durham cows. I firmly believed then, that with my\r\nfine stock and my good bank account, and broad government lands, free to\r\nall Americans, I should have given you a nice home before I was five\r\nyears older; that I would have saved money and would be getting more to\r\nmake us rich before I was old. But see, at the end of twenty-four years,\r\nwhere and how do I find myself? I am still poor, all I have earned is\r\nthe name of \xe2\x80\x98_Squatter_.\xe2\x80\x99 That pretty name (which I hate because you\r\ndespise it) is what I have earned.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t say that either, William. We will only recommence one of numerous\r\nfruitless discussions. We are not poor, because we have enough to live\r\nin comfort, and I do not despise the name of Squatter, for it is\r\nharmless enough, but I do certainly disapprove of acts done by men\r\nbecause they are squatters, or to become squatters. They have caused\r\nmuch trouble to people who never harmed them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey, too, the poor squatters, have suffered as much distress as they\r\nhave caused, the poor hard-worked toilers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is very true, but I am afraid I shall never be able to see the\r\nnecessity of any one being a squatter in this blessed country of\r\nplentiful broad acres, which a most liberal government gives away for\r\nthe asking.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s exactly it. We aren\xe2\x80\x99t squatters. We are \xe2\x80\x98_settlers_.\xe2\x80\x99 We take up\r\nland that belongs to us, American citizens, by paying the government\r\nprice for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhenever you take up government land, yes, you are \xe2\x80\x98settlers,\xe2\x80\x99 but not\r\nwhen you locate claims on land belonging to any one else. In that case,\r\nyou must accept the epithet of \xe2\x80\x98_Squatter_.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDarrell set his teeth so tightly, that he bit a little chip off his\r\npipe. Mrs. Darrell went on as if she had not observed her husband\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nflash of irritation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I hope we will never more deserve such name; I trust that before\r\nyou locate any homestead claim in Southern California, you will first\r\ninform yourself, very carefully, whether any one has a previous claim.\r\nAnd more specially, I beg of you, do not go on a Mexican grant unless\r\nyou buy the land from the owner. This I beg of you specially, and must\r\n_insist upon it_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how am I to know who is the owner of a rancho that has been\r\nrejected, for instance?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf the rancho is still in litigation, don\xe2\x80\x99t buy land in it, or if you\r\ndo, buy title from the original grantee, on fair conditions and clear\r\nunderstanding.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know whether that can be done in the Alamar rancho, which I am\r\ngoing to see, and I know it has been rejected. But of one thing you can\r\nrest assured, that I shall not forget our sad experience in Napa and\r\nSonoma valleys, where\xe2\x80\x94after years of hard toil\xe2\x80\x94I had to abandon our home\r\nand lose the earnings of years and years of hard work.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is all I ask, William. To remember our experience in Napa and\r\nSonoma. To remember, also, that we are no longer young. We cannot afford\r\nto throw away another twenty years of our life; and really and truly, if\r\nyou again go into a Mexican grant, William, I shall not follow you there\r\nwillingly. Do not expect it of me; I shall only go if you compel me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCompel you!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cCompel you, when you know I have\r\nobeyed you all my life.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! no, William, not all your life, for you were well grown before I\r\never saw you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean ever since I went to Washington with my mind made up to jump off\r\nthe train coming back, if you didn\xe2\x80\x99t agree to come North to be my\r\ncommandant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think I have been a very strict disciplinarian,\xe2\x80\x9d she said,\r\nsmiling. \xe2\x80\x9cI think the subaltern has had pretty much his own way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, when he thinks he might. But when the commandant pulls the string,\r\nby looking sad or offended, then good-by to the spirit and independence\r\nof the subaltern.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne thing I must not forget to ask you;\xe2\x80\x9d she said, going back to the\r\npoint of their digression, \xe2\x80\x9cand it is, not to believe what those men\r\nhave been telling you about the Alamar rancho having been finally\r\nrejected. You know John Gasbang could never speak the truth, and years\r\nhave not made him more reliable. As for Miller, Hughes and Mathews, they\r\nare dishonest enough, and though not so brazen as Gasbang, they will\r\nmisrepresent facts to induce you to go with them, for they want you with\r\nthem.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know they do; I see through all that. But I see, too, that San Diego\r\nis sure to have a railroad direct to the Eastern States. Lands will\r\nincrease in value immediately; so I think, myself, I had better take\r\ntime by the forelock and get a good lot of land in the Alamar grant,\r\nwhich is quite near town.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, are you sure it is finally rejected?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI saw the book, where the fact is recorded. Isn\xe2\x80\x99t that enough?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, if there has been no error.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlways the same cautious Mary Moreneau, who tortured me with her doubts\r\nand would not have me until Father White took compassion on me,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nhe, smiling, looking at her fondly, for his thoughts reverted back to\r\nthose days when Miss Mary was _afraid_ to marry him; but, after all, he\r\nwon her and brought her all the way from Washington to his New England\r\nhome.\r\n\r\nWilliam Darrell was already a well-to-do young farmer in those days, a\r\nbachelor twenty-eight to thirty years of age, sole heir to a flourishing\r\nNew England farm, and with a good account in a Boston bank, when Miss\r\nMary Moreneau came to New England from Washington to visit her aunt,\r\nMrs. Newton. As Mrs. Newton\xe2\x80\x99s husband was William Darrell\xe2\x80\x99s uncle,\r\nnothing was more natural than for Mary to meet him at his uncle\xe2\x80\x99s house.\r\nNobody expected that William would fall in love with her, as he seemed\r\nto be proof against Cupid\xe2\x80\x99s darts. The marriageable maidens of William\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nneighborhood had in vain tried to attract the obdurate young farmer, who\r\nseemed to enjoy no other society than that of his uncle Newton and his\r\nwife.\r\n\r\nBut Mary came and William surrendered at once. She, however, gave him no\r\nencouragement. Her coldness seemed only to inflame his love the more,\r\nuntil Miss Moreneau thought it was best to shorten her visit and return\r\nhome about the middle of September.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy are you to return home so early?\xe2\x80\x9d Darrell asked Mary, after Mrs.\r\nNewton had informed him of Mary\xe2\x80\x99s intention of going.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I think it is best,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy is it best?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor several reasons.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay I be permitted to ask what are those reasons?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly. One reason is, that as I came to see my aunt and at the same\r\ntime to rest and improve my health, and all those objects have been\r\naccomplished, I might as well go home. Then, my other aunt, with whom I\r\nreside, is not feeling well. She went to spend the summer in Virginia,\r\nbut writes that her health has not improved much, and she will soon come\r\nback to Washington. Then some of my pupils will want to recommence their\r\nlessons soon, and I want to have some little time to myself before I\r\nbegin to work. You know, Mr. Darrell, I teach to support myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, only because you have a notion to do it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA notion! Do you think I am rich?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, but there is no need of your working.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a need to me to feel independent. I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to be supported by\r\nmy aunts, while I know how to earn my own living.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Mary, please, I beg of you, let me have the happiness of taking\r\ncare of you. Be my wife, I am not a rich man, but I have enough to\r\nprovide for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Darrell, you surprise me. I thank you for the compliment you pay me\r\nwith your honorable offer, but I have no wish to get married.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you reject me, Miss Mary? Tell me one thing; tell me truly, do you\r\ncare for any one else?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I care for nobody. I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to marry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you will marry some time. If you knew how very miserable you make\r\nme, I think you would not have the heart to refuse me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will get over it. I am going soon. Forget me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDarrell made no answer. He staggered out of the room and did not return\r\nuntil the following week, when Mary had left for Washington, accompanied\r\nby Letitia, her colored servant (called Tisha), who was devotedly\r\nattached to her.\r\n\r\nDarrell had become rather taciturn and less sociable than ever, Mrs.\r\nNewton noticed, and since Mary left he seemed to lose flesh and all his\r\nspirits, and passed the winter as if life were a burden to him. But when\r\nspring came, he brightened up a little, though he felt far from happy.\r\nAbout that time Mrs. Newton had a letter from Mary, saying that she was\r\ngoing to spend vacation in Maryland with her other aunt, and Tisha for\r\nher escort.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe don\xe2\x80\x99t come here, because she fears I shall pester her life with my\r\nvisits. As she knows I can\xe2\x80\x99t keep away from her, she keeps away from\r\nyou. She hates me. I suppose you, too, will take to hating me, by and\r\nby,\xe2\x80\x9d said Darrell, when he heard that Mary was not coming that summer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo danger of that, William,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Newton replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, there is. You ought to hate me for driving her away. I hate myself\r\nworse than I hate the devil.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWilliam, you mustn\xe2\x80\x99t feel so. It isn\xe2\x80\x99t right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know it. But when did I ever do anything right, I\xe2\x80\x99d like to know? I\r\nwish I could hate her as I hate myself, or as she hates me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWilliam, she does not hate you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you know she don\xe2\x80\x99t?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause she would have told me. She is very truthful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know it. She gave me my walking papers in a jiffy. I wish I could\r\nhate her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWilliam, do you promise not to get angry, if I tell you why Mary\r\ndeclined your offer?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay on. You couldn\xe2\x80\x99t well make a burning furnace any hotter. I am too\r\nmad already.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you. She likes you, but is afraid of you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfraid? afraid?\xe2\x80\x9d said he, aghast\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cwhy! that is awful! I, an object of\r\nfear, when I worship the ground she treads on! But, how? What have I\r\ndone? When did I frighten her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt no particular time; but often you gave her the impression that you\r\nhave a high temper, and she told me, \xe2\x80\x98If I loved Mr. Darrell better than\r\nmy life, I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t marry him, for I could never be happy with a man of\r\na violent temper.\xe2\x80\x99 Then she spoke, too, of her being a Roman Catholic\r\nand you a Protestant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you are a Catholic and uncle is Protestant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly, I think the barrier is not insuperable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo, my temper frightened her! It is awful!\xe2\x80\x9d He mused in silence for a\r\nfew minutes and then left the room.\r\n\r\nAbout an hour after, he returned dressed for traveling, carrying a\r\nsatchel in one hand and a tin box under his arm. He put the box on the\r\ntable, saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAunt Newton, I am going away for a few days. Please take care of this\r\nbox until I return or you hear from me. Good-by!\xe2\x80\x9d and he hurried away,\r\nfor he had only barely time to catch the train going to New York.\r\n\r\nDarrell was in New York for a few hours. He bought a finer suit of\r\nclothes, a very elegant light overcoat, hat and boots, and gloves to\r\nmatch, and thus equipped so elegantly that he hardly recognized himself,\r\nas he surveyed his figure in a large mirror of the furnishing store,\r\nwhere he was so metamorphosed, he took the night train for Washington.\r\n\r\nIt was early on a Sunday morning that Darrell arrived at Washington. He\r\nwent to a hotel, entered his name, took a room, a bath and a breakfast,\r\nand then called a hack to go in search of Mary. He knew that was not an\r\nhour for calling, but he had _business_ with Mary. His was no friendly\r\nvisit; it was a matter of life and death with him.\r\n\r\nHe rang the bell, and presently he heard Tisha\xe2\x80\x99s flapping steps coming.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLud a massa!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed, stepping back. But recovering herself,\r\nsaid with true heartiness\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome in the parlor, please. It is true glad Miss Mary will be to see\r\nye.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think so, Tisha?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know it; no thinking about it, neither. She is going to mass; but\r\nshe\xe2\x80\x99ll see you for a little while, anyway.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOpening the parlor door for Darrell to walk in, Tisha ran up stairs to\r\nMary\xe2\x80\x99s room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh Miss Mary!\xe2\x80\x9d said she, \xe2\x80\x9cguess who is down stairs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t, Tish, being so early and on Sunday, but I heard a man\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nvoice. Is it a gentleman?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou bet; ah! please excuse me, I mean sure as I live it is, and no\r\nother than Mr. Darrell, from New England.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Mary, affecting indifference, but her hands trembled as\r\nshe tied her bonnet strings.\r\n\r\nDarrell knew he must appear self-contained and not in the least\r\nimpetuous, but when he saw those beautiful dark eyes of Mary\xe2\x80\x99s he forgot\r\nall his pretended calmness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs my aunt well?\xe2\x80\x9d Mary began as she came in.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, yes, everybody is well; don\xe2\x80\x99t be alarmed at my coming, I know it\r\nmust seem strange to you. Two days ago I had no idea of coming to\r\nWashington, but Miss Moreneau, your aunt told me you were not coming\r\nNorth this summer, and this news nearly drove me crazy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Mr. Darrell!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait, don\xe2\x80\x99t drive me off yet. Your aunt told me that you refused me\r\nbecause you believe I have a violent temper. Now, I am not going to deny\r\nthat, but this I am going to say\xe2\x80\x94That I have never violated my word, and\r\nnever shall, and I make a most solemn oath to you, that if you will\r\nmarry me you shall never have occasion to be made unhappy or displeased\r\nby my quick anger, because you will only have to remind me of this\r\npledge, and I shall curb my temper, if it kills me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Darrell, I believe you are perfectly sincere in what you say, but a\r\nstrong trait of character is not controlled easily. It is more apt to be\r\nuncontrollable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor God\xe2\x80\x99s sake don\xe2\x80\x99t refuse me, I feel I must kill myself if you spurn\r\nme. I don\xe2\x80\x99t want life without you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t say that,\xe2\x80\x9d Mary said, trying to keep calm, but she felt as if\r\nbeing carried away in spite of herself, by the torrent of his\r\nimpetuosity. She was afraid of him, but she liked him and she liked to\r\nbe loved in that passionate rebellious way of his; she smiled, adding,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwe must postpone this conversation for I must go to church, and it is\r\nquite a long walk there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe carriage that brought me is at the door, take it, and don\xe2\x80\x99t walk,\r\nit is quite warm out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you go with me to church? You see, that is another obstacle; the\r\ndifference of religions.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed, that is no obstacle; your religion tells you to pity me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will talk to Father White about that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen Mary, my beloved, will you give me hope?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd will you really try to control your anger when you feel it is\r\ngetting the mastery over you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will, so help me God,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, lifting his hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake care, that is an oath.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know it, and mean it,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, much moved.\r\n\r\nThey went to church together. After church, Mary had a few moments\r\nconversation with her pastor. She explained everything to him. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you\r\nlove him, my child,\xe2\x80\x9d asked the good father, knowing the human heart only\r\ntoo well. Mary blushed and said\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, father, I believe I do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, send him to see me to-morrow morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDarrell had a long talk with Father White, and promised solemnly not to\r\ncoerce or influence his wife to change her religion, and that should\r\ntheir union be blessed with children, they should be baptized and\r\nbrought up Catholics.\r\n\r\nAnd his union was blessed. Mary made his New England home a paradise,\r\nand eight children, sharing largely their mother\xe2\x80\x99s fine qualities,\r\nfilled to overflowing his cup of happiness.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER II.\xe2\x80\x94_The Don\xe2\x80\x99s View of the Treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo._\r\n\r\n\r\nIf there had been such a thing as communicating by telephone in the days\r\nof \xe2\x80\x9972, and there had been those magic wires spanning the distance\r\nbetween William Darrell\xe2\x80\x99s house in Alameda County and that of Don\r\nMariano Alamar in San Diego County, with power to transmit the human\r\nvoice for five hundred miles, a listener at either end would have heard\r\nvarious discussions upon the same subject, differentiated only by\r\ncircumstances. No magic wires crossed San Francisco bay to bring the\r\nsound of voices to San Diego, but the law of necessity made the Squatter\r\nand the Don, distant as they were\xe2\x80\x94distant in every way, without\r\nreckoning the miles between them\xe2\x80\x94talk quite warmly of the same matter.\r\nThe point of view was of course different, for how could it be\r\notherwise? Darrell thought himself justified, and _authorized_, to \xe2\x80\x9ctake\r\nup lands,\xe2\x80\x9d as he had done before. He had had more than half of\r\nCalifornia\xe2\x80\x99s population on his side, and though the \xe2\x80\x9c_Squatter\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nSovereignty_\xe2\x80\x9d was now rather on the wane, and the \xe2\x80\x9c_squatter vote_\xe2\x80\x9d was\r\nno longer the power, still, the squatters would not abdicate, having yet\r\nmuch to say about election times.\r\n\r\nBut Darrell was no longer the active squatter that he had been. He\r\ncontrolled many votes yet, but in his heart he felt the weight which his\r\nwife\xe2\x80\x99s sad eyes invariably put there when the talk was of litigating\r\nagainst a Mexican land title.\r\n\r\nThis time, however, Darrell honestly meant to take no land but what\r\nbelonged to the United States. His promise to his wife was sincere, yet\r\nhis coming to Southern California had already brought trouble to the\r\nAlamar rancho.\r\n\r\nDon Mariano Alamar was silently walking up and down the front piazza of\r\nhis house at the rancho; his hands listlessly clasped behind and his\r\nhead slightly bent forward in deep thought. He had pushed away to one\r\nside the many arm-chairs and wicker rockers with which the piazza was\r\nfurnished. He wanted a long space to walk. That his meditations were far\r\nfrom agreeable, could easily be seen by the compressed lips, slight\r\nfrown, and sad gaze of his mild and beautiful blue eyes. Sounds of\r\nlaughter, music and dancing came from the parlor; the young people were\r\nentertaining friends from town with their usual gay hospitality, and\r\nenjoying themselves heartily. Don Mariano, though already in his\r\nfiftieth year, was as fond of dancing as his sons and daughters, and not\r\nto see him come in and join the quadrille was so singular that his wife\r\nthought she must come out and inquire what could detain him. He was so\r\nabsorbed in his thoughts that he did not hear her voice calling him\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat keeps you away? Lizzie has been looking for you; she wants you for\r\na partner in the lancers,\xe2\x80\x9d said Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa, putting her arm under that\r\nof her husband, bending her head forward and turning it up to look into\r\nhis eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, stopping short, thus making her husband\r\ncome to a sudden halt. \xe2\x80\x9cI am sure something has happened. Tell me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing, dear wife. Nothing has happened. That is to say, nothing new.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMore squatters?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked. Se\xc3\xb1or Alamar bent his head slightly, in\r\naffirmative reply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMore coming, you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, wife; more. Those two friends of squatters Mathews and Hager, who\r\nwere here last year to locate claims and went away, did not abandon\r\ntheir claims, but only went away to bring proselytes and their families,\r\nand a large invoice of them will arrive on to-morrow\xe2\x80\x99s steamer. The\r\nworst of it all is, that among the new comers is that terrible and most\r\ndangerous squatter William Darrell, who some years ago gave so much\r\ntrouble to the Spanish people in Napa and Sonoma Counties, by locating\r\nclaims there. John Gasbang wrote to Hogsden that besides Darrell, there\r\nwill be six or seven other men bringing their families, so that there\r\nwill be more rifles for my cattle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, didn\xe2\x80\x99t we hear that Darrell was no longer a squatter, that he is\r\nrich and living quietly in Alameda?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, we heard that, and it is true. He is quite well off, but Gasbang\r\nand Miller and Mathews went and told him that my rancho had been\r\nrejected, and that it is near enough to town to become valuable, as soon\r\nas we have a railroad. Darrell believed it, and is coming to locate\r\nhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStrange that Darrell should believe such men; I suppose he does not\r\nknow how low they are.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe ought to know them, for they were his teamsters when he crossed the\r\nplains in \xe2\x80\x9948. That is, Miller, Mathews, Hughes and Hager, were his\r\nteamsters, and Gasbang was their cook\xe2\x80\x94the cook for the hired men. Mrs.\r\nDarrell had a colored woman who cooked for the Darrell family; she\r\ndespised Gasbang\xe2\x80\x99s cooking as we despise his character, I suppose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDo\xc3\xb1a Josefa was silent, and holding to her husband\xe2\x80\x99s arm, took a turn\r\nwith him up and down the piazza.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it possible that there is no law to protect us; to protect our\r\nproperty; what does your lawyer say about obtaining redress or\r\nprotection; is there no hope?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, with a sigh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cProtection for our land, or for our cattle, you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor both, as we get it for neither,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the matter of our land, we have to await for the attorney general,\r\nat Washington, to decide.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLizzie was telling Elvira, yesterday, that her uncle Lawrence is a\r\nfriend of several influential people in Washington, and that George can\r\nget him to interest himself in having your title decided.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, as George is to marry my daughter, he would be the last man from\r\nwhom I would ask a favor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is that I hear about not asking a favor from me?\xe2\x80\x9d said George\r\nMechlin, coming out on the piazza with Elvira on his arm, having just\r\nfinished a waltz\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI am interested to know why you would not ask it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know why, my dear boy. It isn\xe2\x80\x99t exactly the thing to bother you\r\nwith my disagreeable business.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd why not? And who has a better right? And why should it be a bother\r\nto me to help you in any way I can? My father spoke to me about a\r\ndismissal of an appeal, and I made a note of it. Let me see, I think I\r\nhave it in my pocket now,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94said George, feeling in his breast pocket for\r\nhis memorandum book,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cyes, here it is,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98For uncle to write to the\r\nattorney general about dismissing the appeal taken by the squatters in\r\nthe Alamar grant, against Don Mariano\xe2\x80\x99s title, which was approved.\xe2\x80\x99 Is\r\nthat the correct idea? I only made this note to ask you for further\r\nparticulars.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have it exactly. When I give you the number of the case, it is all\r\nthat you need say to your uncle. What I want is to have the appeal\r\ndismissed, of course, but if the attorney general does not see fit to do\r\nso, he can, at least, remand back the case for a new trial. Anything\r\nrather than this killing suspense. Killing literally, for while we are\r\nwaiting to have my title settled, the _settlers_ (I don\xe2\x80\x99t mean to make\r\npuns), are killing my cattle by the hundred head, and I cannot stop\r\nthem.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut are there no laws to protect property in California?\xe2\x80\x9d George asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, some sort of laws, which in my case seem more intended to help the\r\nlaw-breakers than to protect the law-abiding,\xe2\x80\x9d Don Mariano replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow so? Is there no law to punish the thieves who kill your cattle?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are some enactments so obviously intended to favor one class of\r\ncitizens against another class, that to call them laws is an insult to\r\nlaw, but such as they are, we must submit to them. By those laws any man\r\ncan come to my land, for instance, plant ten acres of grain, without any\r\nfence, and then catch my cattle which, seeing the green grass without a\r\nfence, will go to eat it. Then he puts them in a \xe2\x80\x98_corral_\xe2\x80\x99 and makes me\r\npay damages and so much per head for keeping them, and costs of legal\r\nproceedings and many other trumped up expenses, until for such little\r\nfields of grain I may be obliged to pay thousands of dollars. Or, if the\r\ngrain fields are large enough to bring more money by keeping the cattle\r\naway, then the settler shoots the cattle at any time without the least\r\nhesitation, only taking care that no one sees him in the act of firing\r\nupon the cattle. He might stand behind a bush or tree and fire, but then\r\nhe is not seen. No one can swear that they saw him actually kill the\r\ncattle, and no jury can convict him, for although the dead animals may\r\nbe there, lying on the ground shot, still no one saw the settler kill\r\nthem. And so it is all the time. I must pay damages and expenses of\r\nlitigation, or my cattle get killed almost every day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut this is infamous. Haven\xe2\x80\x99t you\xe2\x80\x94the cattle owners\xe2\x80\x94tried to have some\r\nlaw enacted that will protect your property?\xe2\x80\x9d George asked. \xe2\x80\x9cIt seems to\r\nme that could be done.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt could be done, perhaps, if our positions were reversed, and the\r\nSpanish people\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98_the natives_\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94were the planters of the grain fields,\r\nand the Americans were the owners of the cattle. But as we, the\r\nSpaniards, are the owners of the Spanish\xe2\x80\x94or Mexican\xe2\x80\x94land grants and also\r\nthe owners of the cattle ranchos, our State legislators will not make\r\nany law to protect cattle. They make laws \xe2\x80\x98_to protect agriculture_\xe2\x80\x99\r\n(they say proudly), which means to drive to the wall all owners of\r\ncattle ranchos. I am told that at this session of the legislature a law\r\nmore strict yet will be passed, which will be ostensibly \xe2\x80\x98to protect\r\nagriculture,\xe2\x80\x99 but in reality to destroy cattle and ruin the native\r\nCalifornians. The agriculture of this State does not require legislative\r\nprotection. Such pretext is absurd.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought that the rights of the Spanish people were protected by our\r\ntreaty with Mexico,\xe2\x80\x9d George said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMexico did not pay much attention to the future welfare of the children\r\nshe left to their fate in the hands of a nation which had no sympathies\r\nfor us,\xe2\x80\x9d said Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa, feelingly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI remember,\xe2\x80\x9d calmly said Don Mariano, \xe2\x80\x9cthat when I first read the text\r\nof the treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo, I felt a bitter resentment against\r\nmy people; against Mexico, the mother country, who abandoned us\xe2\x80\x94her\r\nchildren\xe2\x80\x94with so slight a provision of obligatory stipulations for\r\nprotection. But afterwards, upon mature reflection, I saw that Mexico\r\ndid as much as could have been reasonably expected at the time. In the\r\nvery preamble of the treaty the spirit of peace and friendship, which\r\nanimated both nations, was carefully made manifest. That spirit was to\r\nbe the _foundation_ of the relations between the conqueror and\r\nconquered. How could Mexico have foreseen then that when scarcely half a\r\ndozen years should have elapsed the trusted conquerors would, \xe2\x80\x98_In\r\nCongress Assembled_,\xe2\x80\x99 pass laws which were to be retroactive upon the\r\ndefenceless, helpless, conquered people, in order to despoil them? The\r\ntreaty said that our rights would be the same as those enjoyed by all\r\nother American citizens. But, you see, Congress takes very good care not\r\nto enact retroactive laws for Americans; laws to take away from American\r\ncitizens the property which they hold _now_, already, with a recognized\r\nlegal title. No, indeed. But they do so quickly enough with us\xe2\x80\x94with us,\r\nthe Spano-Americans, who were to enjoy equal rights, mind you, according\r\nto the treaty of peace. This is what seems to me a breach of faith,\r\nwhich Mexico could neither presuppose nor prevent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is nothing else, I am sorry and ashamed to say,\xe2\x80\x9d George said. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nnever knew much about the treaty with Mexico, but I never imagined we\r\nhad acted so badly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think but few Americans know or believe to what extent we have been\r\nwronged by Congressional action. And truly, I believe that Congress\r\nitself did not anticipate the effect of its laws upon us, and how we\r\nwould be despoiled, we, the conquered people,\xe2\x80\x9d said Don Mariano, sadly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is the duty of law-givers to foresee the effect of the laws they\r\nimpose upon people,\xe2\x80\x9d said Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat I don\xe2\x80\x99t deny, but I fear that the conquered have always but a weak\r\nvoice, which nobody hears,\xe2\x80\x9d said Don Mariano. \xe2\x80\x9cWe have had no one to\r\nspeak for us. By the treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo the American nation\r\npledged its honor to respect our land titles just the same as Mexico\r\nwould have done. Unfortunately, however, the discovery of gold brought\r\nto California the riff-raff of the world, and with it a horde of\r\nland-sharks, all possessing the privilege of voting, and most of them\r\ncoveting our lands, for which they very quickly began to clamor. There\r\nwas, and still is, plenty of good government land, which any one can\r\ntake. But no. The forbidden fruit is the sweetest. They do not want\r\ngovernment land. They want the land of the Spanish people, because we\r\n\xe2\x80\x98have too much,\xe2\x80\x99 they say. So, to win their votes, the votes of the\r\nsquatters, our representatives in Congress helped to pass laws declaring\r\nall lands in California open to pre-emption, as in Louisiana, for\r\ninstance. Then, as a coating of whitewash to the stain on the nation\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhonor, a \xe2\x80\x98land commission\xe2\x80\x99 was established to examine land titles.\r\nBecause, having pledged the national word to respect our rights, it\r\nwould be an act of despoliation, besides an open violation of pledged\r\nhonor, to take the lands without some pretext of a legal process. So\r\nthen, we became obliged to present our titles before the said land\r\ncommission to be examined and approved or rejected. While these legal\r\nproceedings are going on, the squatters locate their claims and raise\r\ncrops on our lands, which they convert into money to fight our titles.\r\nBut don\xe2\x80\x99t let me, with my disagreeable subject spoil your dance. Go back\r\nto your lancers, and tell Lizzie to excuse me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Don Mariano.\r\n\r\nLizzie would not excuse him. With the privilege of a future\r\ndaughter-in-law, she insisted that Don Mariano should be her partner in\r\nthe lancers, which would be a far pleasanter occupation than to be\r\nwalking up and down the porch thinking about squatters.\r\n\r\nDon Mariano therefore followed Lizzie to their place in the dance.\r\nMercedes sat at the piano to play for them. The other couples took their\r\nrespective positions.\r\n\r\nThe well-balanced mind and kindly spirit of Don Mariano soon yielded to\r\nthe genial influences surrounding him. He would not bring his trouble to\r\nmar the pleasure of others. He danced with his children as gaily as the\r\ngayest. He insisted that Mr. Mechlin, too, should dance, and this\r\ngentleman graciously yielded and led Elvira through a quadrille,\r\nprotesting that he had not danced for twenty years.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have not danced because you were sick, but now you are well. Don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nbe lazy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Mechlin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou would be paying to San Diego climate a very poor compliment by\r\nrefusing to dance now,\xe2\x80\x9d George added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is so, papa. Show us how well you feel,\xe2\x80\x9d Lizzie said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall have to dance a hornpipe to do that,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Mechlin answered,\r\nlaughing.\r\n\r\nTo understand this remark better, the reader must know that Mr. James\r\nMechlin had come to San Diego, four years previously, a living skeleton,\r\nnot expected to last another winter. He had lost his health by a too\r\nclose application to business, and when he sought rest and relaxation\r\nhis constitution seemed permanently undermined. He tried the climate of\r\nFlorida. He spent several years in Italy and in the south of France, but\r\nhe felt no better. At last, believing his malady incurable, he returned\r\nto his New York home to die. In New York a friend, who also had been an\r\ninvalid, but whose health had been restored in Southern California,\r\nadvised him to try the salubrious air of San Diego. With but little\r\nhope, and only to please his family, Mr. Mechlin came to San Diego, and\r\nhis health improved so rapidly that he made up his mind to buy a country\r\nplace and make San Diego his home. William Mathews heard of this, and\r\noffered to sell his place on what Mr. Mechlin thought very moderate\r\nterms. A lawyer was employed to pass upon the title, and on his\r\nrecommendation the purchase was made. Mr. Mechlin had the Mathews house\r\nmoved back near the barn, and a new and much larger one built. When this\r\nwas finished the Mechlins moved into it, and Mr. Mechlin devoted himself\r\nto cultivating trees and flowers, and his health was bettered every day.\r\nThis was the compensation to his wife and two daughters for exiling\r\nthemselves from New York; for it was exile to Caroline and Lizzie to\r\ngive up their fine house in New York City to come and live on a\r\nCalifornia rancho.\r\n\r\nSoon, however, these two young ladies passed their time more pleasantly,\r\nafter making the acquaintance of the Alamar family, and soon their\r\nacquaintance ripened into friendship, to be made closer by the intended\r\nmarriage of Gabriel\xe2\x80\x94Don Mariano\xe2\x80\x99s eldest son\xe2\x80\x94to Lizzie. Shortly after,\r\nGeorge\xe2\x80\x94Mr. Mechlin\xe2\x80\x99s only son\xe2\x80\x94came on a visit, and when he returned to\r\nNew York he was already engaged to Elvira, third daughter of Se\xc3\xb1or\r\nAlamar.\r\n\r\nNow, George Mechlin was making his second visit to his family. He had\r\nfound New York so very dull and stupid on his return from California\r\nthat when Christmas was approaching he told his uncle and aunt\xe2\x80\x94with whom\r\nhe lived\xe2\x80\x94that he wanted to go and spend Christmas and New Year\xe2\x80\x99s Day\r\nwith his family in California.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well; I wish I could go with you. Give my love to James, and tell\r\nhim I am delighted at his getting so well,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Lawrence Mechlin said,\r\nand George had his leave of absence. Mr. Lawrence Mechlin was president\r\nof the bank of which George was cashier, so it was not difficult for him\r\nto get the assistant cashier to attend to his duties when he was away,\r\nparticularly as the assistant cashier himself was George\xe2\x80\x99s most devoted\r\nfriend. George could have only twelve days in California, but to see\r\nElvira for even so short a time he would have traveled a much longer\r\ndistance.\r\n\r\nMr. James Mechlin affirmed repeatedly that he owed his improved health\r\nto the genial society of the Alamar family as much as to the genial\r\nclimate of San Diego County. Mr. Mechlin, however, was not the only one\r\nwho had paid the same tribute to that most delightful family, the most\r\ncharming of which\xe2\x80\x94the majority vote said\xe2\x80\x94was Don Mariano himself. His\r\nnobility of character and great kindness of heart were well known to\r\neverybody.\r\n\r\nThe Alamar family was quite patriarchal in size, if the collateral\r\nbranches be taken into account, for there were many brothers, nephews\r\nand nieces. These, however, lived in the adjoining rancho, and yet\r\nanother branch in Lower California, in Mexico. Don Mariano\xe2\x80\x99s own\r\nimmediate family was composed of his wife and six children, two sons and\r\nfour daughters.\r\n\r\nAll of these, as we have seen, were having a dance. The music was\r\nfurnished by the young ladies themselves, taking their turn at the\r\npiano, assisted by Madam Halier (Mercedes\xe2\x80\x99 French governess), who was\r\nalways ready to play for the girls to dance. Besides the Mechlins, there\r\nwere three or four young gentlemen from town, but there were so many\r\nAlamares (brothers, nieces and nephews, besides) that the room seemed\r\nquite well filled. Such family gatherings were frequent, making the\r\nAlamar house very gay and pleasant.\r\n\r\nGeorge Mechlin would have liked to prolong his visit, but he could not.\r\nHe consoled himself looking forward to the ninth of June, when he would\r\ncome again to make a visit of two months\xe2\x80\x99 duration. On his return East,\r\nbefore renewing his duties at the bank, he went to Washington to see\r\nabout the dismissal of the appeal. Unfortunately, the attorney general\r\nhad to absent himself about that time, and the matter being left with\r\nthe solicitor general, nothing was done. George explained to Don Mariano\r\nhow the matter was delayed, and his case remained undecided yet for\r\nanother year longer.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER III.\xe2\x80\x94_Pre-empting under the Law._\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll aboard for San Diego!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted a voice from a wagon, as it rumbled\r\npast Darrell, who walked leisurely with a satchel in his hand, swinging\r\nit unconsciously, lost in thought. He looked up and saw that the wagon\r\nwhence the voice came carried ten or twelve men, sitting on trunks and\r\npackages and carpet-bags. These men Mathews and Gasbang had presented to\r\nhim, saying that they were settlers already residing at the Alamar\r\nrancho, and others who were going down to take up claims, at the same\r\ntime that he would locate his. Darrell looked at his future neighbors\r\nwith feelings of anything but pleasure. The broad, vulgar face of\r\nGasbang, with its square jaws, gray beard, closely clipped, but never\r\nshaved, his compressed, thin, bloodless lips, his small, pale, restless\r\neyes and flat nose, Darrell soon recognized, though the wagon was going\r\nrapidly. Mathews\xe2\x80\x99 visage was equally noticeable for its ugliness, though\r\nof a different type; for his face was long and shaved; his nose was\r\npinched and peaked and red; his cheeks were flabby; and his long, oily,\r\ndusty, hair dragged over his neck in matted, meshy locks, while a\r\nconstant frown settled on his brow. As he was broad-shouldered and\r\nrather tall, his face seemed made for some other man much weaker than\r\nhimself. His face looked mean and discontented, while his body seemed\r\nstrong and self-reliant.\r\n\r\nThe wagon had arrived and gone away, and the men had walked aboard the\r\nboat, when Darrell, still swinging his satchel abstractedly, stood on\r\nthe wharf looking at the steamer as if not quite resolved to go. He felt\r\nno sympathy, no liking, for any of those men with whom he was now\r\nassociated.\r\n\r\nIt was different to have Gasbang as his hired man, as before, but now he\r\nwas not under orders, and was much older. Years, moreover, had not\r\nimproved his low nature. Darrell had no higher opinion of the others. He\r\nwas sure these were not the sort of people whom his wife would like to\r\nhave for neighbors. He felt self-accused and irresolute. A shout from\r\nGasbang, who was observing him from the steamer\xe2\x80\x99s deck, made Darrell\r\nlook up quickly, ashamed of having betrayed his irresolution. \xe2\x80\x9cI can\r\nreturn immediately, if things don\xe2\x80\x99t suit me,\xe2\x80\x9d he thought, walking\r\ntowards the gang-plank.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome on. Your luggage is all aboard, I took care of it,\xe2\x80\x9d Gasbang said,\r\ncoming to meet him. He snatched Darrell\xe2\x80\x99s satchel, in friendly\r\nobsequiousness, to carry it for him. \xe2\x80\x9cCome along; you\xe2\x80\x99ll be left,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nhe, and Darrell followed him, half-disgusted at his vulgar\r\nofficiousness. \xe2\x80\x9cI got your berth for you. The steamer is so crowded,\r\nthat men have to be crammed into rooms by the bunch, so you and I and\r\nMathews must room together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is all right,\xe2\x80\x9d said Darrell, with a shiver of disgust, and went to\r\ntake a seat on deck where he could be alone.\r\n\r\nThe bustle and hurry of getting off was over at last, and the steamer\r\nwas furrowing her way through the spacious bay of San Francisco towards\r\nthe Golden Gate. Groups of passengers stood here and there, admiring the\r\nbeautiful harbor and its surrounding country. Darrell sat alone, fixing\r\nhis gaze upon the receding verdure of Alameda County. Above that green,\r\nundulating line of diminishing hills, which seemed to fly from him,\r\nDarrell could see plainly one face, one form, beautiful to him as none\r\nother could be, the face and form of his wife, his beloved Mary. This\r\nwas the first time he had ever left her for any longer time than a two\r\ndays\xe2\x80\x99 absence, since they were married. Now he might be absent several\r\nmonths, for if he decided to locate in San Diego County, he would first\r\nbuild a house before he sent for his family. He would first send for\r\nClarence\xe2\x80\x94his eldest son\xe2\x80\x94and then, when a comfortable home was prepared,\r\nthe family would come.\r\n\r\nThe voyage down the coast was made safely. Darrell had managed to keep\r\naway from his fellow-travelers, to think of home unmolested.\r\n\r\nIt was a bright morning of January, 1872, when he stood far forward,\r\nwatching the course of the steamer Orizaba, as she made her way around\r\nPoint Loma, then between Ballast Point and the sandy peninsula, and\r\npassing by La Playa, came in sight of San Diego city.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere we are,\xe2\x80\x9d said John Gasbang; \xe2\x80\x9chow do you like the looks of our\r\nlittle city, Mr. Darrell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well; it is larger than I supposed, and the site of it seems very\r\npleasant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPleasant! I should say it was. A perfect slope, sir, as gentle and\r\nregular as if made to order. The best drained city in the world, sir,\r\nwhen we put in sewers. Too poor for that, yet, sir, but we are coming to\r\nit, sir, growing, growing, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen we get the railroad,\xe2\x80\x9d added Mathews, with a mouth full of tobacco,\r\nspitting profusely on the deck.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly, and we\xe2\x80\x99ll soon have that. Our news from Washington is very\r\nencouraging. Tom Scott will visit us this summer,\xe2\x80\x9d Gasbang said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI like a town with plenty of trees,\xe2\x80\x9d said Darrell, with his gaze fixed\r\non the approaching panorama, thinking that his wife would be pleased\r\nwith the place, she being so fond of trees. \xe2\x80\x9cI had no idea you had so\r\nmany trees about you. Many are small, yet, but all seem healthy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd health-giving trees, they are, too. Most of them are eucalyptus and\r\npepper trees, the healthiest in the world. You never hear of any\r\nmalarial fevers in San Diego, sir, never. Our perfect climate, the fine\r\nsloping ground of our town site, our eucalyptus trees, sea breezes and\r\nmountain air, make San Diego a most healthy little city,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gasbang.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is an excellent recommendation, as life is not worth having\r\nwithout health,\xe2\x80\x9d Darrell observed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have it here,\xe2\x80\x9d Hughes said. \xe2\x80\x9cA man has to be very imprudent not to\r\nkeep well in our climate, sir. All we want now is a little stimulus of\r\nbusiness prosperity, and the railroad is sure to bring us that. Then San\r\nDiego will be the best place on the coast for a residence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe loud report of a cannon, close by, made Darrell jump and look around\r\nquickly, not knowing what that explosion could mean.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is our visiting card to the people of San Diego, to announce our\r\ncoming,\xe2\x80\x9d said the captain, laughingly. \xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry it startled you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is nothing. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know I had nerves. I believe that is what\r\nwomen call it. I was not expecting such a military salute,\xe2\x80\x9d Darrell\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO yes, we always give it. The San Diego people are very military. At\r\nleast, I should say the settlers on Se\xc3\xb1or Alamar\xe2\x80\x99s rancho are, as I hear\r\nthey practice rifle shooting there all the time,\xe2\x80\x9d the captain said,\r\nlooking at Mathews and Gasbang.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is a shot at us,\xe2\x80\x9d Gasbang answered, laughing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it is a blank cartridge, meant not to hurt,\xe2\x80\x9d the captain replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe rifle practice is in dark nights,\xe2\x80\x9d said a young Spaniard, who had\r\nbeen listening at what was said by the others.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOr in the daytime, if the cattle deserve it,\xe2\x80\x9d Mathews said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is very creditable and brave, to shoot tame cows,\xe2\x80\x9d the Spaniard\r\nrejoined.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps you had better come and try it,\xe2\x80\x9d Mathews returned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you. It is the mischievous brutes I would like to shoot, not the\r\ngood, useful cattle;\xe2\x80\x9d so saying, the Spaniard walked away, followed by\r\nthe scowls of the settlers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is impudence for you,\xe2\x80\x9d Gasbang exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThose greasers ain\xe2\x80\x99t half crushed yet. We have to tame them like they\r\ndo their mustangs, or shoot them, as we shoot their cattle,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMathews.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO, no. No such violent means are necessary. All we have to do is to\r\ntake their lands, and finish their cattle,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hughes, sneeringly,\r\nlooking at Darrell for approval. But he did not get it. Darrell did not\r\ncare for the Spanish population of California, but he did not approve of\r\nshooting cattle in the way which the foregoing conversation indicated.\r\nTo do this, was useless cruelty and useless waste of valuable property,\r\nno matter to whom it might belong. To destroy it was a loss to the\r\nState. It was folly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy must cattle be shot? Can\xe2\x80\x99t they be kept off, away from your crops\r\nwithout shooting them?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot always. At first, that is, for the first three years after we\r\nlocated our claims,\xe2\x80\x9d Gasbang said; \xe2\x80\x9cwe had to shoot them all the time.\r\nNow the Don has sold a good many, or sent them to the mountains, so that\r\nfew have been killed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose fencing would be too expensive.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPhew! It would be ruinous, impossible,\xe2\x80\x9d Mathews said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Mechlin is the only one who has attempted to put up any fences,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nRomeo said, who had been listening in silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe did so, because he is an old hypocrite,\xe2\x80\x9d Mathews said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause his daughter Lizzie is going to marry Gabriel Alamar, and of\r\ncourse, they have to be on friendly terms,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hughes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat ain\xe2\x80\x99t the reason. He fenced a hundred acres the first year, and he\r\nnever sows outside, so that he\xe2\x80\x99s not at all troubled by the Don\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncattle,\xe2\x80\x9d said Romeo.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut Gabriel is going to marry Lizzie all the same, and the two families\r\nare as thick as can be. Old Mechlin has gone back on us. I wish he would\r\ngo away,\xe2\x80\x9d Mathews said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy should he go? He paid a very good price for his farm, and has made\r\nmany improvements,\xe2\x80\x9d said Romeo.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho did he buy from?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Darrell.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrom me. I sold him that claim, and took up another a mile up the\r\nvalley,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mathews.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd a good bargain it was, too,\xe2\x80\x9d Romeo observed.\r\n\r\nMathews gave him a black look, but made no answer.\r\n\r\nThe steamer had now reached the wharf. The deck was filled with\r\npassengers and their baggage ready for shore. Pittikin, with wife and\r\ndaughters blonde and freckled, and Hughes, with his wife and daughters\r\ndark and gypsy-looking, were all there, ready for their drive to Alamar.\r\n\r\nThere were several wagons, light and heavy, waiting to convey the\r\nnewly-arrived and their luggage to the Alamar rancho. Darrell, having\r\nhis choice of conveyances, preferred to go in a light wagon with Romeo\r\nHancock, but Gasbang and Mathews joined him. Miller and Hager had come\r\nto meet their prodigal sons, who had been in San Francisco for several\r\nmonths, when they had permission to remain only a few weeks. But they\r\nhad fallen into Peter Roper\xe2\x80\x99s company, and that individual had\r\nrepresented the fascinations of whiskey most alluringly to them,\r\nadvising them to have a good time now that they had the opportunity.\r\nThey yielded to the tempter, and now had returned home like repentant\r\nprodigals.\r\n\r\nIn a few hours Darrell was driving by Don Mariano Alamar\xe2\x80\x99s house, a\r\none-story mansion on a low hill, with a broad piazza in front, and in\r\nthe interior a court formed by two wings, and a row of rooms variously\r\noccupied at its back. That the house was commodious, Darrell could see.\r\nThere was a flower garden in front. At the back there were several\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_corrales_\xe2\x80\x9d for cattle and horses. At the foot of the hill, on the\r\nleft, there was an orchard, and some grain fields enclosed with good\r\nfences.\r\n\r\nDarrell took notice of all these particulars. He also noticed that there\r\nwere females on the front piazza. He was taken to see the best\r\nunoccupied lands to make his selection. He ran his practiced eye over\r\nthe valley from the highest point on the hill. He then came to the next\r\nbench; he stopped there, also, and finally came to the broad slope of\r\nthe foothills.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I\xe2\x80\x99ll locate here,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, \xe2\x80\x9cif no one else has already filed a\r\nclaim to this land.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis he said to his fellow-settlers, all being present, addressing all.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sure I have no objection,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hughes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNor I, neither,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gasbang. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you say, Pittikin and Mathews?\r\nDo you know _if_ this land is located, or who done it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMathews shook his head in the negative, and kept on chewing his tobacco\r\nin silence.\r\n\r\nPittikin said, \xe2\x80\x9cI reckon nobody is located here, and if they _done it_,\r\nwhy don\xe2\x80\x99t they leave stakes? They leave no stakes, no notice to\r\nsettlers; they can\xe2\x80\x99t make any row if somebody else takes the land.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I want to respect everybody\xe2\x80\x99s right; so I want you all to bear\r\nwitness, that I found no stakes or notices of anybody. I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to\r\njump anybody\xe2\x80\x99s claim; I want a fair deal. I shall locate two claims\r\nhere\xe2\x80\x94one in my own name and one for my oldest son, Clarence,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nDarrell.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll take 320 acres?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Hughes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, 320 acres,\xe2\x80\x94according to law,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Darrell.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right. Let us measure them now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gasbang. \xe2\x80\x9cWe have time to\r\nmark the limits and put the corner stakes. I have a cord here in my\r\nwagon, which is a chain\xe2\x80\x99s length. That will do the business.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat will do temporarily, I suppose; but I\xe2\x80\x99ll have the two claims\r\nproperly surveyed afterwards according to law,\xe2\x80\x9d Darrell said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course, you will. We all know you will do the fair thing by\r\neverybody, and follow the law strictly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hughes. In which opinion\r\nall concurred.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you all made your selections?\xe2\x80\x9d Darrell asked Hughes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; Pittikin and I will locate near Hancock. We like that valley; it\r\nis further off, but better soil,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hughes. \xe2\x80\x9cMy oldest boy will put a\r\nclaim near me, and Miller\xe2\x80\x99s two boys have staked theirs also. I think\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99ll like that location better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad you like it. I think this is good enough soil for me,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nDarrell said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is good enough for anybody. The whole rancho is all good soil. Let\r\nus put the stakes now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gasbang; and assisted by Mathews, Romeo\r\nHancock and Sumner Pittikin, Darrell proceeded by making a rough guess\r\nto measure 320 acres (more or less), and put the corner stakes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is what I call business,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gasbang, carrying cheerfully one\r\nend of the rope used for measurement; \xe2\x80\x9cand all inside of the law. That\r\nis the beauty of it\xe2\x80\x94all perfectly lawful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd so it was.\r\n\r\nThe stakes having been placed, Darrell felt satisfied. Next day he would\r\nhave the claim properly filed, and in due time a surveyor would measure\r\nthem. All would be done \xe2\x80\x9caccording to law,\xe2\x80\x9d and in this easy way more\r\nland was taken from its legitimate owner.\r\n\r\nThis certainly was a more simple way of appropriating the property of\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_the conquered_\xe2\x80\x9d than in the days of Alaric or Hannibal.\r\n\r\nThere would have been bloodshed then. Now tears only flowed; silent\r\ntears of helpless discouragement; of a presentiment of impending\r\ndesolation.\r\n\r\nSadly Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa and her daughters had witnessed from the half-closed\r\nshutters of their bedroom windows Mr. Darrell\xe2\x80\x99s performance, and fully\r\nanticipated serious trouble therefrom.\r\n\r\nDon Mariano Alamar, Gabriel and Victoriano\xe2\x80\x94his two sons\xe2\x80\x94had also\r\nsilently witnessed Mr. Darrell\xe2\x80\x99s _lawful_ appropriation of their own\r\nproperty. Gabriel was pale and calm. Victoriano was biting his lips, and\r\nhis face was flushed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe government has for sale hundreds of millions of acres, but yet\r\nthese men must come and take my land, as if there was no other,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nDon Mariano, sadly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd as we pay the taxes on the land that they will cultivate, our taxes\r\nwill double next year,\xe2\x80\x9d Gabriel added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUndoubtedly. That climax to injustice has been the most fatal of all\r\nthe hardships imposed upon us. George could not believe me when I told\r\nhim that we (the land-owners) have to pay the taxes on the land\r\ncultivated by the pre-emptors, and upon all the improvements they make\r\nand enjoy. When he at last understood that such unfair laws did exist,\r\nhe was amazed, but understood then why the settlers wished to prolong\r\nlitigation, since it is \xe2\x80\x98_the natives_\xe2\x80\x99 who must bear the burden of\r\ntaxation, while the titles are in the courts, and thus the pre-emptors\r\nhold the land free.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish we were squatters,\xe2\x80\x9d Victoriano remarked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDuring litigation, yes; but there have been cases where honest men\r\nhave, in good faith, taken lands as squatters, and after all, had to\r\ngive them up. No, I don\xe2\x80\x99t blame the squatters; they are at times like\r\nourselves, victims of a wrong legislation, which unintentionally cuts\r\nboth ways. They were set loose upon us, but a law without equity recoils\r\nupon them more cruelly. Then we are all sufferers, all victims of a\r\ndefective legislation and subverted moral principles.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IV.\xe2\x80\x94_Efforts to Right the Wrong._\r\n\r\n\r\nDarrell was not the man to make any delay in putting into practice a\r\nproject, when once adopted. He therefore immediately wrote home saying\r\nthat he \xe2\x80\x9chad located,\xe2\x80\x9d and wished Clarence to come down as soon as home\r\nmatters permitted it. All the crops must be in first, so that Everett\r\nand Webster could take care of the farm when Clarence left. They had two\r\ngood farm hands and a man to take care of the dairy, but still, Darrell\r\nmade his boys give their personal attention to all the work on the farm.\r\nHe wrote to Clarence that he would build a small house quickly, which\r\nafterwards could be used for the hired men, and would wait until he came\r\ndown to begin building their dwelling house. That he would level the\r\nground for the house, sink a couple of wells and put up two windmills,\r\nthe running stream not being sufficient.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I had better buy the lumber for the house up here and charter a\r\nschooner to send it down,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence said to his mother, after reading\r\nhis father\xe2\x80\x99s letter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid he say anything to you about the condition of the title?\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs.\r\nDarrell asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a word. I suppose the land is vacant,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence replied. Mrs.\r\nDarrell shook her head, as if in doubt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want you to see to that, before there is any house built in which I\r\nshall be expected to reside,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cThe first thing you do when you\r\nget there is to inquire whether the land has been finally rejected and\r\nthere is no litigation for it. If there is, I want you to pay for it to\r\nthe owner. And if he will not or cannot sell, write to me at once.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, mother, I shall do as you say, and I assure you I do not\r\nwish father to take up any land claimed by any one under a Mexican\r\ntitle. I think those Spanish people ought to be allowed to keep the land\r\nthat their government gave them. We ought not to have made any laws that\r\nwould place their titles in a bad light and be questioned. We should\r\nhave accepted the legality they had before their own Mexican government,\r\nwithout making some other legality requisite, to please ourselves,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nClarence said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat has always been my opinion, but I have failed to convince your\r\nfather. However, with our combined efforts, we might dissuade him from\r\nhis present way of thinking,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Darrell.\r\n\r\nClarence would not be able to leave home for a few weeks yet. In the\r\nmeantime, his father had not been idle, he had lost no time in carrying\r\nout his plans, and shortly after making his \xe2\x80\x9clocation\xe2\x80\x9d in the manner\r\ndescribed, he had several men engaged in different employments at his\r\nplace. When he had already begun building the small house, of which he\r\nspoke in his letter to Clarence, Don Mariano, accompanied by his two\r\nsons, rode up to the place where he was then superintending his workmen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood morning, Mr. Darrell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Don Mariano.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood morning,\xe2\x80\x9d Darrell answered, laconically.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan I speak a few words with you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, going a few steps nearer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see you have taken up some land here, and I suppose you think it is\r\ngovernment land, but if so, you are misinformed. This land belongs to\r\nme,\xe2\x80\x9d Don Mariano said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy is it reported rejected then? I have seen the law report, stating\r\nthat your title was rejected.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I know that such is the case. For some mistake or other the entry\r\nwas made placing my title in the list of those rejected, but I assure\r\nyou that it is a mistake. My title is now before the attorney general in\r\nWashington, because, having been approved, the settlers took an appeal.\r\nIf the attorney general sustains the appeal, I suppose he will remand\r\nthe case for a new trial, but I have reasons to suppose he will dismiss\r\nthe appeal and affirm the decision of the District Court in my favor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will see about that,\xe2\x80\x9d Darrell said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUndoubtedly we will; meantime I thought it was best to undeceive you,\r\nand give you warning that you are building on my land.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour land if you get it,\xe2\x80\x9d was the answer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you knew the condition of my title I don\xe2\x80\x99t think that you would\r\ndoubt that this land is mine. However, all I wish to do is to prevent\r\nyou from spending money here and then naturally get into litigation with\r\nme to defend your property,\xe2\x80\x9d said Don Mariano.\r\n\r\nDarrell thought of his wife, and her earnest injunctions. He wished to\r\nkeep his promise to her. He said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf the courts say that this land rightfully belongs to you, I shall pay\r\nyou for your land or vacate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, Mr. Darrell, you will get me into litigation with you, and I wish\r\nto avoid that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I shall not get you into any law suit with me. I shall buy your\r\nland or leave.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, Mr. Darrell, I shall rely on your word. I shall remember\r\nwhat you say; please do the same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not in the habit of forgetting what I say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDon Mariano and his two sons lifted their hats, bowed slightly, turned\r\ntheir horses\xe2\x80\x99 heads and moved off.\r\n\r\nDarrell returned their bow, muttering to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cThey take off their\r\nhats and bow like gentlemen, anyway.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhile he was talking with Don Mariano, Mathews, Hughes, Gasbang, Miller\r\nand Pittikin had come. They heard all that was said and looked\r\ndisappointed. They evidently had counted upon Darrell to help them to\r\nfight the rightful owner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid I understand you to say to the Don that you will not maintain your\r\nclaim, if the attorney general dismisses our appeal?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Gasbang.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what you understood, or what you did not understand. What\r\nI said was that if the Don\xe2\x80\x99s title is decided to be right and legal, I\r\nshall not contest it. Why should I, if the land is his? I came here to\r\ntake up government land, believing his title was rejected. He says it is\r\nnot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe lies; it was rejected,\xe2\x80\x9d Gasbang said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is why we appealed,\xe2\x80\x9d Mathews added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well; we will wait. For my part, I think that if his title was\r\nrejected he will find it hard to get it back,\xe2\x80\x9d said Darrell.\r\n\r\nThe fact of his going on with his building ought to have been sufficient\r\nproof to the other settlers that he had cast his lot with them. But it\r\nwas not. They feared that at any time he might pay the Don for his land,\r\nand cease to be one of them; cease to be a \xe2\x80\x9c_squatter_.\xe2\x80\x9d These doubts,\r\nthese fears, were the perennial theme of endless discussion with the\r\nsettlers of Alamar.\r\n\r\nWith date of February 14, 1872, the Honorable Legislature of California\r\npassed a law \xe2\x80\x9c_To protect agriculture, and to prevent the trespassing of\r\nanimals upon private property in the County of Los Angeles, and the\r\nCounty of San Diego, and parts of Monterey County_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn the very first section it recited, that \xe2\x80\x9cevery owner _or occupant_ of\r\nland, _whether it is enclosed or not_,\xe2\x80\x9d could take up cattle found in\r\nsaid land, etc., etc. It was not stated to be necessary that the\r\n_occupant_ should have a good title. All that was required seemed to be\r\nthat he should _claim to be an occupant_ of land, no matter who was the\r\nowner.\r\n\r\nBefore this law came out, Don Mariano had already had a great deal of\r\ntrouble with the squatters, who kept killing his cattle by the hundred\r\nhead at times. After this law passed, he had the additional annoyance of\r\nhaving to pay money for the release of cattle taken up by _occupants_\r\nwho would not fence their ten-acre crops. Thus, the alternative was,\r\nthat if cattle were not taken up, he was sure to find them shot dead by\r\nsome invisible hand. He had hoped that the Legislature would pass a law\r\nsaying that \xe2\x80\x9cunless _occupants_ of land put fences around their fields,\r\nthey would not be authorized to take up cattle.\xe2\x80\x9d But, instead of this,\r\nthe above-mentioned law was enacted.\r\n\r\nThis was, of course, ruinous to Don Mariano, as well as to all owners of\r\ncattle ranchos where settlers had seen fit to locate homesteads. Now any\r\none man, by planting _one acre_ of grain to attract cattle to it, could\r\nmake useless thousands of acres around it of excellent grazing, because\r\nit became necessary to drive cattle away from the vicinity of these\r\nunfenced fields.\r\n\r\nIn view of all this, and seeing that the new law would confirm the right\r\nto plant fields without fencing, and take up cattle, horses or any other\r\nanimals found therein, Don Mariano thought he would call together all\r\nthe settlers in his rancho, and make some proposition to them that would\r\nbe fair to everybody, and by which he would save his cattle from getting\r\nkilled or captured (when he must ransom them) all the time.\r\n\r\nHe told his idea to Mr. Mechlin, who thought it was a good plan, and\r\nvolunteered to see some of the settlers with whom he was acquainted,\r\nthinking that these could see others, and in this manner a meeting be\r\narranged. He started in the morning on his errand, and in the evening\r\nDon Mariano called to learn the result.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThese men are meaner and lower than I had supposed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Mechlin,\r\nwhose very fine nervous organization ill-fitted him for the rough\r\ncontact of Gasbangs. \xe2\x80\x9cWould you believe it, they suspected I wanted to\r\nlay a trap in which the innocent lambs would fall, and you\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nwolf\xe2\x80\x94catch them. If it had not been that I saw Darrell, I would have\r\nbeen utterly discouraged. And I suspect he would not have been half so\r\npolite and considerate but for the influence of his son, who has just\r\narrived.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI heard he had. You saw him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; and a very gentlemanly, handsome young fellow he is. He made his\r\nfather promise to go with him to see the settlers in person, and arrange\r\nfor you to meet them; he will report to me in the evening the result of\r\ntheir embassy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClarence kept his word to Mr. Mechlin, and immediately after breakfast\r\nhe had his buggy and horses (a fine turnout he had brought from San\r\nFrancisco) at the door. Darrell smiled, and good-naturedly took his seat\r\nbeside his son, saying it would be best to begin by seeing Gasbang and\r\nMathews. Fortunately they met these men, who were driving to see him, to\r\nask his opinion about agreeing to meet Don Mariano. Darrell promptly\r\ntold them that he thought no one of the settlers should refuse a request\r\nso easy to grant.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut don\xe2\x80\x99t you think there is a trap in it?\xe2\x80\x9d Mathews asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone whatever. We are not children,\xe2\x80\x9d Darrell replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut suppose he makes us promise something?\xe2\x80\x9d Mathews argued.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can he coerce any one against his will,\xe2\x80\x9d said Darrell.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo one will be obliged to accede unwillingly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence. \xe2\x80\x9cLet us\r\nat least be courteous.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly. Have you any idea what it is that he wants to say?\xe2\x80\x9d asked\r\nGasbang.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe wants to make some proposition to the settlers, by which he hopes\r\nthat the interests of all concerned will be subserved,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVisionary!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Gasbang, tapping his forehead with his\r\nforefinger; \xe2\x80\x9cnot practical.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut his intentions are perfectly kind and fair,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is to say, Mr. Mechlin thinks they are.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t they be? He certainly can\xe2\x80\x99t coerce anybody. Here we are\r\non what he believes to be his land, and we don\xe2\x80\x99t think it is. Well, what\r\nof that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe certainly won\xe2\x80\x99t propose to fight us single-handed. We are the\r\nmajority,\xe2\x80\x9d said Darrell.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right. We\xe2\x80\x99ll see Hager and Miller, and the other fellows in that\r\nvalley. But we think Mr. Clarence will do better with Hancock, Pittikin\r\nand Hughes. The female element is strong there, but it will weaken in\r\nhis hands, and in that malleable condition, he can shape it to suit\r\nhimself, with one look out of his eyes at the whole troop of girls,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Gasbang.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGoodness! You don\xe2\x80\x99t suppose I would go to play the sweet fellow to\r\nthose ugly old girls, and make a fool of myself,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence, with so\r\ngenuine a look of thorough disgust, that it made John Gasbang indulge in\r\none of his loudest fits of hilarity. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t be alarmed, my young friend.\r\nThere is no harm for you there. I could turn you loose among those girls\r\nand you would be as safe as Daniel among \xe2\x80\x98lions\xe2\x80\x99 or in \xe2\x80\x98fiery furnace.\xe2\x80\x99\r\nYou would not get a single scratch, or feel any flames at all,\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a low, vulgar fellow this is, even too low for a squatter,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nClarence, driving off.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPhew!\xe2\x80\x9d ejaculated the elder Darrell, \xe2\x80\x9cyou speak like _a Don_. Your idea\r\nof _a squatter_ is not flattering.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is flattering thus far, that I think Gasbang is too low for the\r\nsettler, who means no wrong-doing,\xe2\x80\x94the average squatter. As for Mathews,\r\nI am sure he is a cut-throat by instinct.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat may be; but I think their idea of your seeing Pittikin and Hughes\r\nis good. You can have more effect on them than Gasbang or Mathews.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO, I am willing to go to speak to the old men, but why should I see the\r\ngirls?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou manage that part to suit yourself. And now stop. I\xe2\x80\x99ll drop here;\r\nyou needn\xe2\x80\x99t go out of your way. I\xe2\x80\x99ll walk home. I want to see this piece\r\nof land near by. It has not been located. I might put a claim there for\r\nEverett and another for Webster.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClarence sighed, and silently drove on. He had passed by the Pittikin\r\nand Hughes farms the day he arrived, as his father had taken him to see\r\nhow nicely the settlers were doing in Southern California; all expecting\r\ntheir prosperity to increase by the building of the railroad. Clarence\r\nsaw the two houses and began to feel like a mariner of old between\r\nScylla and Charybdis. There might be a troop of ugly old girls in each\r\nhouse. If he could only see some men out in the fields. But the fields\r\nlooked deserted. Where could the men be\xe2\x80\x94this being no Sunday nor Fourth\r\nof July, that they should leave off work?
