On looking about for some\r\nhuman being to guide him, he saw in the distance, under a clump of dark\r\ntrees, several wagons, and horses unhitched, standing harnessed near\r\nthem.\r\n\r\nHe was about to turn to the left, to take the road between two fields,\r\nwhen he heard voices, shouting loudly. He supposed they were calling\r\nsome one. The shouts were followed by a man on horseback galloping\r\ntowards him. Clarence stopped and waited. The rider was no other than\r\nMr. Pittikin, who came in person to invite him to join their picnic, in\r\nhonor of his daughter\xe2\x80\x99s wedding. The opportunity to see _the men_\r\ntogether would be excellent, but the _girls_ would be there, too,\r\nthought Clarence, not over pleased.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease excuse me, I am not dressed to appear in company. I came to see\r\nyou on business,\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe girls said I must bring you.\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence felt a qualm. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd even if I\r\nhave to fight you I must obey; obey the ladies, you know. There ain\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nmany there. Only our two families\xe2\x80\x94Hughes and mine, and neighbor\r\nHancock\xe2\x80\x99s and a few friends. Indeed, we will feel slighted if you don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\njoin us. We will feel you think us too humble a class for you to\r\nassociate with.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing of the kind. If I thought so, I would not hesitate to present\r\nmyself before the ladies in this dress.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome along, anyhow. We\xe2\x80\x99ll make all the allowance you want. But you see,\r\nthis is my daughter Fanny\xe2\x80\x99s birthday and her wedding day. She was\r\nmarried to Romeo Hancock this morning. So we wanted a room as big as all\r\nout doors to celebrate the occasion. We thought the best thing would be\r\nto have a picnic under those beautiful trees. Come, please. If you ain\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nwith us, you are against us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll go home and put on other dress and come back immediately,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nClarence.\r\n\r\nPittikin laughed. \xe2\x80\x9cJust what Fanny said. I tell you she is an awfully\r\nsmart girl. She said, \xe2\x80\x98He\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you he is going home to change his\r\nclothes, but don\xe2\x80\x99t you let him, because he\xe2\x80\x99ll only give us the slip.\xe2\x80\x99 So\r\nyou see, I can\xe2\x80\x99t let you go. Besides, they are setting the table,\xe2\x80\x94I mean\r\nto say, spreading the eatables,\xe2\x80\x94so you have no time to go home now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, look here, Mr. Pittikin, what is to become of my mission? I came\r\nto see you and Mr. Hughes on business, and not on a picnic.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t the business wait till to-morrow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot very well, as I promised Mr. Mechlin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! I know; Hughes told me,\xe2\x80\x9d interrupted Pittikin. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Don wants to\r\nmake speeches to the settlers to fool us into a\xe2\x80\x94into\xe2\x80\x94some terms of his,\r\nso that we\xe2\x80\x99ll kick ourselves out of our farms.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing of the kind. He is not going to make any foolish propositions,\r\nbut even if he were, you can lose nothing by being polite and listening\r\nto him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know but what you are right. I like always to be polite; and as\r\nfor Hughes, he is the politest man going, and no mistake. He never\r\nspeaks loud, and he always listens to you. I think it will be the best\r\nthing, perhaps, to see Hughes, now. Then there is neighbor Hancock, and\r\nneighbor Miller and Jackson, and the boys. Come along, we\xe2\x80\x99ll collar them\r\nin a bunch.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, I can count upon your help?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly you can; for when it is a question of politeness, I won\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\nleft behind, and if I give you my word, you can bet on me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClarence was received with loud demonstrations of pleasure.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere he is,\xe2\x80\x9d said Pittikin, on arriving at the picnic ground; \xe2\x80\x9cI got\r\nhim; but as he has some business to talk to us about, I promised him we\r\nwould attend to that too, and mix business with pleasure, as it were.\r\nSo, you talk to them girls, Mr. Darrell, while we old men see what can\r\nbe done and how, and we\xe2\x80\x99ll let you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClarence was presented by Mr. Pittikin to Mrs. Pittikin, and this lady\r\npresented him to the company, saying that he must make himself at home,\r\nwhich Clarence did not see well how he could do.\r\n\r\nBut the young ladies could not boast of having often the good fortune to\r\nentertain a young gentleman as elegant, handsome and rich as Clarence,\r\nand they made good use of their golden opportunity. Sweet glances and\r\ncomplimentary expressions of pleasure, because the Darrell family were\r\nto be their neighbors, showered upon him, until he was ready to laugh\r\noutright. But he was too kind to have done anything so discourteous, and\r\ntook it all in good part, thinking it was all meant in kindness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, let us show to Mr. Darrell our ice fountain; it is, I think, a\r\ngreat natural curiosity,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Romeo Hancock, the heroine of the\r\nday, being the lady in whose honor the hymeneal festivities took place.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome girls and boys,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, and accompanied by Clarence, and\r\nfollowed by eight or ten others, she guided them to a little cave under\r\na large oak, from which a muffled sound of tiny bells that seemed to\r\ntinkle and sigh and whisper, came forth. It seemed to Clarence as if the\r\nlittle fountain was in sympathy with the dispossessed owners, but did\r\nnot dare to raise its timid voice in behalf of the vanquished, who no\r\nlonger had rights in their patrimony, and must henceforth wander off\r\ndisinherited, despoiled, forgotten.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is a lovely place,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, and Mathews wanted to kill me for it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Romeo.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy so?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Clarence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause he had just sold his place to Mr. Mechlin, intending to locate\r\nhere. So when he went to town to sign his conveyance, I put some boards\r\nin a wagon and came here, and in two hours my father and myself had put\r\nup my cabin. Then we put up this fence around one acre, and by nightfall\r\nwe had placed my boundary stakes. That night I brought my blankets and\r\nmy rifle, to sleep in my cabin. Mother sent father to keep me company,\r\nand we slept soundly, in splendid style. I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t afraid of Mathews.\r\nNext morning, at daybreak, we heard the rumbling of a wagon, and soon\r\nafter we spied old Mathews sitting on the top of his boards. He came\r\nsmack against my fence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat the devil is this?\xe2\x80\x9d said he, and began to swear a perfect blue\r\nstreak. Then he took a hammer from his wagon, and began hammering.\r\n\r\nI jumped up, took my rifle and hallooed to him, as if I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know him,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is there, hammering my fence?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour fence?\xe2\x80\x9d said he; \xe2\x80\x9cyour fence?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir, mine. I located here yesterday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou! you! Get a beard first,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, and with another streak of\r\noaths, began hammering again.\r\n\r\nI came up nearer, holding my rifle in good position. I said, \xe2\x80\x9cLook here,\r\nMr. Mathews, leave my fence alone, or you will get into trouble.\xe2\x80\x9d I\r\nleveled my rifle at him. \xe2\x80\x9cWill you stop? I give you just two minutes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe stopped.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have no right to locate\xe2\x80\x94you are a minor,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, livid with rage.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou just inform yourself better, by asking a polite question or two of\r\nmy parents. They will tell you that I am just twenty-one years and two\r\ndays old, and I can prove it by our family Bible and certificate of\r\nbaptism. I am a Christian, I am, though you don\xe2\x80\x99t seem to be, judging by\r\nyour cursing,\xe2\x80\x94and as for my beard, you be patient, and you\xe2\x80\x99ll see it,\r\nfor it is coming as fast as your gray hairs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy didn\xe2\x80\x99t you say you wanted this place?\xe2\x80\x9d he growled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a question!\xe2\x80\x9d I answered. \xe2\x80\x9cYou ask it because you don\xe2\x80\x99t see my\r\nbeard, but I feel it pushing ahead with all its might. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t tell\r\nyou, because we ain\xe2\x80\x99t exactly bosom friends, and because that is not the\r\nstyle in which we settlers do business. I kept dark, hoping that you\r\nwould hold on a while longer, trying to get a bigger price for your\r\nplace from Mr. Mechlin. I watched you, and when you let Saturday pass I\r\nknew this sweet little spot was mine,\xe2\x80\x94for on Saturday I was twenty-one,\r\nand you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t sign your conveyance to Mr. Mechlin until Monday.\r\nTo-day is Tuesday, Mr. Mathews, I shall be twenty-one years and three\r\ndays old at 11 o\xe2\x80\x99clock A.M. this day, if I live five hours longer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe a word. You ain\xe2\x80\x99t twenty-one. \xe2\x80\x99Tis a lie!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, it ain\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d my father said, coming from the cabin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen he is a jumper. He\xe2\x80\x99s jumped my claim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, he ain\xe2\x80\x99t. Look here, Mathews,\xe2\x80\x9d said father, dragging his rifle\r\nalong as if it was a dead cat, \xe2\x80\x9cyou know well it is yourself who is\r\nlying when you say that. You had no right to this claim while you held\r\nthe other.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I put up my notice that I was going to locate here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, don\xe2\x80\x99t be silly,\xe2\x80\x9d said father, leaning on his rifle. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is painful\r\nto my feelings to hear a grey-headed man talk like a child. You might\r\nhave put twenty notices\xe2\x80\x94what of that? The law don\xe2\x80\x99t allow any circus\r\nperformances like that, and if it did, you ain\xe2\x80\x99t a good enough performer\r\nto ride two horses at once.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think it is a mean performance on your part, too, coming here to\r\nsteal a march on me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA mean performance, you say? Do you remember how I had my notices up\r\nand my stakes on the ground, six years ago, and when I went to town to\r\nbring my lumber, you jumped my claim? My boy has just barely returned\r\nthe compliment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll be even with you yet,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, climbing into his wagon, and\r\nbeginning to whip his horses, and swear at us worse than ever.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe same to you; the same to you,\xe2\x80\x9d father would say, as if answering\r\nprayers, and then we both laughed heartily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is not the worst, but that you jumped the claim of his\r\naffections,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tom, whereupon all laughed, and Fanny bashfully hung\r\ndown her head.\r\n\r\nVoices calling them to dinner were now heard, and they returned to the\r\npicnic grounds.\r\n\r\nNo banquet of the Iliad warriors surpassed this, showing that the\r\nsettlers of Alamar had found the Don\xe2\x80\x99s land and the laws of Congress\r\nvery good.\r\n\r\nThe elder Mrs. Hancock and Mrs. Pittikin were proud of having given a\r\nbanquet which no other settler would dare surpass in Alamar.\r\n\r\nWhen the dessert was being served, Clarence said, \xe2\x80\x9cWe must drink to the\r\nbride and groom.\xe2\x80\x9d All agreed that it should be done.\r\n\r\nHe arose and made a neat little speech, which was so \xe2\x80\x9c_sweetly pretty_,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nMr. P. said, that it brought tears to the eyes of Mrs. Pittikin and Mrs.\r\nHancock, the elder.\r\n\r\nThis put Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s popularity beyond doubt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFill your glasses, for I have something to say to Mr. Clarence Darrell,\r\nbut we must first drink his health,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Pittikin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere is to our friends, the Darrell family, but more particularly to\r\nMr. Clarence. We respect him, we like him, we are proud of him;\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94all\r\ndrank\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cand I now take the occasion to say to Mr. Darrell, in the\r\npresence of our friends here, that I fulfilled my promise to him, and\r\nhave spoken to our friends here, the heads of families, and they will\r\nspeak to those who are not present, and we will meet to hear what the\r\nDon has to say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we don\xe2\x80\x99t promise to accept any proposition, if it don\xe2\x80\x99t suit each\r\none, no matter what anybody votes,\xe2\x80\x9d said old Hughes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is understood; we want to be polite, that\xe2\x80\x99s all,\xe2\x80\x9d explained Mr.\r\nPittikin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd that is all I have requested,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence said. \xe2\x80\x9cI do not ask any one\r\nto accept any proposition against his will.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is fair enough,\xe2\x80\x9d said old Hancock.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd little enough, considering we are in possession of land that the\r\nDon believes to be his own,\xe2\x80\x9d said Romeo.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it ain\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said old Hager.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt has been for more than fifty years,\xe2\x80\x9d Romeo asserted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut he lost it by not complying with the law,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hughes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, if he had not neglected his rights, his title would not have been\r\nrejected; he went to sleep for eight years, and his right was outlawed,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Miller.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was the fault of his lawyers, perhaps,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course it was, but he should have watched his lawyers. The trouble\r\nis, that you can\xe2\x80\x99t teach \xe2\x80\x98an old dog new tricks.\xe2\x80\x99 Those old Spaniards\r\nnever will be business men,\xe2\x80\x9d said Pittikin, sententiously.\r\n\r\nIt was finally agreed that Clarence would call on Mr. Mechlin that\r\nevening, to notify him that the settlers would meet the Don on Monday\r\nafternoon at 2 o\xe2\x80\x99clock on the porch of Gasbang\xe2\x80\x99s house.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER V.\xe2\x80\x94_The Don in his Broad Acres._\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe one great principle of English law,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94Charles Dickens says, \xe2\x80\x9cis to\r\nmake business for itself. There is no other principle distinctly,\r\ncertainly and consistently maintained through all its narrow turnings.\r\nViewed by this light, it becomes a coherent scheme, and not the\r\nmonstrous maze the laity are apt to think it. Let them but once clearly\r\nperceive that its grand principle is to make business for itself at\r\ntheir expense, and surely they will cease to grumble.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe one great principle of American law is very much the same; our\r\nlaw-givers keep giving us laws and then enacting others to explain them.\r\nThe lawyers find plenty of occupation, but what becomes of the laity?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. 189. _An Act to ascertain and settle the private land claims in the\r\nState of California_,\xe2\x80\x9d says the book.\r\n\r\nAnd by a sad subversion of purposes, all the private land titles became\r\n_unsettled_. It ought to have been said, \xe2\x80\x9cAn Act to _unsettle_ land\r\ntitles, and to upset the rights of the Spanish population of the State\r\nof California.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt thus became not only necessary for the Spanish people to present\r\ntheir titles for revision, and litigate to maintain them (in case of any\r\none contesting their validity, should the least irregularity be\r\ndiscovered, and others covet their possession), but to maintain them\r\nagainst the government before several tribunals; for the government,\r\nbesides making its own laws, _appeals to itself_ as against the\r\nland-owners, after their titles might have been _approved_. But this\r\nbenign Act says (in \xe2\x80\x9cSec. 11\xe2\x80\x9d), \xe2\x80\x9cThat the Commissioners, the District\r\nand Supreme Courts, in deciding on the validity of any claim, shall be\r\ngoverned by the treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo; the law of nations; the\r\nlaws, usages, and customs of the government _from which the claim is\r\nderived_; the principles of equity, and the decisions of the Supreme\r\nCourt of the United States, etc., etc.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThus the government washes its hands clean, liberally providing plenty\r\nof tribunals, plenty of crooked turnings through which to scourge the\r\nwretched land-owners.\r\n\r\nDon Mariano had been for some years under the lash of the maternal\r\ngovernment, whom he had found a cruel stepmother, indeed.\r\n\r\nAs it was arranged with Clarence, the meeting would take place that day\r\non the broad piazza of John Gasbang\xe2\x80\x99s house, this being the most central\r\npoint in the rancho.\r\n\r\nThe heads of families all came\xe2\x80\x94the male heads, be it understood\xe2\x80\x94as the\r\nsquatters did not make any pretence to regard female opinion, with any\r\nmore respect than other men.\r\n\r\nAll the benches and chairs that the house contained, with the exception\r\nof Mrs. Gasbang\xe2\x80\x99s sewing rocker, had been brought to the porch, which\r\nwas quite roomy and airy.\r\n\r\nAt ten minutes before two, all the settlers were there, that is to say,\r\nall the old men, with their elder sons.\r\n\r\nClarence, Romeo, Tom and Jack, sat together in a corner, conversing in\r\nlow tones, while Gasbang was entertaining his guests with some broad\r\nanecdotes, which brought forth peals of laughter.\r\n\r\nAt five minutes to two, Se\xc3\xb1or Alamar, accompanied by Mr. Mechlin,\r\narrived in a buggy; his two sons followed on horseback.\r\n\r\nClarence had time to look at them leisurely, while they dismounted, and\r\ntied their horses to a hitching post.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are gentlemen, no doubt,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Clarence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou bet they are,\xe2\x80\x9d Romeo coincided. Evidently he admired and liked\r\nthem.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow much the boys look like the old man,\xe2\x80\x9d Tom said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey look like Englishmen,\xe2\x80\x9d was Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s next observation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, particularly Victoriano; he is so light he looks more like a\r\nGerman, I think,\xe2\x80\x9d said Romeo.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think Gabriel is very handsome,\xe2\x80\x9d Tom said, \xe2\x80\x9conly of late he seems\r\nalways so sad or thoughtful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat won\xe2\x80\x99t do for a man who is to marry soon,\xe2\x80\x9d said Romeo. \xe2\x80\x9cI think he\r\nhas always been rather reserved. He has only a cold salutation to give,\r\nwhile Victoriano will be laughing and talking to everybody. But,\r\nperhaps, you are right, and he is changed. I think he is less reconciled\r\nthan the others, to have us, settlers, helping ourselves to what they\r\nconsider their land. He certainly was far more talkative four or five\r\nyears ago. I used to work with them in ploughing and harvesting time,\r\nand both boys, and the Don, were always very kind to me, and I can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhelp liking them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe ladies, though, ain\xe2\x80\x99t so affable. They are very proud,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tom;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthey walk like queens.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey didn\xe2\x80\x99t seem proud to me, but I never spoke to them,\xe2\x80\x9d said Romeo.\r\n\r\nGasbang went forward to meet his guests, and all came into the porch.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood afternoon, gentlemen,\xe2\x80\x9d said Don Mariano to the settlers, lifting\r\nhis hat and bowing. His sons and Mr. Mechlin did the same. Clarence\r\narose, and so did the other young men with him, returning their\r\nsalutation. The elder Darrell, Pittikin and Hughes followed this\r\nexample; the other settlers nodded only, and remained sitting with their\r\nhats on, looking with affected indifference at the trees beyond.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thank you for your courtesy in complying with my request to have this\r\nmeeting,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. Some nodded, others grinned and winked, others smiled\r\nsilently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake this chair, Se\xc3\xb1or, and you, Mr. Mechlin, take this one. They are\r\nthe best in my establishment,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gasbang. \xe2\x80\x9cThe young gentlemen will\r\nfind seats somewhere on the benches.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClarence came forward and offered three chairs. Mr. Mechlin took his arm\r\nand presented him to the Alamars.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI take pleasure in making your acquaintance, and I hope to have the\r\nopportunity to thank you for your kind co-operation more appropriately\r\nafterward,\xe2\x80\x9d said Don Mariano. His sons shook hands with Clarence\r\ncordially, and accepted the proffered chairs.\r\n\r\nDon Mariano excused himself for not speaking English more fluently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you don\xe2\x80\x99t understand me I will repeat my words until I make my\r\nmeaning clear, but I hope you will ask me to repeat them; or, perhaps,\r\nsome one of these young gentlemen will do me the kindness to be my\r\ninterpreter,\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRomeo talks Spanish; he can interpret for you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Victoriano.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou talk English better,\xe2\x80\x9d Romeo proudly replied, thinking he could tell\r\nhis wife that the Don had asked him to be his interpreter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps Mr. Clarence Darrell would do me the favor,\xe2\x80\x9d said Don Mariano.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou speak very good English, se\xc3\xb1or. We understand you perfectly. You do\r\nnot require an interpreter,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is so; you speak very well,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Mechlin.\r\n\r\nGasbang and Pittikin added: \xe2\x80\x9cCertainly, we understand him very well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course we do,\xe2\x80\x9d said Darrell and others.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are very kind,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Don, smiling, \xe2\x80\x9cand I will try to be brief,\r\nand not detain you long.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have all the afternoon,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hughes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s so, we ain\xe2\x80\x99t in a hurry,\xe2\x80\x9d said several.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly let us out in time to bring the milch cows home, before night\r\ncomes on,\xe2\x80\x9d said old Miller, dryly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly, we want to look after our cows, too,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Don, laughing.\r\n\r\nAll saw the fine irony of the rejoinder, and laughed heartily. Miller\r\nscratched his ear, as if he had felt the retort there, knowing well,\r\nthat with the exception of Mathews and Gasbang, he had killed and\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_corraled_\xe2\x80\x9d more of the Don\xe2\x80\x99s cattle than any other settler.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpeaking about cows, brings us at once to the object of this\r\nmeeting,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94Don Mariano, still smiling, went on, saying: \xe2\x80\x9cYou know that I\r\nhave lost many, and that it is natural I should wish to save those I\r\nhave left. To do this, and yet not ask that you give up your claims, I\r\nhave one or two propositions to make to you. The reason why you have\r\ntaken up land here is because you want homes. You want to make money.\r\nIsn\xe2\x80\x99t that the reason? Money! money!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s it, exactly,\xe2\x80\x9d said many voices, and all laughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I can show you how you may keep your homes and make more money\r\nthan you can by your present methods, while at the same time, I also\r\nsave my cattle. That little point, you know, I must keep in view.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAll laughed again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo fence your fields, you have said, is too expensive, particularly as\r\nthe rainy seasons are too uncertain to base upon them any calculations\r\nfor getting crops to pay for fencing. I believe this is what most of you\r\nsay; is it not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe could have raised better crops if your cattle hadn\xe2\x80\x99t damaged them,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Mathews.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI beg to differ; but supposing that you are right, do you think you\r\ncould be sure of good crops if you killed all my stock, or if I took\r\nthem all away to the mountains? No, most assuredly. The rainy season\r\nwould still be irregular and unreliable, I think. Yes, I may say, I feel\r\nsure, it is a mistake to try to make San Diego County a grain-producing\r\ncounty. It is not so, and I feel certain it never will be, to any great\r\nextent. This county is, and has been, and will be always, a good grazing\r\ncounty\xe2\x80\x94one of the best counties for cattle-raising on this coast, and\r\nthe very best for fruit-raising on the face of the earth. God intended\r\nit should be. Why, then, not devote your time, your labor and your money\r\nto raising vineyards, fruits and cattle, instead of trusting to the\r\nuncertain rains to give you grain crops?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt takes a long time to get fruit trees to bearing. What are we to do\r\nfor a living in the meantime?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Miller.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBegin raising cattle\xe2\x80\x94that will support you,\xe2\x80\x9d the Don replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is the capital to buy cattle with?\xe2\x80\x9d Gasbang asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t require any more capital than you already have. I can let\r\neach of you have a number of cows to begin with, and give you four or\r\nfive years\xe2\x80\x99 time to pay me. So you see, it will be with the increase of\r\nthese cattle you will pay, for I shall charge you no interest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you expect us to do in return? To give back to you our\r\nhomesteads?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Hughes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir; I have said, and repeat again, you will retain your\r\nhomesteads.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd will you stop contesting our claims?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mathews.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will, and will give each one a quit-claim deed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will not fight our claims, but you don\xe2\x80\x99t want us to plant grain on\r\nour land,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gasbang.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can plant grain, if you like, but to do so you must fence your\r\nland; so, as you all say, that fencing is expensive, I suggest your\r\nfencing orchards and vineyards only, but not grain fields\xe2\x80\x94I mean large\r\nfields.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPshaw! I knew there was to be something behind all that display of\r\ngenerosity,\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Mathews.\r\n\r\nDon Mariano reddened with a thrill of annoyance, but quietly answered:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are too good business men to suppose that I should not reserve some\r\nslight advantage for myself, when I am willing you should have many more\r\nyourselves. All I want to do is to save the few cattle I have left. I am\r\nwilling to quit-claim to you the land you have taken, and give you\r\ncattle to begin the stock business, and all I ask you in return is to\r\nput a fence around whatever land you wish to cultivate, so that my\r\ncattle cannot go in there. So I say, plant vineyards, plant olives,\r\nfigs, oranges; makes wines and oil and raisins; export olives and dried\r\nand canned fruits. I had some very fine California canned fruit sent to\r\nme from San Francisco. Why could we not can fruits as well, or better?\r\nOur olives are splendid\xe2\x80\x94the same our figs, oranges, apricots, and truly\r\nall semi-tropical fruits are of a superior quality. When this fact\r\nbecomes generally known, I feel very sure that San Diego County will be\r\nselected for fruit and grape-growing. In two years grape vines begin to\r\nbear; the same with figs, peaches and other fruits. At three years old\r\nthey bear quite well, and all without irrigation. So you would not have\r\nto wait so very long to begin getting a return from your labor and\r\ncapital. Moreover, an orchard of forty acres or vineyard of twenty will\r\npay better after three years\xe2\x80\x99 growth than one hundred and sixty acres of\r\nwheat or barley in good seasons, and more than three hundred acres of\r\nany grain in moderately good seasons, or one thousand acres in bad\r\nseasons. You can easily fence twenty or forty or sixty acres for a\r\nvineyard or orchard, but not so easily fence a field of one hundred and\r\nsixty, and the grain crop would be uncertain, depending on the rains,\r\nbut not so the trees, for you can irrigate them, and after the trees are\r\nrooted that is not required.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is the water to irrigate?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Miller.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe water is in the sea now, for there we let it go every year; but if\r\nwe were sensible, judicious men, we would not let it go to waste\xe2\x80\x94we\r\nwould save it. This rancho has many deep ravines which bring water from\r\nhills and sierras. These ravines all open into the valleys, and run like\r\nso many little rivers in the rainy season. By converting these ravines\r\ninto reservoirs we could have more water than would be needed for\r\nirrigating the fruit trees on the foothills. In the low valleys no\r\nirrigation would be needed. If we all join forces to put up dams across\r\nthe most convenient of these ravines, we will have splendid reservoirs.\r\nI will defray half the expense if you will get together and stand the\r\nother half. Believe me, it will be a great God-send to have a thriving,\r\nfruit-growing business in our county. To have the cultivated land well\r\nfenced, and the remainder left out for grazing. Then there would not be\r\nso many thousands upon thousands of useless acres as now have to be. For\r\nevery ten acres of cultivated land (not fenced) there are ten thousand,\r\nyes, twenty thousand, entirely idle, useless. Why? Because those ten\r\nacres of growing grain must be protected, and the cattle which don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow the \xe2\x80\x98_no fence_\xe2\x80\x99 law, follow their inclination to go and eat the\r\ngreen grass. Then they are \xe2\x80\x98_corralled_\xe2\x80\x99 or killed. Is it not a pity to\r\nkill the poor dumb brutes, because we can\xe2\x80\x99t make them understand the\r\nlaw, and see the wisdom of our Sacramento legislators who enacted it?\r\nAnd is it not a pity to impoverish our county by making the bulk of its\r\nland useless? The foolishness of letting all of the rainfall go to\r\nwaste, is an old time folly with us. Still, in old times, we had, at\r\nleast, the good excuse that we raised all the fruits we needed for our\r\nuse, and there was no market for any more. But we were not then, as now,\r\nguilty of the folly of making the land useless. We raised cattle and\r\nsold hides and tallow every year, and made money. When gold was\r\ndiscovered, we drove our stock north, got a good price for it, and made\r\nmoney. But now no money will be made by anybody out of cattle, if they\r\nare to be destroyed, and no money made out of land, for the grazing will\r\nbe useless, when there will be no stock left to eat it. Thus, the county\r\nwill have no cattle, and the crops be always uncertain. Believe me, in\r\nyears to come, you will see that the county was impoverished by the \xe2\x80\x98no\r\nfence law,\xe2\x80\x99 unless we try to save our county, in spite of foolish\r\nlegislation. If our wise legislators could enact a law obliging rain to\r\ncome, so that we could have better chances to raise grain, then there\r\nwould be some show of excuse for the \xe2\x80\x98_no fence law_,\xe2\x80\x99 _perhaps_. I say\r\n*PERHAPS*, because, in my humble opinion, we ought to prefer cattle\r\nraising and fruit growing for our county. We should make these our\r\nspecialty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think it would be much more foolish to trust to a few cows to make\r\nout a living while trees grow,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miller, \xe2\x80\x9cthan to the seasons to\r\ngive us grain crops.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir; because cattle are sure to increase, if they are not killed,\r\nand you could make cheese and butter, and sell your steers every year,\r\nwhile trees grow. You have been seven years a settler on this rancho. In\r\nthese seven years you have raised two good crops; three poor, or only\r\nmiddling, and two, no crops at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, because your cattle destroyed them,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mathews.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir; my cattle were not all over California; but the bad seasons\r\nwere, and only in few places, moderately good crops were harvested; in\r\nthe southern counties none at all. We had rains enough to get\r\nsufficiently good grazing, but not to raise grain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you are right about the uncertainty of our seasons, and I think\r\na good dairy always pays well, also a good orchard and vineyard,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nDarrell. \xe2\x80\x9cBut the question is, whether we can adopt some feasible plan\r\nto put your idea into practice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, how many cows will you let us have?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Hager.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will divide with you. Next week I shall have my \xe2\x80\x98_rodeo_.\xe2\x80\x99 We can see\r\nthen the number of cattle I have left. We shall count them. I shall take\r\nhalf, the other half you divide pro rata; each head of a family taking a\r\nproportionate number of cattle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is fair,\xe2\x80\x9d Darrell said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want any cattle. I ain\xe2\x80\x99t no \xe2\x80\x98_vaquero_\xe2\x80\x99 to go \xe2\x80\x98_busquering_\xe2\x80\x99\r\naround and _lassooing_ cattle. I\xe2\x80\x99ll lasso myself; what do I know about\r\nwhirling a _lariat_?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mathews.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, don\xe2\x80\x99t take cattle. You can raise fruit trees and vineyards,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nDarrell.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, and starve meantime,\xe2\x80\x9d Mathews replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will not have to be a vaquero. I don\xe2\x80\x99t go \xe2\x80\x98_busquering_\xe2\x80\x99 around\r\n_lassooing_, unless I wish to do so,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Don. \xe2\x80\x9cYou can hire an\r\nIndian boy to do that part. They know how to handle _la reata_ and\r\n_echar el lazo_ to perfection. You will not starve, either, for if you\r\nwish, you can make butter and cheese enough to help to pay expenses. I\r\nthink this State ought to make and export as good cheese as it now\r\nimports, and some day people will see it, and do it, too. Thus, with the\r\nproduce of your dairies, at first, and afterward with your fruits, you\r\nwill do far better than with grain crops, and not work as hard. Let the\r\nnorthern counties raise grain, while we raise fruits and make wine,\r\nbutter and cheese. You must not forget, either, that every year you can\r\nsell a number of cattle, besides keeping as many milch cows as you\r\nneed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere can we sell our cattle?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Hancock.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCattle-buyers will come to buy from you. But if you prefer it, you can\r\ndrive your stock north yourselves, and make a good profit. Since 1850, I\r\nhave sent nine times droves of cattle to the northern counties, and made\r\na handsome profit every time. The first time we took stock north, was in\r\n\xe2\x80\x9950; I took nearly six thousand head\xe2\x80\x94three thousand were mine\xe2\x80\x94and the\r\nothers belonged to my brothers. We lost very few, and sold at a good\r\nprice\xe2\x80\x94all the way from eighteen to twenty-five dollars per head. About\r\nfive hundred of mine I sold as high as thirty dollars per head. I made\r\nsixty thousand dollars by this operation. Then out of the next lot I\r\nmade twenty-seven thousand dollars. Then I made twenty-two thousand, and\r\nso on, until my tame cows began to disappear, as you all know. In four\r\nyears after my cows began to get shot, my cattle decreased more than\r\nhalf. Now I don\xe2\x80\x99t think I have many more than three thousand head. So\r\nyou cannot blame me for wishing to save these few. But believe me, the\r\nplan I propose will be as beneficial to you as to me, and also to the\r\nentire county, for as soon as it is shown that we can make a success of\r\nthe industries I propose, others will follow our example.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you have only three thousand head, you can\xe2\x80\x99t spare many to us, and\r\nit will hardly be worth while to stop planting crops to get a few cows,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Gasbang.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I will be able to spare five or six hundred cows. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nhow many I have left.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will buy from somebody else, if we want more,\xe2\x80\x9d said Darrell. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t want many to begin with; it will be something of an experiment for\r\nsome of us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor all of us here. Perhaps you understand _vaquering_; we don\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nHancock; all laughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen fence your claim and plant grain,\xe2\x80\x9d Darrell retorted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not so big a fool as to spend money in fences. The \xe2\x80\x98_no fence_\xe2\x80\x99\r\nlaw is better than all the best fences,\xe2\x80\x9d Mathews said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what if you make more money by following other laws that are more\r\njust, more rational?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Don.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe \xe2\x80\x98no fence\xe2\x80\x99 law is rational enough for me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miller.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd so say I,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mathews.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gasbang.\r\n\r\nHughes nodded approvingly, but he was too much of a hypocrite to commit\r\nhimself in words.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe did not come to discuss the \xe2\x80\x98no fence\xe2\x80\x99 law, but only to propose\r\nsomething that will put more money in your pockets than killing dumb\r\nbeasts,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Mechlin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen propose something practicable,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mathews.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think what has been proposed is practicable enough,\xe2\x80\x9d Darrell said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly it is,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Mechlin added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mathews.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNor I, either,\xe2\x80\x9d added Gasbang.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNor I, neither,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hughes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, gentlemen,\xe2\x80\x9d said Don Mariano, rising, \xe2\x80\x9cI shall leave you now; you\r\nknow my views, and you perhaps prefer to discuss them, and discuss your\r\nown among yourselves, and not in my presence. Take your time, and when\r\nyou come to a final decision let me know. Perhaps I can advance the\r\nmoney to those of you who do not have it ready to purchase fencing\r\nlumber. I shall charge no interest, and give you plenty of time to pay.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will do that, Se\xc3\xb1or Alamar,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence said; \xe2\x80\x9cif the settlers agree to\r\nfence their lands, I will advance the money to them to put up their\r\nfences.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, and if our crops fail, we will be in debt to the ears, with a\r\nchain around our necks,\xe2\x80\x9d Mathews growled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought you said that if it were not for my cattle, your crops would\r\nnot have failed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Don Mariano, smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI said so, and it is so. But you see, that was before we had the \xe2\x80\x98_no\r\nfence_\xe2\x80\x99 law,\xe2\x80\x9d answered he, grinning.\r\n\r\nDon Mariano shook hands with Clarence, whom he invited to call at his\r\nhouse\xe2\x80\x94this invitation Clarence accepted with warm thanks\xe2\x80\x94and followed by\r\nhis sons and his friend Mr. Mechlin, Don Mariano took his leave, bowing\r\nto the settlers, who nodded and grinned in return.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you, too, think the \xe2\x80\x98_no fence_\xe2\x80\x99 law iniquitous, as you\r\nappear to favor the aristocracy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gasbang to Clarence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is worse than that, it is stupid. Now it kills the cattle,\r\nafterwards it will kill the county,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall we plant no wheat, because the Spaniards want to raise cattle?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nMathews asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlant wheat, if you can do so without killing cattle. But do not\r\ndestroy the larger industry with the smaller. If, as the Don very\r\nproperly says, this is a grazing county, no legislation can change it.\r\nSo it would be wiser to make laws to suit the county, and not expect\r\nthat the county will change its character to suit absurd laws,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence\r\nreplied.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VI.\xe2\x80\x94_Naughty Dog Milord an Important Factor._\r\n\r\n\r\nThree large wagons, each drawn by six horses, were hauling the lumber\r\nfor Mr. Darrell\xe2\x80\x99s house, which was already commenced.\r\n\r\nVictoriano, riding across the valley, had to stop to let the heavily\r\nloaded wagons pass. This gave Clarence time to overtake him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood morning,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, \xe2\x80\x9cI am glad to catch up with you, Don\r\nVictoriano. I have been wanting to speak to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nVictoriano bowed, saying, \xe2\x80\x9cWill you go to my house?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I\xe2\x80\x99d rather not. I am not dressed to be seen by ladies. I would\r\nrather speak to you here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are going to build a large house, Mr. Darrell?\xe2\x80\x9d said Victoriano,\r\nturning his horse so as to ride beside Clarence; \xe2\x80\x9cjudging by the amount\r\nof lumber being hauled.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; rather. We are a large family, and require a good deal of room.\r\nBut before we do any more work I want to speak with your father. I want\r\nto ask him\xe2\x80\x94ask him as a favor\xe2\x80\x94and yet, as a business proposition\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he\r\nhesitated; he was evidently embarrassed; but Victoriano, not guessing\r\nthe drift of his words, remained waiting silently, offering no\r\nassistance. \xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued, \xe2\x80\x9cI mean this: I don\xe2\x80\x99t like this\r\nfashion of taking people\xe2\x80\x99s lands, and I would like to pay to Se\xc3\xb1or\r\nAlamar for what has been located by us, but at the same time I do not\r\nwish my father to know that I have paid for the land, as I am sure he\r\nwould take my action as a reproach\xe2\x80\x94as a disclaimer of his own action,\r\nand I don\xe2\x80\x99t wish to hurt his feelings, or seem to be disrespectful or\r\ncensorious.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI understand, and I think my father will be willing to sell the land.\r\nHe is at home now. Let us go up to see him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHad you not better speak to him, and make an appointment for me to see\r\nhim to-morrow, or some other time? I\xe2\x80\x99d rather not risk being seen by the\r\nladies in this blue flannel shirt and heavy boots. I look too rough\xe2\x80\x94like\r\na smuggler or a squatter, sure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can call my father to speak to you outside, so that the ladies need\r\nnot see you. But if they should, that needn\xe2\x80\x99t disturb you. They have too\r\nmuch sense not to know that you would not be working in white kid\r\ngloves. Come on. The front veranda is empty. Mother and three of my\r\nsisters are at the Mechlin\xe2\x80\x99s. Mercedes is the only one at home, and she\r\nis too busy with her embroidery in Madam Halier\xe2\x80\x99s room to come near you.\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll bring father to the front veranda.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClarence and Victoriano tied their horses by the garden gate and walked\r\nto the piazza. The hall door was ajar. Clarence saw no ladies about and\r\nfelt reassured.\r\n\r\nThere were three steps leading from the walk through the garden up to\r\nthe front veranda. These steps were exactly opposite to the hall door.\r\n\r\nVictoriano took the path to the right, saying: \xe2\x80\x9cGo up and sit down. I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nbring my father here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo not disturb him if he is taking his _siesta_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe _siesta_ hour is past, I\xe2\x80\x99ll find him at the office,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, going\r\nround the corner, leaving Clarence to walk up the front step. As he did\r\nso, he heard a tinkling of little bells and rushing of feet, as if\r\nsomebody was running. Then a laughing voice, the timbre of which was\r\nsweetly pleasing, saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStop, Milord! you bad dog! Milord! Milord!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt the same moment, through the narrow opening of the door, out darted a\r\nlittle white dog, dragging after him a large and much entangled skein of\r\nbright-colored silk. Clarence was nearly stepping on the little runaway,\r\nwhen the door was flung open, and a girl rushed out, coming against him\r\nbefore she could check herself. In her effort to do so she turned her\r\nfoot and staggered forward, but before she realized she was in any one\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npresence, she felt two strong arms holding her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed, as a sharp, hot pain darted through her ankle. She\r\nsaw that the two arms which held her were none of her father or\r\nbrothers\xe2\x80\x99, and that they were covered with blue flannel.\r\n\r\nLooking up to see the face above them, their eyes met. Hers expressed\r\nsurprise, his merriment. But a change in their expression flashed\r\ninstantaneously, and both felt each other tremble, thrilled with the\r\nbliss of their proximity. Her face was suffused with burning blushes.\r\nShe was bewildered, and without daring to meet his eyes again, stammered\r\nan apology; extending her hand, to reach some chair or table to hold\r\nherself, but they all were crowded at both ends of the piazza.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are hurt. I am afraid you are hurt,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, with pale lips,\r\nreflecting the pallor he saw come to her face, succeeding her crimson\r\nblush. \xe2\x80\x9cI know you are suffering. What can I do? I am so sorry!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO no, I only turned my foot a little,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered, venturing to look\r\nat him for an instant. \xe2\x80\x9cI shall be all right in a minute.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you turned your foot, don\xe2\x80\x99t put any weight upon it. Do not try to\r\nwalk, let me carry you to a chair.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO no, no! I am not so much hurt as to require giving all that trouble.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Please_ let me. It will be no trouble; only a great pleasure.\xe2\x80\x9d He was\r\nin earnest and spoke quite seriously. \xe2\x80\x9cAre you afraid I could not carry\r\nyou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, not that, but it is not necessary,\xe2\x80\x9d and she tried to walk. A quick,\r\nsharp, burning pain through her ankle admonished her that she was more\r\nhurt than she had believed. A slight contraction of her brows betrayed\r\nher pain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere! You will hurt yourself worse,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, and before she knew what\r\nhe was going to do, he stooped a little and lifted her as easily as if\r\nshe had been a little child. She had no time to think whether to be\r\ngrateful or offended, for he quickly walked to the further end of the\r\npiazza and carefully placed her in a roomy arm chair. Then bending a\r\nknee before her, said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cForgive my lifting you without your permission. I knew you would not\r\ngive it, and I knew also that you were suffering. Will you forgive me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nHis voice was soft, caressing, pleading, but his eyes seemed to her to\r\nemit rays full of attractive, earnest force which she felt had great\r\npower. They dazzled her, and yet those eyes were so mild, so kind. She\r\nlooked down, making no answer. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen Don Victoriano comes he can carry\r\nyou to bed, and\xe2\x80\x94please\xe2\x80\x94take my advice, stay there until the pain has\r\nentirely left your foot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe ventured to look at his eyes again. Who could this strong young man\r\nbe, so bold, and yet so gentle, so courteous and yet waiting for no\r\npermission to take so positively hold of her, to carry her bodily half\r\nthe length of the piazza. And now so respectfully asking on his knees to\r\nbe forgiven? Asking with tones of tender humility in his voice, while\r\nhis eyes she knew could emanate subduing magnetic beams.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you know Victoriano is coming? He went out riding,\xe2\x80\x9d she said,\r\nevading the question of forgiveness, and for the sake of making some\r\nreply that would hide her confusion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but I met him and he returned with me. He has gone to look for\r\nSe\xc3\xb1or Alamar, I came to see him on business,\xe2\x80\x9d said the respectful young\r\nman, still on his knees.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know my father?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly very slightly.\xe2\x80\x9d They were silent. He added: \xe2\x80\x9cI met him a few days\r\nago when he had that meeting with the squatters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWere you at the meeting?\xe2\x80\x9d said she, avoiding his gaze.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, watching her beautiful face. What would she think of\r\nhim, believing him a _Squatter_, one who came to take land that did not\r\nbelong to him? How he wished that she would look up, that he might see\r\nher lovely eyes again, for if to her his eyes seemed so glorious, to him\r\nhers fascinated, conquered, with a power that he never thought could\r\nexist in any human being. Trembling, he felt that he was madly in love\r\nwith her. Yes, already in love. Love at first sight, surely. But if it\r\nkilled him, no matter, he would love her to the last instant of his\r\nlife.\r\n\r\nVoices were heard approaching through the hall. He stood up and walked\r\ntowards the door. Se\xc3\xb1or Alamar came forward and shook hands with him.\r\nVictoriano explained the reason of his delay being, that he had to look\r\nfor his father all over the house, and at last found him in the furthest\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_corral_\xe2\x80\x9d looking at some new colts just brought in.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad that Mercedes came to converse with you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Victoriano.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did not come to converse. I did not know that the gentleman was here.\r\nI came by accident,\xe2\x80\x9d she hastened to reply. \xe2\x80\x9cI was trying to catch\r\nMilord when I stumbled and would have fallen, had not this gentleman\r\nprevented it.\xe2\x80\x9d So saying, she blushed anew; her blushes being\r\nimmediately reflected on Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s forehead, made them both look like a\r\ncouple of culprits.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI fear the lady\xe2\x80\x99s foot is hurt,\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it?\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Don Mariano, going towards Mercedes. \xe2\x80\x9cDoes it pain\r\nyou baby?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes papa, a little. It burns me. Do you think it would be bad for me to\r\nwalk to my room?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course it would,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence said, and blushed redder yet at his\r\ntemerity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan you stand on your foot?\xe2\x80\x9d Victoriano asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t try. I\xe2\x80\x99ll carry you to your room,\xe2\x80\x9d said her father.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWomen have no business to have such small feet. They are always\r\nstumbling and can\xe2\x80\x99t walk worth a cent,\xe2\x80\x9d said Victoriano, going to look\r\nat his sister\xe2\x80\x99s foot. \xe2\x80\x9cSee here. No wonder they stumble. Look at the\r\nlittle slipper. Why don\xe2\x80\x99t they wear good broad boots?\xe2\x80\x9d So saying he took\r\noff the little slipper, which seemed made for a Cinderella.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are too absurd,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mercedes, blushing again, to see her slipper\r\nbrandished aloft, in the face of a stranger.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI ain\xe2\x80\x99t. It\xe2\x80\x99s women\xe2\x80\x99s feet that are absurd.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen we want the ladies to be infantry soldiers, then we will ask them\r\nto cultivate big feet,\xe2\x80\x9d said Don Mariano, laughing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut not until then, please,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence, smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAha! I see you cherish the general male weakness,\xe2\x80\x9d said Victoriano,\r\nkneeling before his sister to put on the little slipper. \xe2\x80\x9cI am the only\r\nstrong-minded man, I know. Come, pussy, I\xe2\x80\x99ll carry you to your room.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no. You take me, papa, Tano might drop me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNonsense; as if I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t carry a kitten like you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPapa, you take me, but not to bed. Put me on the lounge in mamma\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nroom, and call Madam Halier to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right; anything to please the children,\xe2\x80\x9d said Don Mariano, stooping\r\nto lift her.\r\n\r\nShe put her arms around his neck, and whispered: \xe2\x80\x9cPapa, who is this\r\nyoung man? I never saw him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is a fact,\xe2\x80\x9d said Don Mariano, taking her up, and turning toward\r\nClarence, said: \xe2\x80\x9cMr. Darrell, permit me to present you to my daughter,\r\nMercedes, \xe2\x80\x98our baby.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d So saying, he dandled her a little in his arms.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, papa, you make me ridiculous! How can I bow like a lady, when you\r\nare rocking me like an infant!\xe2\x80\x9d she said, laughing, but blushing again\r\nlike a rose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShake hands with the gentleman, that\xe2\x80\x99s a dear,\xe2\x80\x9d said Victoriano,\r\ntalking baby talk to her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, papa, make Tano hush. Mr. Darrell, I am afraid that I shall always\r\nseem ridiculous to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all; I don\xe2\x80\x99t see why,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence replied, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I fear that your\r\nhurt might be serious.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s it. You might be ridiculous, but your hurt might be serious,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Victoriano.\r\n\r\nIt was Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s turn to blush now, but he smiled good naturedly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou won\xe2\x80\x99t be serious, though. I wish you were, and polite, too,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMercedes. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what Mr. Darrell will think of us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Darrell will see us often, I hope, and think better of Tano,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nDon Mariano, carrying away his precious burden.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy opinion is all that you could wish, Miss Mercedes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence,\r\nand their eyes met, transmitting that strange thrill to both.\r\n\r\nDon Mariano placed Mercedes tenderly on her mamma\xe2\x80\x99s lounge, called Madam\r\nHalier to attend to the sprained ankle, and returned to the veranda.\r\n\r\nClarence made no delay in stating the object of his visit. He said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSince the meeting I have had several talks with the settlers, and the\r\nresult has been my conviction, that they will not accept your generous\r\noffer. They, no doubt, wish to take up more land, and think it cannot be\r\ndone if they bind themselves to put up fences by accepting your\r\nproposition. How short-sighted they are time alone will show, for at\r\npresent they will not listen to reason.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very sorry. There is no alternative for me but to sell all my\r\ncattle as soon as possible, and in the meantime drive all I can to the\r\nmountains.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut that will be ruinous, father. How can we herd them in the\r\nmountains? They will all become wild and run away,\xe2\x80\x9d said Victoriano.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid they will. I am sure of it, in fact. But there is no other\r\nway to save any at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think this \xe2\x80\x98no fence\xe2\x80\x99 law the most scandalous, bare-faced outrage\r\nupon the rights of citizens that I ever heard of,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence,\r\nwarmly. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is like setting irresponsible trespassers loose upon a\r\npeaceable people, and then rewarding their outrage. To let any one take\r\nup your lands right before your eyes is outrage enough, but to cap the\r\nclimax by authorizing people to plant crops without fences and then\r\n_corral_ your cattle, which must be attracted to the green grass, I call\r\npositively disgraceful, in a community which is not of vandals. It is\r\nshameful to the American name. I am utterly disgusted with the whole\r\nbusiness, and the only thing that will make matters a little tolerable\r\nto me will be for you to do me the favor of permitting me to pay for the\r\nland we have located.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes your father wish to pay?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not know whether he would or not. I fear he would not. My father\r\nis a blind worshiper of the Congress of these United States, and\r\nconsequently it is difficult to persuade him that our legislators might\r\npossibly do wrong. He believes that Congress has the right to declare\r\n_all_ California open to pre-emption, and all American citizens free to\r\nchoose any land not already patented. Thus, he thinks he has the right\r\nto locate on your land (according to law, mind you), because he believes\r\nyour title has been rejected. But as my faith in our law-givers is not\r\nso blind, my belief is that Congress had no more right to pass any law\r\nwhich could give an excuse to trespass upon your property, than to pass\r\na law inviting people to your table. I feel a sort of impatience to\r\nthink that in our country could exist a law which is so outrageously\r\nunjust. My pride as an American is somewhat different from that of my\r\nfather. He thinks it is a want of patriotism to criticise our\r\nlegislation. Whereas, I think our theory of government is so lofty, so\r\ngrand and exalted, that we must watch jealously that Congress may not\r\nmisinterpret it; misrepresent the sentiments, the aspirations of the\r\nAmerican people, and thus make a caricature of our beautiful ideal. It\r\nis our duty and privilege to criticise our laws, and criticise severely.\r\nAs long as you, the native Californians, were to be despoiled of your\r\nlands, I think it would have been better to have passed a law of\r\nconfiscation. Then we would have stood before the world with the\r\nresponsibility of that barbarous act upon own shoulders. That would have\r\nbeen a national shame, but not so great as that of guaranteeing, by\r\ntreaty, a protection which was not only withheld, but which was\r\ndenied,\xe2\x80\x94snatched away, treacherously,\xe2\x80\x94making its denial legal by\r\nenactments of retroactive laws. This I call disgraceful to the American\r\nname. Therefore, in my humble way and limited sphere, if I cannot\r\nrepeal, I will at least evade such unjust laws to the best of my\r\nability, and make them ineffective as far as I am individually\r\nconcerned. I only wish I could wipe out those stains on our national\r\nhonor, by repealing at once laws so discreditable to us. Yes, the more\r\nso, as they bear directly upon the most defenseless, the most powerless\r\nof our citizens\xe2\x80\x94the orphaned Spano-Americans. So, then, I hope you will\r\nhelp me to avoid this American shame, by permitting me to pay for our\r\nland whatever price you think just.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well,\xe2\x80\x9d said Don Mariano, pleased with Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s honest warmth,\r\nand to hear him express opinions and sentiments so very similar to his\r\nown. \xe2\x80\x9cYou can pay whatever you wish, or we can make an agreement that I\r\nwill sell to you when I get my patent. Such is my understanding with Mr.\r\nMechlin and also with your father.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is rather vague. I would prefer to pay to you now so much per\r\nacre. With the understanding that my father (or any one else) is not to\r\nknow I have made this purchase. I mean not for the present.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWould your father object to it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps not. And yet he might see in it a disclaimer from my part\xe2\x80\x94a\r\ncriticism. He is a settler\xe2\x80\x94a \xe2\x80\x98_Squatter_\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94you know, and consequently\r\nvery sensitive about (what they call) \xe2\x80\x98_rights of settlers under the\r\nlaw_.\xe2\x80\x99 He knows my sentiments, but one thing is my expressing them to\r\nhim, and another is to pay money for land he thinks he has lawfully\r\nappropriated. It might seem to him, I imply that his locating perhaps\r\nwas not altogether as honorable a transaction in my eyes, as it may be\r\nlawful in the eyes of the lawmakers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are certainly very honorable, and I am willing to abide by your\r\nwishes in the matter,\xe2\x80\x9d said Don Mariano. \xe2\x80\x9cYou view this question exactly\r\nas I do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClarence blushed with pleasure and bowed, saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are very kind, and that you, who are so generous, should be made to\r\nsuffer as you have, it is, I assure you, so revolting to me (as an\r\nAmerican and a civilized being) that I have felt great desire to go away\r\nrather than to live among these short-sighted and unappreciative people\r\nthat have unfortunately fallen upon you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDon Mariano laughed and said, \xe2\x80\x9cNo don\xe2\x80\x99t go away. Let me have one friend\r\nat least, among so many opponents. Pay whatever you wish, and take as\r\nmuch land as you desire to have, but don\xe2\x80\x99t go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thank you, indeed, but will you not name the price? I don\xe2\x80\x99t think it\r\nis right for me to put a price upon your property.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear sir, that would be so if my property was not going into\xe2\x80\x94smoke\r\nof sulphur\xe2\x80\x94but as it is, and growing fast so \xe2\x80\x98beautifully less\xe2\x80\x99 that I\r\nsuppose even the $1.25 of government price ought to be a handsome figure\r\nto my weary eyes. So name any price you wish.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was agreed that Clarence would pay $10.00 per acre, and take up 640\r\nacres where his father had already located. It was also understood that\r\nthe purchase should not be mentioned to any one. Don Mariano excepted\r\nonly his son Gabriel. Clarence said he would except his mother, inasmuch\r\nas she had told him to pay for the land or else she would not come to\r\nreside upon it.\r\n\r\nDon Mariano said that he would like to mention it to his family and the\r\nMechlins, but feared that if only some allusion was overheard by the\r\nservants, it would be repeated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have no objection to Mr. Mechlin knowing it,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, but they have for servants Hogsden and his wife, and they are very\r\ndishonorable. They would repeat it if by accident they heard it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a pity that Mrs. Mechlin don\xe2\x80\x99t send those two thieves away,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nVictoriano said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I hear that the woman Hogsden repeats things she hears at the\r\nMechlins,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course she does, and steals too, and yet Mrs. Mechlin keeps them,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nVictoriano said, impatiently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps it would be best to say nothing, and I will watch my chance to\r\ntell my father myself, that I paid for the land,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence said. He then\r\nrose to go.\r\n\r\nAs he went down the veranda steps he met Milord returning, still\r\ndragging the skein of silk. But this was no longer of bright variegated\r\nhues, it was black with mud and sadly masticated by Milord\xe2\x80\x99s sharp\r\nteeth, which proudly held it as if challenging any one to take it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou wicked Milord. See what you have done with your poor mistress\xe2\x80\x99\r\nsilk. She will be distressed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Victoriano.\r\n\r\nOn hearing himself thus apostrophised, Milord ran off again with his\r\nplunder, and it was with difficulty that by the combined efforts of\r\nVictoriano and Clarence he was at last captured, but the bright colors\r\nof the silk had all disappeared, a blackened skein resembling a piece of\r\nwet rope was pulled from Milord\xe2\x80\x99s sharp teeth.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VII.\xe2\x80\x94_From Alameda to San Diego._\r\n\r\n\r\nThe Darrell house was now finished, the furniture had arrived, been\r\nunpacked and distributed in the rooms, but the house seemed to old\r\nDarrell entirely too sumptuous for the plain folks, that his family\r\nought to be. That was a truth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here, Clarence, haven\xe2\x80\x99t you been too extravagant in buying such\r\nexpensive carpets, such fine furniture? For gracious sake, how big is\r\nthe bill for all this grandeur?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know yet the price of every item, but don\xe2\x80\x99t be alarmed, I am\r\nsure they would not go beyond the limit I gave Hubert (Hubert made the\r\npurchases), and I assure you, it will all be paid with our volunteer\r\ncrop.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t be sure of that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO, but I am sure\xe2\x80\x94only not too much so\xe2\x80\x94which is the right way of being\r\nsure,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied.\r\n\r\nClarence was now a regular caller at the Alamar and the Mechlin houses.\r\nHe felt that in both places the welcome he received was sincere, for\r\neven the silent Gabriel was always ready to talk to him. As for\r\nVictoriano, his attachment to Clarence was now an acknowledged and\r\naccepted fact,\xe2\x80\x94not rejected by Se\xc3\xb1or Alamar, to judge by\r\nappearances,\xe2\x80\x94and certainly fully and sincerely reciprocated by Clarence.\r\nBoth found great pleasure in each other\xe2\x80\x99s society, and saw each other\r\nevery day.\r\n\r\nIt was now time for Clarence to go to Alameda to bring down the family.\r\nHe and Victoriano talked about it walking towards the Alamar house from\r\nthe Darrells, discussing the probable time of his return.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClarence has come to bid us good-by,\xe2\x80\x9d said Victoriano, walking into the\r\nparlor, followed by Clarence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy! Where is he going?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mercedes, rising, dropping the book she\r\nwas reading.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t be alarmed, he is only going to bring his mother and sisters\r\ndown,\xe2\x80\x9d added Victoriano, maliciously, causing the blood to rush to her\r\nforehead.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed, sitting down, with a resentful look toward her\r\nbrother, and a half appealing, half deprecating one to Clarence, who was\r\ncontemplating her in ecstatic silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think the Holman girls will be coming about the same time. I was\r\ntelling Clarence to look after them a little, if convenient, and if they\r\nare not sea-sick,\xe2\x80\x9d said Victoriano.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey will require my services more if they are sick,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence,\r\nlaughing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you are a good nurse,\xe2\x80\x9d Victoriano observed; adding, \xe2\x80\x9cImagine Corina\r\nHolman nursed by a strange young gentleman; that would kill her sure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would try and prevent that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you, for my friends. I do not think they will be very ill; but I\r\nam sure it will be pleasant for them to have so good an escort,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMercedes.\r\n\r\nClarence promised, therefore, to look after the Misses Holman, and let\r\nthem know which steamer would be best to take coming to San Diego.\r\n\r\nMercedes said she would write notifying them of this arrangement.\r\n\r\nThere was a great _something_ in Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s mind that he wished to say\r\nto Mercedes before leaving, but he had neither courage nor opportunity\r\nto say it, so he left, carrying with him the burden of his thoughts\r\nuntold.\r\n\r\nHis voyage was accomplished in safety, the steamer arriving at San\r\nFrancisco at the regular time. Hubert Haverly came to meet him, and\r\ntogether they went to a restaurant for breakfast.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive us the most secluded room and the nicest breakfast your\r\nestablishment can produce, for this gentleman is very particular, and I\r\nam very hungry,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hubert.\r\n\r\nThe waiter smiled, showed them to the best room in the house, and\r\nretired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow let us talk,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hubert, \xe2\x80\x9cI am dying to tell you how rich you\r\nare, and scold you for not letting me keep your stock longer and making\r\nyou richer. Why were you so anxious to sell? The stock kept rising\r\nsteadily. I was a \xe2\x80\x98bull\xe2\x80\x99 all the time. There was a slight break\r\nonce\xe2\x80\x94only once. Some fellows wanted to pull the stock down, and got a\r\nfew \xe2\x80\x98bears\xe2\x80\x99 to work with them. It lowered a little, but only a few of\r\nthe heavy holders had any fear, and it soon recovered, shooting up\r\nhigher than ever. I got your order to sell about that time, and did so,\r\nbut I assure you my heart ached when I did it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wrote you immediately after that, it was only the first hundred\r\nshares I wanted sold.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but that letter I got three days after I had sold all. I almost\r\ncried like a girl, with disappointment, when you wrote that I was to\r\nsend you only $6000. Now, you could have made a whole million with your\r\nthousand shares.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA whole million?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMost assuredly. Look at yesterday\xe2\x80\x99s quotations, and the stock is still\r\nrising.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTruly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence, reading the stock report; \xe2\x80\x9cthe last paper I saw\r\nwas dated six days ago. But even then \xe2\x80\x98Crown Point\xe2\x80\x99 was still very\r\nhigh.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd so it was, but it is very disappointing to get one-half of a\r\nmillion when you might as well get a whole million. I shall never cease\r\nscolding you for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99ll bear the scolding patiently, considering that it was to\r\navoid scoldings that I gave you the order to sell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo avoid scolding? How so? From whom?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrom my father. He is terribly down on mining stocks. He would consider\r\nme next to a thief if he thought I bought stocks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is absurd. You needn\xe2\x80\x99t tell him how much money you have. Here is\r\nmy statement of all I made; my commission and moneys paid for you. I\r\nsold your stock at a fraction over $800 per share. Oh, Clarence, why did\r\nyou make me sell? Look at this. After buying the government bonds as\r\nordered you have left $260,000, when you might have had half a million\r\nover.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind. I made enough. I\xe2\x80\x99d rather let some one else make the\r\nbalance than to sell when things begin to tumble down. Did you say\r\n$260,000?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, $260,000, when it ought to be $400,000 at least.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClarence laughed at Hubert\xe2\x80\x99s rueful face.\r\n\r\nThe waiter brought in their breakfast.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBroiled oysters on toast! Oysters baked in the shell! Broiled chicken.\r\nLet us discuss them in preference to stock,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence.\r\n\r\nHaving helped his friend and then himself, Hubert said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you going to do with your $260,000 now since you are not to\r\nbuy stock?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have not thought about it, but I guess the best thing would be to\r\ninvest all in government bonds.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich is the same as burying your cash.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you what I\xe2\x80\x99d like to do. I would like to make a safe\r\ninvestment that would give me about $30,000 a year, and then I could\r\nafford to let you gamble with the balance, if there was any balance\r\nleft,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll see to-day what government bonds are selling for, and report to\r\nyou this evening.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat can\xe2\x80\x99t be, as I am to take the two o\xe2\x80\x99clock boat for Alameda.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen will you be back?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo-morrow evening if you want me, but if not I shall wait until the\r\nfamily comes down.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a lucky fellow he is,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hubert, walking towards the Stock\r\nExchange, after promising Clarence to see him to the boat at two\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock. \xe2\x80\x9cIn two years he has made a fortune with a capital of $2000.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHubert was right. Clarence had been a lucky investor. With the sum of\r\n$2000 bequeathed to him by Mrs. Darrell\xe2\x80\x99s Aunt Newton, when he was only\r\nfive years old, and which sum she ordered should be put at interest\r\nuntil he was twenty-one years of age, Clarence speculated, and now he\r\nwas worth close on to a million dollars.\r\n\r\nEverything was ready for the journey when Clarence arrived at his\r\nAlameda home.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you know that it pulls my heart string to tear you away from this\r\nplace?\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence said, looking towards the nice orchard and field\r\nbeyond.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll make us cry if you talk like that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Darrell. \xe2\x80\x9cAlice\r\nhas nearly cried her eyes out already.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind, our lease of this place won\xe2\x80\x99t be out for two years yet, and\r\nwe can come back if the other don\xe2\x80\x99t suit,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence encouragingly.\r\n\r\nTwo days after, the Darrells left Alameda on their way to San Diego,\r\nstopping for a couple of days only at San Francisco. On board the\r\nsteamer Clarence met Mr. Alfred Holman, who had accompanied his\r\ndaughters and now placed them under Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s care\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cAccording to\r\ninstructions from Miss Mercedes\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94Mr. Holman added, making Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nblood rush to his head, as it always did whenever that sweetest of all\r\nnames was mentioned in his presence. \xe2\x80\x9cTell the Alamares I shall be down\r\nsoon. I am only waiting for Tom Scott to escort me.\xe2\x80\x9d So saying, Mr.\r\nHolman laughed and hurriedly kissing his daughters, ran down the gang\r\nplank.\r\n\r\nClarence lost no time in presenting the Misses Holman to his mother,\r\nsisters and brothers, all of whom received them with politeness, though\r\nwith different degrees of warmth, according to the natural share of\r\naffability or that diffidence which half of Darrell\xe2\x80\x99s children inherited\r\nfrom him, especially the two eldest daughters. The amiability of Alice\r\nand her mother\xe2\x80\x99s gentle, winning ways, however, soon dispelled the damp\r\nchill that Jane and Lucy\xe2\x80\x99s reserve generally managed to throw over\r\nstrangers, thus before the steamer got under way, all were conversing\r\nand laughing like old friends, discussing things in general and people\r\nin particular.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you have made a conquest,\xe2\x80\x9d said Amelia Holman to Alice. \xe2\x80\x9cOr\r\nperhaps two, for I saw a little yellow haired man with a very red neck,\r\ncome this way and look at you. Then a loose jointed fellow who walks as\r\nif his feet are too heavy to lift and just drags them, follows, and he\r\ntoo looks at you beseechingly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMercy! I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to be so fascinating as all that might indicate,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Alice, laughing, and a little man gesticulating, and a big man with\r\nshuffling gait and hands in the pockets of his pantaloons, listening\r\nwearily, were seen coming.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know who they are,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence. \xe2\x80\x9cThe little one is married, so\r\nAlice can rest her hopes on the big footed one only.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGracious, how very repulsive the small one is,\xe2\x80\x9d Corina exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho are they?\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Darrell asked when they had turned to go back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe large fellow is Dick Mason, brother-in-law of the little\r\nred-skinned one, who told me his name is Peter Roper, and he is a lawyer\r\nbound for San Diego to practice law there (no matter by what means), he\r\nsays. He gave me this information himself when I went to check our\r\nbaggage. He introduced himself and his brother Dick on the strength of\r\nhis being acquainted with father. He also asked permission to present\r\nhis wife, to my mother and sisters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you give that permission?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Jane, sternly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did, of course; but if his skin is not so thick as it is red he will\r\nnever avail himself of it. I noticed he had been drinking, so I told him\r\nthat at present my mother and sisters wished to converse alone with the\r\nMisses Holman, of whom we are the escort, but that before we reached San\r\nDiego I thought there might be an opportunity to present his wife,\r\nperhaps.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did he say to that?\xe2\x80\x9d Alice asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe grinned and said: \xe2\x80\x98Pretty large escort, ain\xe2\x80\x99t it? About a dozen\r\npeople.\xe2\x80\x99 Yes, I said, but the young ladies are very nice, and require a\r\ngreat deal of attention. \xe2\x80\x98Do they?\xe2\x80\x99 said he, and his yellow eyes leered,\r\nand sticking his tongue to one side of his mouth, made his cheek bulge\r\nout; he then raised his shoulders and lifted his elbows, as if he would\r\nhave flown aloft had his arms been wings.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow impertinent and vulgar,\xe2\x80\x9d Jane exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is of the genus _hoodlum_. A bird aboriginal of the San Francisco\r\nsand dunes, resembling the peacock,\xe2\x80\x9d said Corina Holman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did you do when he made those grimaces?\xe2\x80\x9d Alice asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing. I looked at him as if I expected nothing else, considering\r\nthat it must be natural to him to act like a monkey. My impassibility\r\nrather disconcerted him, as evidently he expected me to consider him\r\nvery funny, and laugh at his droll antics. He added, \xe2\x80\x98Any time will do,\r\nas my wife is not over-anxious to make acquaintances generally.\xe2\x80\x99 So\r\nsaying, he threw back the lappels of his coat, putting his thumbs in the\r\narm-holes of his vest, and strutted off, leaving me to guess whether he\r\nwas making fun of his wife\xe2\x80\x99s exclusiveness or ours.
