He turned back soon,\r\nthough, and said, \xe2\x80\x98We\xe2\x80\x99ll call it square, if you come and take a drink.\xe2\x80\x99\r\nWhen I declined that also, he went off again, and this time angry in\r\ngood earnest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope he will remain so, and not come near you again,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jane.\r\n\r\nVain wish! When the boat stopped at Santa Barbara, Roper took that\r\nopportunity to present his wife to Mrs. Darrell on the strength of his\r\nacquaintance with her husband. He grinned and suppressed a giggle,\r\nthinking it was very funny to claim friendly relations with Darrell,\r\nwhom he had never seen. It was a matter of perfect indifference to him\r\nthat Mrs. Darrell would find out his falsehood afterward. All that he\r\nwanted now was to become acquainted with the Darrell and Holman ladies.\r\nIn this he succeeded, and what is more, succeeded according to his\r\nprinciples, in utter disregard of truth or self-respect. He trusted to\r\nhis inventive genius to explain how he came to imagine he was acquainted\r\nwith Mr. Darrell.\r\n\r\nWhen the boat arrived at San Diego, Gabriel and Elvira came to the wharf\r\nto meet the Misses Holman. They thanked Clarence for the excellent care\r\nhe had taken of them, and Elvira asked him to present her to his mother\r\nand sisters. This was done with pleasure, and he was glad to see that\r\nElvira and Gabriel seemed pleased with his family.\r\n\r\nThe Holmans would remain in town for a couple of days at a friend\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhouse, after that they would go to the Alamar rancho to make their visit\r\nthere. Elvira and Gabriel would remain with them to be their escort.\r\nSuch was Elvira\xe2\x80\x99s message home sent with Clarence.\r\n\r\nMr. Darrell came on board to meet his family, but Mr. Peter Roper was\r\ntoo intently occupied with his baggage to renew his acquaintance; in\r\nfact, he rather hurried off the boat to avoid him.\r\n\r\nThe Darrells arrived at the hotel about the same time, but Peter was\r\nthen particularly engaged making important inquiries from one of the\r\nhotel clerks.\r\n\r\nHe was saying: \xe2\x80\x9cSo, you think there is no lawyer of any prominence; not\r\none that might be called a leading lawyer?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t say that; I only said I don\xe2\x80\x99t know of any.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly. You hear, though, who has the largest practice?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you call a large practice to get people into trouble by spying about\r\npeople\xe2\x80\x99s business and getting commercial agencies (I believe that is\r\nwhat he calls to spy and pry into people\xe2\x80\x99s affairs), then old Hornblower\r\nis the leading lawyer, for he leads people into long law suits always,\r\nand bleeds them and makes money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the man for me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Roper, showing his purple gums in a broad\r\ngrin, and the orange and green of his eyes expanding with feline\r\ninstincts.\r\n\r\nRomeo Hancock had been engaged by Clarence before leaving, to take\r\ncharge of hauling their effects to the rancho. Romeo, therefore, was\r\nthere with three large wagons, and two vaqueros to convey Mrs. Darrell\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npretty Jersey cows. But Clarence had to see that everything started in\r\ngood order before he joined his family at the hotel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI brought the Concord wagon for the women folks and the light spring\r\nwagon for the boys and Tisha,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Darrell. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Concord holds six\r\npeople well, and at a pinch, eight. The light wagon the same; so you\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t have to have any extra conveyances.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, father, I have not hired any,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence replied, and exchanging a\r\nlook with his brothers, said that everything was ready to start, and all\r\nwalked down stairs.\r\n\r\nIn front of the ladies\xe2\x80\x99 entrance was a very handsome carriage which Mrs.\r\nDarrell and her daughters had admired very much on board the steamer;\r\nnext to it was a pretty ph\xc3\xa6ton which they also had admired, and behind\r\nthe ph\xc3\xa6ton was Mr. Darrell\xe2\x80\x99s Concord. He frowned and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere was no use in hiring those carriages, Clarence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCount noses, father,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence, going about busily carrying\r\nparcels to the carriages assisted by his brothers, allowing no time for\r\ndiscussion\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cLet us see. Mother and father in the back seat; Jane and\r\nLucy in the front, Clementina with Everett, the driver. In the ph\xc3\xa6ton I\r\nwill take Alice, her lap dog and our two satchels, and last but not\r\nleast, Webster will take \xe2\x80\x98the Concord\xe2\x80\x99 with Willie in the front seat and\r\nTisha in the back in state, with the cockatoos and canaries and\r\nparcels,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence, patting Tisha on the back.\r\n\r\nAll laughed, approving the disposition of forces.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre these carriages ours, Clary?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Clementine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt looks like it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence, lifting her to her place, \xe2\x80\x9cand you\r\nshall see how soon the ph\xc3\xa6ton distances the big carriage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VIII.\xe2\x80\x94_Victoriano and His Sister._\r\n\r\n\r\nThe golden rays of a setting sun were vanishing in the west, and a\r\nsilvered moon was rising serenely over the eastern hills, when the\r\nph\xc3\xa6ton, having distanced the other carriages by a full half hour,\r\nreached the foot of the low hill where the Alamar house stood. The\r\nFrench windows opening upon the front veranda, sent broad streams of\r\nlight across the garden and far over the hill. Sounds of music greeted\r\nAlice and Clarence on their arrival. He checked his horses saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see there are two roads here; one goes directly to our house, while\r\nthe upper one passes close to the gate of the Alamares. I can take the\r\nupper road if you would like to hear the music.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would, indeed, unless it might seem intrusive.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are too kind hearted to think that, besides, I have a message of\r\nDo\xc3\xb1a Elvira to deliver,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, guiding his horses to the left, slowly\r\nclimbing the hill to approach the gate silently. The ph\xc3\xa6ton stood in the\r\npenumbra between the lights of two windows, and it had not been heard.\r\nThe singing had ceased, the prelude of a Spanish song was begun and\r\ninterrupted. The lady at the piano arose and selected another piece of\r\nmusic, and began the accompaniment of the old and well known \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you\r\nRemember Sweet Alice, Ben Bolt?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is that lady?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Alice in a whisper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is Miss Mercedes,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Clarence, glad of the excuse to\r\nwhisper, and with a preparatory checking of breath and swallowing of\r\nsomething that seemed to fill his throat always, when her name was\r\nmentioned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope she will sing,\xe2\x80\x9d said Alice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps,\xe2\x80\x9d was the laconic reply, and both waited in silence. Clarence\r\ncould distinctly hear his heart throbs.\r\n\r\nA man\xe2\x80\x99s voice, a fine tenor, began the song. He sang the first stanza so\r\ncorrectly and with so much feeling that it seemed to Clarence that he\r\ncould not have listened to the simple melody before now attentively\r\nenough to appreciate its pathos, for it sounded most sweetly touching to\r\nhim. Only one verse was sung.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never thought that song capable of so much expression, or Tano\r\ncapable of giving it so well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe reason why Victoriano interrupted this song was because Mercedes had\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cSing something else, Tano, that song is too sad. It will give me\r\nthe blues.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMe too. Those American songs always speak of death or dying. Ugh! You\r\nsing something lively.\xe2\x80\x9d Then he added, \xe2\x80\x9cI wonder why the Darrells\r\nhaven\xe2\x80\x99t come? I suppose they are going to remain in town until\r\nto-morrow.\xe2\x80\x9d So saying he walked to the window. His eyes were too well\r\ntrained to distinguish objects in the darkness not to have quickly\r\nperceived the ph\xc3\xa6ton, though it could not be seen very distinctly. He\r\nsaw it, but thought it must be Gabriel and Elvira returning\r\nunexpectedly. He ran to the gate, exclaiming:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHallo! What made you return? Didn\xe2\x80\x99t the Holmans come? What has\r\nhappened?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence answered. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Holmans came all safe and sound, and\r\nI delivered them into the hands of Don Gabriel, who, accompanied by Do\xc3\xb1a\r\nElvira, came to meet them. Do\xc3\xb1a Elvira requested me to say that they\r\nwill remain in town a couple of days and then come home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd where is your family?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Tano, coming to the ph\xc3\xa6ton.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are coming, and here is a small part and parcel of the same\xe2\x80\x94called\r\nour sister Alice. Don Victoriano permit me to present Miss Alice\r\nDarrell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Alice, your humble servant,\xe2\x80\x9d said Victoriano, bowing. \xe2\x80\x9cAllow me to\r\ngo to the other side of the ph\xc3\xa6ton to try a more graceful bow a little\r\nnearer, and the honor of shaking hands, _a la Americana_\xe2\x80\x9d.\r\n\r\nMercedes came now, tripping down in the path, also thinking that their\r\ncarriage had returned, because some accident had happened to somebody.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that you, Gabriel?\xe2\x80\x9d said she.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou come and see,\xe2\x80\x9d said Victoriano.\r\n\r\nShe came close to the ph\xc3\xa6ton, right between the wheels, but still\r\nthinking she saw Gabriel, said: \xe2\x80\x9cWhat has happened? Ah! it is Mr.\r\nDarrell,\xe2\x80\x9d she added, with a tremor in her voice, that made Clarence\r\nthink she was alarmed.\r\n\r\nHe hastened to reply: \xe2\x80\x9cNothing has happened. Your friends are all safe\r\nand well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is Miss Alice Darrell. Can you bow to her in the dark, and shake\r\nhands?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Victoriano.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I can, but she might not see my bow,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mercedes, laughing,\r\nand extended her hand, saying: \xe2\x80\x9cI am glad to make your acquaintance,\r\nMiss Darrell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClarence took her hand, as Alice had not seen it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSee here, that hand was for me,\xe2\x80\x9d Alice said, laughing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence, putting Mercedes\xe2\x80\x99 hand in hers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you not shake hands with Clarence?\xe2\x80\x9d said Victoriano. \xe2\x80\x9cI declare,\r\nsolemnly, girls are very ungrateful. Here Clarence has been so sorry,\r\nbecause you hurt your foot, and you have never thanked him for his kind\r\nsympathy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Darrell has never expressed his kind sympathy to me, how was I to\r\npresume he felt it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe presumption would have been mine had I expressed all I felt,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nhe, taking off his glove, which action she rightly understood to mean\r\nthat he wished to shake hands with her.\r\n\r\nShe extended her hand, and he clasped it in his. That ineffable thrill\r\nwhich he felt for the first time in his life when he lifted her in his\r\narms was now felt again. It coursed through his veins with the warm\r\nblood that rushed to his heart.\r\n\r\nNeither one took any notice of what Victoriano and Alice were saying\r\nuntil they heard him say:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s all right. He is going to be married soon, then he\xe2\x80\x99ll be on the\r\nshelf. That\xe2\x80\x99s a comfort.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho will be on the shelf?\xe2\x80\x9d Mercedes asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGabriel, of course; and I am glad of it, as Miss Alice has just coolly\r\ntold me that he is the handsomest man she ever saw, forgetting that\r\nClarence is here, and poor me, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPresent company is always excepted,\xe2\x80\x9d Alice argued; \xe2\x80\x9cand the rule, I\r\nsuppose, applies now, though I cannot well see whether it does or not,\r\nyou being in the dark.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is so. Come out of the shadow.\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence suggested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t now. I feel too abashed,\xe2\x80\x9d Victoriano replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe will soon recover. His fits of diffidence don\xe2\x80\x99t last long,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMercedes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo he is diffident now?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Alice, laughing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; that is why I don\xe2\x80\x99t want you to tell me that Gabriel is handsome;\r\nit abashes me too much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is a good reasoner, too, you see that, Miss Darrell; though by\r\nmoonlight his logic shines but dimly. Come, we must not keep Miss\r\nDarrell longer, since they will not come in,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mercedes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you might stop and take supper with us,\xe2\x80\x9d said Victoriano.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO, no, thank you,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence answered. \xe2\x80\x9cWe came in advance to light the\r\nlamps, and attracted by the music, took the liberty of coming over the\r\nroad.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry. Then you must have heard me sing. Bah! Mercedes, it is your\r\nfault,\xe2\x80\x9d said Victoriano.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t say that. You sing very well, only the song is very plaintive,\r\nand the better it is sung, the sadder is its melody,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt must have seemed like a lugubrious welcome to Miss Alice. I shall\r\nnever sing that song again,\xe2\x80\x9d said Victoriano, emphatically. \xe2\x80\x9cSee if I\r\ndo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad to hear you say that, for you are constantly singing it,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nMercedes said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope it will not be a prophetic coincidence that you should sing it\r\nas I came,\xe2\x80\x9d said Alice, and as she spoke the supper bell rang.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is the prophecy I meant,\xe2\x80\x9d said Victoriano, and all laughed, glad\r\nof the timely turn thus given to the conversation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith this assurance we must go home comforted,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence, and all\r\nbade each other good night.\r\n\r\nThe lamps were lighted, and the windows and doors opened. The Darrell\r\nhouse looked as if there was an illumination for a national celebration.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us go and see how the house looks from the front outside, all\r\nlighted up,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence.\r\n\r\nThey went out to look at it from the garden.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow could you build such a nice house, Clary, and how could papa allow\r\nit?\xe2\x80\x9d Alice said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHush! You must never speak about the cost of this house or its\r\nfurniture. I have made lots of money in stocks, and can afford it, but\r\nfather thinks stock gambling is next to robbery.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMercedes and Victoriano remained for a few moments standing by the gate,\r\nwatching the ph\xc3\xa6ton.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy Jove! but isn\xe2\x80\x99t she sweet! She has just left me deaf and dumb!\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nVictoriano, as the ph\xc3\xa6ton disappeared down the hill.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps you are deaf, since you don\xe2\x80\x99t hear the supper bell ringing\r\nagain, but as for being dumb I am sure the greatest beauty on earth\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t produce that effect.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I tell you I am, and I will go to see her and tell her so\r\nto-morrow,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, following his sister to the supper room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will do nothing of the kind. The idea!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not, pray? Clarence told me to call soon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but he supposed you would have the good taste to wait at least two\r\nor three days.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThree days! Three days! Not if I am alive!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is that about being alive?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rosario.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet him tell you,\xe2\x80\x9d Mercedes replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat I am going to see that sweet little Alice Darrell to-morrow, dead\r\nor alive,\xe2\x80\x9d explained Victoriano.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho will be dead or alive?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Carlota.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI, of course! What a question?\xe2\x80\x9d Victoriano exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs you could not go there if you were dead, I thought you meant that\r\nyou were to go and see her in that insensible state,\xe2\x80\x9d said Carlota.\r\n\r\nVictoriano looked at his sister reproachfully, saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow mean to talk so about that sweet girl.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was to correct you from expressing yourself in that style of yours,\r\nmixing up things and ideas so incongruously. You ought to take care not\r\nto confuse things so absurdly,\xe2\x80\x9d Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy don\xe2\x80\x99t you talk like Gabriel? He always uses good language\xe2\x80\x94in\r\nSpanish or in English,\xe2\x80\x9d Carlota added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBother Gabriel, and Gabriel, and Gabriel! Everybody throws him at my\r\nteeth,\xe2\x80\x9d said Victoriano, beginning to eat with very good appetite.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe operation don\xe2\x80\x99t hurt your teeth, though,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosario, \xe2\x80\x9cto judge\r\nby the very effective manner in which you use them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course, I do, because I am an amiable good fellow, who bears nobody\r\nill-will, even towards his harassing sisters, and much praised elder\r\nbrother, who is hoisted up to the skies a million times a day for my\r\nspecial edification and good example. It is a good thing, I tell you,\r\nladies and gentlemen, a very fortunate thing, that I am so amiable, and\r\nGabriel so good a fellow, or else I would have punched his head into\r\ncalf\xe2\x80\x99s head-jelly, twice a day, many times.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is your confusion of ideas again. You are thinking that yours\r\nmight have been the calf\xe2\x80\x99s head made into jelly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosario.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, miss. I meant what I said.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGabriel is very strong and a good boxer,\xe2\x80\x9d Don Mariano said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere it is again! Sweet Alice says he is the handsomest man she ever\r\nsaw; Lote says he uses beautiful language, and now father implies that\r\nthe fellow could whip me! Give me some more of that chicken _pipian_ to\r\nconsole myself with. Say, mother, why is this delicious chicken stew\r\ncalled \xe2\x80\x98_pipian_?\xe2\x80\x99 Because it makes a fellow \xe2\x80\x98_pio_\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98_pio_\xe2\x80\x99 for more?\r\nor because the chicken themselves would cry \xe2\x80\x98_pio_,\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98_pio_\xe2\x80\x99, if they\r\nwere to see their persons cooked in this way?\xe2\x80\x9d Without waiting for an\r\nanswer to his question, he added: \xe2\x80\x9cI say, mother, arn\xe2\x80\x99t you and the\r\ngirls going to call on the Darrells?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d laconically answered Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy should we?\xe2\x80\x9d queried Carlota.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause they are neighbors like the Mechlins,\xe2\x80\x9d Victoriano replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOld Mathews is our neighbor, too,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosario.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut he is a thief,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Victoriano.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t to steal land robbery?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Carlota.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Darrells occupy the land they selected, with my consent, so I hope\r\nno one in my family will do them the injustice to say that they have\r\nstolen our land, or that they are squatters,\xe2\x80\x9d said Don Mariano firmly.\r\nThen added: \xe2\x80\x9cBut I do not desire any one of you to speak of this matter\r\nwith anybody. Only remember, the Darrells are not squatters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat shall we say, for instance, if the Holmans should notice that we\r\nare very friendly to the Darrells, but not so towards the squatters?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nRosario asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think the Holmans will be too well-bred to ask questions,\xe2\x80\x9d said Do\xc3\xb1a\r\nJosefa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are well-bred, but they are very intimate friends,\xe2\x80\x9d Rosario said\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd very inquisitive ones, too,\xe2\x80\x9d added Victoriano.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRefer them to me,\xe2\x80\x9d Don Mariano said; \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll give them quite a\r\nsatisfactory answer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMeantime, are we not to visit them?\xe2\x80\x9d Victoriano asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVisit whom?\xe2\x80\x9d Carlota asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Darrells, of course,\xe2\x80\x9d Victoriano answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought you meant the Holmans, as we spoke of them last.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBother, with your grammar, you had better keep school,\xe2\x80\x9d Victoriano\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou had better go to one,\xe2\x80\x9d Carlota retorted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have enough of it here. The question now is the visit to the\r\nDarrells. Is this family to visit them or not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you are to do so to-morrow, dead or alive,\xe2\x80\x9d Rosario said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBother! You will call, Mercita, won\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith pleasure, if mamma will permit me,\xe2\x80\x9d Mercedes replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are a sweet pussy always, and the best of sisters. Can\xe2\x80\x99t she go,\r\nmother?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly, if her father does not object.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI not only do not object, but I shall be pleased to have Mercedes and\r\nher mamma and sisters all call, for I think Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s mother must be a\r\nlady.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHurrah for father, he is a man after my own heart,\xe2\x80\x9d said Victoriano,\r\nclapping his hands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPapa feels proud of your approval,\xe2\x80\x9d Carlota said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would suggest that Tano make a _reconnoitering_ visit before Mercedes\r\ngoes, as a leader of a forlorn hope,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosario.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGoodness, how military your terms, but how little your courage,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nVictoriano, derisively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI admit that I always dread to face squatters,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosario.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I said that the Darrells are not to be considered squatters nor\r\ncalled so by any Alamar, and I repeat that such is my wish. Moreover,\r\nnot every settler is necessarily a squatter,\xe2\x80\x9d said Don Mariano.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI beg pardon. I forgot that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosario.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t do it again, Rosy Posy, don\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Victoriano, rising from the\r\ntable, stroking his sister\xe2\x80\x99s back as if to pacify a fractious colt. Then\r\ngoing to a window, said: \xe2\x80\x9cMercedes come here. Look at that; isn\xe2\x80\x99t that\r\nfountain lovely?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn the front garden of the Darrell house, opposite to the front door and\r\nsurrounded by flowers and choice plants, Clarence had erected a fountain\r\nwhich was to emit its numerous jets of chrystaline water for the first\r\ntime, when his mother should drive up to the door. She had done so, and\r\nthe fountain was sending upwards its jets of diamonds under the rays of\r\nthe reflectors at the front door. The effect was pretty and brilliant.\r\nClarence\xe2\x80\x99s filial love was sweetly expressed in the music of the\r\nfountain.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IX.\xe2\x80\x94_Clarence is the Bearer of Joyful News._\r\n\r\n\r\nThe Darrell family had been the happy dwellers of their fine house on\r\nthe Alamar rancho for nearly two months, and the three Misses Holman had\r\nbeen the guests of the Alamar family for the same length of time, and\r\nnow the month of September, 1872, had arrived.\r\n\r\nThe awnings at the east and south side of the front veranda were down,\r\nand in that deliciously cool place, the favorite resort of the Alamar\r\nladies, they now sat with their guests\xe2\x80\x94the Holmans\xe2\x80\x94engaged in different\r\nkinds of fancy work, the greater portion of which was intended to be\r\nwedding presents for Elvira and Lizzie Mechlin, who were to be married\r\nin a few months.\r\n\r\nMercedes was the only one not at work. She was reclining on a hammock,\r\nreading.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cArn\xe2\x80\x99t you going to work anything for the girls, Mercedes?\xe2\x80\x9d Rosario\r\ninquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich girls?\xe2\x80\x9d Mercedes asked, with her eyes fixed on her book.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLizzie and Elvira, of course,\xe2\x80\x9d Rosario answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will when the wedding day is fixed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will soon be, when George arrives,\xe2\x80\x9d Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat will be time enough for my work,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mercedes, looking from her\r\nbook down the valley, towards the Darrell house, as if casually\r\nobserving from under the awning the green meadows below.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat made you come here? The back veranda is entirely shaded, and much\r\ncooler. I have been waiting for you there. Pshaw!\xe2\x80\x9d said Victoriano,\r\ncoming forward and stretching open a hammock to throw himself into it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHad we known that, we would all have rushed there,\xe2\x80\x9d said Corina Holman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn a perfect stampede,\xe2\x80\x9d added Rosario.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought you had gone with your father,\xe2\x80\x9d Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, he said he might stay to lunch at aunt\xe2\x80\x99s. It is too hot a day to be\r\nriding about in the sun.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly, after having been in the moon for two months the change of\r\ntemperature might hurt you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Amelia Holman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a fact; I have been in the moon ever since _you_ came,\xe2\x80\x9d assented\r\nVictoriano.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour moon stays in the valley, it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t rise to this level,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nAmelia.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a lovely moon wherever it may shine. I say, Baby, won\xe2\x80\x99t you go\r\nwith me to the Darrells this evening?\xe2\x80\x9d asked he, addressing Mercedes,\r\nwho had made no reply, for the reason that she had just seen Clarence\r\ncoming on horseback, and, as usual, when any one spoke of him, or she\r\nunexpectedly saw him, she found it necessary to take a little time, in\r\norder to steady her voice, which otherwise might betray her heart\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ntumult.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMercedes\xe2\x80\x99 French novel must be very interesting,\xe2\x80\x9d Carlota said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not a novel\xe2\x80\x94it is French History,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madam Halier.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMercedes, Tano wants you to escort him this evening,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosario.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you take him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere?\xe2\x80\x9d Mercedes asked, without moving.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo the moon,\xe2\x80\x9d said Corina.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe means to the third heaven,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined Victoriano.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI declare, the God of Love is truly miraculous. I think it could even\r\npoetize the Pittikin girls, or the Hughes, in Tano\xe2\x80\x99s opinion,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nCarlota.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTalk of the angels, and you hear the clatter of their hoofs,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nRosario; \xe2\x80\x9cthere he is at the gate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis awning is too low\xe2\x80\x94we don\xe2\x80\x99t see people until they are upon us,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Carlota.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am off. I suppose he will stay to lunch, that will give me all I want\r\nof his charming society,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosario, rising to go as Clarence\r\ndismounted at the gate.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStay, he has seen us all; it would be discourteous to leave now,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nDo\xc3\xb1a Josefa, and Rosario remained.\r\n\r\nVictoriano jumped out of the hammock to meet Clarence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHallo, yourself and welcome! Any news?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, _big_ news,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence replied, blushing crimson,\xe2\x80\x94not at the news\r\nhe brought, as one might have supposed,\xe2\x80\x94but because he had just seen two\r\nlittle feet, in a tiny pair of slippers, with blue rosettes, which he\r\nwell knew. These little blue rosettes had set his heart to beating,\r\nsending more than the normal amount of blood to his head.\r\n\r\nOn leaving her hammock to take a chair Mercedes had shown those\r\ntantalizing tip ends of her slippers, half hidden in a mass of lace\r\nruffles. That was all, and yet poor Clarence was disconcerted, and\r\nbecame more and more so, on perceiving that there were not less than\r\nnine ladies on that veranda; nine pairs of eyes which had undoubtedly\r\nobserved his own, devouring the blue rosettes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the news, pray? Don\xe2\x80\x99t kill us with suspense,\xe2\x80\x9d pleaded Miss\r\nCorina Holman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe news is that Colonel Scott has arrived at San Francisco, and will\r\nbe in San Diego next week.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHurrah,\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Victoriano, \xe2\x80\x9cnow we\xe2\x80\x99ll all be rich.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you make that out?\xe2\x80\x9d Rosario asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind how, we\xe2\x80\x99ll not go into particulars.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, better not,\xe2\x80\x9d Rosario advised.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPapa will be so glad to hear this news,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mercedes, \xe2\x80\x9cand Mr.\r\nHolman, also. He\xe2\x80\x99ll come down now, will he not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, father will come down with Col. Scott, and may be build us a house\r\nright away,\xe2\x80\x9d said Amelia.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is papa now; I am so glad,\xe2\x80\x9d Mercedes exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must run with the news to him,\xe2\x80\x9d said Victoriano, rushing madly\r\nthrough the hall, to the _patio_, or court, where Don Mariano had just\r\ndismounted.\r\n\r\nThe news was so gladdening to Don Mariano, that he came immediately to\r\npropose to the young ladies to have a dance that evening.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut where are the gentlemen? There are plenty of ladies, but unless you\r\ninvite squatters\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94Rosario began, but Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa stopped her with a\r\nlook.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us see,\xe2\x80\x9d said Don Mariano, counting on his fingers, \xe2\x80\x9cthere are\r\nthree or four Darrells, and six or eight Alamares, if my brothers and\r\nhalf of my nephews come. That ought to be enough, I think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlenty. I\xe2\x80\x99ll send a vaquero to aunt\xe2\x80\x99s to call the boys, and you bring\r\nyour brothers and sisters, Clarence,\xe2\x80\x9d said Victoriano.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith pleasure,\xe2\x80\x9d was Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s reply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere will surely be some fellows from town this evening, and we\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nmake them stay,\xe2\x80\x9d added Victoriano.\r\n\r\nThe dance took place and was followed by many others. The Alamar family\r\nwere very hospitable, and had many visitors, who were only too glad to\r\nspend their evenings, dancing with charming and refined young ladies,\r\nwhose society was certainly most attractive.\r\n\r\nThere were several young gentlemen from the Eastern States stopping at\r\nthe principal hotel in San Diego, and they came to Alamar almost daily,\r\nto have a dance, or picnic, or musicale, or a card party.\r\n\r\nThese gayeties were not confined to the Alamar and Mechlin and Darrell\r\nfamilies, nor was the Alamar rancho only made happy because Tom Scott\r\nwas coming. The entire county of San Diego was buoyed up with hopes of\r\nprosperity, which now seemed founded upon a solid basis.\r\n\r\nAs for the town of San Diego itself, the dwarfed and stunted little\r\ncity, she went crazy with joy. Her joy, however, was not of the\r\nboisterous, uproarious kind, it was of a mild character, which smiles at\r\neverybody, and takes all that comes in good part, ready always to join\r\nin the laugh on herself, provided everybody enjoys it. She was happy,\r\nseeing a broad vista of coming prosperity in the near future. Why not?\r\nShe had every reason and every right to expect that the Texas Pacific\r\nwould be built.\r\n\r\nAt last, Col. Scott arrived, and drove to the principal hotel, where a\r\ndeputation of the most prominent citizens immediately waited on him to\r\npay their respects, and learn his wishes as to how his time should be\r\noccupied during his stay in San Diego. The city desired to honor the\r\ndistinguished guest with liberal hospitality, but the business of the\r\nrailroad was the main point in view. There were speeches to be made at\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cArmory Hall,\xe2\x80\x9d with meetings and consultations to be had at nights,\r\nbesides drives to examine the town site and surrounding country during\r\nthe day. The ladies wished to give him a ball, but the business men said\r\nTom Scott did not come to dance, he came to work. There was a banquet\r\ngiven to him, but no ladies were present, only men, and plenty of\r\nrailroad speeches. The ladies could only meet him at private receptions\r\nin the evening, when he was tired out with driving. Yet, this was the\r\nbest that could be done, as his time was limited. But he was amiable,\r\nthe ladies were amiable, and the gentlemen were amiable. So the little\r\ncity of San Diego gave all she had to give; all the lands that had\r\nbelonged to the old \xe2\x80\x9cSan Diego & Gila R. R. Co.,\xe2\x80\x9d all that had been\r\ntransferred to \xe2\x80\x9cThe Memphis & El Paso R. R. Co.,\xe2\x80\x9d all the town lands,\r\nwater front and rights of way that could by any means be obtained, all\r\nwas most generously proffered, adding more lands than those originally\r\ngiven to the road under the old names of \xe2\x80\x9cThe San Diego & Gila Railroad\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nor \xe2\x80\x9cThe Memphis & El Paso Railroad.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCol. Scott left well satisfied with the people of San Diego, and the\r\npeople were charmed with Col. Scott. Speculation then ran wild. Town\r\nlots were bought and sold at fancy prices, but in the madness of the\r\nhour folly seemed wisdom.\r\n\r\nAmong the heaviest investors, Don Mariano Alamar, Mr. James Mechlin, and\r\nMr. Alfred Holman were the most prominent. They bought block after block\r\nof building lots, and only stopped when their money was all invested.\r\nClarence also bought a few blocks, and George and Gabriel risked all\r\nthey dared. Many other people followed this (which proved to be\r\ndisastrous) example, and then all sat down to wait for the railroad to\r\nbring population and prosperity.\r\n\r\n                                  \xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nThe day of the double wedding which was to tie together (with a double\r\nloop) the Alamar and Mechlin families, was set for the 24th of May,\r\n1873. On that day Gabriel and George would lead to the altar their\r\nrespective sisters, Lizzie and Elvira.\r\n\r\nDon Mariano wished to celebrate that double wedding in the same\r\nold-fashioned way in which his own had been solemnized. He wanted at\r\nleast three days of good eating and drinking, and dancing; to have noise\r\nand boat racing; to have a day\xe2\x80\x99s sailing on the bay, and a day\xe2\x80\x99s picnic\r\nin the woods, to which picnic even the stubborn, hostile squatters\r\nshould be invited. But with the sole exception of Victoriano, no one of\r\nhis family approved this programme.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m afraid my dear husband that we are too closely surrounded by\r\nAmericans for us to indulge in our old-fashioned rejoicings,\xe2\x80\x9d Do\xc3\xb1a\r\nJosefa said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe would be laughed at,\xe2\x80\x9d Carlota added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho cares for that?\xe2\x80\x9d Victoriano asked, scornfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe that the right thinking and kind-hearted Americans\r\nwould say anything, except that such is customary among us. But if\r\nGeorge and Gabriel desire to run off in the steamer, as though they were\r\nashamed of matrimony, I say let them have their way. But they will have\r\na wedding that will look like a funeral,\xe2\x80\x9d said the disappointed Don\r\nMariano.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGeorge and Gabriel are willing to have their wedding celebrated as you\r\npropose, but it is the girls that object; they wished to run off and\r\nhide for a month in a fashionable hotel in San Francisco; afterward they\r\ncame to the conclusion that they didn\xe2\x80\x99t want to go to a hotel, so\r\nGabriel proposed that they will take the steamer that goes to Mazatlan\r\nand Guaymas and La Paz, thus to visit all of those places on their\r\nwedding tour,\xe2\x80\x9d Victoriano said. \xe2\x80\x9cAs George had been wishing to see the\r\nMexican coast, this plan suited all very well, and George has written to\r\nhave the steamer stop for them on her way south,\xe2\x80\x9d Victoriano explained,\r\nhalf apologetically, half resentfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is all right; if they are satisfied I am,\xe2\x80\x9d said Don Mariano,\r\nphilosophically, with characteristic amiability.\r\n\r\nThe steamer running between San Francisco and the Mexican ports on the\r\nGulf of California stopped at San Diego to take the newly married\r\ncouples; a large party of friends escorted them on board.\r\n\r\nDon Mariano was kind and affable to all, but many days passed before he\r\nbecame reconciled to the fact that the marriage of his two children was\r\nnot celebrated as his own had been, in the good old times of yore.\r\n\r\nThe brides and grooms had been gone for some time, and might now be\r\ncoming back in a few days.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad we three are alone, for there is something of which I wish to\r\nspeak with you two when no one of the family is with us,\xe2\x80\x9d said Do\xc3\xb1a\r\nJosefa to Carlota and Rosario, as they sat in their favorite front\r\nveranda, sewing.\r\n\r\nThe girls looked up, and casting a quick glance to see whether any one\r\nwas approaching, waited to hear what their mother had to say. The\r\nawnings being only half down no one could come from the outside\r\nunobserved.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it, mamma?\xe2\x80\x9d Carlota asked, seeing that her mother seemed to\r\nhesitate; \xe2\x80\x9canything unpleasant?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, no\xe2\x80\x94yes. That is to say, to me it is, very. Have you noticed\r\nMercedes\xe2\x80\x99 manner lately? She seems absorbed, silent, thoughtful, sad,\r\nand\xe2\x80\x94and\xe2\x80\x94you know what I fear. She says she is not sick, then it is some\r\nmental trouble, I am sure. So, then, I have been thinking that she had\r\nbetter go with Elvira and visit New York for a while, the change will do\r\nher good. I do not approve of young girls going from home on visits, but\r\nas she will go with her married sister, and\xe2\x80\x94and\xe2\x80\x94I hope it will be for\r\nher good.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd yet it may not,\xe2\x80\x9d said Carlota.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps, if it is as you\xe2\x80\x94as we three\xe2\x80\x94fear, absence might be worse for\r\nher,\xe2\x80\x9d added Rosario; \xe2\x80\x9cMercedes is very gentle, but she is very loving\r\nand constant, so it might do more harm than good to send her away now.\r\nRemember what the poet says about it:\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x98La ausencia es para el amor\r\n    Lo que el aire para el fuego;\r\n    Si es poco, lo apaga luego,\r\n    Si es grande, lo hace mayor.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nand I fear that Mercedes is too deeply interested already.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is so. Have you spoken to papa about it?\xe2\x80\x9d Carlota asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mentioned it only once, knowing his partiality to Clarence,\xe2\x80\x9d Do\xc3\xb1a\r\nJosefa replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe might be partial, but when it comes to the danger of his daughter\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmarrying a _squatter_ I should think there would be a limit to\r\npartiality,\xe2\x80\x9d Carlota said, warmly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI fear your father views the matter differently. The one time I\r\nmentioned to him that Clarence seemed to be more and more in love with\r\nMercedes, and my fear that she also liked him more than I care to\r\nbelieve. He said, \xe2\x80\x98Has he made love or proposed to her?\xe2\x80\x99 I told him I\r\nhoped he had not been so audacious as that. \xe2\x80\x98Audacious!\xe2\x80\x99 said he, and\r\nlaughed. \xe2\x80\x98I tell you, wife, if all that is necessary for Clarence to\r\npropose be courage, neither you nor I can stop him, for the boy is no\r\ncoward. I reckon that it is Mercedes herself who gives him no\r\nencouragement; that is what deters him, but none of our _sangre azul_,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nand he laughed again. I said to him, you take very coolly a matter that\r\nmight be a question of our child\xe2\x80\x99s fate for life, but he only appeared\r\namused at my anxiety. He said: \xe2\x80\x98Don\xe2\x80\x99t borrow trouble; Clarence is a most\r\nexcellent young fellow\xe2\x80\x94bright, energetic and honorable. Don\xe2\x80\x99t bother\r\nthem or yourself; if they feel true love they have a right to it. Trust\r\nhim, he is all right.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut a squatter! The idea of an Alamar marrying a squatter! For\r\nsquatters they are, though we dance with them,\xe2\x80\x9d Carlota said. \xe2\x80\x9cI am\r\nshocked at papa\xe2\x80\x99s partiality. I must say yes, mamma, send poor Mercita\r\naway.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; with all due respect to papa, I fear I will not be reconciled to\r\nthe idea of Mercedes being a daughter-in-law of old Darrell,\xe2\x80\x9d Rosario\r\nsaid, with a shudder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNeither could I,\xe2\x80\x9d added Carlota.\r\n\r\nAnd thus felt and thus reasoned these proud ladies _in those days_. For\r\nalthough the shadows of black clouds were falling all around, they had\r\nnot observed them, or suspected their proximity; they held up their\r\nheads proudly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd has Clarence the means of supporting a wife?\xe2\x80\x9d Rosario asked. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\r\nis another question to be considered.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know. I heard he had made money in stocks, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t know how\r\nmuch,\xe2\x80\x9d Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have no faith in stocks,\xe2\x80\x9d said Carlota.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us not mention this to Mercedes yet. When Elvira returns we will\r\nconsult with her,\xe2\x80\x9d Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa said.\r\n\r\nNothing was said to Mercedes about her journey, but she was never\r\nallowed to see Clarence alone.\r\n\r\nElvira returned, and the project mentioned to her. She, as a matter of\r\ncourse, was delighted at the prospect of having her favorite sister with\r\nher. The pain of leaving her home would be lessened in her company.\r\n\r\nA day or two after, when Elvira was alone in her room, Mercedes came in,\r\nlooking rather pale, and letting herself drop into the first chair she\r\ncame to, said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is this unexpected news about my going to New York with you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood news, I think. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you like it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly. But it is too sudden. Why hadn\xe2\x80\x99t mamma thought of it\r\nbefore?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause she did not think your health required any change.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI tell you what, mamma alarms herself unnecessarily, and puts but poor\r\nreliance on me. I understand it all, but as a trip to New York is a most\r\ndelightful medicine, I am willing to take it, and that she should\r\nconsider my health in a precarious state.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you _do_ look pale and thin, Mercita.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNonsense!\xe2\x80\x9d Mercedes exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cI have been keeping late hours, and\r\ndancing too much. If I go to bed early I shall get back my good color\r\nand flesh again. However, I am glad to play the invalid until I get on\r\nboard the cars.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well. I\xe2\x80\x99ll be alarmed for you, too, until we get off.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMercedes laughed, and went to her room singing, but once there her\r\ngayety vanished. She locked her door, and threw herself on the bed,\r\nburying her face in her pillow to stifle her sobs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan anything tear his image from my heart? No. Nothing! nothing! They\r\nmay send me away to the other end of the world, they shall not part us,\r\nfor you will still fill my heart, my own darling, holding my very soul\r\nforever in full possession.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMercedes, being not quite seventeen, her grief at parting from Clarence\r\nwas wild, vehement and all-absorbing. But she had been trained to\r\nobedience, and her battles with the spirit always took place after she\r\ncarefully locked her bedroom door. Then Clarence was wildly\r\napostrophized, and a torrent of tears relieved the overcharged, aching\r\nheart.\r\n\r\nThe day of departure arrived, and she had not had one minute\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nconversation alone with Clarence.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER X.\xe2\x80\x94_But Clarence Must Not be Encouraged._\r\n\r\n\r\nThe wharf was over-crowded. The steamer was about to leave. The last\r\ncar-load of baggage had been quickly shipped, and Clarence had not been\r\nable to say a word to Mercedes which might not have been heard by the\r\npersons surrounding her. He was pale and desperate. He had gone on board\r\nthe steamer just to ask her one question, but she had never been alone\r\nfor an instant. And thus they must part,\xe2\x80\x94for the embodied \xe2\x80\x9c_Fuerza del\r\ndestino_\xe2\x80\x9d now came in the shape of a boy clanging in deafening clatter a\r\nmost discordant bell, saying that those who were not going on the\r\nsteamer must go ashore. A hurried hand-shaking, and the troop of friends\r\nmarched down the gang-plank to turn round and look many more tender\r\nadieus from the wharf.\r\n\r\nDon Mariano had observed Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s deathly pallor, and how faithfully\r\nit was reflected on Mercedes\xe2\x80\x99 face; he saw the unhappy young man\r\nstanding aloof from the crowd on the extreme edge of the wharf. He went\r\nto him, and laying his hand gently on his shoulder, said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat position is dangerous\xe2\x80\x94you might lose your balance,\xe2\x80\x9d and he pulled\r\nhim gently away. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are very pale. I fear, my dear boy, that you are\r\nmore troubled than you have admitted to any one. What is it? Tell me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClarence shook his head, but suppressing his emotion, said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot express my misery. She is sent away that I may not even have\r\nthe pleasure of seeing her. No one can love her as I do, impossible!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy have you not spoken to me of this before?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Don Mariano,\r\nkindly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I did not dare. I thought of doing so a thousand times, but did\r\nnot dare. I did not fear unkindness or rejection from _you_, but from\r\nDo\xc3\xb1a Josefa and the young ladies I did, and I have never had an\r\nopportunity to speak alone to Miss Mercedes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was an additional reason for speaking to me. Cheer up. \xe2\x80\x98_Faint\r\nheart never won fair lady._\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell me that again. Say you do not reject me, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll jump aboard and\r\nfollow her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not reject you, and I repeat what I said, follow her if you wish,\r\nand try your luck. I want to see you both happy, and both of you are\r\nvery unhappy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClarence looked toward the boat. The gang-plank had been removed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a happy girl you are, Mercedes, to visit New York. How I wish I,\r\ntoo, could go,\xe2\x80\x9d he heard Corina Holman say.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome on, it is not too late yet,\xe2\x80\x9d George replied.\r\n\r\nClarence looked up, and met Mercedes\xe2\x80\x99 eyes. It seemed as if George\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwords were intended for him.\r\n\r\nHe clasped Don Mariano\xe2\x80\x99s hand, saying hurriedly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I understand you, I have your permission to go. May I? Tell me\r\n\xe2\x80\x98yes.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98_Faint heart never won fair lady_,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated, smiling, and\r\nreturning the warm pressure of his hand, added: \xe2\x80\x9cYes, go and try your\r\nluck.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClarence turned, and without another word quickly made his way through\r\nthe crowd.\r\n\r\nThe steamer\xe2\x80\x99s wheels began to move; the captain was already on the\r\nbridge, over the starboard wheel, and had given the order to let go the\r\nhawsers. In another instant the steamer would leave the wharf.\r\n\r\nClarence felt himself pulled by the arm, he turned impatiently, and met\r\nEverett, who handed him two telegrams, saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have looked for you everywhere. These telegrams followed each other\r\nquickly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I know,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence said, taking them; adding, without stopping his\r\nhurried walking, \xe2\x80\x9cRetty, I am going. Tell them at home I got three\r\ntelegrams calling me to San Francisco.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you haven\xe2\x80\x99t read them,\xe2\x80\x9d urged Everett, trying to follow him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I know what they are; I have another in my pocket.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLifting his arm with the telegrams in his hand, he said to the captain:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCaptain, one moment. I must go north. Please take me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe captain did not hear him, and at the same time called out:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet go that hawser! Do you want it to snap?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe crowd ran off, giving a wide berth to the heavy rope, which now, by\r\nits own tension, made it impossible to be slipped off the pile, although\r\nmany pairs of hands were tugging at it manfully.\r\n\r\nThe stern expression of the captain\xe2\x80\x99s face softened as he saw Clarence\r\nstanding on the brink of the wharf.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStep back, Mr. Darrell, quickly, the rope might part,\xe2\x80\x9d said he; but\r\nnoticing that Clarence desired to speak to him, motioned to the first\r\nofficer to take his place, and ran down to hear what Clarence said.\r\n\r\nA minute after the steamer stood still for an instant, then the wheels\r\nbegan to revolve in reversed motion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere she is, Mr. Darrell; she\xe2\x80\x99ll be alongside in a minute,\xe2\x80\x9d the\r\ncaptain said, pleased with the opportunity to oblige Clarence.\r\n\r\nAnd the steamer, propelled by one wheel, began to back as if with the\r\nside-long motion of a highly intelligent crab who understood the\r\nsituation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRead your telegrams,\xe2\x80\x9d Everett repeated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right\xe2\x80\x94to please you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence, tearing them open. Adding,\r\nafter reading a few words, \xe2\x80\x9cIt is as I expected. I am wanted by Hubert.\r\nSend him a dispatch to-night saying I left, and to accept M.\xe2\x80\x99s offer,\r\nand pay the money at once.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, Mr. Darrell, come on,\xe2\x80\x9d the captain said.\r\n\r\nHurriedly Clarence shook hands with Don Mariano, Gabriel, Everett and\r\nVictoriano.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake care, jump in on the downward swing, when about on a level with\r\nthe wharf,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel.\r\n\r\nClarence nodded, gave him his hand, and planting his foot firmly on the\r\nwharf, gave one spring, and wiry as a cat, alighted on the steamer\r\nbeside the captain, who hugged him, saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBravo, my boy, I could have done that twenty years ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDon Mariano and Gabriel lifted their hats in congratulatory salutation;\r\nVictoriano and Everett twirled theirs in the air hurrahing; the ladies\r\nwaved their handkerchiefs, and the steamer giving a dip and a plunge\xe2\x80\x94by\r\nway of a very low courtesy\xe2\x80\x94bounded up and started onwards, as if\r\nsatisfied she had been good natured long enough, and now must attend to\r\nbusiness. In a few minutes she had made up for lost time, and was\r\nheading for Ballast Point, leaving San Diego\xe2\x80\x99s shore to be merged into\r\nthe blue hills of Mexico beyond, as if obeying the immutable law which\r\nsays that all things must revert to their original source.\r\n\r\nElvira\xe2\x80\x99s beautiful eyes were so filled with tears that she could see\r\nnothing. Still, she kept her gaze riveted upon that fast receding wharf.\r\nGeorge stood a few feet apart, prudently thinking that the two sisters\r\nwould perhaps prefer to be by themselves while taking their last look at\r\nthe dear ones standing on the wharf. He, too, felt much moved; he would\r\nhave preferred to remain with his family at Alamar. He would come next\r\nyear\xe2\x80\x94he thought\xe2\x80\x94and perhaps remain in California permanently. With this\r\nthought in his mind, almost shaped into resolve, he came to Elvira\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nside, and quietly slipping his arm round her waist, said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t cry, sweetest, I will bring you back next year, and we will make\r\nour home near our parents. No matter if I make less money, we will have\r\nmore happiness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nElvira looked unutterable thanks.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you hear him, Mercedes?\xe2\x80\x9d she said, and Mercedes nodded, but moved a\r\nlittle further off, not yet trusting her voice to make any reply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here, this won\xe2\x80\x99t do; this will spoil our blue eyes,\xe2\x80\x9d said George,\r\nputting his other arm around Mercedes\xe2\x80\x99 pretty shoulders. \xe2\x80\x9cI insist upon\r\nyou turning your thoughts toward New York, Long Branch, Newport and\r\nWashington; think of all the fun we will have visiting all those places.\r\nThen we will come back gay and happy, and our dear ones will be so glad\r\nto see us again. Think of all that,\xe2\x80\x9d and thus George exerted his\r\neloquence to administer consolation. \xe2\x80\x9cI am sure all at home will be\r\nthinking of our return by to-morrow morning,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, by way of climax\r\nto his consoling rhetoric.\r\n\r\nBut George was mistaken. The Alamar ladies found it very hard and\r\ndifficult to reconcile themselves to be separated from Elvira and\r\nMercedes.\r\n\r\nThe fact that Clarence had gone in the same steamer, added much\r\nbitterness to Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa\xe2\x80\x99s sorrow at separating from both daughters.\r\nShe did not even wish any one to mention Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s name in her\r\npresence. Don Mariano\xe2\x80\x99s arguments in favor of the bold young man were at\r\nfirst ineffectual, but after a while she began to think that she ought\r\nto trust more in Mercedes\xe2\x80\x99 pride and Elvira\xe2\x80\x99s vigilance.\r\n\r\nIn the meantime the travelers continued their voyage very happily.\r\nClarence rightly conjectured that Mercedes would suppose he had followed\r\nher to declare his love, and this supposition would redouble her\r\nshyness. Her manner at first, fully confirmed this surmise, so, to put\r\nher at her ease, he was very kind and attentive, but never betrayed by\r\nword or look, his heart\xe2\x80\x99s devotion. His manner was exactly all that she\r\ncould wish, the behavior of a devoted brother, and in consequence she\r\nbegan to be less shy. He spoke of having received three telegrams,\r\ncalling him north; this surely was a good reason for his unexpected\r\njourney.\r\n\r\nThey visited Los Angeles, went ashore at Port Harford and Santa Barbara,\r\nand as George was naturally devoted to his bride, there seemed no\r\nalternative for Mercedes but to accept Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s escort, and lean on\r\nhis arm whenever that operation became necessary.\r\n\r\nThe nights were lovely, with a full moon in the azure sky, and the sea\r\nair, neither cold nor warm, but of that California temperature, which\r\nseems to invite people to be happy, giving to all an idea of the perfect\r\nwell-being we expect to find in the hereafter.\r\n\r\nThere was a great deal of freight to be landed at Santa Barbara. The\r\npassengers going to San Francisco were already on board. Still the\r\nsteamer tarried. Some lady friends of Elvira, who were going north had\r\ncome aboard, and as they had much to say, took her away to their\r\nstaterooms.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait for me here, I\xe2\x80\x99ll return in half an hour,\xe2\x80\x9d said she to George; but\r\nhe thought he knew how ladies measure time when engaged in talking, so\r\nhe slowly arose and said he would go to play cribbage with the captain.\r\n\r\nThe steamer now shivered and trembled, as if awakening from a nice nap.\r\nThe wheels revolved lazily and then she was off, dragging a luminous\r\nwake of myriads of evanescent diamonds.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you wish to go, Mr. Darrell, please do so; do not remain on my\r\naccount,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mercedes, when George rose to go.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all. I remain entirely on my own, as I do not particularly\r\ndesire to play cut-throat cribbage, and as it is too early for you to\r\nretire, suppose you permit me to remain until your sister returns.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly, do so, else I\xe2\x80\x99ll stay,\xe2\x80\x9d said George, going.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave I offended you in any way?\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, of course not. What a question. What makes you ask that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause you must know it would be cruel punishment to send me off.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t think anything of the kind, only I didn\xe2\x80\x99t wish to be selfish\r\nand keep you from going if you wished it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow could I wish to go anywhere and leave you; I would not go to\r\nheaven, if to do so I would have to renounce you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease do not talk like that, some one might hear you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is not a soul within hearing. Our only witness is that lovely\r\nmoon, and she will not betray.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo matter, please do not speak like that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLike what? That I love you? I have never yet said it in words, but you\r\nknow it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! Mr. Darrell!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, you know it, and to avoid me you are going away; going from me, no\r\nmatter if it killed me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not my choice, I only obey,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, clasping her trembling\r\nhands, now cold as ice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it so? Did you not wish to avoid me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease do not ask me, you\xe2\x80\x99ll make me very miserable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would not cause you one single pang, if to avoid it I had to die.\r\nBelieve me, all I wish to know is, whether I have been so blind as not\r\nto see your dislike; whether it was your own choice to go, or you were\r\ncompelled to do so by your mother?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease don\xe2\x80\x99t blame mamma.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not blame her in the least. She has a perfect right to object to\r\nme if she wishes, but I too, have at least, the sad privilege of asking\r\nwhether you also object to me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have nothing against you; I like you very much, as\xe2\x80\x94as a friend,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid, trembling, painfully agitated.\r\n\r\nClarence laughed a hoarse, discordant laugh that made her feel\r\nmiserable.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been told that young ladies say that always, when they mean to\r\nlet down easily a poor devil whom they pity and perhaps despise. Thanks,\r\nMiss Mercedes, for liking me \xe2\x80\x98as a friend,\xe2\x80\x99 thank you. Perhaps I am a\r\npresumptuous fool to love you, but love you I must, for I can not help\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe stood up and looked down at the dark ocean in silence. She looked up\r\nto his face and her beautiful features looked so pleadingly sad, that he\r\nforgot his own misery and thought only of the pain those superb eyes\r\nrevealed.\r\n\r\nHe seated himself very near her, and took both of her hands in his own.\r\nSurely there was something troubling her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow cold these dear little hands are. Have I caused you pain?\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nasked. She nodded but did not speak.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I have pained you, when I would give my heart\xe2\x80\x99s blood to make you\r\nhappy. Oh! Mercedes, I cannot give you up, it is impossible while I\r\nlive. Do you command me to do so? Do you wish it? You know that I have\r\nloved you from the first moment I saw you; when I lifted you in my arms.\r\nThe exquisite pleasure I felt then, and the yearning I have felt ever\r\nsince, to hold you in my arms again, as my own sweet wife, that longing\r\ntells me incessantly that I can never love any one else; that I must win\r\nyou or renounce love forever on earth. Tell me, will you cruelly repel\r\nme?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe was silent, listening with averted face, as if afraid to meet his\r\ngaze, but she did not withdraw her hands, which he still held in both\r\nhis own, as if he would never willingly release them again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMercedes, say that you reject me only to obey your mother, and I will\r\nnot despair, for I know that your father does not object to me; on the\r\ncontrary, he sanctions my love, he would accept me as his son-in-law.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe turned quickly, gazed at him with an eager, inquiring look.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, he gave me permission to follow you and ask you to be my wife.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat? He? My papa did that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. When he saw me looking so wretched with the pain of parting from\r\nyou, he said to me, \xe2\x80\x98Cheer up; faint heart never won fair lady.\xe2\x80\x99 I said\r\nto him, if you tell me that in earnest, I\xe2\x80\x99ll jump aboard the steamer and\r\nfollow her. He repeated the quotation, adding: \xe2\x80\x98Go and try your luck.\xe2\x80\x99\r\nIs not that sufficient?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDarling papa, he is so kind,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, eluding Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s question,\r\nbut her evident gratitude toward her father spoke volumes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed he is. His heart is full of nobility. He does not permit unjust\r\nprejudices to influence him into dislikes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must not blame my poor mamma. She thinks you did some wrong act,\r\nbut she is not prejudiced against you, nor does she dislike you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did some wrong act? What is it? When?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t tell you, for I do not know, and perhaps I am wrong to\r\nhave said so much. But I spoke because it was painful to me to think\r\nthat you believe my own loving, lovely mamma prejudiced, for she is not.\r\nShe might be mistaken, but she is kindness itself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClarence mentally demurred to this warm praise, but wisely held his\r\npeace.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPromise me you will not think mamma is prejudiced,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, without\r\nthe least suspicion of the tyranny, the unreasonableness of such a\r\nrequest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI promise it, of course, if you desire it, but I would at the same\r\ntime, like to know what is the _wrong_ act of which I am accused, that\r\nhas brought upon me her censure. I assure you I have not the slightest\r\nidea; I think my record as an honest man can well bear scrutiny. Can it\r\nbe that I have made money in mining stocks?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no. She does not know that, and if she did, she would not think it\r\nwrong, for she knows nothing about stocks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I vow I have not the remotest idea of what it is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThink no more about it now, and when you return, you ask papa. He will\r\nsoon find out the mistake and vindicate you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, he will do so I am sure. I would blindly trust my life and honor\r\nin his hands,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, warmly, and quick as a flash came his reward,\r\nfor she pressed his hands most gratefully. \xe2\x80\x9cAh! Mercedes why did you do\r\nthat?\xe2\x80\x9d The poor young man was trying to make up his mind not to press\r\nhis suit until he had been vindicated, and Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa had nothing\r\nagainst him. But that pressure made him ambitious, impatient; he wished\r\nto have some promise that she would not accept any one else\xe2\x80\x99s suit. She\r\nwas going from him, out of his sight. He was certain that dozens, yes\r\nhundreds, would fall in love with her as soon as they saw her. Would she\r\nnot love some one? It would be natural to prefer to him, some of those\r\nelegant New Yorkers, or some fascinating foreigner whom she might meet\r\nin Washington. This thought made him wretched.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m so glad you appreciate papa,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, withdrawing her hands,\r\nwhich she considered he had held long enough. Noticing that he looked\r\ntroubled, and that his hand trembled, she added: \xe2\x80\x9cI fear I have been\r\nindiscreet, and have caused you pain by what I said; if so, I am very\r\nsorry. Have I pained you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have never done anything dishonorable. I can prove that to Do\xc3\xb1a\r\nJosefa at any time. But\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he broke off, and after a paused, added: \xe2\x80\x9cOh!\r\nMercedes! how wretched I shall be, thinking that you might love some one\r\nelse. Is not your refusal to give me any encouragement a proof that you\r\nfeel you never can care for me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease don\xe2\x80\x99t say that. I do care for you. That is, I mean, I ought not\r\nto tell you so, but\xe2\x80\x94but\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94she did not finish, for the rash young man had\r\nagain seized her little hands, and was covering them with kisses,\r\nforgetting that any passenger had the right to come and sit there on the\r\nsame bench to enjoy the silvery moonlight, sailing over the broad,\r\nsublime Pacific.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! Mr. Darrell! Don\xe2\x80\x99t do that. Please let us go now to call Elvira.\r\nShe thinks George is with me,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, rising.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe don\xe2\x80\x99t want Elvira, we don\xe2\x80\x99t want George. Let them be. Why do you\r\ngrudge me this happiness of being alone with you for the first and,\r\nperhaps, for the last time in my life? Please sit down. I will behave\r\nmyself. I will not kiss your hands, I promise; but won\xe2\x80\x99t you reward my\r\nself-restraint by answering one question?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the question?\xe2\x80\x9d said she, sitting down again, only a little\r\nfurther off; \xe2\x80\x9ctell me, and then we must go to find Elvira.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want you to tell me\xe2\x80\x94I mean, I beg and entreat you to tell me this\xe2\x80\x94if\r\nI can prove that I have never done anything dishonorable, and your\r\nmother ceases to object to my marrying you, will you then consent to be\r\nmy wife?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe question gave Mercedes exquisite pleasure, for she loved him with\r\nall her heart. The word wife soundly so sweetly coming from his lips,\r\nbut she had promised her mother \xe2\x80\x9c_not to encourage him_.\xe2\x80\x9d So she must\r\nnot. It would be dishonorable to break her word. What could she say, not\r\nto make him unhappy, and yet not commit the sin of disobedience to her\r\nmother\xe2\x80\x99s command?\r\n\r\nShe looked down, and her expressive features again showed that she was\r\ntroubled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! I was mistaken. Your silence tells me I cannot hope.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo not be impatient, please. I was trying to think how I could explain\r\nto you my position.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour position?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. How much what papa said to you might alter things. But I cannot\r\nsee how I can say anything to you, except to be patient. Yes, let us\r\nboth be patient.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPatience and despair do not travel together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDiscard despair, and trust to patience, and\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94she was going to say,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9ctrust me,\xe2\x80\x9d but remembered her mother\xe2\x80\x99s commands, and that to say so\r\nmuch even would be _to encourage him_. She was silent. She could have\r\nrejected an offer of marriage easily without taking away all hope, but\r\nas she \xe2\x80\x9c_must not encourage him_,\xe2\x80\x9d that was the most difficult dilemma\r\nfor the poor girl. \xe2\x80\x9cTrust to papa, and\xe2\x80\x94and do not be blaming me in your\r\nheart. I cannot bear that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall not blame you. I shall do whatever you order me. But at all\r\ntimes I do not understand you,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, sadly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is because my position is so\xe2\x80\x94so difficult, so unnatural. I wish you\r\ncould understand it without my explaining it. Can\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll try,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, in most dejected tones, again thinking of the\r\nelegant New Yorkers, and fascinating Washingtonians, on their knees\r\nbefore her. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I do not understand why you refuse me one word of\r\nencouragement.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! that is just _the word_ I cannot give,\xe2\x80\x9d she sighed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is all the work of Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa,\xe2\x80\x9d thought he, and the form of the\r\nhandsome matron seemed to rise before him from the billows of the\r\nPacific, and stand with Juno\xe2\x80\x99s lofty majesty in severe impassibility\r\nbefore his sad gaze.\r\n\r\nMercedes, too, was looking at the immense sea, as if trying to discover\r\nin that vast expanse some consoling words that a good, obedient daughter\r\nmight speak on such an occasion.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XI.\xe2\x80\x94_George is a Christian Gentleman._\r\n\r\n\r\nIn vain did Mercedes scan the broad bosom of the Pacific Ocean in search\r\nof something to say that would be soothing to Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s feelings, very\r\nproper for her to utter, and very acceptable to her mamma\xe2\x80\x99s sentiments,\r\nhad she been there to hear it. But that vast sea was dark and mute. It\r\ndid not respond. It only made her shudder to think of its awful silence\r\nthat was so solemn, but not in the least comforting. It was so dark, so\r\nlimitless, so cold. She turned her eyes to the luminous wake trailed by\r\nthe steamer where such wealth of diamonds was wasted. \xe2\x80\x9cFitful\r\nscintillations and then all lost in gloom,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, adding: \xe2\x80\x9cNo, all\r\nis not wasted, those bright diamonds are not as evanescent as we, they\r\nwill sink, but reappear again and remain there always to gladden or\r\namuse poor travelers for ages to come; yes, when our two poor hearts\r\nhave ceased forever to throb with joy or pain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it not, then, wrong when life is so flitting to refuse pure and holy\r\nhappiness which God has permitted to the children of man?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will be talking bookish, like Corina Holman, if we sit here alone\r\nwith the silent Pacific. Let us go to find Elvira,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, rising.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, there she is now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nElvira was bidding good night to her two lady friends who stood at the\r\ndoor of their state-room, and (as all ladies must) had something very\r\ninteresting to say at the last moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd so I am to be patient whether there is hope or not,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou said you would speak with papa. You forget how very kind he is to\r\neverybody in general, and how partial to you in particular.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, he is most generous, almost too noble for this world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have often thought that, but as he is past fifty, I trust that a kind\r\nProvidence will spare him to us for many years yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course, he will be spared to you. If no good man could live, then\r\nthe gift of life would be a brand upon man\xe2\x80\x99s forehead. But a character\r\nas his, is truly very rare. He comes nearer to my standard of excellence\r\nthan any other man I ever saw, and I revere and love him for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall treasure those words in my heart, believe me. Let them remain\r\nthere forever,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, her voice vibrating with emotion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, well, and where is George?\xe2\x80\x9d said Elvira, looking around for her\r\nmissing husband.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe went to the captain\xe2\x80\x99s room to play cribbage about two minutes after\r\nyou left,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mercedes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood chaperone he is; and what have you been talking about here like\r\ntwo little owls who know they musn\xe2\x80\x99t jump into the water because they\r\nare not ducks?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne isn\xe2\x80\x99t, any way,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence said, smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs my married experience is yet fresh and limited, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know whether\r\nit would be proper or not for us three to take a turn on deck and see\r\nwhether George is enjoying himself. What do you think, Mr. Darrell,\r\nwould a husband object to that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should say not. Why should he? To my way of thinking no husband of\r\nordinary good sense could object to his wife showing that interest in\r\nhim. Mr. Mechlin will not, I am sure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet Mr. Darrell take a look first,\xe2\x80\x9d suggested Mercedes.\r\n\r\nClarence arose to go, Elvira said: \xe2\x80\x9cOnly pass by, as if by accident, and\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99ll go or not, according to circumstances.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen Clarence had gone beyond hearing, Elvira said: \xe2\x80\x9cHe looks pale\r\nagain, have you made him unhappy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have not made him happy, that is sure, and I am miserable, but you\r\nknow mamma\xe2\x80\x99s feelings, what can I do? Oh, what can I do?\xe2\x80\x9d said she,\r\nputting her arms around her sister and the hot tears she had been\r\nrepressing flowed fast. \xe2\x80\x9cI am so sorry I have to make him so unhappy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must say I feel sorry for him myself. I am not sure that mamma does\r\nhim justice,\xe2\x80\x9d Elvira observed reflectively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd to think that papa himself told him to follow me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; and he is disappointed, but what can I do, dear, when mamma told\r\nme _not to encourage_ him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI certainly am under no pledge, and papa\xe2\x80\x99s authority is entitled to as\r\nmuch respect as mamma\xe2\x80\x99s,\xe2\x80\x9d Elvira said significantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is true, but you see mamma made me promise not to _encourage_\r\nhim,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mercedes with sad insistence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, and Rosa and Lota urged her to it. There is George now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will go to my room; they will see by my red eyes that I cried.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo and bathe them. Drink some water, too, and come back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I\xe2\x80\x99ll bring you some by way of an excuse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy did Mercedes run off?\xe2\x80\x9d George asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe will be back in a minute; she went to take a glass of water.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! why did she not tell me to bring it to her?\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence\r\nregretfully. \xe2\x80\x9cI ought to have thought of bringing it. Wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t she\r\nrather have a glass of wine or lemonade? and you, too, Mrs. Mechlin? I\r\nshall take it as a favor if you will accept. A glass of champagne with\r\nice I think would do very well for all of us; don\xe2\x80\x99t you think so Mr.\r\nMechlin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, champagne with ice would be very nice, provided the champagne be\r\ngood,\xe2\x80\x9d George replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us try any way,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence, going to order the wine. George and\r\nElvira watched him, and when out of hearing George said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you know I like that young man very much. What is your mother\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nobjection to him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis family, I believe, or rather his father.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOld Darrell looks like a decent, honorable sort of a man to me.\r\nCertainly Clarence is very gentlemanly, and (what is equally to be\r\nconsidered) Mercedes likes him more than is good for her peace of mind\r\nif she is not to have him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy poor little sister, she is so unhappy, and, just think of it, papa\r\ntold Clarence to come, to follow Mercedes and propose to her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe did? That is just like him. Doubtless he thought of the times when\r\nhe would ride eighty miles to go and serenade Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa, and his\r\nsympathies all went to Darrell. It is a pity your mother doesn\xe2\x80\x99t feel as\r\nkindly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what makes me feel more for Mercedes is, that she loves Clarence\r\ndearly, but in obedience to mamma\xe2\x80\x99s wishes she will not even give him\r\nany encouragement at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen _we_ must, that\xe2\x80\x99s all. Only let us first be sure that she loves\r\nhim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, as to that, if you had only seen her beautiful eyes filled with\r\nsuch sad tears because she cannot accept his love, you would have no\r\ndoubts as to her feelings.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen my course is clear. I am a Christian gentleman and will not see\r\nsavage torture inflicted on my blue-eyed _hermanita_. I think I know how\r\nto fix it up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat will you do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Quien sabe_ just this minute, but it will be _something_, depend upon\r\nit. There he is now,\xe2\x80\x9d and Clarence came, followed by a waiter bringing\r\nthe champagne and ice. He looked disappointed at not finding Mercedes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat little sister of ours I fear has given us the slip. I think I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\ngo and fetch her bodily,\xe2\x80\x9d George said, rising to go.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; let me go,\xe2\x80\x9d said Elvira.
