When George was left alone with Clarence\r\nhe said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI fear that Mercedes is very unhappy, she left when she saw us coming,\r\nElvira says, because she feared her eyes showed traces of tears.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClarence clenched his hands as if he would like to throttle all bad luck\r\nin general, and this one in particular, looked haggard, but remained\r\nsilent. George continued:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpanish girls are trained to strict filial obedience, and it is a good\r\nthing when not carried too far. Now, Mercedes made to her mother some\r\nvery foolish promise, and if her heart was to break into little pieces\r\nshe would not swerve\xe2\x80\x94not she\xe2\x80\x94though she be fully aware that her\r\nhappiness would be wrecked for ever, she would not disobey her mother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut is it alone her mother\xe2\x80\x99s wishes? In obeying her mother, does she\r\nnot follow her own inclination?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGeorge laughed, saying: \xe2\x80\x9cShe must be a strange girl, indeed, if she\r\nweeps so bitterly and is so unhappy to follow her inclination.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, if I only could think that! Are you sure?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy did Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa wish to send her away? Only for the hope that she\r\nmight get over her love for you. Mercedes is not yet eighteen, and,\r\nbeing so young, her mother thought that by sending her away from you and\r\nyours, she might forget you. Only such hope as that could have prevailed\r\nupon Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa to part with her baby. Spanish mothers will never let a\r\ndaughter go out of the maternal sight until they are married; but for\r\nthe fear that Mercita\xe2\x80\x99s attachment to you might become incurable if not\r\neffaced early, the mother was ready to sacrifice her feelings. For it\r\nwas a terrible sacrifice, it was like pulling her heart strings to send\r\nher baby off.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, how she must hate me then to have such strong objections to me,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Clarence, sadly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, she does not hate you\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94and George hesitated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I know she thinks I have done something wrong or dishonorable, but\r\nwhat that is, I have not the slightest idea.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcuse me for saying so, but I think it was a mistake not to tell\r\nher\xe2\x80\x94and Mercedes also\xe2\x80\x94that you bought the land you occupy. Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa\r\ncannot think it is honorable to take up land as your father did. She\r\ncannot understand how any law of Congress can authorize a man to take\r\nthe property of another against his will and without paying for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd she is perfectly right. I see the mistake now, and I regret it more\r\nthan words can tell. You knew why I asked Don Mariano not to mention\r\nthat I had paid him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Gabriel told me first, and he, too, thinks it is a mistake to let\r\nthe Alamar ladies have a wrong idea of you. He thinks you do an\r\ninjustice to yourself. We were talking about it when Don Mariano joined\r\nus, and he agreed with Gabriel and said that he would speak to you about\r\nit very soon. Doesn\xe2\x80\x99t any of your family know about it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Everett and mother do. She would not have come down if I had not\r\ntold her I paid for the land. But she and I thought that for the present\r\nwe had better say nothing about it to father, knowing how sensitive he\r\nis about his views of \xe2\x80\x98_Squatter rights_?\xe2\x80\x99 He has had so much trouble\r\nabout those same rights.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you will have to tell him soon\xe2\x80\x94I mean when the attorney\r\ngeneral dismisses the appeal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen will that be, do you think?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust as soon as the Supreme Court is in session. It would have been\r\ndone last fall had not the solicitor general interfered in the most\r\nabsurd and arbitrary manner.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI heard he had, and I heard the settlers rejoicing about it, but I\r\nnever knew how it happened\xe2\x80\x94I would like to hear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, ladies and gentlemen,\xe2\x80\x9d said Elvira, coming, \xe2\x80\x9cif my eloquence and\r\npersuasive powers were not of the unprecedented quality they really are,\r\nI would never have been able to persuade the se\xc3\xb1orita to come. Would you\r\nbelieve it? she was actually in bed for the night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence exclaimed, regretfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I told her that if she didn\xe2\x80\x99t come, you would take the champagne\r\nto her room, and this so frightened her, that she began to dress herself\r\nimmediately, but the poor little thing trembles as if she had the ague.\r\nI gave her a cashmere wrapper and soft shawl to wrap up and not take\r\ncold.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo and tell her we have good news for her,\xe2\x80\x9d suggested George.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99ll think you are jesting,\xe2\x80\x9d Elvira answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot if you tell her that we know what it is that Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa has\r\nagainst Darrell, and we\xe2\x80\x99ll make it all right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t deceive the poor little thing when she seems as if all her\r\nstrength is already gone from her,\xe2\x80\x9d Elvira said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we are not deceiving her,\xe2\x80\x9d George insisted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHush! here she comes,\xe2\x80\x9d Elvira said, and Mercedes slowly approached\r\nthem. \xe2\x80\x9cCome, sweet Baby, these gentlemen say they have some awful nice\r\nnews for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNews that the wine is good, I suppose, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t like wine,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, it isn\xe2\x80\x99t the wine,\xe2\x80\x9d George said, rising for Mercedes to take his\r\nplace. \xe2\x80\x9cSit down here between Darrell and myself and you shall hear all\r\nabout it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d Mercedes asked, looking from one to the other.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t tell you, little sister, for they haven\xe2\x80\x99t told me,\xe2\x80\x9d Elvira\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDarrell, you fill the glasses now while I tell these se\xc3\xb1oritas what\r\nsort of a black sheep Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa thinks you are, and so thinking,\r\nobjects to you.\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence proceeded to put ice into the glasses, while\r\nGeorge continued: \xe2\x80\x9cThe objection is, that she believes the Darrells are\r\n\xe2\x80\x98_squatters_,\xe2\x80\x99 like all the others at the rancho, whereas Clarence\r\nbought their land from Don Mariano and paid for it even before they\r\nbuilt their house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! I am so glad to hear that!\xe2\x80\x9d Elvira exclaimed with a sigh of relief.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why don\xe2\x80\x99t papa tell it to mamma? It is an injustice to the Darrells\r\nto let her ignore it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is my fault, Mrs. Mechlin,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence said; \xe2\x80\x9cmy father holds the\r\naccepted but very erroneous popular opinions about \xe2\x80\x98_squatter rights_,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nand I, to avoid painful discussions with him, requested Se\xc3\xb1or Alamar not\r\nto say, for the present, that I had paid for the land.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see, little sister, how, after all, you have not been loving a\r\nsquatter? What a pity,\xe2\x80\x9d said George, putting his arm around Mercedes,\r\nwho buried her face in the lappels of his coat. \xe2\x80\x9cIt isn\xe2\x80\x99t half so\r\nromantic to love a plain gentleman as to love a brigand, or, at least, a\r\nsquatter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo\xc3\xb1a Josefa\xe2\x80\x99s objection to me is perfectly proper and correct. I would\r\nnot let a daughter of mine marry a squatter no more than to marry a\r\ntramp. I shall, of course, request Don Mariano to put me right in her\r\nestimation, and tell her I do not feel authorized by Congress to steal\r\nland, though my father and many other honest men hold different opinions\r\nabout it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere! Do you hear that? Let us have a bumper, and drown the squatter\r\nin champagne! Exit tramp! Enter gentleman! Here is to Baby\xe2\x80\x99s health,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid George.\r\n\r\nAll emptied their glasses, except Mercedes, whose hand shook so\r\nviolently that she spilled more wine than she drank.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t lose your courage now,\xe2\x80\x9d Elvira said to her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe pussy is regretting she lost her squatter. Isn\xe2\x80\x99t that so,\r\npussy? You have not said one word. Are you regretting that, after all,\r\nyou cannot sacrifice to love your patrician pride by marrying a\r\nland-shark, thus proving you are a heroine?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, what a silly boy,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, laughing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally, I think our romance is spoiled. It would have been so fine\xe2\x80\x94like\r\na dime novel\xe2\x80\x94to have carried you off bodily by order of infuriated,\r\ncruel parents, and on arriving at New York marry you, at the point of a\r\nloaded revolver, to a bald-headed, millionaire! Your midnight shrieks\r\nwould have made the blood of the passers-by curdle! Then Clarence would\r\nhave rushed in and stabbed the millionaire, and you, falling across his\r\nprostrate body, said: \xe2\x80\x98Tramp or not, I am thine!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, George, stop your nonsense,\xe2\x80\x9d Elvira said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhereas now,\xe2\x80\x9d George went on, \xe2\x80\x9cthe unpoetical fact comes out that\r\nDarrell is a decent sort of a fellow, and there is no reason why a\r\nproper girl shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have him for her husband; and our romance is\r\nstripped of its thrilling features, as the hero will not steal, even\r\nwhen Congress tells him to. And that is the _d\xc3\xa9nouement_, with the\r\naddition only that I am hungry. What have you got to eat in those two\r\nlittle baskets that Tano brought on board, and which smell so nice?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, yes, I had forgotten. Mamma put up a nice lunch, thinking we might\r\nwant it if we felt sick, or didn\xe2\x80\x99t want to go to the table. I\xe2\x80\x99ll go and\r\nbring it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Elvira, setting down her glass, and rising.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me go,\xe2\x80\x9d said George, \xe2\x80\x9cas I am the hungry one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBring both baskets. Let us see what they have. Ah, I was forgetting, I\r\nhave the three little silver plates in my satchel; we must have those,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nadded Elvira, following her husband.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan you forgive my stupidity? See what a world of anxious thoughts we\r\nwould have avoided by explaining to Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa everything,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nClarence to Mercedes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it was unfortunate. But you will return soon and ask papa to tell\r\nher all, will you not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed I will, by the next steamer; and will have better heart to await\r\nyour return. My precious angel, don\xe2\x80\x99t ever forget how devotedly I\r\nidolize you! Will you let me send you a ring, if your mother allows me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCouldn\xe2\x80\x99t you _bring_ it yourself?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Mercedes, my beloved! how happy you make me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here,\xe2\x80\x9d said George, groping in the dark; \xe2\x80\x9cWhere are the magic\r\nbaskets? I don\xe2\x80\x99t smell them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI knew you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t, that is why I came to find them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here! if you follow a fellow like that, you\xe2\x80\x99ll get kissed,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nhe, taking his wife in his arms, and covering her face with kisses.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStop, George, some one might pass who didn\xe2\x80\x99t know you are my husband.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s so,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, desisting. \xe2\x80\x9cBut the fact of the matter is, that I\r\nwant to kiss you all the time, you are so pretty and such a sweet\r\ndarling. Give me the basket, and let your hungry husband go before he\r\neats you up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere they are. I\xe2\x80\x99ll carry the plates and knives.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTano said something about boned turkey, _a la espanola_, stuffed with\r\nmashed almonds and \xe2\x80\x98_ajonjoli_,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d said George, setting the baskets on a\r\nchair before Clarence; \xe2\x80\x9cand something about a \xe2\x80\x98_tortita de aceituna_,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nwith sweet marjoram, and I think we got them, to judge by their\r\nfragrance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall I go and order more wine?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Clarence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no, no,\xe2\x80\x9d said Elvira, \xe2\x80\x9cthis is plenty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow strange it is that I haven\xe2\x80\x99t felt this wine at all,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mercedes;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cone-half glass only will make my face unpleasantly warm always, for\r\nthat reason I dislike wines; but see, I drank this whole glassful, and I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t feel it any more than if it was water.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut don\xe2\x80\x99t you feel warmer? You were shivering when you came from your\r\nroom,\xe2\x80\x9d George said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I feel better,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, timidly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow eat a little and you will sleep better. Take one of these\r\n\xe2\x80\x98_empanaditas de pollo_,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d said Elvira, offering one.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive me one,\xe2\x80\x9d George said. \xe2\x80\x9cI know them by experience, and the trouble\r\nabout them is that you can never have enough, though you feel you have\r\neaten too many. Try them, Darrell, and when you have filled our glasses\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll satisfy your curiosity, telling you why the Solicitor General would\r\nnot dismiss the appeal of the squatters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I want to know all about that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence, filling the\r\nglasses.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XII.\xe2\x80\x94_Why the Appeal was Not Dismissed._\r\n\r\n\r\nAt the time when this moonlit picnic of four took place on the steamer\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndeck, as it glided northward over the glassy surface of the immense\r\nPacific, the people of California had not yet heard about the disclosure\r\nof the famous _Colton suit_. This suit was hidden in the mists of a\r\ndistant future, and therefore the famous \xe2\x80\x9c_Huntington Letters_\xe2\x80\x9d had not\r\ncome forth to educate the American mind in the fascinating, meandering,\r\nshady ways of \xe2\x80\x9c_convincing_\xe2\x80\x9d or of \xe2\x80\x9c_bribery and corruption_,\xe2\x80\x9d as the\r\nnewspapers and committee reports have harshly stigmatized Mr.\r\nHuntington\xe2\x80\x99s diplomacy(!) At that time, 1872, people yet spoke of\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_bribery_\xe2\x80\x9d with a degree of shamefacedness and timidity. It was\r\nreserved for Mr. Huntington to familiarize the American people with the\r\nfact that an American gentleman could go to Washington with the avowed\r\npurpose of influencing legislation by \xe2\x80\x9c_convincing_\xe2\x80\x9d people with money\r\nor other inducements, and yet no one lose caste, or lose his high social\r\nor public position, but on the contrary, the _convinced and the\r\nconvincer_ be treated with the most distinguished consideration. So\r\nafter drinking half of his second glass, George said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe the stories about Washington being such a corrupt\r\nplace, where people get everything by bribing. That is a shameful\r\nslander. I went there about that dismissal of the Squatters\xe2\x80\x99 Appeal, and\r\nwas treated like a gentleman, even by the Solicitor General, who was\r\noutrageously unjust to us. After my uncle had sent to the Attorney\r\nGeneral Don Mariano\xe2\x80\x99s letter explaining the case and stating how the\r\ntranscript had been in Washington two years, I went as Don Mariano\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nattorney to look after the case. I saw the Attorney General immediately,\r\nand he told me to return at ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock next morning. I did so, and was\r\nshown in at once. He said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98I looked at the case again last evening, and don\xe2\x80\x99t see where those\r\nsettlers can find a hook on which to hang their appeal. There isn\xe2\x80\x99t any.\r\nIt is very singular that this case has not been dismissed before by my\r\npredecessor. So I was just telling the Solicitor General, as you\r\nentered, to have it dismissed this morning. I have explained my opinion\r\nto him. He is going now to the Supreme Court and can make the motion and\r\ntell the clerk to enter the dismissal to-day. The United States have no\r\ncase against Se\xc3\xb1or Alamar, his title is perfectly good,\xe2\x80\x99 said he,\r\nlooking at the Solicitor, who stood by silent and motionless. \xe2\x80\x98You have\r\nonly this one case to attend to this morning, besides the one I want\r\ncontinued until I return. The others, you understand, I leave you to\r\nmanage as you think proper, and at such times as you think best.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thanked the Attorney General, and as I took my leave I said I did not\r\nknow he was going away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Yes,\xe2\x80\x99 he said, \xe2\x80\x98I am going this evening to Oregon to see my\r\nconstituents, but my absence will not affect your case, the Solicitor\r\nGeneral takes my place during my absence, and he has only to say before\r\nthe Supreme Court that I enter a dismissal, and that ends the matter.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs I went out I said to the Solicitor, \xe2\x80\x98I suppose then this business is\r\nfinished now?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Such is the supposition,\xe2\x80\x99 said he, and we went out together. I had a\r\ngreat mind to follow him to the Supreme Court and see what he was going\r\nto do, but I thought he might not like being watched. Well, sir, would\r\nyou believe it? That man went to the Supreme Court and never said _boo_\r\nabout our dismissal. Next morning I went to ask him if the dismissal was\r\nentered. He sent word he was engaged,\xe2\x80\x94to call again. I called in the\r\nafternoon, and he had left the office. Next morning I called again, and\r\nhe of course was engaged. I went to the clerk of the Supreme Court, and\r\ngiving him the number of the case, asked if it had been dismissed. He\r\nsaid no, that the Solicitor General had been at the Supreme Court every\r\nday, but had entered no dismissal. I telegraphed to Uncle Lawrence to\r\ncome, and as soon as he arrived we went to see the President about it. I\r\nlaid the whole case before him. I told him how the squatters were\r\ndestroying Don Mariano\xe2\x80\x99s cattle, and how by a law of the California\r\nlegislature, any one could plant grain field without fencing, and take\r\nup cattle that went to those fields, no matter whether there was any\r\ntitle to the land or whether the field was no larger than one acre.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98But the law does not open to settlers private property, private\r\nlands?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Yes it does, because land is not considered _private property_ until\r\nthe title to it is confirmed and patented. As the proceedings to obtain\r\na patent might consume years, almost a life time, the result is that the\r\nnative Californians (of Spanish descent) who were the land owners when\r\nwe took California, are virtually despoiled of their lands and their\r\ncattle and horses. Congress virtually took away their lands by putting\r\nthem in litigation. And the California legislature takes away their\r\ncattle, decreeing that settlers need not fence their crops, but put in a\r\n_corral_ the cattle that will surely come to graze in their fields. As\r\nthe cattle don\xe2\x80\x99t know the law, they eat the crops and get killed.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98But that is very hard on those land owners.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Certainly. They are being impoverished with frightful rapidity. In a\r\nfew years the majority of them will have been totally ruined, socially\r\nobliterated. I doubt if a dozen families will escape ruin. There seems\r\nto be a settled purpose with our law-givers to drive the natives to\r\npoverty, and crowd them out of existence. If we don\xe2\x80\x99t turn them all into\r\nhardened and most desperate criminals, it will be because they are among\r\nthe most incorruptible of the human race. But there is no denying that\r\nour laws are doing all that can be done to drive them into squalid\r\nhovels, and thence into the penitentiaries or the poor houses.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98This is certainly very sad,\xe2\x80\x99 said the President, with genuine\r\nsympathy, adding after a short pause:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Wait for me here. I\xe2\x80\x99ll run across the street to the Attorney General\xe2\x80\x99s\r\noffice, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll ask the Solicitor what it all means in this Alamar\r\ncase,\xe2\x80\x99 so saying he put on his hat and went out.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98That is what endears General Grant to all his friends,\xe2\x80\x99 said my uncle;\r\n\xe2\x80\x98the idea of his going personally to see the Solicitor, he the\r\nPresident, and only because he wishes to do a kindness.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98I wish he had sent for the proud Solicitor to come here. This visit of\r\nthe President will make him more over-bearing,\xe2\x80\x99 said I. \xe2\x80\x98I am disgusted\r\nat his most arbitrary conduct.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98Wait,\xe2\x80\x99 said my uncle, \xe2\x80\x98let us hear\r\nfirst what he has to say to the President.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn a short time the President returned. He said: \xe2\x80\x98Well, gentlemen, I\r\ncannot make out why the Solicitor did not dismiss the case, as he was\r\nordered. He says he found that the Attorney General had not looked into\r\nthe record carefully, and so he did not think the case should be\r\ndismissed.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98But how could he have found out that the Attorney General had not\r\nlooked into the case carefully only by riding from the office to the\r\nSupreme Court? He must have _disobeyed the instructions of the Attorney\r\nGeneral first_, and then to justify his disobedience, trumps up the\r\npretext that the case had not been examined,\xe2\x80\x99 said uncle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98The Attorney General did not tell him to look into the case and give\r\nhis opinion. He was told that the case _had been examined_; that the\r\npleadings and allegations were trivial; that the United States had _no\r\ncase_, and the matter should be dismissed,\xe2\x80\x99 I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98It is clear, that without authority he took upon himself to review and\r\nreverse the decision of the Attorney General,\xe2\x80\x99 said my uncle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t understand his motive or object,\xe2\x80\x99 the President said. \xe2\x80\x98But I\r\ntold him I presumed he could state his opinion in writing, and he said\r\nhe would. Perhaps he will give a better reason for his action than he\r\ndid verbally.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98No, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 uncle said, \xe2\x80\x98he will give no better reason, as he has none\r\nto give. He has some spite against the Attorney General, and is laying\r\nin wait to catch something to hurt him. Fortunately, he can\xe2\x80\x99t use this\r\ncase for any such purpose, for it is a very clear one, and the hands of\r\nthe Attorney General are very clean.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Of course they are,\xe2\x80\x99 the President said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98And now, sir, what do you advise us to do?\xe2\x80\x99 asked uncle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe President smiled, mused a little, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98My advice would be to wait until the Attorney General returns from\r\nOregon. I know it is a hardship for the rightful owner of the land to\r\nwait so long, but the question is, would it not be longer if the\r\nSolicitor finds other reasons to take this case into his own hands. Now\r\nhe has promised me to let the matter rest until the Attorney General\r\ncomes back.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Yes,\xe2\x80\x99 my uncle said, \xe2\x80\x98I think what you advise is the best thing to do.\r\nEvidently the Solicitor is beating the bush to start some game, and will\r\nbe satisfied with a \xe2\x80\x98mare\xe2\x80\x99s nest,\xe2\x80\x99 if he can only entangle the Attorney\r\nGeneral in it. But this is a very paltry and picayunish business for a\r\nSolicitor General, Mr. President, and it is silly, too, because he has\r\nshown his hand to little purpose. He has plainly demonstrated how\r\nanxious he is to find something against the Attorney General, but that\r\nsomething he hasn\xe2\x80\x99t got yet.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe President laughed, and said: \xe2\x80\x98You mustn\xe2\x80\x99t be so hard on the\r\nSolicitor.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was decided that my uncle would return to New York by the four\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock train that afternoon, and I would remain to receive the opinion\r\nin writing which the Solicitor had promised the President he would give.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did not have to wait until next day for that profound opinion. As I\r\nwas going to dinner at six o\xe2\x80\x99clock, a messenger handed me a closed\r\nofficial envelope which felt quite heavy. But that was all the weight\r\nthe thing possessed, for it was the lightest, most vapory composition\r\nthat a grown-up man, long past boyhood, could evolve from a mature\r\nbrain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt made me angry to read it. \xe2\x80\x98The man is evidently not a fool, but\r\nthinks we are,\xe2\x80\x99 I said to myself, and made up my mind I would go next\r\nmorning and tell him to his face what I thought of his conduct and his\r\ndocument.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPromptly at ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock next morning I presented myself at the Attorney\r\nGeneral\xe2\x80\x99s office, and was immediately ushered before the august presence\r\nof the great Solicitor, the mighty hunter of \xe2\x80\x98mare\xe2\x80\x99s nests.\xe2\x80\x99 He\r\nevidently thought I had come to thank him for his vapory effusion, for\r\nhe received me quite smilingly, and without a trace of that hauteur\r\nwhich he had at first meant should be so crushing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTaking the chair he so graciously offered me, I said: \xe2\x80\x98Sir, without\r\nmeaning any disrespect to the Solicitor General of the United States, I\r\nwould like to inquire what is the meaning of the document I had the\r\nhonor to receive from you yesterday?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe colored up, but still smiling, answered: \xe2\x80\x98Did you not understand it?\r\nI thought I wrote in very plain English.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98The English was plain enough, but I failed to catch your idea. Will\r\nyou permit me to make a few enquiries?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Certainly.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98You remember I was present when the Attorney General told you that he\r\nhad examined the transcript carefully, and not finding that the\r\ngovernment has any case at all, ordered you to dismiss it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe bowed, but did not speak.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI continued: \xe2\x80\x98The Attorney General did not request you or authorize you\r\nto review his opinion. He merely said you were to dismiss the appeal,\r\nand have the clerk of the court enter in the record the order of\r\ndismissal that same morning. Had you obeyed the Attorney General\xe2\x80\x99s\r\norder, you could not have had time to review his opinion, and find that\r\nit was incorrect. Has the Solicitor General the right, and is it\r\nincumbent upon him, to correct the Attorney General\xe2\x80\x99s acts and\r\nopinions?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98You evidently do not understand our relative positions, and I have not\r\nthe time to instruct you.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Whose positions do you mean?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Ours\xe2\x80\x94mine and the Attorney General\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Who is the head of the department\xe2\x80\x94the Attorney General or the\r\nSolicitor?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98The Attorney General.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Then he was your chief\xe2\x80\x94your superior\xe2\x80\x94when he gave the order to make\r\nthe dismissal?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98But I was not his clerk. You do not know how far it was discretionary\r\nwith me to execute the order that day or not.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Ah, I see. The chief might issue an order, but the subaltern might\r\nonly execute it if he deems it proper.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98I am not a subaltern\xe2\x80\x94I have as much authority\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Yes, in the absence of the Attorney General.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Always\xe2\x80\x94when absent or present\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Then the department has two heads. That is, I suppose, what confused\r\nthings in my mind. The matter then is to rest as it now is until the\r\nAttorney General returns?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Yes, I shall not remand the case, as I might have done; it will wait.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI took my leave then, having seen that he understood I saw through the\r\ncontemptible impertinence of his conduct. That is all the satisfaction I\r\ncould have then, but next winter, as soon as the Supreme Court convenes,\r\nthe matter will be settled.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd will the squatters have to go then?\xe2\x80\x9d Elvira asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot immediately, unless they were to be guided by honorable motives.\r\nThe rancho will be surveyed first, and then the patent issued after the\r\nsurvey is approved by the Surveyor General,\xe2\x80\x9d George replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! The endless red tape,\xe2\x80\x9d said Elvira.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor papa, he has so much trouble,\xe2\x80\x9d Mercedes sighed. \xe2\x80\x9cIn another year\r\nall the cattle will have been killed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the squatters will be more murderous, when they learn that their\r\nappeal is dismissed,\xe2\x80\x9d George said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I was thinking what will be the best to do to meet the emergency.\r\nI shall speak about that to Don Mariano on my return,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, you help him all you can,\xe2\x80\x9d George said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMost undoubtedly. I will be able to do much more if I can persuade my\r\nfather to take a correct view of the matter. But he might not, for as he\r\nhas had so much trouble sustaining the rights of squatters, he has got\r\nto feel as if he were the champion of a misunderstood cause and much\r\nmaligned people,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence said, smiling sadly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo doubt, if Mr. Darrell is to be unfriendly, papa will have much more\r\ntrouble to manage the others,\xe2\x80\x9d Elvira said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow singular that a man as bright and honorable as he is, can find any\r\nreason to justify \xe2\x80\x98_squatterism_,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d said George.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think he began by being persuaded to take a claim in the Suscol\r\nrancho, honestly thinking it was government land. Afterwards the grant\r\nwas confirmed, I think, but then he already felt compelled to maintain\r\nhis position to justify his action, and so he began by a mistake which\r\nhis pride will not let him acknowledge. I was a little child then, but I\r\nknow he has had a great deal of trouble. For the last ten years we have\r\nbeen leasing land, but he had been wishing to have a farm of his own, so\r\nas not to be putting his labor and time and money to improve some one\r\nelse\xe2\x80\x99s land. Thus he was induced to come south on the representations\r\nthat there was plenty of vacant government land, and that the Texas\r\nPacific railroad would soon be built and southern California be\r\nprosperous,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence, anxious to extenuate his father\xe2\x80\x99s errors.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall telegraph to Don Mariano when the appeal is dismissed, so you\r\ncan prepare the ground the best way you can,\xe2\x80\x9d said George. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd now\r\nyoung ladies it is near midnight, and is time for well regulated\r\nchildren to be asleep.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe moon is so lovely I could sit here for hours, watching its flashes\r\non the water,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mercedes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo could I,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I could not allow it, and let you both run the risk of being\r\nconsidered moon struck,\xe2\x80\x9d said George, laughing.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIII.\xe2\x80\x94_At San Francisco._\r\n\r\n\r\nThe sun was quite high above the horizon when George joined Clarence on\r\ndeck; and both began to promenade and talk while waiting for the ladies\r\nto come, that the four might go to breakfast together.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy private opinion is that these young ladies are going to oversleep\r\nthemselves,\xe2\x80\x9d George said, as they passed the door of his room, after\r\npromenading for half an hour.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo they are not,\xe2\x80\x9d Elvira said, coming out as fresh and beautiful as a\r\n_rosa de castilla_. \xe2\x80\x9cGood morning, Mr. Darrell, I hope you are well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thank you,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Clarence, \xe2\x80\x9cI believe I never felt better. I am\r\ndelighted to see you so bright and blooming; you are evidently an\r\nexcellent sailor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes,\xe2\x80\x9d Elvira answered, \xe2\x80\x9cI really enjoy it; but where is the Se\xc3\xb1orita\r\nMercita; is she not yet up?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think not. She has not come from her room,\xe2\x80\x9d was Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s reply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am going to peep through her window,\xe2\x80\x9d said Elvira. She did so by\r\nturning the slats and pushing aside the curtain just a little. She then\r\nmotioned to George to come and look.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy jove, Darrell, you ought to see this picture.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHush! you will awake her by speaking so loud,\xe2\x80\x9d Elvira said, still\r\nlooking at her sister.\r\n\r\nA tiny sunbeam played over Mercedes\xe2\x80\x99 forehead, making the little curls\r\nover it look like golden threads. Her head was thrown back a little and\r\nturned towards the window, displaying her white throat, partially\r\ncovered by the lace frills of her night dress. Her left arm rested\r\ngracefully over her head, with the sleeve pushed off displaying part of\r\nthe forearm and the perfect curve of her delicate wrist. The right hand\r\nrested over the coverlet, and it looked like a child\xe2\x80\x99s hand, so dimpled\r\nand white and soft. It was a perfect picture of a \xe2\x80\x9csleeping beauty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoesn\xe2\x80\x99t she look like a baby? My own sweet sister; I am so glad she is\r\nsleeping so sweetly. She has slept very poorly for months,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered\r\nElvira. \xe2\x80\x9cCome away, we mustn\xe2\x80\x99t talk near her window, she must have all\r\nthe sleep she wants.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo saying, she pulled back the curtain, shut down the window slats, and\r\nall walked noiselessly away.\r\n\r\nAs they went down to breakfast, Elvira said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope no one will come smoking some nasty cigar by her window,\r\npoisoning the air and making her miserable, for she cannot bear tobacco\r\nsmoke when the boat is in motion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought she was not subject to sea-sickness,\xe2\x80\x9d George said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, not at all, as long as there are no tobacco fumes near, but it\r\nseems that tobacco smoke, combined with the rocking of the sea, make her\r\ndeathly sick, whereas the tobacco alone or the rocking by itself, will\r\nnot affect her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI understand that well, for I don\xe2\x80\x99t like to smoke while sailing either,\r\nif there is much motion, and I think no one ought to be allowed to smoke\r\non deck where ladies are,\xe2\x80\x9d George said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think so too. We have too many rights, and more than our share of\r\nprivileges,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait until we have woman suffrage. We will make things uncomfortable\r\nfor inebriates and tobacco smokers,\xe2\x80\x9d Elvira said, laughing.\r\n\r\nTheir pleasant voyage came to its end, as all things must in this\r\nfleeting life, and the names of Mr. George Mechlin and party, from San\r\nDiego, were duly entered in the hotel register.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI put your name down, Darrell, for we want you with us while in the\r\ncity,\xe2\x80\x9d said George.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thank you sincerely; that is exactly what I wished.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will be ready for dinner at six.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall be on hand promptly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClarence was anxious to see his broker and afraid he would leave the\r\noffice before he got there, but it was more imperative yet to visit his\r\ntailor. He did so, and though in haste, selected with care the cut and\r\nstyle and color which he knew was most becoming. He left a list of all\r\nthe articles of clothing he desired to be sent to his hotel by five\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock, and then directed his driver to take him to his broker\xe2\x80\x99s\r\noffice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust in time,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hubert Haverly, coming forward to meet him. \xe2\x80\x9cAs\r\nsoon as the steamer was signaled at the gate, I sent to look for our\r\nArizona men. They are now at the back office waiting for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell me something about the matter, to guide me. And tell me too, how\r\npoor or how rich I am, before I make any bargain to purchase mines.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, on the whole, I guess I\xe2\x80\x99ll call you rich. I bought the farm as\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x94or rather as Everett\xe2\x80\x94telegraphed. I paid\xe2\x80\x94well, how much do you think\r\nI paid for it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHundred and forty thousand?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHubert shook his head, saying \xe2\x80\x9cTry again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHundred and twenty?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNinety thousand only, lucky fellow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat? You said he asked a hundred and fifty thousand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, and you\xe2\x80\x94or Everett\xe2\x80\x94telegraphed to pay the money, but you see the\r\npoor fellow lost heavily in stocks that day, and as the bank was going\r\nto foreclose on the farm for a loan of forty thousand, he thought the\r\nbest thing he could do was to sell out quick. He came to see me and said\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Do you think Clarence will buy for one hundred and twenty thousand?\xe2\x80\x99 I\r\ntold him I had telegraphed to you and probably you would come up. He\r\nsaid \xe2\x80\x98If you pay me ninety thousand cash down _to-day_, Clarence can\r\nhave the farm for that price.\xe2\x80\x99 I told him to let me have the refusal for\r\nyou, for that price, until the next morning. I got your telegram in the\r\nevening. Next morning he came looking very dejected, and asked if I had\r\nheard from you. \xe2\x80\x98Yes,\xe2\x80\x99 I said. He waited, but as I said no more, he\r\nadded, \xe2\x80\x98I hope Clarence is not going to pinch me hard. The farm is worth\r\ntwo hundred thousand, but as the Darrells made all the improvements on\r\nit, I am willing he should have it cheaper than any one else. How much\r\ndoes he offer?\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98He left it to me to make the best bargain I can. I will\r\nlet you have the ninety thousand, of course.\xe2\x80\x99 You never saw a man so\r\nrelieved. He lifted his head and said, \xe2\x80\x98I will pay all my debts and have\r\nthirty thousand clear, anyway, to make a beginning,\xe2\x80\x99 and so the papers\r\nwere drawn up and the farm is yours. I congratulate you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThanks,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence said, squeezing Hubert\xe2\x80\x99s hand. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd now about the\r\nbalance on hand and the Arizona mines.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you have about one hundred thousand dollars. If you sell all your\r\nstocks, you could have two hundred thousand,\xe2\x80\x9d Hubert replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBesides the interest on the bonds?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly. I never figure on that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat about the Arizona mines?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, the men say they are yet \xe2\x80\x98_a prospect_,\xe2\x80\x99 but a very good one.\r\nTheir proposition is that you pay them five hundred dollars down if you\r\naccept their proposal. Then you are to send an expert to examine the\r\nmines. If on his report you conclude to buy them at once, you can have\r\nthem for ten thousand dollars. If you prefer to bond them to prospect\r\nfurther before buying, then you can have six months to prospect; but\r\nthen you must pay two thousand down, and at the end of the six months\r\nyou must pay fifty thousand dollars if you want both mines, or twenty\r\nthousand if you only take one. The shaft they have sunk is the dividing\r\nline between the two mines.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBetween the two prospects,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence suggested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that is more proper, the shaft is only about one hundred feet\r\ndeep. But you had better talk to them. They brought rock similar to that\r\nwhich they sent me last month.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRather rough looking men were the three waiting, but all had good faces.\r\nAfter exchanging salutations with them, Clarence asked:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you had any assays made?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said the oldest of the three handing to him three slips of\r\npaper. \xe2\x80\x9cHere are three certificates from assayers recommended to us as\r\nthe best in San Francisco.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat! One hundred silver and one hundred and fifty gold? And two\r\nhundred, and three hundred and fifty? But that is enormous for surface\r\nrock.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe miners laughed. The oldest said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the ledge is so wide that it almost takes the half of the hill. We\r\ntook two claims and put our prospect shaft in the middle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you make your locations in good legal form?\xe2\x80\x9d was the next question.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes sir, we have our papers,\xe2\x80\x9d said the spokesman, handing to Clarence\r\nsome papers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see you are four partners, where is the other?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is at the mine, working at the shaft.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell gentlemen,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence said, \xe2\x80\x9cI have just come, an hour ago. I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow how soon I will find an expert, but I think I will do so between\r\nnow and to-morrow by mid-day. I will consult with him and see how soon\r\nhe can go to look at your mines. Meantime I\xe2\x80\x99ll have some of the rock\r\nassayed. From what depth was the rock assayed taken?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrom fifty, seventy and ninety feet. We have some few pieces from the\r\nlast we took the day we left, at a depth of one hundred feet.\xe2\x80\x9d So\r\nsaying, he handed to Clarence other pieces of rock which looked much\r\nricher, adding, \xe2\x80\x9cThis is the ore we have not had assayed yet. My opinion\r\nis that the rock hasn\xe2\x80\x99t changed much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was agreed that Clarence would meet them at eleven next morning and\r\nnotify them if he had found an expert. When they had left the room\r\nClarence asked Hubert where his brother Fred was.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is here, he came yesterday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you did not mention that fact to me, when you know I want a good,\r\nreliable expert.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did not, because I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t urge his services upon any one\xe2\x80\x94even\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x94and then I think he might be already engaged to go to examine some\r\nmines in Nevada, as parties have been looking for him for that purpose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease don\xe2\x80\x99t be so proud as to deprive me of the services of so good a\r\nman, but tell him to come to my hotel at once.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell him, but he will not be here until five o\xe2\x80\x99clock.\r\nShall I tell him to call on you after dinner?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, at half-past seven exactly, to send his card to me to any place I\r\nmay be at the hotel. And now I\xe2\x80\x99ll go to have two or three assays more of\r\nthis rock. Remember, I shall be looking for Fred at half-past seven.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll remember. He will be there promptly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was very evident that the \xe2\x80\x9cparty from San Diego\xe2\x80\x9d made an impression\r\nand quite a stir among the guests of the hotel, who were at dinner when\r\nthey entered the dining-room. Preceded by the head waiter, they had to\r\ncross the entire length of the room, for the seats assigned to them were\r\nat the furthest corner from the door. Everybody turned to look, to see\r\nwhat everybody else was looking at, and all acknowledged that they had\r\nnever seen handsomer or more graceful people than those two couples.\r\nExclamations of surprise were uttered in suppressed tones, and\r\nunqualified praises were whispered everywhere. The head waiter was\r\ncalled here and there to say who these four people were, so very\r\nhandsome and _distingu\xc3\xa9_.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are from Southern California, on their way east. Mr. George\r\nMechlin and bride, her sister, and their friend Mr. Darrell, travelling\r\nwith them,\xe2\x80\x9d was the answer that the steward had to give twenty times.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich is the bride, the blonde or the brunette?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe brunette.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter dinner several young gentlemen remained in the corridors to see\r\nthem pass, and some four eastern tourists who were dining at the next\r\ntable, made a pretext of drinking more wine, to remain looking at the\r\nsouthern beauties. One of them especially looked at Mercedes so\r\npersistently that Clarence began to feel angry, and when they arose from\r\nthe table he looked at the admirer with a bold stare of defiant\r\nreproval. But that in no way checked the admiration of the New Yorker,\r\nand he followed as near to Mercedes as he could, and when he saw her\r\ndisappear into her parlor, he looked at the number on the door and went\r\nstraight to the office to make all the enquiries he could concerning\r\nthose two beautiful ladies. The clerk gave all the information he could,\r\nand added laughing:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have had to answer those questions a dozen times already.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nImmediately after dinner a waiter came from the office and handed to\r\nClarence a card, with \xe2\x80\x9cFred Haverly\xe2\x80\x9d written on it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay to the gentleman I shall be down immediately,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence said to the\r\nservant; and then to George, \xe2\x80\x9cThis is the expert I want to send to\r\nArizona. It is lucky for me to find him in town.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll go down with you,\xe2\x80\x9d George said. \xe2\x80\x9cOne of the clerks promised to get\r\nme a box at the opera, or if that can\xe2\x80\x99t be had, to get the four best\r\nseats he could find disengaged. Do you think you will have finished with\r\nyour expert in half an hour? I want the girls to see the opera bouffe;\r\nthey have never seen it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall be with you in fifteen minutes,\xe2\x80\x9d was the reply.\r\n\r\nGeorge was talking with the clerk about the seats at the opera, when he\r\nfelt a hand laid softly on his shoulder. Looking back, he saw his\r\nfriend, Charles Gunther, of New York, standing by him, and behind him\r\nthe four gentlemen who had dined at the next table. After shaking hands\r\nmost cordially, and congratulating him on being a married man, Gunther\r\npresented to George his four friends, and his brother Robert, who now\r\ncame in; then he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI heard you say you wanted a box at the opera, and that there are\r\nladies with you. Permit me to offer you our box, we can take seats\r\nanywhere else. I shall be glad if you will accept.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut there are no seats that you can have that I would offer you in\r\nexchange,\xe2\x80\x9d was George\xe2\x80\x99s reply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThose I got for you are good seats for gentlemen,\xe2\x80\x9d the clerk suggested,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cand I think you can get two more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGunther was so urgent that George, only by being very rude, could have\r\ndeclined making the exchange. There was nothing else to do but accept,\r\norder a carriage for eight o\xe2\x80\x99clock, and then go up stairs to tell the\r\nladies that they were to get ready for the opera.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe opera! Why didn\xe2\x80\x99t you tell us before?\xe2\x80\x9d was Elvira\xe2\x80\x99s exclamation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I was not sure I could get seats,\xe2\x80\x9d was George\xe2\x80\x99s reply; and he\r\nthen explained how he obtained their box by casually meeting Gunther,\r\nadding: \xe2\x80\x9cBy the by, he introduced me to his brother Robert and those\r\nfour admirers of yours, Mercedes, who dined at the next table. They are\r\nall of the same party. The young fellow of the little saffron whiskers,\r\nwho stared at you so persistently, making Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s ears red, is a Mr.\r\nSelden, of New York; he and Robert Gunther have been in Europe several\r\nyears. His father I know is a millionaire, and he is the only son. So he\r\nconsiders himself a good catch, I suppose, Se\xc3\xb1orita Mercedes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBah!\xe2\x80\x9d ejaculated Mercedes; \xe2\x80\x9cwho cares!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe ready with your hats and cloaks on at five minutes to eight.\r\nClarence and I will come for you. I am going to look for him now, and\r\nsee Gunther for a few moments,\xe2\x80\x9d George said, leaving the two sisters to\r\ngo to their bedrooms to delve for their opera cloaks and white hats in\r\nthe deep recesses of their Saratoga trunks.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a lucky thing for me that Lizzie\xe2\x80\x99s aunt sent this pretty cloak\r\nand bonnet to her. Poor Lizzie! I am to _splurge_ in her fine Parisian\r\nthings, while she remains at the _rancho_, buried alive,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mercedes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is perfectly willing to have that sort of burial as long as she has\r\nGabriel near her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Lawrence Mechlin had sent to Elvira and Lizzie their wedding\r\ntrousseau, which she ordered from Paris. To do this was a pleasure to\r\nMrs. Mechlin, which she could well afford, being rich, and which she\r\ndelighted in, being devoted to her sister\xe2\x80\x99s children.\r\n\r\nThe theatre was filled to its utmost capacity when our four _San\r\nDieguinos_ arrived and occupied their proscenium box, which was on the\r\nleft of the auditorium, very roomy and elegantly furnished. Elvira\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nseat faced the stage, and Mercedes\xe2\x80\x99 faced the audience, so that the\r\nperfect contour of her features was clearly seen when she looked at the\r\nactors. Between the sisters sat their cavaliers. The curtain rose as\r\nthey took their seats, so that not one of them gave a thought to the\r\naudience, until the curtain fell on the first act.\r\n\r\nThen they all looked at the house which was filled with a brilliant\r\naudience. Immediately in front, in the first row of orchestra chairs,\r\nwere Mr. Gunther and the party of New Yorkers. They were all looking at\r\ntheir box. Mercedes blushed when she met the steady gaze of Mr. Selden,\r\nand his face reproduced the blush, while his heart beat with wild throbs\r\nof delight. Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s face also flushed, and then turned pale. He had\r\nseen the two blush, and a cold feeling of undefinable fear and\r\nsavageness seized him\xe2\x80\x94a desire to go and choke Mr. Selden where he\r\nwas\xe2\x80\x94right there in his orchestra chair.\r\n\r\nGeorge by this time was exchanging bows with the New Yorkers. They spoke\r\namong themselves, and soon after all arose and left their seats.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think Gunther is bringing his brother and friends to present them to\r\nyou, ladies,\xe2\x80\x9d said George.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBeing your friends, we shall be pleased to see them,\xe2\x80\x9d Elvira answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope those gentlemen will cease to stare when they are acquainted.\r\nThat young man of the red whiskers made me blush by looking at me so\r\nsteadily. I hope that that is not the custom of New Yorkers,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMercedes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid it is. You had better try to get used to it, and don\xe2\x80\x99t mind\r\nit,\xe2\x80\x9d George replied.\r\n\r\nMr. Gunther now presented himself at the door, followed by his brother\r\nand the four others, already well known by sight, the ceremony of\r\nintroduction being performed by George, with the ease and grace of one\r\nused to those society duties. All took seats, there being room enough\r\nfor a dozen people in the spacious box.\r\n\r\nGeorge and Clarence had left their seats to receive the guests, so very\r\nnaturally Mr. Selden slipped by and sat next to Mercedes.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIV.\xe2\x80\x94_Of Miscellaneous Incidents._\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you think of the opera\xe2\x80\x94are you enjoying it much?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mr.\r\nSelden, by way of opening conversation, having turned his chair to face\r\nMercedes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am enjoying the novelty of the thing, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what I shall\r\nthink of the opera. I suppose I shall like it better when I understand\r\nit. Thus far it is to me only a very puzzling maze of hastily uttered\r\nFrench, imperfectly heard and mixed with music, all of which is rather\r\nunintelligible to me, so unprepared to judge of it as I am,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMercedes, smiling, watching to see the effect that her candid avowal of\r\nignorance would have upon such a \xe2\x80\x9c_muscadin_\xe2\x80\x9d and well traveled young\r\nman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! you never saw the opera before to-night!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot the French opera. I was at two matinees of the Italian opera about\r\nfive years ago, when I left my San Francisco school. Mamma thought I was\r\ntoo young to go out at night, and since then I have been living at the\r\n_rancho_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, yes; Mr. Mechlin said you had not been in San Francisco since you\r\nwere twelve months old.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwelve months?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA laugh immediately behind him, made Mr. Selden turn quickly around. He\r\nmet the eyes of Mr. Robert Gunther, who had taken the chair next to him,\r\nand made no secret of being amused at Mr. Selden\xe2\x80\x99s mistake.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you laughing at?\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Selden asked, sharply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose Mr. Gunther thinks that girls must grow very quickly in\r\nCalifornia if I was twelve months old five years ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Selden could not escape now the raillery of his friends. Each one\r\nhad something to say on the subject of Mr. Selden\xe2\x80\x99s ideas of the wonders\r\nof California, until the bell rung for the curtain to rise for the\r\nsecond act.\r\n\r\nThey all arose to go. George said: \xe2\x80\x9cWill not some of you remain? there\r\nis room for two or three more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I am not going to crowd you, I shall accept your kind invitation and\r\nhide about here,\xe2\x80\x9d said Robert Gunther, taking a chair behind Elvira.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBob Gunther is always such a good boy that I always like to follow his\r\nexample; so, with your kind permission, Mr. Darrell, I shall sit here\r\nbehind you. Keep your chair,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Selden, refusing to change seats\r\nwith Clarence.\r\n\r\nBut Mercedes saw that this arrangement was not as satisfactory as might\r\nbe, so she moved her chair, and making room for Clarence on her left,\r\ntold Mr. Selden to push his chair further to the front, on her right.\r\nThis was a more desirable distribution, and it pleased Clarence better,\r\nfor she would turn her face to him on looking at the stage. Still, there\r\nwas that odious little fellow with his red mutton chops sitting so near\r\nher, that he wanted to pitch him out of the box. Mercedes watched for an\r\nopportunity to say to him:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou look unhappy; have I done anything to displease you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, never!\xe2\x80\x9d he quickly answered, but did not dare to look at her.\r\nPresently he added: \xe2\x80\x9cIt is too painful to think that only for one day\r\nmore I can see you, then we must part, and\xe2\x80\x94and others will be with you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCould you not go with us as far as the Yosemite?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClarence turned quickly to look at her, and her eyes had that sweet,\r\nloving expression which, to him, was always irresistible, entrancing. He\r\nhad never seen it in any other eyes, and in hers only very seldom.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! if you will only let me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet you! Your pleasure is the only thing to consult.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I know what I shall do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n                                  \xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nNeither Mr. Selden nor Mr. Gunther could sleep that night. Those little\r\ngolden curls over the blue eyes floated in a hazy mist and music in\r\ntantalizing recurrence until dawn.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you make a satisfactory bargain?\xe2\x80\x9d George asked Clarence next\r\nmorning, when the ladies had gone to church.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, as far as we can see at present. I am to send an expert to look at\r\nthe mines to-morrow, and on his report will decide what to do. But I am\r\nin a quandary now about one thing. Have you positively decided to leave\r\nto-morrow at seven A.M.?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf we don\xe2\x80\x99t oversleep ourselves,\xe2\x80\x9d was George\xe2\x80\x99s reply. \xe2\x80\x9cBut that\r\ndepends. Why do you ask? If by waiting a few days we can have your\r\ncompany further on, we will wait, of course. The girls are enjoying\r\nthemselves very much, and will be glad to wait for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThanks, thanks,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence, warmly. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, I would like to go as far\r\nas the Yosemite with you; but as I would like to have one final talk\r\nwith the miners to-morrow before I pay them any money, I would be much\r\nobliged if you could wait until Tuesday morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMost willingly, my dear fellow, particularly as these se\xc3\xb1oritas are not\r\nin a hurry to leave fascinating San Francisco.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have not driven anywhere around the city, and Miss Mercedes wishes\r\nto see more of San Francisco,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence, \xe2\x80\x9cas she has not seen it\r\nsince she was _twelve months old_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor Selden; those fellows will never cease laughing at his mistake,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nGeorge said.\r\n\r\nAfter mass, our travelers went immediately to luncheon. At their table\r\nwere already seated the six New Yorkers, but four chairs were carefully\r\nturned, in token of being reserved. Clarence sat next to Mercedes, but\r\nSelden was opposite, and anxiously expected the moment when she would\r\nlift her veil. He dreaded to be disenchanted by finding her to be less\r\nbeautiful in daylight, but such was not the case. She appeared to him\r\neven prettier, seeing better the lovely dark blue of her eyes. He looked\r\nat her in silence, saying to himself mentally: \xe2\x80\x9cShe is exquisite; am I\r\ngoing to love her hopelessly!\xe2\x80\x9d And he looked at Clarence with a pang of\r\njealousy, for he could not deny to himself that he was handsome, yes,\r\nbeautiful as an Apollo, and very manly.\r\n\r\nNext to Selden sat Robert Gunther, making almost the same mental\r\nobservations, and resolving to try and win her in spite of all\r\nobstacles.\r\n\r\nLuncheon was much enjoyed by all excepting Mr. Selden, who seemed to get\r\nmore and more nervous as he sat there trying not to look at Mercedes as\r\nmuch as he wished.\r\n\r\nThe Gunther brothers were very brilliant conversationalists, and so was\r\nGeorge, who was in his element in the company of such polished gentlemen\r\nas were now before him. On leaving the table, Mr. Charles Gunther begged\r\nElvira\xe2\x80\x99s permission to pay their respects, asking if it would suit her\r\nconvenience for them to call that evening after dinner, to which she\r\ngracefully assented, and all walked towards the parlor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall we go to the Cliff House this afternoon?\xe2\x80\x9d George asked his wife.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou may, but Mercedes and I are going to vespers,\xe2\x80\x9d she replied, and\r\nsoon after the two sisters retired to their rooms.\r\n\r\nAs all of the gentlemen walked down to the reading room, Selden said:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how in thunder are we going to kill time this afternoon until\r\ndinner? It will be intensely stupid here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought we all were going to drive to the Cliff,\xe2\x80\x9d Bob Gunther said,\r\nmaliciously. \xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps you would rather go to church.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou judge others by yourself,\xe2\x80\x9d Selden retorted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe I do. But our sudden access of religion, I fear, would not be\r\nappreciated. My dear fellow, our piety, like that of his satanic\r\nmajesty, would be distrusted. It would edify no one, only make us\r\nridiculous. Let us go to the Cliff.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd to the Cliff all went, but the drive was not much enjoyed. Bob and\r\nSelden were quarrelsome, and all the others laughed at them, which ended\r\nby making them surly. Selden ridiculed the San Franciscans for their\r\nstupid Cliff House, while all sat in arm-chairs on the broad veranda and\r\nlooked at the Pacific Ocean, and Pacific sea lions, and Pacific rocks,\r\nand thought them all equally monotonous. To watch the ugly sea beasts\r\nawkwardly dragging their unwieldly hulks up the rocks, there to spread\r\nthemselves in the sun, was not a very exhilarating spectacle for young\r\ngentlemen who desired to see other kinds of lions. Sunday not being the\r\nfashionable day for San Franciscans to drive to the Cliff, the New\r\nYorkers concluded that the elite would not be seen that afternoon and\r\nreturned to the hotel.\r\n\r\nAfter dinner several lady friends, who had received Elvira\xe2\x80\x99s wedding\r\ncards and had seen her and Mercedes at church that morning, called.\r\n\r\nThe cards of the New Yorkers, also, were brought, and they followed\r\nimmediately. Elvira presented them very gracefully, while George watched\r\nwith delighted attention the perfect ease and natural elegance with\r\nwhich she did the honors as hostess.\r\n\r\nRobert Gunther and Arthur Selden seated themselves in a corner, on the\r\nright of Mercedes\xe2\x80\x99 chair, but Clarence held his place on the end of the\r\nsofa, very near her.\r\n\r\nAbout ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock, Mr. Charles Gunther said to them:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMuch as it pains me to tear myself away, young men, it must be done,\r\nfor we have made _a first call_ of nearly two hours\xe2\x80\x99 duration.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt has not seemed to us nearly so long,\xe2\x80\x9d Mercedes said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was no more than two minutes,\xe2\x80\x9d Bob Gunther added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow you exaggerate,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Selden exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAsk him how long it has seemed to him,\xe2\x80\x9d Bob suggested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would not dare. He thinks you exaggerate, that is enough,\xe2\x80\x9d replied\r\nMercedes.\r\n\r\nSelden gave her a look of tender reproach, and a savage one at Bob, as\r\nhe bowed, leaving the room.\r\n\r\nBy nine o\xe2\x80\x99clock Monday morning Clarence had received the certificates of\r\nassay he had ordered on Saturday afternoon. It seemed to him that there\r\nmust be a mistake somewhere about the rock, for these assays gave even a\r\nhigher percentage than those shown him by the miners. He went to\r\nHubert\xe2\x80\x99s office and found Fred already there waiting for him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here, Hubert, are you sure that these men did not bring us this\r\nrich rock from some other mine? The assays are very high. One goes as\r\nhigh as $2000 per ton.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey might have selected the specimens, but I can vouch for their being\r\nfrom the same ledge, for I know the rock. I can also vouch for the\r\nhonesty of the men, for I know them well; besides, what would be the\r\ngood of telling a falsehood that would be found out the minute the\r\nexpert got there? Their reputation is worth more to them than the five\r\nhundred dollars that you will pay now,\xe2\x80\x9d was Hubert\xe2\x80\x99s reply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are good men. I have known them for years, and have had them\r\nworking with me,\xe2\x80\x9d Fred added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen let us finish this business now, for I go out of town to-morrow\r\nmorning,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence said, and in half an hour he had explained his views\r\nand wishes and made his contract with Fred Haverly, the terms of which\r\nhad been already mentioned on Saturday night and Sunday morning. The\r\nminers now came and the contract with them, also, was made and\r\nacknowledged in due form.\r\n\r\nBy twelve o\xe2\x80\x99clock that day Clarence had dispatched his business with the\r\nminers and with Fred Haverly, reserving until he returned instructions\r\nregarding his Alameda farm.\r\n\r\nIn the afternoon all drove to the Cliff House. The ugly sea lions did\r\nnot seem so clumsy to Mr. Selden, as Mercedes laughed, amused to see\r\ntheir ungainly efforts at locomotion, and as she pronounced the Pacific\r\nOcean to be grand and the wild surf dashing madly against the impassive\r\nrocks very impressive, Mr. Selden was of the same way of thinking, and\r\nfound the sea lions rather graceful and dignified, the black rocks more\r\ninteresting than they had been the day before.\r\n\r\nThe gayeties of San Francisco made time slip away magically, and a week\r\npassed in receptions, drives and yacht sailing, in honor of Elvira,\r\nseemed very short indeed. But now another week had begun, and the\r\njourney eastward must be resumed.\r\n\r\nOur travelers took an early breakfast on Tuesday morning, and by seven\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock they left the hotel. Half an hour later, they were on the\r\nOakland boat, crossing San Francisco Bay on their way to New York.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is plenty of room here for all the navies of the world,\xe2\x80\x9d George\r\nobserved, looking at the harbor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I believe the bay is forty miles across,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Clarence. \xe2\x80\x9cFor\r\nall intents and purposes at present, however, San Diego Bay is as good\r\nas this.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I only wish we had commerce enough for ships to be crowded there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf Colonel Scott succeeds in constructing his railroad, there is no\r\ndoubt that San Diego will be a large city in a few years.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe that, but the question is, will Colonel Scott succeed?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think he will, but he has a hard crowd to fight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClarence mused a little, then, changing his position so as to face\r\nGeorge, said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have had an idea in my head, a sort of project, I want to talk to you\r\nabout. Of course, its practicability, I fear, will entirely depend upon\r\nthe building of the Texas Pacific Railroad; for if San Diego is not to\r\nhave population, my plan will be impracticable. It is this: The two\r\nbanks in San Diego, I don\xe2\x80\x99t think, have a paid-up capital of more than a\r\nhundred thousand dollars. I think we could establish a bank of two or\r\nthree hundred thousand dollars that would be a paying institution. I\r\nheard you say that you thought you would like to come to California, so\r\nas to be near your family. That gave me the idea of starting a bank. You\r\ncould be the president and manager, and I would furnish as much of the\r\ncapital as suited you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour idea is splendid, nothing could suit me better; but I suppose we\r\nwill have to see whether we are to have a railroad or not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that is the sole and unavoidable condition.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose we will know next winter, and if it be decided that the Texas\r\nPacific is to be built, I will immediately accept your proposition, and\r\nput in some money with you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can take half, or a third of the stock, and put in some money for Don\r\nGabriel and Victoriano; and Everett can come in, too. You can easily\r\ninstruct Don Gabriel in the banking business.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe would make a good cashier; he is a good bookkeeper already. I think\r\nI could put in twenty-five or thirty thousand dollars.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you put in twenty-five thousand, I will put in that much for each of\r\nthe others, Don Gabriel, Tano and Retty, and one hundred thousand for\r\nmyself, or will put in thirty thousand for Don Gabriel and ninety-five\r\nthousand for myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou ought to be the president.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I want you and Don Gabriel to have the entire management. You can\r\ntake in Tano and Retty, if you like, if they prove themselves efficient;\r\nbut as for myself, I want to be free to attend to those mines (if they\r\nare worth working) and take care of my Alameda farm. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you think\r\nthat two hundred thousand will be enough to start? I can put in more, if\r\nnecessary, by selling some of my United States bonds. I have seven\r\nhundred and fifty thousand in United States securities, which I can\r\nconvert into money at any time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwo hundred thousand is more than enough. We can increase the capital,\r\nif we wish, afterward. I am glad you are so well fixed in government\r\nsecurities.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI could have had a round million if I had not sold my stock too soon;\r\nbut my father kept talking to me so much against dealing in mining\r\nstocks, that I ordered Hubert Haverly to sell all I had. Fortunately he\r\nheld on for a few days to my Crown Point, and sold for nine hundred\r\nthousand dollars. I was sorry enough to have lost a million for being so\r\nobedient a son, and when in that mood I promised Hubert I would not\r\ninterfere again, but let him manage my stocks as he thought best. Since\r\nthen he has done very well, so that now I have seven hundred and fifty\r\nthousand in United States bonds, my farm, for which Hubert paid ninety\r\nthousand, some town lots in San Francisco, and about one hundred and\r\nsixty thousand dollars in bank, besides the interest on my bonds, which\r\nI have not drawn for over a year.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, that makes you worth over a million.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but if I had kept my Crown Point for a few days longer I could\r\nhave sold for a million and a half. However, I think the Arizona mines\r\nwill reward my filial obedience,\xe2\x80\x9d added he, smiling, \xe2\x80\x9cand if we can\r\nstart that bank I shall be satisfied. I think it is a pity that such men\r\nas Don Mariano and his sons do not have some other better-paying\r\nbusiness than cattle-raising. It used to pay well, but I fear it never\r\nwill again, while such absurdities as the \xe2\x80\x98_No-Fence Laws_\xe2\x80\x99 are allowed\r\nto exist.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I heard Don Mariano say to my father: \xe2\x80\x98I am sure I am to be\r\nlegislated into a _rancheria_, as there is no poor-house in San Diego to\r\nput me into,\xe2\x80\x99 he said it smiling, but his smile was very sad. However,\r\nwhen the appeal is dismissed and he is rid of squatters, he will\r\nrecuperate, provided, of course, there be a Texas Pacific to make San\r\nDiego lands valuable. Without it the prospect is gloomy indeed, I may\r\nwell say dead.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s it; it all depends upon that railroad, I am sorry to say, when\r\nwe are so powerless to counteract hostile influences.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must hope and wait.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XV.\xe2\x80\x94_Journeying Overland._\r\n\r\n\r\nThe crashing and thundering of Yosemite\xe2\x80\x99s falls plunging from dizzy\r\nheights, in splendor of furious avalanches, had been left behind.\r\n\r\nGeorge and his three companions had given the last lingering look\r\ntowards the glorious rainbows and myriads of dazzling gems glittering in\r\nthe sun\xe2\x80\x99s rays, which pierced the vertical streams and played through\r\nthe spray and mist enveloping them.\r\n\r\nThe memory of the mirror lakes, with their gorgeous borders of green,\r\ntheir rich bouquets of fragrant azaleas and pond lilies, as well as the\r\ntowering cliffs, the overpowering heights of that wonderful valley, all\r\nmade a picture to remain forevermore a cherished souvenir.\r\n\r\nBut alas, for the fatality of human joys, all is evanescent in this\r\nworld of ours; the moment of parting at last came for the lovers.\r\n\r\nThe west-bound train would pass the station first, so Clarence must be\r\nthe one to leave his friends.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWrite to us soon, won\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d George said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly, as soon as I get to San Diego.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWrite before, and let us know what you are doing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, I will do so,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, and looked at Mercedes, who with\r\ndown-cast eyes, felt his gaze but dared not look up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t fail to write the long letter you promised, after you have your\r\ntalk with papa, and he has explained to mamma your position,\xe2\x80\x9d Elvira\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is my all-absorbing thought. There is no danger of my failing to\r\nsee Don Mariano the first minute I can do so. I will write immediately.\r\nTo whom shall I direct my letter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo me, of course,\xe2\x80\x9d Elvira replied, \xe2\x80\x9cand you will write to Mercita also,\r\nafter matters have been explained to mamma.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe distant rumbling as if of coming earthquake, and a far off shriek\r\nwere now heard. In another minute the round-eyed monster was there, and\r\nsnorting maliciously, rushed off with Clarence, leaving Mercedes leaning\r\non George\xe2\x80\x99s arm, scarcely able to stand, and hardly realizing that\r\nClarence had left them.\r\n\r\nShe was still very pale, and her hands yet trembled, when the thundering\r\nof the east-bound train was heard in the distance. Two shrieks pierced\r\nthe air simultaneously, as the two trains passed each other. Her heart\r\ngave accelerated throbs when she heard those shrieks, because she knew\r\nthat one of them came from the train which bore Clarence away, and it\r\nseemed to her as if expressive of his pain at being torn from her. Yes,\r\nthat magician, the locomotive, understood it all, and shrieked to say he\r\ndid so, because he knew she, too, wished to shriek like that.\r\n\r\nWhat would you, my reader? She was so young\xe2\x80\x94only seventeen\xe2\x80\x94and in love.\r\nThe poor child was naturally indulging in all sorts of foolish fancies\r\nwhile looking at the woods through which he had disappeared.\r\n\r\nBut there was now the east-bound train, and George taking her towards\r\nit.\r\n\r\nHe laughed loudly as they walked to the cars, and Elvira asked why he\r\nlaughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI declare, Mercedes, you must have fascinated those two fellows more\r\nthan is good for them\xe2\x80\x94for there they are as large as life.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho, George?\xe2\x80\x9d Elvira asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, who should it be but Selden and Bob Gunther.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d ejaculated Mercedes. \xe2\x80\x9cPlease George get a compartment where we can\r\nbe by ourselves,\xe2\x80\x9d implored she.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will; you shall have it if money or influence or anything short of\r\nmurder can get it,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, helping them up the car steps. \xe2\x80\x9cBut in the\r\nmeantime I am going to locate you here, while I go to interview the\r\nconductor and porter. This is the last car\xe2\x80\x94you will be here unobserved.\r\nThose fellows did not see us get in.\xe2\x80\x9d So saying, George went off,\r\nlaughing heartily.\r\n\r\nNeither conductor nor porter were to be found in the next car, or the\r\nnext to that, and George made his way through them as quickly as their\r\njolting and swinging permitted.\r\n\r\nAt the further end of the fourth car he spied a porter talking with two\r\nforeign-looking gentlemen, who were none other than Messrs. Gunther and\r\nSelden. Their backs were turned toward him, so he had time to approach\r\nthem unobserved, near enough to hear Selden say, in his anglicised\r\naccents:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut my good fellah, we were told positively that travelers going from\r\nthe Yosemite east must get on the train here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd so they do,\xe2\x80\x9d George said, laying his hand on Selden\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy Jove! we\xe2\x80\x99ve got \xe2\x80\x99em!\xe2\x80\x9d ejaculated Gunther.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere they are,\xe2\x80\x9d Selden said, with radiant face, seizing hold of\r\nGeorge\xe2\x80\x99s hands, which he shook emphatically.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here! let me have one of his hands, won\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d said Gunther;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwhat an all-absorbing fellow you continue to be, I am sure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhile George gave a hand to each, he told the porter he wanted a\r\ncompartment, if such was to be had.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are none disengaged, sir, except some of those little ones at the\r\nend of the car, which no one wants; but you can have a section if you\r\nlike,\xe2\x80\x9d the porter replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have that already; but the ladies with me want a good, large\r\ncompartment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have one which we will be most happy to place at your service,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nGunther said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd rob you of it.
