That wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be fair.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes it would, as we don\xe2\x80\x99t care for it. And it is very nice and private,\r\nand the ladies should have it,\xe2\x80\x9d Selden said, warmly.\r\n\r\nAs the section which George\xe2\x80\x99s tickets assigned to him was the very next\r\nto the apartment in question, it was very clear to Mr. Selden that no\r\narrangement could have been more fortunate, and he said so.\r\n\r\nThe three then went to bring the ladies to their room.\r\n\r\nMercedes pleaded a headache, and George knew that she wished to be\r\nalone, to have a cry all to herself, as most girls would, when their\r\nsweethearts have just left them. So he said to Elvira:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMercedes had better lie down for a while. If she sleeps she will feel\r\nbetter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think so; I will join you presently,\xe2\x80\x9d Elvira answered. And hearing\r\nthis the gentlemen retired.\r\n\r\nMercedes took her hat and gloves and cloak off, and sat at the window\r\n_to enjoy_ her misery in a thorough womanly fashion. She fixed her eyes\r\non the far-off, flying wall of verdure, seeing nothing, not even the\r\ntall trees which, close by, indulged in such grotesque antics, as if\r\nforgetting their stately dignity only to amuse her\xe2\x80\x94making dancing\r\ndervishes of themselves, and converting that portion of the Pacific\r\nslope into a flying gymnasium to perform athletic exercises, rushing on\r\nmadly, or even turning somersaults for her recreation.\r\n\r\nElvira left her alone with her thoughts, and silently devoted herself to\r\nunpacking their satchels, arranging their toilet things, traveling\r\nshawls and night-dresses and comfortable slippers all in their proper\r\nplaces. She then took her hat off, and tying a large black veil over her\r\nhead (Spanish fashion), told her sister to sleep if she could, and not\r\nto cry, for, after all, Clarence would soon be in New York.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you really think so?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mercedes\xe2\x80\x99 sad voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course, I do. Clarence is too energetic and too much in love to be\r\nkept away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut mamma\xe2\x80\x94you know mamma\xe2\x80\x99s feelings.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich will be entirely changed when she hears that Clarence is no\r\nsquatter. Leave all that to papa. Come, give me a kiss, and if you can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nsleep, put a veil over your head and come out. I am going to join the\r\ngentlemen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, darling, you go; but at present I\xe2\x80\x99d rather sit here by the\r\nwindow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd she sat there, but the sad blue orbs saw nothing\xe2\x80\x94for her mental gaze\r\nwas fixed on that other flying train, that was rushing away, carrying\r\nher beloved with such frightful rapidity. She felt, she _knew_, Clarence\r\nwas sitting by a car window, thinking of her, gazing blankly at his\r\nmisery.\r\n\r\nAnd so he was.\r\n\r\nIt is to be feared that his misery would have been greatly intensified\r\nhad he caught a glimpse of Messrs. Gunther and Selden, as they rushed\r\npast him on their eastward journey. This aggravation, however, was\r\nspared him. And, as when he arrived at San Francisco, Charles Gunther\r\nand his three companions had already left for Oregon, Clarence remained,\r\nfor the present, in blissful ignorance of the whereabouts of those two\r\npersistent young gentlemen, traveling so near Mercedes.\r\n\r\nBut could magician of old have shown to him in enchanted mirror the\r\nimage of his beloved, he would have read in those expressive eyes how\r\nsadly she felt his absence.\r\n\r\nWhen she had sat there, motionless, for two hours, Elvira came to tell\r\nher to get ready for dinner, which she declined doing, saying that she\r\nwas not a bit hungry. And so the day passed\xe2\x80\x94the night came\xe2\x80\x94and she did\r\nnot gladden the hearts of their traveling companions, by letting them\r\nsee her that day. Next day the morning hours also passed. She had her\r\nbreakfast in her room.\r\n\r\nMr. Selden began to feel piqued and Mr. Gunther nervous. They and Elvira\r\nwere playing a three-handed game of casino; George was elsewhere,\r\ntalking to an acquaintance he had met on the train.\r\n\r\nPresently, softly and unexpectedly, the sliding-door of the compartment\r\nmoved, and Mercedes stood beside Mr. Selden, sweet as a rosebud, smiling\r\nin her most bewitching way. The blood mounted to Mr. Selden\xe2\x80\x99s temples,\r\nand those of Mr. Gunther\xe2\x80\x99s assumed the same hue. Then she, of course,\r\nblushed also\xe2\x80\x94for she could never see any one blush without doing the\r\nvery same thing herself.\r\n\r\nElvira alone kept her composure, and said: \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Baby! I am so glad you\r\nfeel better. Come, take a hand, for these gentlemen will cut your\r\nsister\xe2\x80\x99s throat, or she theirs. We are having a fierce battle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right. Will you have me for a partner, Mr. Gunther? I warn you that\r\nI am a very poor player,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mercedes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll have you for a partner, Miss Mercedes, on any terms, and be most\r\nhappy to do so,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Gunther, with more emphasis than the occasion\r\nrequired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat being the case, I am ready,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, sitting by her sister,\r\nthereby being diagonally opposite to Mr. Gunther.\r\n\r\nFrom that time the five travelers were constantly together, and the days\r\npassed delightfully for all during the entire journey, especially so to\r\nGunther and Selden. They had no occasion to complain of Mercedes for\r\nstaying away. She most amiably took part in all their games and other\r\namusements, their walks while waiting at stations, their conversations\r\nduring the sentimental and delightful twilight hours. She had found that\r\nboth young gentlemen were a most excellent protection against one\r\nanother, as neither one was ever willing to go leaving her alone with\r\nthe other. As for ardent loving looks, she knew that the best way of\r\neluding them was by having recourse to her little trick of dropping her\r\ngaze, as if she must look down for something missing near by. That\r\nlittle trick came to her from sheer timidity and bashfulness long ago.\r\nIn fact, she was unconscious of it, until Corina Holman had told her\r\nthat whenever Clarence Darrell was present she became sly, and did not\r\ndare to look at people squarely in the face\xe2\x80\x94that she was the veriest\r\nhypocrite. Thus she learned that her bashful timidity had been entirely\r\nmisunderstood, but she was also made aware that she had accidentally\r\ndiscovered how to avoid looks which were best not to meet\xe2\x80\x94best to avoid\r\nby simply dropping her gaze. As her long, curly lashes veiled her eyes\r\nwith a silken fringe, they could hide under that cover like two little\r\ncherubs crouching under their own wings.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVI.\xe2\x80\x94_Spanish Land Grants Viewed Retrospectively._\r\n\r\n\r\nSan Francisco seemed deserted, dusty and desolate to Clarence after his\r\nreturn from the Yosemite and the society of Mercedes. It was the step\r\nfrom the sublime to the ridiculous; so he ran off to his Alameda farm\r\nand remained there until the day before the steamer would leave for San\r\nDiego. He then came back late to the dusty city and went in search of\r\nHubert to take him to dinner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome for pity\xe2\x80\x99s sake to dine with me and talk to me. I can\xe2\x80\x99t eat alone,\r\nI am too blue,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, going to Hubert\xe2\x80\x99s desk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, my boy. You are the very man I wanted to see, for I have\r\nbeen slashing into your stocks like all possessed;\xe2\x80\x9d and he made cuts and\r\nthrusts in the air illustrative of a terrible havoc.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat have you done?\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence asked, laughing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, in the first place, I have sold all your Yellow Jacket, all your\r\nSavage and half of your Ophir, and I bought you some Consolidated\r\nVirginia and California. What do you say to that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot one word, for I suppose you know what you are about.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I do, and, as a proof of it, I made for you twenty thousand\r\ndollars clear profit by the operation, besides buying your Consolidated\r\nVirginia. So if that last venture is a failure, I shall not feel I have\r\nswamped all your cash.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should say not. You are the prince of brokers, Berty. You have not\r\nmade a single mistake in managing my stock.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes I have. I sold your Crown Point too soon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut that was my mistake, not yours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes it was. I ought to have sold half to fool you, and kept the other\r\nhalf ten days longer to make a million with it. I was stupidly honest\r\nthat time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI forgive you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I don\xe2\x80\x99t forgive myself, nor you either.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know that. You are only piling coals of fire on my head. Now I have\r\nto bear twenty thousand more fresh coals, and I forbearingly say: \xe2\x80\x98Pile\r\non Macduff,\xe2\x80\x99 _et cetera_. Where shall we go to dinner\xe2\x80\x94the Poodle Dog or\r\nCalifornia?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us go to the California House. John keeps the best.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTo the California House they went, and had a most excellent dinner with\r\nChateau Yquem and a bottle of Roderer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you know I like some of our California wines quite as well as the\r\nimported, if not better? I suppose I ought to be ashamed to admit it,\r\nthus showing that my taste is not cultivated. But that is the simple\r\ntruth. There is that flavor of the real genuine grape which our\r\nCalifornia wines have that is different from the imported. I think\r\nsooner or later our wines will be better liked, better appreciated,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nClarence said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think so too, but for the present it is the fashion to cry down our\r\nnative wines and extol the imported. When foreigners come to California\r\nto tell us that we can make good wines, that we have soils in which to\r\ngrow the best grapes, then we will believe it, not before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe two friends went after dinner to Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s rooms, where they spent\r\nthe evening together. Twelve o\xe2\x80\x99clock found them still busy talking of a\r\nthousand things. Next morning Hubert came to breakfast with Clarence and\r\naccompanied him to the steamer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-by, old fellow; take care of yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-by, my boy; good luck to you,\xe2\x80\x9d said they, with a lingering grip of\r\nthe hands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope Fred has had a safe journey,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think so, and I hope soon to get his telegram\xe2\x80\x94about his \xe2\x80\x98_first\r\nimpression_\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94which I shall transmit to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOnce more Clarence was crossing San Francisco Bay\xe2\x80\x94on to the Golden Gate,\r\non to the broad Pacific.\r\n\r\nThe surrounding scenery recalled Mercedes\xe2\x80\x99 image so vividly to his mind\r\nthat it made his heart long to see her, and the entire voyage was\r\npainful to him with the keen regret of her absence.\r\n\r\nBut now, again, on the fourth morning\xe2\x80\x94a lovely one in the sunlit July\xe2\x80\x94he\r\nwas once more making his way between Ballast Point and the sandy\r\npeninsula, facing La Playa and then turning to the right towards San\r\nDiego City.\r\n\r\nSan Diego at that time\xe2\x80\x94in July, 1873\xe2\x80\x94be it remembered, was fresh and\r\nrosy with bright hopes, like a healthy child just trying to stand up,\r\nwith no sickness or ill-usage to sap its vitality and weaken its limbs.\r\nOnly ten months before Col. Scott had come to say that the Texas Pacific\r\nRailroad would be built through the shortest, most practicable route,\r\nmaking San Diego the western terminus of _the shortest transcontinental\r\nrailway_. It was true that on the following winter Congress had done\r\nnothing further to help the Texas Pacific. But many reasons were given\r\nfor this singular lack of interest in so important a matter on the part\r\nof Congress. Among the many reasons, _the true one_ was not mentioned,\r\nhardly suspected; it would have seemed too monstrous to have been\r\nbelieved all at once; incredible if revealed without preparing the mind\r\nfor its reception. Yes, the mind had to be prepared\xe2\x80\x94slowly educated\r\nfirst. Now it has been. The process began about that time and it has\r\ncontinued up to this day, this very moment in which I write this page.\r\nMr. Huntington\xe2\x80\x99s letters have taught us how San Diego was robbed,\r\ntricked, and cheated out of its inheritance. We will look at these\r\nletters further on.\r\n\r\nWhen the steamer arrived near enough to the wharf for persons to be\r\nrecognized, Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s heart leaped with pleasure, for he saw the well\r\nknown, tall form of Don Mariano sitting in his buggy leaning back,\r\nlooking at the approaching steamer. A minute after, he saw Victoriano\r\nand Everett standing together near the edge of the wharf ready to\r\nreceive him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Mr. Runaway, welcome back!\xe2\x80\x9d Victoriano said, clasping Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhand as soon as he was upon the wharf. He gave the other hand to\r\nEverett, who said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will have to _lazo_ you to keep you home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think we will have to put a yoke on him,\xe2\x80\x9d added Victoriano.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly; only let me select my yokefellow,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence said, laughing.\r\n\r\nAs Don Mariano intended returning home that day, Clarence proposed that\r\nVictoriano should drive with Everett, and he go with Don Mariano, an\r\narrangement which was very satisfactory to all parties. He was very\r\nanxious to unburden his mind, and Don Mariano\xe2\x80\x99s inquiries about his\r\ndaughters and their voyage to San Francisco soon gave him the desired\r\nopportunity. He told Don Mariano what George had said, and how firmly\r\nand sincerely Mercedes wished to abide by her mother\xe2\x80\x99s wishes. Don\r\nMariano listened very attentively, then said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had intended suggesting to you the same thing. Gabriel has spoken to\r\nme about the matter several times, insisting that all the ladies of our\r\nfamily ought to know that _you_ paid for your land. Since we cannot\r\ndivest them of the resentment they have towards squatters, let them know\r\nthe truth. Let them see that Congress, if it does not always follow\r\nmoral principles, can certainly subvert them most arbitrarily and\r\ndisastrously. Do you still wish to keep the matter from your father?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClarence thought for a moment, then answered:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but only for a short time. I suppose we will have to define our\r\nposition as soon as the appeal is dismissed. Before that comes, I shall\r\nexplain all to him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey rode on in silence for a few moments; then Don Mariano said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, I shall tell my wife that, for the present, the matter must\r\nnot be mentioned outside the family or in the hearing of servants.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thank you,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence said: \xe2\x80\x9cit is very painful to me to find my\r\nfather adhering so tenaciously to his old conviction that all Mexican\r\ngrants not finally confirmed to their owners are public land, and being\r\nso, they are open for settlement to all American citizens. Thus, he\r\nstill insists that, being an American citizen, he has the right to\r\nlocate on your land or any other unconfirmed grant. This idea has been\r\nthe bane of his life for many years, but for the very reason that in\r\nmaintaining it he has caused so much trouble to himself and to others,\r\nhe seems to cling to it most pertinaciously. He believes your land was\r\nrejected, and that the rejection will be sustained.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, my land was reported rejected, but it was by some mistake of the\r\nclerks, because at that time the title had not been either finally\r\nrejected or confirmed. It had been before the Land Commission, and that\r\n(of course) decided adversely, as it generally did. Then I appealed to\r\nthe United States District Court. This said that there was not\r\nsufficient testimony to confirm my title, but did not affirm the opinion\r\nof the Land Commission, nor reverse their decision, nor enter a decree\r\nof rejection. It simply left the case in that uncertain condition until\r\n1870, when I discharged my lawyer and engaged another to attend to the\r\nsuit. Then the case was reopened, and a decree of confirmation was\r\nentered. In the meantime, squatters had been coming, and they now have\r\ncarried their appeal to Washington, to the United States Supreme Court,\r\nagainst me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see it all now,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence said, thoughtfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd don\xe2\x80\x99t you know,\xe2\x80\x9d Don Mariano continued, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I don\xe2\x80\x99t find it in my\r\nheart to blame those people for taking my land as much as I blame the\r\nlegislators who turned them loose upon me? And least of all I blame your\r\nfather, for he has not killed my cattle, as the others have.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course, he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t, he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t, he shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t do that. That would\r\nbe worse than the lowest theft.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is true, but there is a law to protect him if he did; in fact, to\r\n_authorize_ him to do so. Thus, you see, here again come _our\r\nlegislators_ to encourage again wrong-doing\xe2\x80\x94to offer a premium to one\r\nclass of citizens to go and prey upon another class. All this is wrong.\r\nI hold that the legislators of a nation are the guardians of public\r\nmorality, the teachers of what is right and just. They should never\r\nenact laws that are not founded upon rectitude, as Herbert Spencer says,\r\nno matter if expedience or adventitious circumstances might seem to\r\ndemand it. But I need not tell you this, for you hold the same opinion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed I do, and understanding your rights better than I did, I think\r\nyou were too generous in making the offer you made to the settlers at\r\nthe meeting with them last year.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was rather generous, but not as much so as you perhaps think. I was\r\nlooking out for myself, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI heard them talk about an appeal that was pending, and I thought it\r\nwas your appeal, not theirs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe position then was this: In the first place, I was willing to give\r\nthem a chance of getting good homes for their families, for I shall\r\nalways consider that the law has deluded and misled them, and helped\r\nthem to develop their natural inclination to appropriate what belonged\r\nto some one else; so they should bear only half the blame for being\r\nsquatters\xe2\x80\x94Congress must bear the other half. Then, in the second place,\r\nabout the time I had that meeting, I had just received a letter from\r\nGeorge, written at Washington, telling me how the Solicitor General had\r\ndisobeyed the order of the Attorney General, instructing him to dismiss\r\nthe appeal against the confirmation of my title. As I did not know that\r\nthe Solicitor General was acting thus out of pique or personal animosity\r\nagainst the Attorney General, I naturally feared that he was going to\r\nmake me suffer other worse outrages, judging by his arbitrary,\r\nirresponsible conduct. I thought that there might be many more years of\r\ndelay while waiting for the dismissal of the appeal, and while thus\r\nwaiting all my cattle would be killed. Reasoning thus, I concluded that\r\nit would be less ruinous to me to make the concessions I offered than to\r\nwait for tardy justice to restore my land to me\xe2\x80\x94restore it when all my\r\ncattle shall have been destroyed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think your reasoning was correct\xe2\x80\x94it did seem as if the Solicitor\r\nmeant mischief. It was fortunate that he dropped the matter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, for which I am devoutly thankful. I hope the mischief he has done\r\nmay soon be corrected by the Attorney General. Of course, the additional\r\neighteen months of depredations on my cattle which I have had to endure,\r\nmust go unredressed together with all else I have had to suffer at the\r\nhands of those vandals.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt the hands of our law-givers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly. I shall always lay it at the door of our legislators\xe2\x80\x94that they\r\nhave not only caused me to suffer many outrages, but, with those same\r\nlaws, they are sapping the very life essence of public morality. They\r\nare teaching the people to lose all respect for the rights of others\xe2\x80\x94to\r\nlose all respect for their national honor. Because we, _the natives_ of\r\nCalifornia, the Spano-Americans, were, at the close of the war with\r\nMexico, left in the lap of the American nation, or, rather, huddled at\r\nher feet like motherless, helpless children, Congress _thought_ we might\r\nas well be kicked and cuffed as treated kindly. There was no one to be\r\nour champion, no one to take our part and object to our being robbed. It\r\nought to have been sufficient that by the treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo\r\nthe national faith, the nation\xe2\x80\x99s honor was pledged to respect our\r\nproperty. They never thought of that. With very unbecoming haste,\r\nCongress hurried to pass laws to legalize their despoliation of the\r\nconquered Californians, forgetting the nation\xe2\x80\x99s pledge to protect us. Of\r\ncourse, for opening our land to squatters and then establishing a land\r\ncommission to sanction and corroborate that outrage, _our California\r\ndelegation_ then in Washington, must bear the bulk of the blame. They\r\nshould have opposed the passage of such laws instead of favoring their\r\nenactment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy did they favor such legislation?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause California was expected to be filled with a population of\r\nfarmers, of industrious settlers who would have votes and would want\r\ntheir one hundred and sixty acres each of the best land to be had. As\r\nour legislators thought that we, the Spano-American natives, had the\r\nbest lands, and but few votes, there was nothing else to be done but to\r\ndespoil us, to take our lands and give them to the coming population.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut that was outrageous. Their motive was a political object.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly. The motive was that our politicians wanted _votes_. The\r\nsquatters were in increasing majority; the Spanish natives, in\r\ndiminishing minority. Then the cry was raised that our land grants were\r\ntoo large; that a few lazy, thriftless, ignorant natives, holding such\r\nlarge tracts of land, would be a hindrance to the prosperity of the\r\nState, because such lazy people would never cultivate their lands, and\r\nwere even too sluggish to sell them. The cry was taken up and became\r\npopular. It was so easy to upbraid, to deride, to despise the conquered\r\nrace! Then to despoil them, to make them beggars, seemed to be, if not\r\nabsolutely righteous, certainly highly justifiable. Any one not\r\nacquainted with the real facts might have supposed that there was no\r\nmore land to be had in California but that which belonged to the\r\nnatives. Everybody seemed to have forgotten that for each acre that was\r\nowned by them, there were thousands vacant, belonging to the Government,\r\nand which any one can have at one dollar and twenty-five cents per acre.\r\nNo, they didn\xe2\x80\x99t want Government land. The settlers want the lands of the\r\nlazy, the thriftless Spaniards. Such good-for-nothing, helpless wretches\r\nare not fit to own such lordly tracts of land. It was wicked to tolerate\r\nthe waste, the extravagance of the Mexican Government, in giving such\r\nlarge tracts of land to a few individuals. The American Government never\r\ncould have been, or ever could be, guilty of such thing. No, never! But,\r\nbehold! Hardly a dozen years had passed, when this same economical,\r\nfar-seeing Congress, which was so ready to snatch away from the Spanish\r\npeople their lands (which rightfully belonged to them) on the plea that\r\nsuch large tracts of land ought not to belong to _a few_ individuals,\r\nthis same Congress, mind you, goes to work and gives to railroad\r\ncompanies millions upon millions of acres of land. It is true that such\r\ngifts were for the purpose of aiding enterprises for the good of the\r\npeople. Yes, but that was exactly the same motive which guided the\r\nSpanish and the Mexican governments\xe2\x80\x94to give large tracts of land as an\r\ninducement to those citizens who would utilize the wilderness of the\r\ngovernment domain\xe2\x80\x94utilize it by starting ranchos which afterwards would\r\noriginate \xe2\x80\x98pueblos\xe2\x80\x99 or villages, and so on. The fact that these\r\nland-owners who established large ranchos were very efficient and\r\nfaithful collaborators in the foundation of missions, was also taken\r\ninto consideration by the Spanish Government or the viceroys of Mexico.\r\nThe land-owners were useful in many ways, though to a limited extent\r\nthey attracted population by employing white labor. They also employed\r\nIndians, who thus began to be less wild. Then in times of Indian\r\noutbreaks, the land-owners with their servants would turn out as in\r\nfeudal times in Europe, to assist in the defense of the missions and the\r\nsparsely settled country threatened by the savages. Thus, you see, that\r\nit was not a foolish extravagance, but a judicious policy which induced\r\nthe viceroys and Spanish governors to begin the system of giving large\r\nland grants.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never knew that this was the object of the Spanish and Mexican\r\ngovernments in granting large tracts of land, but it seems to me a very\r\nwise plan when there was so much land and so few settlers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPrecisely. It was a good policy. In fact, the only one in those days of\r\na patriarchal sort of life, when raising cattle was the principal\r\noccupation of the Californians.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must say that to establish the Land Commission seems to me rather a\r\nsmall subterfuge for the Congress of a great nation to resort to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat makes this subterfuge a cold-blooded wrong, of premeditated\r\ngravity, is the fact that at the time when we were forced to submit our\r\ntitles for revision, and pending these legal proceedings, we, the\r\nland-owners, began to pay taxes, and the squatters were told that they\r\nhave the right to take our lands and keep them until we should prove\r\nthat we had good titles to them. If the law had obliged us to submit our\r\ntitles to the inspection of the Land Commission, but had not opened our\r\nranchos to settlers _until it had been proved that our titles were not\r\ngood_, and if, too, taxes were paid by those who derived the benefit\r\nfrom the land, then there would be some color of equity in such laws.\r\nBut is not this a subversion of all fundamental principles of justice?\r\nHere we are, living where we have lived for fifty or eighty years; the\r\nsquatters are turned loose upon us to take our lands, and we must pay\r\ntaxes for them, and we must go to work to prove that our lands are ours\r\nbefore the squatter goes. Why doesn\xe2\x80\x99t the squatter prove first that the\r\nland is his, and why doesn\xe2\x80\x99t he pay his own taxes? We, as plaintiffs,\r\nhave to bear heavy expenses, and as the delays and evasions of the law\r\nare endless, the squatter has generally managed to keep the land he\r\ntook, for we have been impoverished by heavy taxation while trying to\r\nprove our rights, and the squatter has been making money out of our\r\nlands to fight us with. Generally the Californians have had nothing but\r\nland to pay their taxes, besides paying their lawyers to defend their\r\ntitles. Thus, often the lawyer has taken all that was left out of the\r\ncost of litigation and taxes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt makes me heartsick to think how unjustly the native Californians\r\nhave been treated. I assure you, sir, that not one American in a million\r\nknows of this outrage. If they did, they would denounce it in the\r\nbitterest language; they would not tolerate it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey would denounce it perhaps, but they would tolerate it. I used to\r\nthink as you do, that the American people had a very direct influence\r\nupon the legislation of the country. It seems so to hear public speakers\r\nin election times, but half of all their fire goes up in smoke, and\r\nCongress is left coolly to do as it pleases. And the worst of it is,\r\nthat this very arbitrary Congress, so impervious to appeals of\r\nsufferers, is also led by a few persistent men who with determination do\r\nall things, spoil or kill good bills, and doctor up sick ones; and then\r\nthey half-fool and half-weary the nation into acquiescence, for what can\r\nwe do? The next batch that is sent to the Capitol will have the same\r\nelements in it, and repeat history.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems to me there ought to be some way to punish men for being bad\r\nor ineffectual legislators, when sense of honor or dread of criticism\r\nfail to make them do their duty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDon Mariano sighed and shook his head, then in a very sad voice said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat should be so, but it is not the case. No, I don\xe2\x80\x99t see any remedy\r\nin my life-time. I am afraid there is no help for us native\r\nCalifornians. We must sadly fade and pass away. The weak and the\r\nhelpless are always trampled in the throng. We must sink, go under,\r\nnever to rise. If the Americans had been friendly to us, and helped us\r\nwith good, protective laws, our fate would have been different. But to\r\nlegislate us into poverty is to legislate us into our graves. Their very\r\ncontact is deadly to us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd yet you do not seem to hate us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHate you? No, indeed! Never! The majority of my best friends are\r\nAmericans. Instead of hate, I feel great attraction toward the American\r\npeople. Their sentiments, their ways of thinking suit me, with but few\r\nexceptions. I am fond of the Americans. I know that, as a matter of\r\nfact, only the very mean and narrow-minded have harsh feelings against\r\nmy race. The trouble, the misfortune has been that the American people\r\nfelt perfect indifference towards the conquered few. We were not in\r\nsufficient numbers to command attention. We were left to the tender\r\nmercies of Congress, and the American nation never gave us a thought\r\nafter the treaty of peace with Mexico was signed. Probably any other\r\nnation would have done the same. Why should I then hate them? No,\r\nindeed. But I confess my heart collapses when I think what might be the\r\nfate of my family if I am not able to avert the ruin which has overtaken\r\nthe majority of Californians. We have not been millionaires, but we have\r\nnever known want. We are all ill prepared for poverty; and yet this\r\nlong-delayed justice, and the squatters crowding me so relentlessly\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nstopped short, then added: \xe2\x80\x9cI am not giving you a cheerful welcome with\r\nmy gloomy conversation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I want you to talk to me frankly and give me your views. You have\r\ntold me much that I had never heard before, and which I am glad to\r\nlearn. But as for feeling gloomy about the future of the family, I think\r\na plan that Mr. George Mechlin and myself have been forming will make\r\nthings rather better for the future, and we trust you will approve it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the plan?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVII.\xe2\x80\x94_Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa at Home._\r\n\r\n\r\nDon Mariano had only said, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the plan?\xe2\x80\x9d a very natural and simple\r\ninquiry, and yet it threw Clarence into something of a flutter, as it\r\nflashed vividly before his mind that the said _plan_ was based entirely\r\nupon the fate of the Texas Pacific Railroad, and that as a natural\r\nsequence it depended upon the wisdom, the moral sense and patriotism of\r\nCongress. If Congress acted right and did its duty as the mentor,\r\nguardian and trustee of the people, all would be well. But would it?\r\nWould it, indeed? The past promised nothing to the future, judging by\r\nthe light of Don Mariano\xe2\x80\x99s experience. But why should the Texas Pacific\r\nnot be granted aid? The public treasure had been lavished to help the\r\nCentral Pacific, a northern road\xe2\x80\x94why should the southern people not be\r\nentitled to the same privilege? These thoughts flashed through\r\nClarence\xe2\x80\x99s mind before he answered, then he said, somewhat timidly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe plan is to establish a bank in San Diego, with Mr. George Mechlin\r\nfor President, and Don Gabriel for Cashier. The only drawback is, of\r\ncourse, the delay there might be in constructing the Texas Pacific\r\nRailroad\xe2\x80\x94the delay in the growth of San Diego. As yet, however, we are\r\nhopeful, and the prospect seems good.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe prospect is perfectly good, and I would have entire confidence in\r\nit, if the fate of the railroad did not depend upon right and just\r\nlegislation. The Congressmen from the north do not seem to feel all the\r\ninterest they should in reviving the south. They are angry yet. The fact\r\nthat they coerced back into the Union the southern people has not\r\nappeased them yet, it seems. I wish Tom Scott would build his road\r\nwithout Congressional aid. The success of your banking project must, of\r\ncourse, depend upon the amount of population in San Diego.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUndoubtedly. And if there is no railroad, there will be no population.\r\nBut Mr. Mechlin and myself are ready with our money, and with the least\r\nencouraging sign we start our bank. I think we will begin at first with\r\ntwo hundred thousand dollars. Mr. Mechlin says he can subscribe\r\ntwenty-five or thirty thousand dollars, and I will put the balance in,\r\nsubscribing thirty thousand for Don Gabriel, twenty-five thousand for\r\nVictoriano and twenty-five thousand for Everett, with ninety thousand\r\nfor myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must be prudent in incurring risks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am. I have more than two hundred thousand that I can put in this bank\r\nwithout troubling my government bonds or my farm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClarence then explained to Don Mariano his financial affairs.\r\n\r\nDon Mariano smiled as he said: \xe2\x80\x9cI had no idea you were so well off.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI expect to make a fortune out of my Arizona mines,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, laughing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake care. Do not put any of your government bonds in them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed, I shall not. The interest on those bonds gives me nearly\r\nthirty-five thousand dollars per year, and this income is for\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d here\r\nClarence blushed and was silent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo take care of your wife,\xe2\x80\x9d Don Mariano said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir; for that alone. But do you think Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa will object to\r\nme after you explain my position?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs her only objection is that she thinks you are squatters, she would\r\nbe very unreasonable should she hold the same objections after she knows\r\nthat you are not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou make me very happy telling me that. I hope you will let me know\r\nsoon what answer she gives to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly. You can come to-morrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have some little packages that Mrs. Mechlin sends. I can bring them\r\nthis evening\xe2\x80\x94the ladies might wish to see the contents.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course, they will. They wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be women if they didn\xe2\x80\x99t. They\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nwant you to relate all the incidents of the voyage, too, and the trip to\r\nthe Yosemite. If you can, come this evening. I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell them you are\r\ncoming.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEverett and Victoriano overtook them now as they entered the valley.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay, Clary,\xe2\x80\x9d Everett called out, \xe2\x80\x9cdon\xe2\x80\x99t you want to get out here and\r\nchange seats with Tano?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll take him home,\xe2\x80\x9d Don Mariano answered; and they all drove toward\r\nthe Darrell house.\r\n\r\nAt the door were Mr. Darrell and Alice. Immediately after, Darrell came\r\nout to greet his son. He was rather cordial to Don Mariano, and asked\r\nhim to come in and take lunch. This was so very unexpected to all his\r\nhearers, that, with the exception of Don Mariano, all showed their\r\nsurprise. This kind invitation, however, was politely declined\xe2\x80\x94whereupon\r\nVictoriano, pretending to feel slighted because he was not invited,\r\ntossed his head at Clarence and Everett, and marched majestically\r\ntowards his father\xe2\x80\x99s carriage.\r\n\r\nEverett overtook him, and would not let him get in, insisting upon his\r\nremaining to luncheon. Victoriano then indicating that he was entirely\r\npacified, remained, perfectly happy, knowing his seat would be near\r\nAlice, and that was the allurement, but he said to Tisha, as she came to\r\nset a plate for him:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour cooking is so good, Tisha, that I always come sneaking around,\r\nbegging for an invitation, for I am sure you have something nice to give\r\nus.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLa massa! and right welcome ye are, too, by everybody in this \xe2\x80\x99ere\r\nfamily, and I knows it exactly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd Tisha winked to herself in the pantry, indicating to the crockery on\r\nthe shelves that she knew why Massa Tano liked her cooking, \xe2\x80\x9cand Miss\r\nAlice knows it, God bless her,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tisha, nodding her head to the rows\r\nof preserves and pickle jars, in sheer exultation, for there was nothing\r\nso interesting to Tisha on the face of the earth as a love affair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_All the world love the lover_,\xe2\x80\x9d says Emerson, and Tisha could certify\r\nto this aphoristic truth, for who more humble than Tisha? And yet her\r\nheart went headlong to the lover, whoever he might be. Therefore, a love\r\naffair in the Darrell family was to Tisha perfectly entrancing. She had\r\nbeen in a state of undefined bliss ever since her perceptive organs and\r\nother means of information had indicated to her that _Clarence was in\r\nlove_! She had taken upon herself to watch and see that the affair\r\nprogressed and ended happily.\r\n\r\nIn the evening Clarence proceeded to deliver the packages sent by Elvira\r\nto her mother and sisters.\r\n\r\nWith beating heart he timidly ascended the steps of the front veranda of\r\nthe Alamar house, for he did not feel entirely certain that Do\xc3\xb1a\r\nJosefa\xe2\x80\x99s objections would be withdrawn. He was not kept in suspense\r\nabout the matter, however, as now, preceded by woolly Milord, the\r\nhandsome matron herself came forward to meet him, extending her hand in\r\nwelcome most gracious. She never had seemed to him so handsome, so\r\nregally beautiful. He thought that he had been right in imagining Juno\r\nmust have looked like her. And when she smiled, as she extended her hand\r\nto him, he thought that such was surely the smile, the manner and the\r\nbeauty of a goddess.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am so glad to welcome you, Mr. Darrell,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, \xe2\x80\x9cand knowing that\r\nyou wish to speak to me, and as I, too, wish to speak with you alone, I\r\nthought I would meet you here by myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMilord barked, wagged his tail in token of friendship, and sat up to\r\nlisten.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are very kind,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence said, placing the packages on a table near\r\nhim, not knowing, however, what else to say.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSit down,\xe2\x80\x9d Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa said, pushing one of the large arm-chairs for\r\nClarence to sit near. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd let me begin our conversation by apologizing\r\nfor the very wrong, very unjust opinion I have had of you. Believe me,\r\nit gives me great pleasure to know I was mistaken.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer voice, her manner, were more gracious than her words, and Clarence\r\nthought that it was not to be wondered that the daughters were so very\r\ncharming.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am the one who should apologize,\xe2\x80\x9d he hastened to reply; \xe2\x80\x9cI ought to\r\nhave asked Don Mariano to explain my position to you before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish you had, for that would have saved us many anxious thoughts. But\r\nlet us not regret the past too much, only enough to cause us to\r\nappreciate the present. I understand how you felt, not wishing to seem\r\ndisrespectful to your father, and yet not agreeing with him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt has been the source of very painful feelings to me to see my father\r\nso misled, but I have found very great comfort in the fact that my\r\nmother agrees with me. She told me she would never come down if I did\r\nnot pay for the land.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; Mariano told me this, and I beg of you to convey to her my regrets\r\nat having been in error about this matter. Will you do so, please?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly, madam; with great pleasure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI trust that her good influence will be of great assistance to you in\r\npersuading your father to change his views.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I hope so; in fact, I feel pretty sure that, more or less warmly,\r\nall of my brothers and sisters will agree with me, especially Everett\r\nand Alice. Another fact, also, is in my favor, that my father promised\r\nto Don Mariano, when he first took up the land, that he would pay for it\r\nif the Courts decided against the settlers. That promise, I think, will\r\nhave a good effect, for he always keeps his word. When the appeal is\r\ndismissed I shall remind him of it. In the meantime I shall watch my\r\nopportunities to conciliate him, for I feel sure he will resent my\r\nhaving paid for the land without his consent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is a pity. I am very sorry for that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is unpleasant that he should take so decided a view of so clear a\r\nsubject, but I feel perfectly justified in acting as I did. What I do\r\nregret sincerely is that you and\xe2\x80\x94and Miss Mercedes should not have known\r\nthe truth sooner,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence, reddening to the roots of his hair,\r\nfor he felt that he was touching on most delicate ground; with anxious,\r\nbeating heart he waited for her reply.\r\n\r\nHer face flushed a little. Was it pride, or was it because the heart of\r\nwoman must always flutter when in her presence the subject of love is\r\napproached, in which ever direction it may be, and no matter if the\r\nsnows of eighty winters rest placidly on her brow? Love is woman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nspecial province\xe2\x80\x94she has, or has had, or will have, power there. Man\r\nmight take, and absolutely appropriate, monopolize and exclude her from\r\nmoney-making, from politics and from many other pursuits, made difficult\r\nto her by man\xe2\x80\x99s tyranny, man\xe2\x80\x99s hindrances, man\xe2\x80\x99s objections\xe2\x80\x94but in the\r\nrealms of love he is not the absolute dictator, not the master. He must\r\nsue, he must wait, he must be patient. Yes, the lord of creation often\r\nhas to take snubbing quite meekly, for he can\xe2\x80\x99t help it.\r\n\r\nClarence knew all this, but he saw Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa smile, and grew brave.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; Mercedes, poor child, was very unhappy, and it went to my heart\r\nlike a knife to send her away, but I deemed it to be my duty\xe2\x80\x94I hoped it\r\nwould be for the best.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd so it was. You did right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but it did not enter into my calculations that you were to jump on\r\nboard the steamer,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, laughing.\r\n\r\nClarence\xe2\x80\x99s face and ears became crimson.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope you have forgiven me for it,\xe2\x80\x9d he stammered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose I must,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, still laughing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI assure you I had no idea of doing such a thing, but when I saw her\r\ngoing I didn\xe2\x80\x99t care what I did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd as you received some dispatches, you thought it was best to\r\ndispatch other matters as well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, after all, she left everything for _you_ to dispatch. My fate is\r\nin your hands.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was now Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa\xe2\x80\x99s turn to blush.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought that George and Mariano had decided that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, indeed. It is all left to you. Please be merciful,\xe2\x80\x9d he pleaded,\r\nfeeling very nervous, for he heard steps and voices approaching from\r\nthrough the hall.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat shall I say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay _yes_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, smiling, with a kind look in her beautiful eyes.\r\n\r\nHe glanced quickly toward the front door, and seeing no one in sight,\r\ndropped on his knees, and seizing her hand, covered it with hurried and\r\nvehement kisses, saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThanks! thanks!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd all before she knew what he was about.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cImpetuous boy! is that the way you rushed and assaulted my poor little\r\nMercedes?\xe2\x80\x9d said she, laughing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have said yes\xe2\x80\x94God bless you for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, yes to what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! your heart will tell you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is that? What about the heart?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Don Mariano, standing in the\r\ndoor. \xe2\x80\x9cThis looks like love-making. I am interested. Let me hear a\r\nlittle of it,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, pulling after him a chair, to sit between\r\nClarence and his wife.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is love-making, only it is by proxy, and I am to guess at things\r\nwithout being told,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, still laughing.\r\n\r\nClarence was greatly embarrassed. He knew he had not formally asked for\r\nthe hand of Mercedes in the serious manner that the subject merited, but\r\nhe had been carried away by his fears, then by his hopes, and the matter\r\nwas launched before he could scarcely say how. When for months past he\r\nhad thought, time and again, of a probable interview with Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa,\r\nhe had imagined himself talking to that queenly lady in his most stately\r\nSpanish. But now he had taken hold of Cervantes\xe2\x80\x99 language\xe2\x80\x94I may say,\r\njumped into it, just as he had jumped on the steamer\xe2\x80\x99s deck, thinking of\r\nno difficulties in the way, except that they must be overcome in order\r\nto reach Mercedes.\r\n\r\nHe gave a most appealing look to Don Mariano, whose kind heart\r\nimmediately responded by saying to his wife:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf it is love-making, and you are to guess at it, there won\xe2\x80\x99t be much\r\ndelay, for no woman was ever slow to guess such matters. I know _you_\r\nunderstood me very quickly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHear him! but please do not learn such frightful lessons in vanity and\r\nconceit,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, laughing again, but blushing also.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know she understood what I meant, when I would ride eighty miles on\r\nhorseback for the pleasure of serenading her. To do that, or jump aboard\r\nthe steamer after it is under way, means about the same thing, I think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDon Mariano kept talking in that strain until Clarence recovered his\r\ncomposure.\r\n\r\nHe then said: \xe2\x80\x9cI have been your ambassador before this queen, and her\r\nmajesty has granted your petition. So you have nothing more to do now\r\nthan to fall on your knees and kiss her hands.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhereupon, down went Clarence again on his knees, and seizing her hand,\r\nkissed it warmly and repeatedly, in spite of Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nprotestations, saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat will do. Once is enough\xe2\x80\x94once is enough. Reserve your kisses for\r\nyounger hands.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll warrant he has plenty more in reserve,\xe2\x80\x9d Don Mariano said,\r\nlaughing.\r\n\r\nAnd it was true, for Clarence was so happy that he could have kissed the\r\nentire Alamar family\xe2\x80\x94all, all\xe2\x80\x94irrespective of age or sex.\r\n\r\nThe days now passed pleasantly and peacefully enough at the Alamar\r\nrancho.\r\n\r\nDon Mariano knew that he would have to go through many disagreeable\r\nscenes with the squatters when the appeal should be dismissed, but as\r\nthe law would be on his side _finally_, he confidently hoped to see the\r\nend of his troubles, intending to allow the squatters to keep their\r\nhomes, provided only that they would fence their crops and pay their own\r\ntaxes.\r\n\r\nClarence reconciled himself to wait until the fall to take that ring\r\nwhich Mercedes had told him to bring himself. This would be the most\r\njudicious plan, as he would thus take the necessary time to have the\r\nmines prospected and to decide about their purchase, before going to New\r\nYork. In the meanwhile he worked in the garden, fenced and prepared\r\nground for planting grapevines and fruit trees. He read and wrote love\r\nletters, and passed nearly all of his evenings at the Alamar house,\r\nholding Milord, who always came to be held by him as soon as he arrived.\r\n\r\nThe telegram from Fred Haverly came in due time, a few words only, but\r\nhow exhilarating they were to Clarence, making his pulse beat high.\r\n\r\nIt read thus:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cProspect splendid. Far better than described. Have written to-day.\r\nHurrah!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLike the telegram, Fred\xe2\x80\x99s letter came promptly in the early part of\r\nAugust.\r\n\r\nThe ledge was so wide, Fred said, that the miners had sunk their\r\nprospect shaft in the center of the vein, and consequently all the rock\r\ntaken out was a high-grade ore. That he was going to run two drifts, and\r\nwould then have a more correct idea of the character of the mine, its\r\nvolume, formation, etc. Only a small portion of the hanging wall was\r\nvisible at the entrance, as the shaft went immediately into the very\r\nheart of the broad vein.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut,\xe2\x80\x9d Fred added, \xe2\x80\x9cIf the mine proves to be one-tenth as good as it\r\nseems, \xe2\x80\x98there are millions in it,\xe2\x80\x99 literally.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo Clarence must make up his mind to wait developments.\r\n\r\nIn the meantime the settlers had harvested their crops of hay and grain,\r\nand were hauling them to town. Don Mariano, as a matter of course, had\r\npaid dearly for these same crops, with the sacrifice of his fine cows,\r\nbesides very heavy taxes. He had sent half of his cattle away to the\r\nsierra, and those left had been as carefully guarded as possible, but\r\nstill the dumb brutes would be attracted by the green grain, and would\r\nobey the law of nature, to go and eat it, in utter disregard of the \xe2\x80\x9cno\r\nfence law.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThus, every night the fusilade of the law-abiding settlers would be\r\nheard, as they, to protect _their_ \xe2\x80\x9c_rights under the law_,\xe2\x80\x9d would be\r\nshooting the Don\xe2\x80\x99s cattle all over the rancho. In vain did he, or his\r\nsons and servants, ride out to find who fired. There was never a man to\r\nbe seen with a gun or rifle in his hands; it never could be proved that\r\nany one of these peaceful farmers had fired a shot. The cattle were\r\nkilled, but who had done it no one could say. Day after day the\r\n_vaqueros_ would come in and report the number of cattle found shot,\r\ndead or wounded, that morning, and Gabriel would make a note of the\r\nnumber; at the end of the month he would add these figures, and the Don\r\nhad the sad satisfaction of knowing how many of his cattle were killed\r\n_under the law_. For although the law did not enjoin upon any one to\r\nkill cattle in this manner, the effect was the same as if it had said so\r\nplainly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think Southern California isn\xe2\x80\x99t such a very dry country as people try\r\nto make it out. The settlers on this rancho, I reckon, will realize nice\r\nlittle sums on their crops this year,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Darrell observed at breakfast\r\none morning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd with their little sums they should pay the Don for the cattle they\r\nhave shot. It is a shame to take his land, have him pay taxes, and then\r\nkill his cattle also,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Mrs. Darrell. \xe2\x80\x9cThose heartless people\r\nkeep me awake sometimes with their cattle-shooting. I think the Don and\r\nhis family are too kind to bear all these daily (and nightly) outrages\r\nso patiently.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought you had given it up as a bad job to be the Don\xe2\x80\x99s champion,\r\nMrs. Darrell,\xe2\x80\x9d said her husband.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf by being his champion I could save his cattle there would be no\r\ndanger of giving up my championship. What I regret is that my sympathy\r\nshould be so useless.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind, mother, the Don will soon have the power to drive all this\r\n_canaille_ out of his rancho,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you include me with the _canaille_?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Darrell.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, father, I do not. I suppose you have not forgotten you promised Don\r\nMariano to pay for the land you located when the title should be\r\napproved.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen there is no more _dispute_ about it,\xe2\x80\x9d Darrell explained.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI understood you had said that when the government did not dispute it.\r\nWe all know that the squatters will dispute it as long as they can find\r\nlawyers, who for a fee will fight against right and justice,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will keep to what I said\xe2\x80\x94but I am not going to have my words\r\nconstrued to suit everybody,\xe2\x80\x9d Darrell said, doggedly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow is the Don to have power to drive off the settlers, Clary? Tell\r\nus,\xe2\x80\x9d Webster inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you tell him, Clary. He\xe2\x80\x99ll go and tell it to the _squatters_,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nWillie interposed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd since when did you learn to call the settlers squatters, Master\r\nWillie? Ain\xe2\x80\x99t you a squatter yourself?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mr. Darrell.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I\xe2\x80\x99m not. Am I, mamma?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Willie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope not, my dear. If I thought any one in this family were to\r\ndeserve such a name I would not have come down to this place,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs.\r\nDarrell replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is a squatter, anyhow, mamma?\xe2\x80\x9d Clementine inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA squatter is a person who locates a land claim on land that belongs to\r\nsome other person,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Darrell explained.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn land that other persons say belongs to them, but which land, as no\r\none knows to whom it belongs, it is free to be occupied by any American\r\ncitizen,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Darrell added with emphasis.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere you are again mixing the wilful squatter with the honest settler,\r\nwho pre-empts his land legitimately. The dividing line between the\r\nsquatter and the settler is very clear to any one who honestly wants to\r\nsee it,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Darrell said, and three or four of her children started to\r\nexplain how well they did see that line.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is as plain as the nose on your face,\xe2\x80\x9d Willie\xe2\x80\x99s voice said in a high\r\nkey. \xe2\x80\x9cThe honest settler only pre-empts government land, but the\r\nsquatter goes into anybody\xe2\x80\x99s land before he knows who has title.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBravo!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Everett; \xe2\x80\x9cyou got it straight this time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen a squatter is a land thief?\xe2\x80\x9d Clementine inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is a severe term,\xe2\x80\x9d Alice observed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut isn\xe2\x80\x99t it true?\xe2\x80\x9d Clementine argued.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, because the squatter might not _intend_ to steal. He might\r\nmistakenly take land which belongs to some one else. The intention is\r\nwhat makes the action a theft or not,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Darrell explained.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why should they make such mistakes? Ain\xe2\x80\x99t somebody there to say to\r\nwhom the land belongs?\xe2\x80\x9d Master Willie inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but that somebody might not be believed, Master Willie, and there\r\nis where the shoe pinches,\xe2\x80\x9d Webster explained.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d was Willie\xe2\x80\x99s exclamation, and he became thoughtful.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI give it up,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clementine with a sigh, making them all laugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is a very wise resolve,\xe2\x80\x9d Darrell observed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve got it, papa,\xe2\x80\x9d Willie\xe2\x80\x99s voice again was heard saying.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what have you got?\xe2\x80\x9d his father asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe government ought to say first to whom the land belongs, and not let\r\nanybody take a single acre until the government says it is public land.\r\nIsn\xe2\x80\x99t that the way you say, Clarence?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you are quoting Clarence. I thought it was your own original idea\r\nyou were giving us,\xe2\x80\x9d Darrell said, and all laughed at Willie.\r\n\r\nBut he held his ground, saying; \xe2\x80\x9cIt is Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s idea, but I only\r\nunderstood it this minute, so now it is mine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is right, Willie. That is the way correct ideas are disseminated\r\nand take root,\xe2\x80\x9d Everett said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd erroneous ones, too,\xe2\x80\x9d Darrell added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich is the correct, papa?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Willie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour mind is even more inquisitive than usual this morning, Willie,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Jane.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuppose it is, do you object to it?\xe2\x80\x9d Willie queried.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you had better be a lawyer,\xe2\x80\x9d Lucy suggested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean to be. Then I will be the Don\xe2\x80\x99s lawyer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut suppose he don\xe2\x80\x99t want you?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Webster.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut he will, for I will be honest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill he want you if you are stupid, only because you are honest?\xe2\x80\x9d asked\r\nClementine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hate girls, they talk so silly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Willie, again bringing the\r\nlaugh on himself.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVIII.\xe2\x80\x94_At Newport._\r\n\r\n\r\nMr. George Mechlin and traveling companions had a most delightful\r\njourney across the continent in spite of the hot weather.\r\n\r\nMr. Lawrence Mechlin and wife came to New York to meet George\xe2\x80\x99s bride\r\nand her sister and take them to Long Branch, where they had been\r\nsojourning for the last two months.\r\n\r\nMrs. Lawrence Mechlin was most favorably impressed with her nephew\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwife and her sister. The two young beauties captivated her at once. She\r\nwas enthusiastic.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear,\xe2\x80\x9d said she after dinner, addressing Elvira, \xe2\x80\x9cbefore I saw you\r\nand your sister I had been deliberating in my mind whether we should not\r\ngo directly to our cottage in Newport and spend the remainder of the\r\nsummer there. But now I think we had better go to Long Branch first, and\r\nthen, unless you wish to visit Saratoga, we will go to Newport. How will\r\nthat do?\xe2\x80\x9d She looked at George.\r\n\r\nGeorge smiled. He knew his aunt must be much pleased to put herself to\r\nthe trouble of this traveling in hot weather. He replied:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sure these young ladies will be most happy to follow you, aunt.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you all get too tired. And this reminds me that people who have\r\nbeen in the cars for ten days should have some rest. The day will be\r\ncool to-morrow; we need not go back to Long Branch until the day after,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid the senior Mechlin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe do not intend going to-morrow. We have something to do in town yet,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Mrs. Mechlin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSome shopping, I suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Lawrence observed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly,\xe2\x80\x9d his wife assented.\r\n\r\nAfter Mrs. Mechlin accompanied Elvira and Mercedes to their respective\r\napartments, she returned to the library, where her husband and nephew\r\nwere engaged in conversation. There was in Mrs. Mechlin\xe2\x80\x99s step and\r\nmanner a degree of pleased elasticity, an amiable buoyancy of contented\r\nalacrity, which betokened that her mind was in a state of subdued\r\npleasurable excitement which was to her very enjoyable. She came to\r\nGeorge and kissed him twice, saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must repeat my kiss and congratulations, dear George. Your wife is\r\nperfection. Where in the world did such beauties grow? I assure you I am\r\nperfectly carried away by those two girls. No wonder you were so\r\nimpatient to get married. They will be the rage next winter, and I shall\r\ngive several dinners and receptions in honor of your wife, of course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are always so kind to me, dear aunt.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo more than I ought to be, but this time pleasure and duty will go\r\ntogether. I know I shall be proud to present my beautiful niece to New\r\nYork society. Her manners are exquisite. She is lovely. She will be\r\ngreatly admired, and justly so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will have to arrange for your parties and dinners to be in December\r\nand February, because George is going to Washington in January, and the\r\nyoung ladies will take that opportunity to visit the Capital with him,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Mr. Mechlin, senior.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is a pity. Couldn\xe2\x80\x99t they go in December?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, because George\xe2\x80\x99s business is with the Attorney General, and he\r\nwrote to me that he would not be ready until January. However, January\r\nis six months off yet. For the present, you have enough on your hands\r\nwith your plans for the summer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is very true. We will order some summer things to be made\r\nimmediately. But I feel quite sure that we can find imported dresses\r\nready made that will suit. I saw some lovely batists and grenadines at\r\nArnold & Constable\xe2\x80\x99s, just from Paris, also beautiful embroidered\r\nmuslins at Stewart\xe2\x80\x99s. We will see to-morrow and be ready to return the\r\nday after.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLife at Long Branch in the Mechlin cottage was very delightful to Elvira\r\nand Mercedes. When they had been there about two weeks, Mr. Robert\r\nGunther appeared on the scene, and next day Mr. Arthur Selden followed.\r\nAs they were old friends of the Mechlins, Mrs. Mechlin thought it was a\r\nnatural thing that these two young gentlemen, on their return from their\r\ntravels, should come to see her at Long Branch.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn a day or two we are going to Newport, young gentlemen,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou had better join our party and we\xe2\x80\x99ll all go together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall be most happy. My mother and sister have been with friends in\r\nthe White Mountains, but will be at Newport next week, so this\r\narrangement will suit me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gunther.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will suit me, also, as I promised my mother and sisters I should be\r\nat Newport in two weeks. Saratoga is too hot for me. I left them there\r\nunder father\xe2\x80\x99s care. He likes Saratoga,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Selden said.\r\n\r\nIf their sojourn at Long Branch had seemed so delightful to Elvira and\r\nMercedes, their pleasures increased ten-fold at Newport. The Mechlin\r\nvilla, shaded by tall elms and poplars, and surrounded by shrubbery and\r\nflowers, with a beautiful lawn and fountains in front, facing the ocean,\r\nand well-kept walks and arbors in different places on the grounds, was\r\ncertainly a charming abode, fit to please the most fastidious taste.\r\nThen the drives, croquet playing, boat sailing and promenades, were also\r\nmuch enjoyed by our two little Californians. In the evenings, music and\r\ndancing would add variety to their pleasures, until such life seemed to\r\nthem too charming to be real.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd is this life repeated every summer, year after year?\xe2\x80\x9d asked\r\nMercedes one evening as in the coming twilight she was sitting with Mr.\r\nBob Gunther in a cozy bower of roses located on a little knoll in the\r\ngrounds of the Gunther villa. They were looking at the gay equipages\r\nwhich drove by. Gunther sighed as he answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you like this life?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery much, but perhaps because it is a novelty to me. However, I am\r\nnever tired of things that I once like, so I suppose I would like it\r\nalways.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe did not look at Gunther; her attention was all given to the\r\nbeautiful carriages driving by. If she had looked at him she would have\r\nseen the intensity of his passion in the workings of his features. For a\r\nmoment the struggle with himself was terrible; but controlling his voice\r\nall he could, he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can have this life if you wish, and continue in the winters in a\r\nbeautiful residence in New York or in Paris, should you desire it. You\r\nknow it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I do not. I have no fairy god-mother to give me palaces. Come, let\r\nus go. Where is everybody?\xe2\x80\x9d said she, hurrying out of the arbor, looking\r\nabout the grounds for Elvira and Miss Gunther, who had but a moment\r\nbefore been near her. \xe2\x80\x9cAh! there they are; let us go to them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo I frighten you? or am I tiresome?\xe2\x80\x9d said he, pale to the lips,\r\nfollowing her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNeither; but young ladies who\xe2\x80\x94who are\xe2\x80\x94I mean any young lady, should not\r\nhave such _tete-\xc3\xa1-tetes_ with fascinating young gentlemen in rosy\r\nbowers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYoung ladies who are\xe2\x80\x94what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho are judicious.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWere you not going to say \xe2\x80\x98who are engaged?\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I had, I might not have said the truth, _strictly_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, in Heaven\xe2\x80\x99s name, tell me the truth! Are you engaged?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAsk me no questions, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell thee no lies.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are cruel; you are trifling with me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe stopped and looked up quickly into his eyes. For a moment she\r\nhesitated, then resolutely said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Gunther, I like you very much. Don\xe2\x80\x99t talk to me like this. I want\r\nto find pleasure in your society, but I shall not if you talk so to me.\r\nI am not and have never been cruel, and it never entered my head to\r\ntrifle with you\xe2\x80\x94never!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cForgive me this time. I shall never offend again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe looked so distressed that Mercedes felt very sorry for him. She would\r\nhave comforted him if she could. They walked in silence a few steps, but\r\nas he still looked pale, she did not wish the other ladies to see him.\r\nThey were walking towards the house. Pointing to a narrow path leading\r\ntowards the seashore, she said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat path goes to your boat-house, I suppose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. I have a new boat; would you like to see it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it far? You see it is getting late.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe can come back in ten minutes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTruly? No longer than ten minutes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a second longer unless you wish it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, turning quickly into the little path, and he followed\r\nher. She did not care a straw to see the boat, but she wanted to give\r\nhim time to get back the color to his face. She walked so fast down the\r\nhill; she almost ran. She looked back; he was following close. She began\r\nto laugh and started to run. He ran after her, and they did not stop\r\nuntil they got to the beach.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow long is it since we started?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked. He looked at his watch.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot quite two minutes,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI beg your pardon for running, obliging you to run, but I felt like it\r\nwhen I saw the blue water. It reminded me of home, of San Diego.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo apology is needed. If it gave you pleasure to run, I am glad you did\r\nso.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne look only, and then we must go back. Perhaps we had better return;\r\nI hear horses coming,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, and at the same time Mr. Selden and his\r\nyoungest sister came down by the boat-house at a gallop. His face\r\nflushed and became pale, but he lifted his hat as he passed. Gunther did\r\nthe same, in answer to Miss Selden\xe2\x80\x99s salutation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us return. More riders might be passing,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mercedes, and began\r\nto walk back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat! without seeing the boat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will be dark inside the house. I\xe2\x80\x99ll come some other time, earlier.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you promise me that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly. You see, we can\xe2\x80\x99t run as fast up hill; it will take more\r\nthan two minutes to return.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAgain the galloping of horses was heard, and Selden, with his sister,\r\npassed on their way back just as Mercedes and Gunther reached the bank\r\nat the edge of the lawn and sat down to rest.\r\n\r\nSelden\xe2\x80\x99s sister had noticed how he flushed and how livid he became a\r\nminute after, and faithfully reported the fact to her vigilant mamma as\r\nsoon as she got home. Arthur would be watched now. His mamma knew that\r\nhe was a millionaire and considered \xe2\x80\x9ca catch.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Seldens had been abroad many years, the greater time in England, and\r\nhad acquired some English habits, one of which was to dine late. That\r\nevening Arthur did not come down to dinner until half-past eight\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock. He was afraid he would be questioned regarding the young lady\r\nwith Robert Gunther. He felt too angry with his friend to hear his name\r\nspoken. But it was unavoidable. As soon as he took his seat at the table\r\nhis mother asked:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho was the lady with Robert on the beach?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis evening as we rode by his boat-house,\xe2\x80\x9d explained his sister.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWere they coming out of the boat-house?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the elder sister.\r\nArthur\xe2\x80\x99s lips became white again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t be alarmed. They did not go into the boat-house,\xe2\x80\x9d said he,\r\nsneeringly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI? I alarmed? It seems to me you are the one alarmed. I might say\r\nfrightened,\xe2\x80\x9d she replied, reddening like a peony, trembling with anger,\r\nas she well understood her brother meant to allude to her well known\r\nfondness for Bob.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is the lady, any way? I ask,\xe2\x80\x9d reiterated Mrs. Selden.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is George Mechlin\xe2\x80\x99s sister-in-law,\xe2\x80\x9d Arthur replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! That is the beauty I hear half a dozen fellows are raving about,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Miss Selden, to pique her brother.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs she so very pretty, Arthur?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the younger sister.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never saw any woman so beautiful in all my life,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered, with\r\ndogged resolution as if about to pull the string of his shower bath.\r\n\r\nThe mother and daughters exchanged looks. They understood it all now.\r\nPoor Arthur, he, too, was raving.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy-the-by, I met Mechlin in the street to-day, and he asked if any one\r\nwas sick in our family,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Mr. Selden, senior.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI understand. We will call to-morrow,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Selden said, sententiously.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will see the beauties.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd they did. When they were in their carriage riding home, Mrs. Selden\r\nsaid:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry for Arthur if he is in love with that girl. I fear he will\r\nnever get over it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo much the better if he marries her,\xe2\x80\x9d said the younger sister.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but if he does not, he\xe2\x80\x99ll never have any heart left.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you suppose she would refuse him?\xe2\x80\x9d said the elder sister, haughtily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know; I must have a talk with Arthur.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe had a talk with Arthur, and when he saw evasion would be useless, he\r\ntold her all about his love and why he believed it hopeless, judging by\r\nwhat he heard George say.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut if she is not positively engaged to that Darrell, why should you\r\nfear him more than you fear Gunther?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I believe she loves him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps. But we are not sure of it. Moreover, he is far off in\r\nCalifornia, and you are here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArthur shook his head despondingly, but, nevertheless, he was pleased to\r\nhear his mother say that they must entertain those two Californians, and\r\nMrs. Mechlin would think it was all intended as a compliment to herself.\r\n\r\nThe Gunthers being more intimate with the Mechlins, should not be\r\noutdone by the Seldens in courtesy to these two ladies (at least such\r\nwas the opinion expressed by Robert to his mother), and thus a day\r\nhardly passed without some entertainment for their amusement.\r\n\r\nArthur closed his eyes to the future and let himself float down this\r\nstream of sweet pleasures, knowing that they were but a dream, and yet\r\nfor that reason more determined to drink the last drop of that nectar so\r\nintoxicating, and enjoy being near her, within the sound of her voice,\r\nwithin the magic circle of her personality. The thought that he had seen\r\nher with Gunther rambling on the beach had been at first very bitter and\r\ndisconcerting, but when he had learned that she had intended going to\r\nsee the boat, but changed her mind, he consoled himself, and more easily\r\nyet, when he observed that Gunther and other admirers made no more\r\nprogress as suitors than he did himself.\r\n\r\nMisery loves company, sure. Thus it will be seen that Mr. Arthur Selden\r\ndid not deceive himself with any very great hopes of success; still,\r\nsuch is the complexity of man\xe2\x80\x99s aspirations and man\xe2\x80\x99s reasoning, that he\r\ndetermined to speak to Mercedes of his love, for he had never done\r\nso\xe2\x80\x94had never offered himself to her. He would know the worst from her\r\nown lips. So one morning in the month of September, when George Mechlin\r\nand his uncle had gone to New York on business on account of Jay Cook\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfailure, Selden saw the two Misses Mechlin out driving on the road\r\ntowards Fort Adams. He concluded that the two Misses Mechlin must be\r\ngoing to call on the officers\xe2\x80\x99 wives, stationed at the Fort, and\r\nMercedes must be alone at home. He immediately took a side road thus to\r\navoid meeting the Mechlins and drove directly to the Mechlin villa. He\r\nfound Mercedes alone in the library, where she had gone for a book to\r\ntake to her room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Arthur Selden,\xe2\x80\x9d said the tall servant at the library door, and\r\nbehind his broad shoulders peeped Arthur\xe2\x80\x99s red whiskers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI took the liberty of following the servant,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause I hoped\r\nwe would be less interrupted here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUndoubtedly,\xe2\x80\x9d replied she, laughing and offering him a chair; \xe2\x80\x9cvery\r\nlogical deduction.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t laugh at me, please,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, blushing; \xe2\x80\x9cI know you are thinking\r\nthat others might follow you here as well as I, and it is so, but you\r\nsee, Miss Mercedes, I am in despair at times. I have been wishing to\r\nspeak to you alone, but I never have a chance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Mr. Selden, you see me very often.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but not alone, not where I could tell you all I feel for you, and\r\nbeg you not to drive me to despair. You know I have loved you from the\r\nfirst instant I saw you. Can I hope ever to win your love? May I hope,\r\nor is my love hopeless?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Selden, I like you very much, but please do not ask me to love you.\r\nIt is not possible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not? Is it because I am not handsome like Bob Gunther?\xe2\x80\x9d said he,\r\nwith a painful sneer. \xe2\x80\x9cBelieve me, I shall be a devoted, loving husband;\r\nnone can love you more passionately and devotedly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not doubt it. But I cannot. Please don\xe2\x80\x99t ask me, and don\xe2\x80\x99t hate\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Robert Gunther,\xe2\x80\x9d said the tall waiter, and Bob\xe2\x80\x99s broad brow and\r\ngood-natured smile shone at the door.\r\n\r\nThe delightful sojourn at Newport was now over. The Mechlin family were\r\nagain at their town residence in New York City. Elvira and Mercedes, as\r\nit was their habit, were that evening having their cosy chat before\r\ngoing to bed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo Clarence will be here next month,\xe2\x80\x9d Elvira remarked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, he says he will spend Christmas with us, and if we\xe2\x80\x99ll let him, he\r\nwill go with us to Washington.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat will be delightful.
