About that time he received the\r\nletter in which Clarence proposed to buy all of his cattle, advising him\r\nto restock the rancho afterwards, when cleared of all trespassers. He\r\nliked the proposition, and immediately gave orders to drive all the\r\ncattle to his sister\xe2\x80\x99s rancho as they were got together; there to be put\r\nin a valley and kept in a sort of depot, as they were gathered and\r\nbrought in bands of any number, to wait until Clarence returned. But as\r\nafterwards Don Mariano feared that by the time Clarence came back, there\r\nwould be no cattle left to sell, he now hastened their gathering and\r\ndecided to send them off as soon as possible. Patiently, and without a\r\nword of complaint, Don Mariano and his two sons would ride out every day\r\nto superintend personally the collecting of the cattle and sending them\r\noff to his sister\xe2\x80\x99s rancho to the valley, where the rendezvous or depot\r\nhad been established. Victoriano named this valley the \xe2\x80\x9c_rodeo triste_,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\ninsisting that the cattle knew it was a \xe2\x80\x9c_rodeo triste_,\xe2\x80\x9d and walked to\r\nit sadly, guessing that they were to be exiled and butchered. \xe2\x80\x9cJust like\r\nourselves, the poor natives,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9ctossed from one cruelty to\r\nanother still worse, and then crushed out.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9c_Rodeo triste_\xe2\x80\x9d was a very\r\nappropriate name, considering the fact of its being different from the\r\ngay and boisterous gatherings of other years, when \xe2\x80\x9cthe boys\xe2\x80\x9d of the\r\nsurrounding ranchos all assembled at Alamar to separate their cattle and\r\nhave a grand time marking and branding the calves; twisting the tails of\r\nstubborn ones by way of a logical demonstration, a convincing argument\r\nconveyed in that persuasive form, which was to a calf always\r\nunanswerable and irresistible. Then the day\xe2\x80\x99s work and fun would wind up\r\nwith a hilarious barbecue. But this was all in the past, which had been\r\nhappy, and was now a fading tableau.\r\n\r\nAlice, watching from behind the honeysuckle, saw Don Mariano, his two\r\nsons and three _vaqueros_ ride down the valley. There they separated,\r\neach followed by a _vaquero_, going in different directions.\r\n\r\nBut Alice was not the only one watching the riders going out to gather\r\nstray cattle. Though with very different sentiments from those which\r\nagitated her loving heart, the entire population of the rancho had been\r\nattentive, though unseen, spectators of the Don\xe2\x80\x99s proceedings. In the\r\nevenings the neighbors would come to relate to Darrell how many head of\r\ncattle and horses they had seen pass by their farms, and renew their\r\ncomments thereon.\r\n\r\nThus six weeks passed. The remittitur from the Supreme Court to the\r\nUnited States District Court at San Francisco came. This caused a ripple\r\nof excitement among the settlers. Then a bigger one\xe2\x80\x94a perfect tidal\r\nwave\xe2\x80\x94was expected with the surveyors that would come to make the survey\r\nof the rancho; and when this should be finished, then the grandest and\r\nlast effort must be made by the settlers to prevent the approval of it.\r\nThus, at least, they would have more litigation, and while the case was\r\nin the courts, they would still be on the rancho raising crops, and\r\npaying no taxes and no rent, as they knew perfectly well that the Don\r\nwould never sue them for \xe2\x80\x9crents and profits.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEverett had gone to town for the mail that day; letters from Clarence\r\nwere expected. The neighbors knew it, for by dint of asking questions\r\nthey had learned to time the arrival of his letters, and would drop in\r\nquite accidentally, but unerringly, and in an off-hand manner ask if\r\nthere was \xe2\x80\x9cany news from Mr. Clarence?\xe2\x80\x9d The Don, with his two sons and\r\nthree _vaqueros_, had gone out in search of his cattle, as usual, just\r\nas if no _remittitur_ had come. The settlers thought this was a most\r\nexcellent subject to ventilate with their neighbor, Darrell; they came\r\nin goodly numbers, \xe2\x80\x9cto _revolve_ the matter, and talk it over in a\r\n_neighborly_ way,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Hughes had said, with his perennial smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust so; sit down, sit down,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Darrell replied; and when all having\r\ndragged chairs and pulled them forward from between their knees, had\r\ndropped upon them, he added, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat may happen to be the matter we are to\r\nrevolve?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, the remittitur, of course,\xe2\x80\x9d Hughes replied, in his oiliest tones.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I thought something new,\xe2\x80\x9d Darrell remarked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is a clincher, you know,\xe2\x80\x9d Hughes replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but we knew it was coming.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you think it queer that the Don be hurrying off his cattle, now\r\nthat he\xe2\x80\x99s won his suit? Don\xe2\x80\x99t that look as if he don\xe2\x80\x99t put much trust in\r\nhis victory?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe trusts his victory, but he knows that more stock has been shot for\r\nthe last six weeks than for six months previous. He wants to save _a\r\nfew_ head,\xe2\x80\x9d said Romeo Hancock, smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRoper told me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hughes, ignoring what Romeo said, \xe2\x80\x9cthat, if the\r\nsettlers wish it, this case might be kept in the courts for fifty\r\nyears.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter the land is surveyed?\xe2\x80\x9d Darrell asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, after the survey.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe begin our new war by _objecting_ to the survey, I suppose; ain\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nit?\xe2\x80\x9d Miller asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is what Roper says,\xe2\x80\x9d Hughes replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd, meantime, harass the enemy like the deuce,\xe2\x80\x9d Gasbang added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly; that is Roper\xe2\x80\x99s advice,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mathews.\r\n\r\nWith a gesture of disgust, Romeo said: \xe2\x80\x9cOf course, no cattle having been\r\nshot in this rancho before Roper advised it, let the harassing begin\r\nnow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here, young man, you had better get more years over your head\r\nbefore you talk so glibly,\xe2\x80\x9d Billy Mathews snarled at Romeo.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is a settler like yourself, Mr. Mathews, and he has as good a right\r\nto express his opinion, though he may not have the happiness of being\r\nold,\xe2\x80\x9d interposed Everett.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems to me that all the young bloods on this rancho are either on\r\nthe fence or have bolted clean over to the other side, Mr. Darrell,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Mathews, addressing his remarks to the elder Darrell, \xe2\x80\x9cbut they\r\nforget that there aren\xe2\x80\x99t girls enough to go round. There are only two\r\nleft, if, as rumor says, Mr. Clarence has taken the blue-eyed one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRoper says those girls must have done good service in Washington to get\r\nthe appeal dismissed so quick.\xe2\x80\x9d Gasbang said, grinning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd Roper is a dirty-minded dog to say that, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll make him eat his\r\ndirty words, or I\xe2\x80\x99ll take his hide off of his filthy carcass,\xe2\x80\x9d Everett\r\nsaid, jumping up from his seat, livid with anger.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSit still, Retty,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Darrell said, \xe2\x80\x9cnobody minds what Roper says,\r\nexcept, perhaps, in law matters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSome people do mind what the whelp says, as he is quoted here,\xe2\x80\x9d Everett\r\nargued.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt oughtn\xe2\x80\x99t to be so. I don\xe2\x80\x99t like women\xe2\x80\x99s names mixed up in men\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbusiness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRoper only said that, because we heard that those girls were in\r\nWashington with a gay crowd, who took them from New York,\xe2\x80\x9d Gasbang\r\nexplained.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, a crowd who went as guests of Mr. Lawrence Mechlin,\xe2\x80\x9d Everett\r\nreplied; \xe2\x80\x9ca New York banker, and brother of this Mr. Mechlin here. Mr.\r\nMechlin engaged a special car, as George wanted to take his wife and\r\nsister-in-law to visit the capital, and then two other families (of the\r\nhighest and best in New York) were invited, and all made a party to\r\nspend three weeks in Washington. Clarence being a friend of George\r\nMechlin\xe2\x80\x99s, was invited, also.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat may all be, but we heard that the crowd was a gay one, running\r\nabout the corridors and taking lunches at the Capitol with Senators,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nGasbang explained. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd as that is the way things are managed when there\r\nare any axes to grind, Roper guessed that the girls had been pressed\r\ninto service to help with their smiles to bamboozle Senators.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe vile little reptile; I\xe2\x80\x99ll put my heel on him yet,\xe2\x80\x9d said Everett,\r\nwith white lips.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt isn\xe2\x80\x99t likely that Clarence would have stayed by, seeing Mercedes\r\nsmiling improperly on anybody, if he cares for her. He wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be a son\r\nof mine if he did,\xe2\x80\x9d said Darrell, frowning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; that is all a very mean talk of Roper\xe2\x80\x99s. Attorney General Williams\r\nhad promised George Mechlin\xe2\x80\x99s uncle, six months ago, to dismiss the\r\nappeal as soon as the Supreme Court should be session, and, though it\r\ncuts us all to pieces, I must say he kept his word like a man; that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nall.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it was that infernal, dandified puppy George Mechlin, who did the\r\nmischief. I\xe2\x80\x99ll be even with him yet for it,\xe2\x80\x9d Old Mathews growled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t George Mechlin help his father-in-law? Because it upsets\r\nthe liver of the amiable Mr. Mathews?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Romeo, laughing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKeep quiet, Romeo,\xe2\x80\x9d Old Hancock said, smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf George Mechlin hadn\xe2\x80\x99t helped, the thing would have been done in some\r\nother way. It had to come,\xe2\x80\x9d Darrell said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know about that; these Californians are too ignorant to know\r\nhow to defend their rights, and too lazy to try, unless some American\r\nprompts them,\xe2\x80\x9d Mathews replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey know enough to employ a lawyer to defend their rights,\xe2\x80\x9d Old Miller\r\nobserved.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; but, after all, they have to use influence in Washington,\xe2\x80\x9d Old\r\nMathews insisted. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd what influence have they, unless it is by the aid\r\nof some American?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the pretty daughters,\xe2\x80\x9d added Gasbang.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind the pretty daughters,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miller, seeing that Everett\r\nclenched his fists as if ready to pounce upon Gasbang at the next\r\nprovocation. \xe2\x80\x9cThe question now is, what is to be done? and who is for\r\nus, and who against? The time has come when we have to count noses.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, what are you going to do, Mr. Darrell?\xe2\x80\x9d asked velvety Hughes, with\r\nhis sickly smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing. What is there for me to do? You heard me promise to the Don\r\nthat I would pay him for the land I was locating, if it was decided that\r\nthe title was his.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou said _when the title is settled_,\xe2\x80\x9d Gasbang said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe title is settled as far as the Government is concerned. As you\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nsettlers\xe2\x80\x94and the Government were on one side, and the Don on the other,\r\nI guess he now naturally supposes I must regard the title as _settled_,\r\nsince the principal opponent (the Government) has thrown up the sponge,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nDarrell answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we haven\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mathews; \xe2\x80\x9cand as long as we keep up the fight I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t see how the title can be considered settled.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is settled with the Government, which was the question when I made\r\nmy location,\xe2\x80\x9d Darrell answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you ain\xe2\x80\x99t going to desert our cause?\xe2\x80\x9d Hughes asked. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll be our\r\nfriend to the last, won\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuch is my intention, but what I might think I ought to do,\r\ncircumstances will point out to me. Probably we will see our way better\r\nafter the survey is made. Meantime, as the Don don\xe2\x80\x99t trouble any one\r\nwith orders to vacate, the best thing to do is to keep quiet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd spare his cattle,\xe2\x80\x9d Romeo added, looking at Mathews.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou seem to want to pick a quarrel with me, youngster,\xe2\x80\x9d growled\r\nMathews.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat makes you think so? Did _you_ ever shoot any of the Don\xe2\x80\x99s cattle,\r\nthat you should appropriate my remarks to yourself? If you never did, I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t mean you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe boys, the young men, all laughed. Mathews arose, too angry to remain\r\nquiet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNext time I come to talk business\xe2\x80\x94serious business\xe2\x80\x94with men, with men\r\nof my age\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to be twitted by any youngster. Children should\r\nbe seen, and not heard,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, putting on his hat energetically.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Mr. Mathews, you shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t call me a youngster. You forget I am a\r\nmarried man,\xe2\x80\x9d Romeo replied, with great amiability. \xe2\x80\x9cI am a papa, I am.\r\nOur baby is now six months old; he weighed twelve pounds when he was\r\nborn. Now, can you show us a baby of _your own_, only as old as that,\r\nand weigh half as much?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe shout of laughter that followed these words was too much for\r\nMathews. The banging of doors as he left was the only answer he deigned\r\nto give.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Mathews! Five pounds! Two-and-a-half, Mr. Mathews!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Romeo\r\nfrom the window, to the retreating form of Billy, swiftly disappearing\r\nin long strides along the garden walk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is the hardest hit Mathews ever got. He is awfully sensitive about\r\nhaving always been jilted and never been married,\xe2\x80\x9d Miller said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99ll never forgive you,\xe2\x80\x9d added old Hancock.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe never has forgiven me for locating my claim either, but I manage to\r\nsurvive. One more grievance can\xe2\x80\x99t sour him much more,\xe2\x80\x9d Romeo replied,\r\nlaughing.\r\n\r\nAfter Mathews had made his exit, the conversation went on more\r\nharmoniously. Gasbang was now the only malignant spirit present, but\r\nbeing very cowardly, he felt that as Mathews\xe2\x80\x99 support was withdrawn, and\r\nthe other settlers were inclined to abide by Darrell\xe2\x80\x99s advice, he would\r\nbe politic; he would listen only and report to Peter Roper. Gasbang knew\r\nwell how unreliable Roper was, but as they were interested in sundry\r\nenterprises of a doubtful character, he consulted Peter in all matters\r\nwhen found sober.\r\n\r\nDarrell\xe2\x80\x99s advice being to \xe2\x80\x9ckeep quiet,\xe2\x80\x9d the meeting soon broke up and\r\nthe settlers went home by their separate ways, all more or less\r\npersuaded that, after all, peace was the best thing all around. Old Mr.\r\nHancock gave utterance to this sentiment as he stopped by the gate of\r\nthe Darrell garden to say good-night to his neighbors.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI heard the Don say that he does not blame us settlers so much for\r\ntaking his land as he blames our law-givers for those laws which induce\r\nus to do so\xe2\x80\x94laws which are bound to array one class of citizens against\r\nanother class, and set us all by the ears,\xe2\x80\x9d Romeo said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I heard him say about the same thing, but I thought he said it\r\nbecause he was a hypocrite, and to keep us from shooting his cattle,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nGasbang added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo matter what might be his motive, the sentiment is kind anyway,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nHancock, senior, said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps,\xe2\x80\x9d said the others, still unwilling to yield.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIII.\xe2\x80\x94_Home Again._\r\n\r\n\r\nOn the 25th day of May, of \xe2\x80\x9974, Elvira and Mercedes found themselves\r\nagain under the paternal roof of their California home, in the Alamar\r\nrancho. They could have arrived ten days sooner had they left New York\r\non the first of the month, as was first intended. This they were not\r\nallowed to do, because when Mrs. L. Mechlin heard that Mercedes\xe2\x80\x99\r\nbirthday would be on the 5th of May, she immediately said she could not\r\nand would not think of allowing Mercedes to spend her eighteenth\r\nbirthday in the cars. Consequently, invitations would be issued the\r\nfollowing day (which was the 22d of April) for \xe2\x80\x9c_A f\xc3\xaate in celebration\r\nof Miss Alamar\xe2\x80\x99s birthday, on the 5th day of May_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe invitations were issued thus early to prevent friends of Mrs.\r\nMechlin\xe2\x80\x99s from going into the country for the summer, as many of them\r\ndid every year, in May. All, however, accepted, and waited most\r\ngraciously.\r\n\r\nThe season was already too far advanced, and the nights were getting too\r\nwarm to enjoy dancing, so Mrs. Mechlin thought it would be better to\r\nhave an excursion to West Point; to charter a river steamer, and thus\r\npass the day on the water; to take breakfast on board on the way to the\r\nPoint; visit the Post; see the cadets drill and review; and re-embark;\r\ntake dinner on board, and then the young people dance on deck, as there\r\nwould be a full band to give them music.\r\n\r\nThis was the programme\xe2\x80\x94which though decided upon hurriedly, on their\r\nreturn from Baltimore, where they had been visiting\xe2\x80\x94was carried out\r\nsuccessfully. All their pleasure excursions had been equally delightful.\r\nThey had visited Boston first, then they went to Philadelphia, intending\r\nto remain only three or four days, but when they were there, Mrs.\r\nMechlin\xe2\x80\x99s relatives in Baltimore sent urgent invitations to visit them,\r\nso there was nothing else to do but accept. Thus the jaunt to\r\nPhiladelphia was extended to Baltimore, and might have been prolonged,\r\nhad not Elvira refused to be separated from George one day more. This\r\nyoung gentleman, on his part, seemed to have thought, too, that Elvira\r\nhad been away long enough. For as the party were waiting for the train\r\nto move out of the depot who should come aboard but this same young\r\ngentleman, George Mechlin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe darling,\xe2\x80\x9d said Elvira, perfectly overjoyed at the sight of that\r\nbeloved apparition, throwing her arms about his neck.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPrecious,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, clasping her to his heart.\r\n\r\nAnd now Elvira and Mercedes, surrounded by their beloved family, were\r\nrelating this episode and many other occurrences of their eastern visit,\r\nall sitting in their favorite front veranda.\r\n\r\nThe Holman girls were there, too. They had made several visits to\r\nCarlota and Rosario within the last ten months, but this time they came\r\nto see Elvira and Mercedes. Mr. Holman himself had accompanied them,\r\nthat being a good pretext to question George closely regarding Texas\r\nPacific matters. Mr. Holman had invested all his ready money in San\r\nDiego, placing implicit faith in the fact that the building of the Texas\r\nPacific was a measure of national importance so manifest that Congress\r\nwould never have the hardihood to deny it assistance, nor would be so\r\nlacking in sense of honor, sense of justice, as to deprive millions of\r\nAmerican people of a railway so much needed. These had been the reasons,\r\nhe alleged, for plunging headlong into real estate speculations,\r\nfollowed closely by his friends, Don Mariano and Mr. James Mechlin.\r\n\r\nThese three gentlemen now sat at the eastern end of the veranda,\r\nlistening to what George said that he and his uncle had learned in\r\nWashington regarding the prospect of that unlucky railroad; while the\r\nladies were equally entertained, listening to Elvira and Mercedes, on\r\nthe western end of the same veranda.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what has become of the handsome Clarence? Why is he not here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nCorina inquired, seeing Everett and Victoriano riding up with Gabriel\r\ntowards the house.\r\n\r\nElvira informed her that on their arrival at San Francisco, Clarence\r\nfound it necessary to visit his farm, and thence to go to Arizona on\r\nbusiness, but would return about the first of July.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe heard that his mine is in bonanza,\xe2\x80\x9d Amelia said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat it has been in bonanza ever since he bought it\xe2\x80\x94hasn\xe2\x80\x99t he told you\r\nthat?\xe2\x80\x9d Corina added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; he only said that the ore was very rich,\xe2\x80\x9d Elvira replied.\r\n\r\nVictoriano and Everett now came in and took seats near the ladies.\r\nGabriel joined the gentlemen, and soon was deeply interested in their\r\nconversation, it of course being upon that subject\xe2\x80\x94the railroad\xe2\x80\x94which\r\nfilled the minds and hearts of all the San Diego people, absorbing all\r\ntheir faculties and all their money.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow are all the ladies of your family? Well?\xe2\x80\x9d Amelia asked of Everett.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, thank you. They are all well, and I think they will be up this\r\nevening\xe2\x80\x94at least, some of them will. I heard words to that effect,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nEverett replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope all will come,\xe2\x80\x9d Elvira said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat? Mr. Darrell, senior, also?\xe2\x80\x9d Corina asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly. Why not?\xe2\x80\x9d Mercedes answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe were speaking of the ladies\xe2\x80\x94but if Mr. Darrell should call, we will\r\nbe happy to receive him with sincere cordiality,\xe2\x80\x9d Elvira added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll of which would be thrown away upon the stiffest neck in San Diego\r\nCounty,\xe2\x80\x9d Victoriano observed.\r\n\r\nEverett laughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Tano! What makes you talk like that?\xe2\x80\x9d Mercedes exclaimed,\r\nreddening with evident annoyance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause his \xe2\x80\x98_butt-headedness_\xe2\x80\x99 is like that of a vicious old mule,\r\nwhich no one began to break until he was ten years old, and loves to\r\nkick from pure cussedness,\xe2\x80\x9d Victoriano explained, with free use of\r\nslang.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf Mr. Darrell has said or done anything to vex you, the best thing is\r\nnot to go to his house, but it is not very courteous to speak as you\r\nhave in the presence of his son,\xe2\x80\x9d Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI forgive him,\xe2\x80\x9d Everett said, patting Tano on the back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot go to his house!\xe2\x80\x9d Tano exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is exactly what the old\r\npirate wants. It would be _nuts_ for the old Turk if I stayed away. Not\r\nmuch\xe2\x80\x94I won\xe2\x80\x99t stay away. I\xe2\x80\x99ll go when he is at _the colony_ with his\r\nsweetly-scented pets.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is the colony?\xe2\x80\x9d Mercedes asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is the new name for the large room next to the dining-room, which\r\nClarence said he built for a \xe2\x80\x98growlery.\xe2\x80\x99 Alice called it the\r\n\xe2\x80\x98_squattery_,\xe2\x80\x99 because father always receives the settlers there; but\r\nmother changed the name to \xe2\x80\x98_colony_\xe2\x80\x99 to make it less offensive, and\r\nbecause the talk there is always about locating, or surveying, or\r\nfencing land\xe2\x80\x94always land\xe2\x80\x94as it would be in a new colony,\xe2\x80\x9d Everett\r\nexplained.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhether he be at the colony or not, you should not go if he does not\r\nwish you to visit his house,\xe2\x80\x9d Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa said to Tano.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we all wish it\xe2\x80\x94my mother and every one of her children. Father\r\ndoesn\xe2\x80\x99t say anything about Tano\xe2\x80\x99s coming or not, but he is cross to all\r\nof us, and don\xe2\x80\x99t have the politeness to be more amiable in Tano\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npresence\xe2\x80\x94which, of course, is very disagreeable,\xe2\x80\x9d Everett replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think Mrs. Darrell ought to put her foot down, and have it out with\r\nthe old filibuster,\xe2\x80\x9d Tano asserted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will see what he will do when Clarence comes,\xe2\x80\x9d Everett said.\r\n\r\nEverett thought as all the family did\xe2\x80\x94that Clarence, being the favorite\r\nchild of the old man, and having naturally a winning manner and great\r\namiability, combined with persuasiveness, would influence his father,\r\nand dispel his bad humor. But if the family had known what was boiling\r\nand seething in the cauldron of their father\xe2\x80\x99s mind, they would have\r\nperceived that, for once, neither Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s influence, nor yet the more\r\npowerful one wielded by Mrs. Darrell, would at present be as effective\r\nas they heretofore had been.\r\n\r\nTime alone must be the agent to operate on that hard skull. Time and\r\ncircumstances which, fortunately, no one as yet was misanthrophic enough\r\nto foresee. The fact was, that no one of his family had understood\r\nWilliam Darrell. It can hardly be said that he understood himself, for\r\nhe sincerely believed that he had forever renounced his \xe2\x80\x9c_squatting_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\npropensities, and honestly promised his wife that he would not take up\r\nland claimed by any one else. But no sooner was he surrounded by men\r\nwho, though his inferiors, talked loudly in assertion of their \xe2\x80\x9c_rights\r\nunder the law_;\xe2\x80\x9d and no sooner had he thousands of broad acres before\r\nhis eyes\xe2\x80\x94acres which, by obeying the laws of Congress, he could make his\r\nown\xe2\x80\x94than he again felt within him the old squatter of Sonoma and Napa\r\nvalleys. That mischievous squatter had not lain dead therein; he had\r\nbeen slumbering only, and unconsciously dreaming of the advantages that\r\nthe law really gave to settlers. Alongside the sleeping squatter had\r\nalso slumbered Darrell\xe2\x80\x99s vanity, and this was, as it is generally in\r\nevery man, the strongest quality of his mind, the chief commanding\r\ntrait, before which everything must give way.\r\n\r\nMrs. Darrell had heretofore been the only will that had dared stand\r\nbefore it, but Mrs. Darrell, being a wise little woman, not always made\r\ndirect assaults upon the strong citadel\xe2\x80\x94oftener she made flank movements\r\nand laid sieges. This time, however, all tactics had thus far failed,\r\nand Mrs. Darrell withdrew all her forces, and waited, in \xe2\x80\x9cmasterly\r\ninactivity,\xe2\x80\x9d reinforcements when Clarence returned.\r\n\r\nWhat exasperated Darrell the most, and had ended by putting him in a bad\r\nhumor, was a lurking self-reproach he could not silence, a consciousness\r\nthat having promised Don Mariano to pay for his land whenever the title\r\nwas considered settled, that it was fair to suppose he ought to pay now.\r\nBut on the other hand, he had also promised the settlers to stand by\r\nthem, and was determined to do so. Thus he stood in his own mind\r\nself-accused, unhappy and unrepentant, but resolutely upholding a lost\r\ncause. He avoided the society of his family with absurd persistency.\r\nAfter meals he would fill his pipe, and march himself off to the farther\r\nend of his grain fields; resting his elbows on the fence boards, and\r\nturning his back upon the house which contained his dissenting family,\r\nwould puff his smoke in high dudgeon, like an overturned locomotive\r\nwhich had run off its track, and became hopelessly ditched. In that\r\nframe of mind, he thought himself ready to do battle against all his\r\nfamily, but he knew he dreaded Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s return.\r\n\r\nHowever, that event had at last arrived, and there was Clarence now on\r\nthe porch\xe2\x80\x94just come from Arizona\xe2\x80\x94kissing all the ladies of the family\r\nand hugging all the males, not omitting the old man, who was literally\r\nas well as figuratively taken off his feet by the strong arms of the\r\ndreaded Clarence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClary is so much in love, father, that he comes courting you, too,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nEverett said, laughing, as they all went into the parlor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose so,\xe2\x80\x9d Darrell answered, not looking at any one\xe2\x80\x99s face,\r\nexcepting that of the clock on the chimney mantel.\r\n\r\nMrs. Darrel\xe2\x80\x99s eyes, however, were not in the least evasive\xe2\x80\x94they met\r\nthose of Clarence, and he read in them a volume of what was troubling\r\nhis father\xe2\x80\x99s mind. He longed to have a talk with that true-hearted and\r\nclear-headed, well-beloved mother, but he must wait\xe2\x80\x94for now came Tisha\r\nto announce that luncheon was on the table. She was grinning with\r\ndelight to see her favorite Massa Clary again, and Clarence jumped up\r\nand ran to throw his arms around her, making that faithful heart throb\r\nwith unalloyed happiness, for she loved him from his babyhood, just if\r\nhe had been her own child.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI love them all, missis\xe2\x80\x94all your dear children,\xe2\x80\x9d she would say to Mrs.\r\nDarrell; \xe2\x80\x9cand they are all good children; but Massa Clary I love the\r\nbest of all. Next comes Miss Alice. But Massa Clary took my heart when\r\nhe was six months old, and had the measles. He was the best, sweetest\r\nbaby I ever saw, and so beautiful.\xe2\x80\x9d Thus Tisha would run on, if you let\r\nher follow the bent of her inclination, for Clarence was a theme she\r\nnever tired of.\r\n\r\nAll sorts of questions now showered upon Clarence about New York, about\r\nWashington, about San Francisco, and about Arizona\xe2\x80\x94all of which he\r\nanswered most amiably.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd are the Mechlins very grand? As rich as one might suppose? hearing\r\nthe Holman and Alamar girls talk of the parties and excursions that Mr.\r\nLawrence Mechlin gave in honor of Elvira?\xe2\x80\x9d Jane asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe excursion to West Point was to celebrate Mercedes\xe2\x80\x99 birthday,\xe2\x80\x9d Alice\r\nobserved.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, the Mechlins must be rich, to judge by their style of living.\r\nTheir social position is certainly very high,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou had a delightful time, Clary?\xe2\x80\x9d Everett said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, indeed; most delightful,\xe2\x80\x9d was the answer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe, too, have had lots of fun, with old Mathews on the rampage, like an\r\nold hen who got wet and lost her only chicken,\xe2\x80\x9d said Willie, at the top\r\nof his voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWillie!\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Darrell said, to impose silence, but as Clarence and\r\nEverett laughed, and his father did not seem particularly displeased,\r\nWillie added:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the old man gets so mad, that he perspires, and smoke comes out of\r\nhis back, as if his clothes were on fire.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Willie! how you exaggerate,\xe2\x80\x9d Lucy exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t. He snorts and clucks and growls and snarls. Romeo says he\r\nmiauls like a disappointed hyena.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat will do. You must not repeat such unkind criticisms. Romeo is\r\nalways ridiculing Mr. Mathews,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Darrell said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOld Mathews is in worse humor since the Don began to send his cattle\r\naway,\xe2\x80\x9d Webster said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy so?\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause they made nice targets for his rifle,\xe2\x80\x9d Everett replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cScandalous!\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe threatens to shoot George Mechlin, Tom Hughes says,\xe2\x80\x9d Webster added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause he got the appeal dismissed,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Webster.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is foolish to suppose that if George hadn\xe2\x80\x99t had it dismissed that no\r\none else would,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI met the old man this morning. He stopped his wagon to ask me if\r\nfather knew that Congress had passed the appropriation for money to\r\nsurvey lands in California. I told him I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t heard, and he went off\r\nwhipping his horses, and swearing at Don Mariano and George Mechlin,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nEverett said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought there would be a better feeling when the Don\xe2\x80\x99s cattle should\r\nbe sent off, as they were the principal cause of irritation,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence\r\nobserved.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd it is so. Only those boys\xe2\x80\x94Romeo, Tom and Jack Miller\xe2\x80\x94are always\r\nridiculing or teasing Mathews,\xe2\x80\x9d Darrell said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, father!\xe2\x80\x9d Everett exclaimed; \xe2\x80\x9cthe fathers of those boys are as bad\r\nas Mathews, and old Gasbang is worse yet!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGasbang was always dishonest, but he is worse now, at Peter Roper\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ninstigation,\xe2\x80\x9d Darrell said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGasbang says that he and Roper will send the Don to the poor-house,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nEverett said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot while I live,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence replied; adding, \xe2\x80\x9cand how is everybody at\r\nthe Alamar house\xe2\x80\x94all well?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nUp started Willie and Clementine, eager to be the first to tell Clarence\r\nthe great news.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey had two arrivals,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Darrell said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Clary! you never saw prettier babies in all your life! Both have\r\nthe loveliest blue eyes,\xe2\x80\x9d Clementine exclaimed, joining her hands, as if\r\nin prayer, as Tisha always did when speaking of Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s babyhood.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe boy has gray eyes,\xe2\x80\x9d Willie interposed, with authority not to be\r\ncontroverted. \xe2\x80\x9cHe hasn\xe2\x80\x99t no blue eyes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you know? You haven\xe2\x80\x99t seen them, but _I_ have,\xe2\x80\x9d Clementine\r\nasserted; \xe2\x80\x9cand the little girl is exactly the image of Miss Mercedes.\r\nShe has Miss Mercedes\xe2\x80\x99 blue eyes, exactly, with long, curling lashes,\r\nthe little thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe girl looks like Don Gabriel, as she ought to,\xe2\x80\x9d Willie stated in a\r\nperemptory manner, not to be contradicted, and whilst he discussed with\r\nClementine the looks of the babies, Clarence was informed by his mother\r\nand sisters that Elvira was the happy mother of a big, handsome boy, and\r\nLizzie rejoiced in the possession of a beautiful little girl, which\r\nweighed nearly as much as her boy cousin. That Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa and Mrs.\r\nBeatrice Mechlin were nearly crazy with happiness, but that the craziest\r\nof all was Mr. James Mechlin, who made more \xe2\x80\x9c_fuss_\xe2\x80\x9d over those two\r\nbabies than either Gabriel or George, and went from one house to the\r\nother all day long, watching each baby, and talking about them by the\r\nhour.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIV.\xe2\x80\x94_The Brewers of Mischief._\r\n\r\n\r\nEight delicious weeks passed\xe2\x80\x94the most delightful that Clarence and\r\nMercedes had ever lived. The first of September had dawned, and on the\r\n16th they would be married. With the first rays of the coming morn,\r\nClarence arose and went to the west window of his chamber, which looked\r\ntowards the Alamar House. As he peeped through the closed shutters,\r\nthinking it would seem foolish to open them so early, he saw the\r\nshutters of one window\xe2\x80\x94in that well known row where Mercedes\xe2\x80\x99 room was\r\nlocated, and which looked to the east\xe2\x80\x94pushed open, and a white hand and\r\npart of a white arm came out and fastened it back. His heart told him\r\nwhose white arm that was, and of course he could not think of going back\r\nto bed. He began to dress himself, deliberating whether he should or not\r\ngo to town that day and telegraph to Hubert to do as he thought best\r\nabout selling another cargo of ores, or say to wait for him, that he\r\nwould be at San Francisco on the 20th. When he was dressed, he sat by\r\nthe west window and tried to read, but that white arm would come across\r\nthe page and that white hand would cover the letters, so that he threw\r\nthe book down and began to walk, trying to think about that business of\r\nselling the ore to the Austrian house, of which Hubert had been writing\r\nto him. Yes, he thought, the best thing would be to go to town that same\r\nday and ask Hubert couldn\xe2\x80\x99t the matter wait until the 20th. But should\r\nHubert be coming, or should it be necessary to wait for telegrams, he\r\nmight not be back until the following day in the evening. He would go\r\nimmediately after breakfast to tell Mercedes that he could not see her\r\nthat evening.\r\n\r\nMercedes and Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa were on the front piazza when he arrived, and\r\nGabriel was talking to George in quite an excited manner, for him, as he\r\nwas always so calm and self-contained. As soon as Clarence came up the\r\npiazza steps, George began to tell him that some of the last lot of\r\ncattle which had been sent off to the mountains, had got away from the\r\nherders and returned to the rancho on the previous day, and that morning\r\na couple of cows of a very choice breed were found shot through the\r\nbody, in a dying condition. The poor brutes had to be shot dead by\r\nGabriel himself, to save them from further suffering. No one knew who\r\nhad fired on the poor dumb animals, but circumstantial evidence clearly\r\npointed to Old Mathews.\r\n\r\nClarence was very angry, of course. He reflected in silence for a few\r\nmoments, then said to Gabriel:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think if Don Mariano would make now, to-day, a deed of sale of _all_\r\nhis cattle and horses to me, they would have a better chance of being\r\nspared. Not that Mathews, or Gasbang, or Miller like me any better, but\r\nthey are not so anxious to annoy me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s idea is a good one,\xe2\x80\x9d George said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think so, too, and have thought so for some time,\xe2\x80\x9d Gabriel replied.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are going to drive off the last lot to-day. Father and Tano are down\r\nin the valley. I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell him what you say as soon as I go down. I think\r\nwe will return by to-morrow night, and he can draw up the deed then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell him that I shall consider that the cattle are mine _now_, and will\r\nlet our friends, the settlers, know it, so that they can have the\r\nsatisfaction of killing _my cattle_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you really mean it?\xe2\x80\x9d Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly. Don Mariano can buy all the cattle he wants to restock his\r\nrancho after he gets rid of the two-legged animals,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is, if he wants to restock it. He was talking with George and me\r\nlast night, and he said if the Texas Pacific is built, he will have all\r\nhis land surveyed to sell it in farming lots, and will not put cattle in\r\nit. But if the railroad is not built, then the best use he can make of\r\nthe rancho will be to make it a cattle rancho again, after the squatters\r\ngo away,\xe2\x80\x9d Gabriel said, adding that he must be going to join his father.\r\nHe then went into the hall to go to the court-yard, where his saddled\r\nhorse and his _vaquero_ waited for him. Clarence and George followed to\r\nbid him good-by. Clarence said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wrote to Hubert about procuring for you a place at a bank, to get\r\nbroken into the banking business, and he replied that he can, and will\r\nget you a place. Would you like to try it, now that you will have less\r\nto do here, when there will be no cattle at the rancho? I am going to\r\nwrite and telegraph to Hubert to-day\xe2\x80\x94or he might be down in to-morrow\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsteamer\xe2\x80\x94so that I can tell him about what time you might go up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you had better go about the time Clarence and Mercedes get\r\nmarried, as they will immediately go to their house in San Francisco,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nGeorge suggested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I think that will be the best time,\xe2\x80\x9d Gabriel said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well; I\xe2\x80\x99ll write to Hubert that we will be up by the 20th of this\r\nmonth,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGabriel can take his place on the 1st of October. That will do\r\nsplendidly, as Lizzie and Mercedes will be together,\xe2\x80\x9d George said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we must live in the hope that we will all come down to make our\r\nhomes here,\xe2\x80\x9d Gabriel added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course. That is understood,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThough at times I feel discouraged, still, I can\xe2\x80\x99t well see how the\r\nTexas Pacific is to be defeated permanently. That would be too\r\noutrageous. Let us hope that by next year our banking scheme will be\r\ncarried out,\xe2\x80\x9d George said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope so, and as I have made more money than I had when we first\r\ntalked about it we can put in more capital. We can, if you advise it,\r\nput in a whole million now,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo much the better,\xe2\x80\x9d George said, and both shook hands with Gabriel,\r\nwho quickly jumped on his horse and was off at a gallop, followed by his\r\n_vaquero_.\r\n\r\nIt was the hour when the babies got their morning bath. George had great\r\npleasure in seeing his boy enjoy the sensation of floating in the water;\r\nso he let Clarence return to the porch where Mercedes was now alone, and\r\nhe went to watch the bathing of his boy.\r\n\r\nClarence sat close to Mercedes and said: \xe2\x80\x9cDoes the sweetest thing that\r\nGod created realize that this day is the first day of September?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you mean me, though you make me feel very foolish with your\r\nexaggerated praise, I must say that I do realize that to-day is the\r\nfirst of September,\xe2\x80\x9d she replied, smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd does the loveliest rosebud and the prettiest hummingbird remember\r\nthat in two weeks more she is to be mine, mine forever?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHush, Clarence, some one might hear you,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, putting her hand\r\nover his lips, blushing and looking around, alarmed. He took that hand\r\nand kissed the palm of it, then turned it over and kissed the back of it\r\nmost ardently, and held it in his own, saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have a piece of information that is going to make your dear heart\r\nglad. What will you give for it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it? Do tell me. Is it about papa?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, but it is about Gabriel and Lizzie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat Gabriel will get a place at a San Francisco bank to learn the\r\nbanking business, and they will live with us, so you and Lizzie will be\r\ntogether.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! Clarence, is that so? Oh! you make me so glad! How can I ever thank\r\nyou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHaven\xe2\x80\x99t you said that you love me? Haven\xe2\x80\x99t you promised to marry me,\r\nand thus make me the happiest man upon the entire face of all this\r\nearth? That is enough for thanks. But for telling you the news I want to\r\nbe paid _extra_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMercedes blushed crimson.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am going to town now, to be away a long time; won\xe2\x80\x99t you give me one\r\nsingle kiss to say good-by?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMust you go? Why don\xe2\x80\x99t you write your letters or telegrams and send\r\nthem from here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I may have to answer some dispatches immediately. Or it is\r\npossible that Hubert might have run down to see me for a few hours.\r\nTo-morrow is steamer day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen this will be a good chance to send up your photograph I want to\r\nhave enlarged and painted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; give it to me; I\xe2\x80\x99ll send it up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll bring it,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, going to the parlor. He followed her. He\r\nclosed the door, saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, one sweet kiss to give me good luck and bring me back all safe.\r\nP-l-e-a-s-e don\xe2\x80\x99t refuse it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Clarence! Mamma don\xe2\x80\x99t approve of such things, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t either.\r\nYou are not my husband yet,\xe2\x80\x9d she pleaded, but in vain, for he had put\r\nhis arm around her and was holding her close to his heart.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not your husband yet? Yes I am. In intention I have been ever\r\nsince January, 1872. More than two years, and, in fact, I shall be in\r\ntwo weeks. So you see how cruel it is to be so distant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you call this distant, holding me so close?\xe2\x80\x9d For sole answer he\r\nlooked into her eyes, kissed her forehead and blushing cheeks, then he\r\nkissed the heavily fringed eyelids, kept partly closed, afraid to meet\r\nthe radiant gaze of his expressive eyes. Then he put his lips to hers\r\nand held them there in a long kiss of the purest, truest love. \xe2\x80\x9cMy\r\ndarling! My wife! My own for ever! The sweetest, loveliest angel of my\r\nsoul!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNo doubt he would have been willing to hold her thus close to his heart\r\nfor hours, but she disengaged herself from his embrace with gentle\r\nfirmness. Such warm caresses she intuitively felt must be improper in\r\nthe highest degree, even on the eve of marriage. No lady could allow\r\nthem without surrendering her dignity. That was the effect of Do\xc3\xb1a\r\nJosefa\xe2\x80\x99s doctrines, which she had carefully inculcated into the minds of\r\nher daughters.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I hope that at last you have kissed me enough,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mercedes,\r\nrather resentfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever enough, but I hope sufficiently to give me good luck,\xe2\x80\x9d answered\r\nthe happy Clarence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Clarence, that reminds me of my horrible dream of last night. I\r\ndreamed that papa went to look for you in the midst of a snow storm and\r\nnever came back. You returned, but he never did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must not believe in dreams, dearest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not, but this seemed prophetic to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cProphetic of a snow storm in San Diego?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe snow was symbolic of bereavement, perhaps.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe rested her head on his shoulder and seemed lost in thought, and he\r\nheld the little hand, so soft and white and well shaped, and thought of\r\nher beauty and lovely qualities and his coming happiness. He was\r\nthinking that he would have been content to pass the day thus, when she\r\nraised her eyes to his, saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must not keep you if you must go. Remember how superstitious my dream\r\nhas made me. I wish you could wait until to-morrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would, but Hubert might come to-morrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had forgotten that.\xe2\x80\x9d One more long kiss and they parted, her heart\r\nsinking under a load of undefined terrors.\r\n\r\nFrom the seventh heaven Clarence had to come down again to prosaic\r\nearth; and after bidding adieu to Mercedes, he drove back home to speak\r\nto his father. The old man was sitting in his easy chair on the porch,\r\nsmoking his pipe, alone, behind the curtain of honeysuckle, white\r\njasmine and roses, so carefully trained over the porch by Mrs. Darrell\r\nand Alice. Seeing his son driving back towards the front steps, he\r\nwalked down to meet him. Clarence was glad that he seemed in a better\r\nhumor. He at once said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFather, I came back to ask a favor of you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA favor? You alarm me. You never did that in all your life,\xe2\x80\x9d he said,\r\nsmiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean I never did anything else. I know it. But this is a very\r\nespecial one, and a business favor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us hear it. Of course I\xe2\x80\x99ll do anything I can for you or any other\r\nof my children.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThanks, father. The favor is this. That in talking with the\r\nsettlers\xe2\x80\x94especially those who have been most ready to shoot the Don\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncattle\xe2\x80\x94that you tell them I have bought all his stock and all will be\r\ndriven to the Colorado river just as soon as cold weather sets in. I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t think many of the settlers like me any better than they like the\r\nDon, but if they think they might displease _you_ by killing your son\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncattle they might spare the poor animals.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll do it. I expect Mathews and Miller now. They sent me word they are\r\ncoming to bring me some special news as soon as Gasbang returns from\r\ntown. But have you really and truly bought the stock? or is it only to\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have made a bona fide purchase; five hundred head are already at the\r\nmine, and as soon as the hot weather is over, the others will follow. I\r\nmust buy cattle somewhere, for we have to feed five hundred men now at\r\nwork, and as the Don is losing his all the time, I proposed to him to\r\nsell all to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what is he to do with his land? Queer that he should sell his\r\ncattle when he gets his land. Doesn\xe2\x80\x99t he believe he\xe2\x80\x99ll get rid of us\xe2\x80\x94the\r\n_squatters_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO yes, but he figures thus: If the Texas Pacific is built, it will pay\r\nbetter to sell his land in farming lots; if not, he can restock it when\r\nhe gets rid of his troublesome neighbors.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has more sense than I gave him credit for. I guess you put him up to\r\nthat dodge.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo indeed. He thought it himself, but it seems that Gabriel and George\r\nthought the same thing at the same time, and as I was thinking where I\r\ncould get cattle for my mines, it struck me I might buy his and suit us\r\nboth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right. I\xe2\x80\x99ll speak to the settlers, but of course I cannot promise\r\nthat they will do what I ask.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI understand that. Many thanks. Good-by.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen will you return?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo-morrow,\xe2\x80\x9d and he was off at a tearing speed for his horses were tired\r\nof waiting, and longed to be on the road.\r\n\r\nThere was a little _arroyo_ which passed about 500 yards on the west of\r\nDon Mariano\xe2\x80\x99s house and marked the west line of Darrell\xe2\x80\x99s land; as\r\nClarence approached this dried brook, he saw Gasbang and Roper coming\r\ndown from the opposite hill, evidently unable to check their horses.\r\nRoper was so intoxicated that he could with difficulty keep his seat,\r\nand as Gasbang seemed much frightened, Clarence took his ph\xc3\xa6ton well off\r\nthe road and waited so as to lend his assistance, if it should be\r\nrequired. But \xe2\x80\x9cthe kind Providence which takes care of drunken sailors,\r\nchildren and the United States,\xe2\x80\x9d was watchful of Roper, and though he\r\nswayed and swung beyond possible equilibrium, he stuck to his seat with\r\ndrunken gravity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGoing to invest in more real estate?\xe2\x80\x9d Gasbang shouted as soon as he\r\nfelt reassured by passing the great danger of sand and pebbles which his\r\ncowardice had magnified to him into a precipice. Roper laughed heartily,\r\nbut Clarence, not understanding the allusion, made no answer and drove\r\non without looking at them. If a kind fairy could have whispered to him\r\nwhat was the errand of these two men, he most assuredly would have\r\nturned back. There being no fairy but the blue-eyed one who had already\r\ntold her dreams and fears, which he had not believed, he went on to\r\ntown, and Gasbang took Roper to his house, carefully putting him to bed\r\nto take a nap that would sober him before he spoke with Darrell; for it\r\nwas to speak with Darrell that he came.\r\n\r\nWhile Roper slept, Gasbang went to see Mathews, Miller and Hughes, and\r\ntogether they held a consultation, at the end of which it was decided\r\nthat, as Roper was too intoxicated yet, and Darrell disliked drunkards,\r\nthey would go and have a preliminary talk with him themselves, and Roper\r\nwould be pressed into service, if advisable, in the morning, when he\r\nwould be sober.\r\n\r\nDarrell had got tired of waiting for Mathews; so, after thinking of what\r\nClarence had said, he decided that it would be better to have a talk\r\nwith Hancock and Pitikin, who were about the most reliable of all the\r\nsettlers. They perhaps knew what it was that Mathews had to say. He told\r\nWebster to saddle a horse and bring it around; he would go on horseback,\r\nas the wagon road to Hancock\xe2\x80\x99s was very long, around the fields.\r\n\r\nBut now when Webster had brought the saddled horse to the front steps,\r\nDarrell saw Mathews, Gasbang, Miller and Hughes coming in a two-seated\r\nwagon, and all seemed to be talking very excitedly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTie the horse there. I\xe2\x80\x99ll wait for those men,\xe2\x80\x9d said Darrell, sitting\r\ndown again. Webster did as he was told, and then walked straight\r\nup-stairs to his mother\xe2\x80\x99s room. Everett and Alice were with her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMother, if I were you, I would go and sit in the parlor and do my\r\nsewing there by the windows on the piazza, while those bad men are\r\ntalking to father,\xe2\x80\x9d Webster said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Webster, go and listen unseen!\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Darrell exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly, and do it quickly, for those old imps mean mischief to\r\nClarence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo Clarence!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Alice and her mother at the same time.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, mother, Web. might be right. You might just be in time to unmask\r\nsome lie against Clary,\xe2\x80\x9d Everett suggested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne thing is sure, that those men already have too much influence over\r\nfather, and we have done nothing to oppose it,\xe2\x80\x9d Alice said. Mrs. Darrell\r\nwas silent, then, looking at her children, said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou might be right, my children, but that would not justify my\r\nlistening at the keyhole.\xe2\x80\x9d Everett shrugged his shoulders, saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, mother. Come on, Web.\xe2\x80\x9d And both boys left the room. When\r\nthey were out, Everett said: \xe2\x80\x9cWeb, get a horse saddled and tie him at\r\nthe back porch for me. I am going to listen from Jane\xe2\x80\x99s room; one of her\r\nwindows is right over those men. If what I hear makes it necessary for\r\nme to see Clarence, I shall go to town. Get a horse saddled immediately\r\nand come to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXV.\xe2\x80\x94_The Squatter and the Don._\r\n\r\n\r\nEverett found Lucy in Jane\xe2\x80\x99s room. Both were sewing by the window he\r\nwanted. He squeezed himself into a seat between his sisters, saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGirls, I want to hear. Hush! Listen!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nVoices were heard below. All listened. As Webster was coming down stairs\r\nhe saw John Gasbang going out at the other end of the parlor, pushing a\r\nlarge arm-chair out upon the porch. He heard them laughing at some of\r\nGasbang\xe2\x80\x99s coarse, vulgar jokes, and then all sat down. After some\r\ndesultory talk, Mathews, evidently anxious to begin at what they had to\r\nstate, said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid, neighbor Darrell, that somebody has been fooling you and\r\nlaughing at you, or if not, then the thing will look as if _you\r\nyourself_ had been fooling us and laughing at us. This we can hardly\r\nbelieve.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe don\xe2\x80\x99t believe at all,\xe2\x80\x9d Hughes explained, seeing Darrell\xe2\x80\x99s brow\r\ndarken.\r\n\r\nJane\xe2\x80\x99s window was possessed of most favorable acoustic qualities. Every\r\nword could be distinctly heard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t understand you,\xe2\x80\x9d says Darrell, gruffly. \xe2\x80\x9cI am not given to\r\njoking or laughing much, and I never knew that anybody dared to laugh at\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPrecisely!\xe2\x80\x9d Hughes exclaimed, bowing deferentially.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you ever give us to understand that this land you occupy you had\r\nbought and paid for?\xe2\x80\x9d Mathews asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. I said to the Don I would pay when the title is settled; that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nall. You all heard that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you never paid him any money?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a red cent. But see here, what do you mean by coming here to\r\ncatechise _me_? You don\xe2\x80\x99t know William Darrell, if you think you can set\r\na trap for him in this way. I tell you he would kick your trap to the\r\nold \xe2\x80\x98Nick\xe2\x80\x99 in two minutes,\xe2\x80\x9d Darrell said, getting white with anger.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know it,\xe2\x80\x9d Gasbang said, shaking with laughter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStop your nonsense,\xe2\x80\x9d Darrell said to him; then to Mathews, \xe2\x80\x9cSpeak out\r\nlike a man\xe2\x80\x94what is it all about?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat we are told that it is recorded that you paid six thousand four\r\nhundred dollars to the Don for six hundred and forty acres of land,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nMiller explained.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen did I do that?\xe2\x80\x9d Darrell asked, with increasing pallor, the sight\r\nof which his interlocutor did not relish.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn the 13th day of February, 1872,\xe2\x80\x9d Gasbang replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho saw the record?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did. I saw the entry made by the notary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, the notary lies, that\xe2\x80\x99s all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe couldn\xe2\x80\x99t. He could be prosecuted for it,\xe2\x80\x9d Miller said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, he shall be; for it is a lie that I bought any such land or\r\npaid any such price for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps the Don had the entry made,\xe2\x80\x9d Hughes suggested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen the Don lied, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell him so,\xe2\x80\x9d Darrell retorted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you sure that Mr. Clarence did not buy the land unbeknown to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nMathews asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean, sir? Do you mean to say that my own son would put me\r\nin such a ridiculous position? No, I think it is more likely that, as\r\nHughes says, the Don had the false entry made on the strength of my\r\nhaving promised to pay him for the land I would take. But I\xe2\x80\x99ll teach him\r\nthat I am not to be dragged into a bargain like that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat would the Don gain by that? Nothing. No, if you will not be\r\noffended, I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you what I heard is the most likely theory of it\r\nall,\xe2\x80\x9d Gasbang began.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd who in the devil has been building theories on my affairs? I\xe2\x80\x99d like\r\nto see the fellow who does that,\xe2\x80\x9d thundered Darrell.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was my lawyer, who casually discovered that entry in the notary\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbooks, and told me of it. He meant no harm,\xe2\x80\x9d Gasbang explained, with\r\nconciliatory amiability.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course, he meant no harm. Lawyers of the Peter Roper stamp never do.\r\nWhen they go sticking their noses into people\xe2\x80\x99s business, they do so\r\n_casually_. And your lawyer\xe2\x80\x94Mr. Roper, I suppose\xe2\x80\x94being a very innocent\r\nand straightforward and honorable, high-toned man, who never gets drunk,\r\nhe did not mean any harm, and _accidentally_, purely so, made this\r\ndiscovery, and no danger of his having been too drunk to read straight,\r\neither. Look here, John, don\xe2\x80\x99t you talk to me as if you thought me\r\nidiotic, for I am not. But what is this innocent theory of this\r\nunsophisticated, honorable Peter Roper? Let us see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, he thinks that Mr. Clarence being in love with the Don\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndaughter, probably bought the land to propitiate the family, and dated\r\nback the deed of sale,\xe2\x80\x9d Gasbang said.\r\n\r\nDarrell was silent, but shook his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see, the Don could have had no object in putting on record that he\r\nhad received six thousand four hundred dollars, unless he did so,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMiller.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo you think he received the money?\xe2\x80\x9d Darrell asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo doubt of it,\xe2\x80\x9d all the others answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere he goes now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hughes, and all could see the Don riding\r\ntowards home, accompanied by his two sons. Behind them the _vaqueros_\r\nwere driving a lot of cattle towards the \xe2\x80\x9c_corral_\xe2\x80\x9d at the back of the\r\nhouse.\r\n\r\nSeeing the cattle, Darrell said: \xe2\x80\x9cBy the way, these cattle now belong to\r\nClarence. He bought every head on this rancho belonging to the Don, and\r\nwill drive them to the Colorado River as soon as the weather cools off.\r\nSo I hope that if any stray cows or calves come up to your places you\r\nwill corral them and send _me_ word. I ask this of all of you, as a\r\nfavor to me, not to Clarence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly! certainly!\xe2\x80\x9d said Hughes, Miller and Gasbang.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill it be too much trouble to do that?\xe2\x80\x9d Darrell asked Mathews, who had\r\nremained silent.\r\n\r\nHe was compelled to reply: \xe2\x80\x9cOf course not\xe2\x80\x94not for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you see, I ask only what the law gives.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd Clarence knows that if his cattle go to your fields you must corral\r\nthem and give him notice. And now I want to go and speak with the Don.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAll arose.\r\n\r\nHughes said: \xe2\x80\x9cAs we all wish to know more about that land sale, we will\r\ncome back this evening to hear what the Don says.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well. I am going to ask all he knows about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe knows everything, the greaser!\xe2\x80\x9d Mathews growled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you think Clarence paid the money?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Darrell.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course he did, to get the girl,\xe2\x80\x9d laughed Gasbang; then added: \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\nwas all a put-up job, and they kept the secret well, so we never smelled\r\nthe rat, while they laughed at us. But I don\xe2\x80\x99t care so long as you, Mr.\r\nDarrell, wasn\xe2\x80\x99t in it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo says I,\xe2\x80\x9d added Miller.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hughes, and they drove off, laughing.\r\n\r\nDarrell remained standing on the front steps. He ground his teeth and\r\nclenched his fists as he heard the laughter from the wagon, which\r\nsounded louder as the wagon went further away. He walked to the stable\r\nand took a heavy whip, one of those which teamsters call \xe2\x80\x9cblack snakes,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nwhich are used to drive mules with. The old man trembled with suppressed\r\nanger, so much that he could not fasten on his spurs, and this only\r\nincreased the more his senseless rage.\r\n\r\nEverett was scarcely less angry or less pale. He was waiting for his\r\nfather to start, to follow him. Webster came up-stairs and said to him:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRetty, father means mischief. He has a \xe2\x80\x98black snake,\xe2\x80\x99 and trembles with\r\nrage as if he had the ague.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor father, how unfortunate it is that he got into such a wrong train\r\nof reasoning,\xe2\x80\x9d Jane said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is bound to keep wrong as long as he permits such men to influence\r\nhim. I am ashamed of father,\xe2\x80\x9d Lucy added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, don\xe2\x80\x99t say that,\xe2\x80\x9d Jane begged.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I am,\xe2\x80\x9d Lucy maintained; \xe2\x80\x9cvery much ashamed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I also\xe2\x80\x94bitterly ashamed,\xe2\x80\x9d Everett said.\r\n\r\nThe old gentleman at last succeeded in fastening his spurs and getting\r\non his horse. He trotted off to meet the Don. Everett and Webster went\r\ndown stairs. Webster had saddled two horses; he was not going to let\r\nEverett go alone, when he might need help. So the two boys followed\r\ntheir father at a short distance.\r\n\r\nLucy and Jane went to Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s room, from which they had a better view\r\nof that part of the valley through which passed the main road, in front\r\nof the Alamar house. They saw their father take the main road. The Don\r\nwas coming slowly with his two sons, watching the _vaqueros_ driving the\r\ncattle up the hill.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMamma, see father going to meet the Don. What does he mean?\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed\r\nAlice, alarmed.\r\n\r\nMrs. Darrell went to the window, and both sat there to watch\r\nproceedings.\r\n\r\nTwo or three head of cattle got separated, and Gabriel turned back at a\r\nfew paces to head them off. Don Mariano and Victoriano kept on, and soon\r\nafter met Darrell.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood afternoon, Mr. Darrell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Don Mariano, pleasantly. \xe2\x80\x9cYou see we\r\nare Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s _vaqueros_ now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDarrell muttered something gruffly, and stopped his horse in the middle\r\nof the road. The others did the same. Don Mariano saw that Darrell was\r\nvery angry, and waited for him to speak first.\r\n\r\nThe enraged man gasped twice, but no sound came. On the third effort his\r\nharsh tones said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want you to tell me what is all this trickery and lies about my\r\nhaving paid you six thousand four hundred dollars for land. You know\r\nthat to be a lie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course I do. You never paid me a cent, nor the other settlers\r\neither. No settler wants to pay, and I never said you had, or expected\r\nthey would, for I know they believe themselves authorized by law to\r\nappropriate my property.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDidn\xe2\x80\x99t Clarence pay you for the land I took?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here, Mr. Darrell, business matters between Clarence and myself\r\nare not to be mentioned, and unless he authorizes me to speak I cannot\r\nrepeat anything which he wishes to keep quiet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you have some private business together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDon Mariano bowed, but did not speak. Darrell came closer to the Don,\r\nand shaking at him the fist in which he held the whip, said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I tell you, you ought to be ashamed of yourself to be bargaining\r\nwith my son in a clandestine manner, fooling me, and making me appear\r\nridiculous. But I tell you to your face\xe2\x80\x94for I am not a sneaking coward\xe2\x80\x94I\r\ntell you, that you have acted most dishonorably, inveigling Clarence\r\ninto bargains unbeknown to me, inducing him, with seductive bribes, to\r\nact most dishonorably towards me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat were those bribes?\xe2\x80\x9d Don Mariano asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat were they? Your daughter\xe2\x80\x99s pretty looks, by G\xe2\x80\x94!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, father!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Everett, turning very pale.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPshaw! That is too low,\xe2\x80\x9d the Don said, turning his horse towards his\r\nhouse.\r\n\r\nDarrell spurred his and stood in the way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cToo low, you say? And isn\xe2\x80\x99t it low to act as you have? And now you want\r\nto sneak off like a coward, and not give me any satisfaction.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am ready to give you any satisfaction you want, but demand it like a\r\ngentleman. I am no Peter Roper, or Gasbang, or Billy Mathews, to have a\r\ntumble-down fist-fight in the dirt with you. If you forget your dignity,\r\nI do not,\xe2\x80\x9d the Don replied, again trying to go towards his house.\r\n\r\nDarrell again placed his horse in front to intercept his road, and said,\r\nlivid with rage:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd why didn\xe2\x80\x99t you think of your dignity when you paraded your daughter\r\n(like a pretty filly for sale) before my son, to get his money! Damn\r\nyou! can\xe2\x80\x99t I make you fight? Won\xe2\x80\x99t you be insulted, you coward? I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\npublish your cowardice all over California.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo saying, he lifted his whip and struck a severe blow at the Don.\r\nQuickly, at the same instant, Victoriano and Everett had dashed their\r\nhorses between, and the blow fell right upon the backs of the two young\r\nmen.\r\n\r\nThis act of devotion was scarcely necessary, for as Darrell lifted his\r\nwhip, and before it fell down, Don Mariano touched his horse with one\r\nspur only, giving a quick touch to the reins to one side. The horse\r\njumped aside, sat on his haunches for an instant, half-crouching,\r\nhalf-rearing, and in a second he was up again. Don Mariano smiled at\r\nDarrell\xe2\x80\x99s clumsy horsemanship, conscious of being able to ride him down\r\nand all around him before the belligerent squatter could tell what was\r\nhappening. Still smiling, the Don rode slowly away. Darrell followed\r\nclose, and again lifted his whip to strike, but instantaneously he felt\r\nas if he had been struck by lightning, or as if an aerolite had fallen\r\nupon him. His arm fell powerless by his side, and an iron hoop seemed to\r\nencircle him. He looked down to his breast surprised, and there the coil\r\nof a _reata_ held him in an iron grip, and he could not move. He looked\r\nabout him amazed, and saw that the other end of the _reata_ was neatly\r\nwound around the pommel of Gabriel\xe2\x80\x99s saddle, and that young gentleman\r\nsat quietly on his horse, as if waiting Mr. Darrell\xe2\x80\x99s orders to move,\r\nhis handsome face a little pale, but quite composed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo home and bring me my pistols, Webster. I\xe2\x80\x99ll fix this brood of\r\ngreasers,\xe2\x80\x9d said Darrell, half choked with rage and the tight embrace of\r\nthe _reata_.\r\n\r\nWebster hesitated, and looked towards his brother for advice. Everett\r\nwinked, and Webster understood at once that Everett meant that he should\r\ngo, but bring no pistols. He galloped off towards home.\r\n\r\nThe horse that Darrell was riding was the mate of the one that Webster\r\nrode, so that when he saw his partner go off towards home, he thought he\r\nmust do the same, and followed. As the reins hung loosely upon his neck,\r\nhe naturally supposed that he was to follow at the pace his companion\r\nwent, so he started at a gallop to catch up with Webster.\r\n\r\nThus now began a most ridiculous steeple-chase going home. Darrell could\r\nnot check his horse or do anything but hold to the pommel of his saddle,\r\nhis arms being pinioned to his body. Gabriel, fearing to let go the\r\n_reata_, which, if loosened, might entangle the horse, and thus pull the\r\nold man off his saddle, followed, maintaining the _reata_ at an even,\r\ngentle tension, carefully keeping at the same distance. Victoriano and\r\nEverett saw nothing to do but follow, trying to get near Darrell to\r\ncatch him in case he should lose his balance going over the rough ground\r\nof the plowed field.\r\n\r\nThe two Indian _vaqueros_ after putting their cattle in the _corral_,\r\ncame down to inquire for further orders, and seeing the race going on,\r\nthey thought they could join in, too. So, putting spurs to their horses,\r\nthey began to run and shout in high glee. Noticing that the patron, Don\r\nGabriel, held a _reata_ in his hands, the _lazo_ end of which was\r\nattached to Darrell, they thought that for sport Don Gabriel had thrown\r\nthe _lazo_ on the old squatter. Having come to this conclusion, they\r\nbegan to shout and hurrah with renewed vigor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cApa! viejo escuata \xc3\xb3 cabestreas \xc3\xb3 te \xc3\xb3rcas,\xe2\x80\x9d cried one.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo le afloje patroncito Gabriel,\xe2\x80\x9d said the other.\r\n\r\nNow the ground being very rough, Darrell began to sway, as if losing his\r\nbalance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cApri\xc3\xa9tate viejo! apri\xc3\xa9tate m\xc3\xadralo! ya se ladea!\xe2\x80\x9d cried again one\r\n_vaquero_.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCreo que el viejo escuata va chispo,\xe2\x80\x9d said the other.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQue es eso? A que vienen ac\xc3\xa1? Quien los convida? C\xc3\xa1llense la boca, no\r\nsean malcreados, Vallense!\xe2\x80\x9d said Victoriano, turning to them in great\r\nindignation.\r\n\r\nThis rebuke and imperative order silenced them immediately, and not\r\nunderstanding why these gentlemen were having all that fun, and did not\r\nlaugh, nor wished any one else to laugh, quietly turned and went home.\r\n\r\nDarrell\xe2\x80\x99s horse now came to a hollow made by the old bed of a brook\r\nwhere the road passed diagonally. To gallop down hill was too much\r\nequestrianism for the pinioned rider; he began again to topple to one\r\nside. Quick as a flash Victoriano darted forward, and grasping the\r\nbridle with one hand, caught with the other the body of Darrell, which\r\nhaving entirely lost balance, was toppling over like a log.\r\n\r\nGabriel immediately gathering the _reata_ quickly in successive loops,\r\nall of which he hung on the pommel of his saddle, came to Darrell\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nside.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll take that _lazo_ off, Mr. Darrell, if you permit me,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nGabriel, very quietly, when Victoriano had straightened him on the\r\nsaddle, and he had again a perpendicular position.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, damn you, and you\xe2\x80\x99ll pay for it, too!\xe2\x80\x9d was Darrell\xe2\x80\x99s courteous\r\nreply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, but don\xe2\x80\x99t be abusive. Use better language; and if you want\r\nto fight I\xe2\x80\x99ll accommodate you whenever you wish, with any weapons,\r\nexcept the tongue,\xe2\x80\x9d Gabriel answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you think a _lazo_ is a very genteel weapon. It is good\r\nenough for cowardly, treacherous greasers,\xe2\x80\x9d said the irate Darrell,\r\neager to be as insulting as possible.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd to subdue wild cattle,\xe2\x80\x9d Gabriel added. \xe2\x80\x9cI threw my _lazo_ on you to\r\nkeep you from striking my father. He was unarmed, and you made a brutal\r\nattack upon him with a heavy mule whip. I would _lazo_ you again fifty\r\ntimes, or any other man, under the same circumstances. If you think it\r\nwas cowardly to do so, I will prove to you at any time that I was not\r\nprompted by cowardice. Victoriano, loosen the _reata_ off Mr. Darrell\xe2\x80\x99s\r\narms.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nVictoriano dismounted, and endeavored to loosen the tight noose, but it\r\nwas so firmly drawn that he could not move it. Everett came to his\r\nassistance, but he, too, failed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot loosen the noose without hurting Mr. Darrell,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nVictoriano, giving up the task.\r\n\r\nGabriel dismounted, and examined the noose carefully. He shook his head,\r\nsaying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir; we cannot loosen that _reata_ while you are sitting down. We\r\nwill have to put you on your feet, Mr. Darrell, and you will be slimmer\r\nthen. Thus by collapsing a little the loop will lose the tension that\r\nkeeps it tight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome on, Mr. Darrell, Retty and I will let you down nicely,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nVictoriano.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLean on me, father,\xe2\x80\x9d said Everett, but as he held up his arms towards\r\nhis father, he became convulsed with laughter. Victoriano was laughing,\r\ntoo, so heartily, that Darrell was afraid to trust his weight into their\r\nhands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor shame, Victoriano, to be so discourteous,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel,\r\nreprovingly\xe2\x80\x94his handsome features perfectly serious.\r\n\r\nBut Victoriano had suppressed his desire to laugh too long, and now his\r\nrisibility was beyond control. Everett was overcome in the same manner,\r\nso that he hung on Victoriano\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder, shaking with ill-suppressed\r\nlaughter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Darrell, be not afraid to trust to my strength, I am slender, but I\r\nam stronger than I look. Lean your weight on me slowly, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll take\r\nyou off your horse while those boys laugh,\xe2\x80\x9d Gabriel said, putting up his\r\nhands for Darrell to lean on them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think we had better go home first,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir. It will be painful for Mrs. Darrell to see you as you are, and\r\nthen you ought to have that _reata_ off now, quickly. It will sicken\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I feel a very strange sort of cold feeling.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGabriel was afraid that impeded circulation might make the old man\r\nfaint, so he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, Mr. Darrell, quick.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe slipped off one stirrup, then quickly went around slipped off the\r\nother, and pulled Darrell to him gently. Down like a felled tree came\r\nthe old fighter, almost bearing Gabriel down to the ground. Everett and\r\nVictoriano, checking their laughter somewhat, lent their assistance to\r\nhold him up, and as he had begun to look bluish, they saw the necessity\r\nof establishing the old man\xe2\x80\x99s circulation. While Everett and Victoriano\r\nheld him up, Gabriel loosened the coil, rubbing briskly and hard the\r\nbenumbed arms to start circulation by friction, moving them up and down.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan you get on your horse now?\xe2\x80\x9d Gabriel asked, after Darrell had moved\r\nhis arms several times.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I think I can,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, looking towards his house. A new shadow\r\npassed over his face.\r\n\r\nWebster was coming back, leading his horse. Would he bring pistols? No.\r\nHis mother was walking with him. Mrs. Darrell saluted the Alamares, and\r\nthey lifted their hats respectfully in response. Webster had told her\r\nall that had happened, and she understood everything, excepting the\r\nsteeple-chase performance. She had seen all running behind her husband,\r\nbut she did not know that the chase was most involuntary on his part.\r\nSeeing them stop for so long a time in the hollow she thought he had\r\nfallen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the matter, William? Did you fall?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. And if I had, you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t pick me up.
