What did you come out here\r\nfor?\xe2\x80\x9d was the characteristic answer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause, not seeing you when down in this hollow I feared you were\r\nhurt, but since it is only foolish anger that ails you, I need not waste\r\nmy sympathy,\xe2\x80\x9d she said in her sweet, low voice\xe2\x80\x94which Clarence insisted\r\nalways was like Mercedes\xe2\x80\x99 voice, having that same musical vibration, so\r\npleasing to the ear and sure to go straight to the heart.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Darrell, allow me to assure you that all this trouble came most\r\nunexpectedly to us. We don\xe2\x80\x99t know what caused it, but no matter what the\r\ncause may be, I certainly could do nothing else than prevent anybody\r\nfrom striking my father,\xe2\x80\x9d Gabriel said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly, Don Gabriel, you did your duty. I do not blame you\xe2\x80\x94no one of\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x94at all. Express my regrets to your father, please. I am grieved to\r\nthe heart about this,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, and there was a sad note in her tones,\r\nwhich plainly told that her expressions of regret were but too true.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will tell my father what you say, and let us hope that the cause of\r\nall this misunderstanding may be explained,\xe2\x80\x9d Gabriel replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope so,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, offering her hand to him, which he took and\r\npressed warmly.\r\n\r\nWhen Darrell saw that friendly demonstration, he turned his back upon\r\nall, and muttering that he was \xe2\x80\x9cto be made the scape-goat of all,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nwalked home.\r\n\r\nMrs. Darrell then asked Gabriel to explain everything to her, which he\r\ndid, while she listened to him very attentively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you only had heard what those squatters said, and prevented father\r\nfrom riding out,\xe2\x80\x9d Everett exclaimed.\r\n\r\nMrs. Darrell sighed, shook hands with the Alamares, and, followed by her\r\nsons, walked home.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVI.\xe2\x80\x94_Mrs. Darrell\xe2\x80\x99s View of Our Land Laws._\r\n\r\n\r\nOf all the horrible tortures that the human mind is capable of conjuring\r\nup with which to torment itself, none was greater to William Darrell\r\nthan the consciousness of being ridiculous\xe2\x80\x94the conviction that people\r\nwere laughing at him. He had seen Victoriano and his own Everett so\r\nconvulsed with laughter, laughing at him, laughing in his presence,\r\nlaughing so heartily that they could scarcely stand up. This laughter of\r\nthe two boys was the most vivid picture in the panorama of living scenes\r\nwhich he himself had evoked. Surely if his own son laughed so heartily,\r\neverybody else would do the same. And when on his return home,\r\nClementine had said to him most unceremoniously:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, papa, what made you sit on your horse so stiff? Why did you want\r\nto keep that rope? You looked so funny.\xe2\x80\x9d And Clementine laughed\r\nheartily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGet out of my way,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, and went to the \xe2\x80\x9ccolony\xe2\x80\x9d straight and\r\nbanged the door; which meant that he wanted no one else within the\r\nprecincts of that asylum. \xe2\x80\x9cSo I looked funny and stiff; they were all\r\nlaughing at me,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, and with a groan of mental and physical pain,\r\nflung himself on the lounge.\r\n\r\nPresently, Tisha came to say that supper was on the table. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want\r\nany supper,\xe2\x80\x9d said he in the gruff tones he used when he was angry, or\r\npretended to be. Tisha retired, but in about ten minutes she returned,\r\ncarrying a tray, which she deposited on a table, saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMissus says that mayhap when you rested awhile you might feel a little\r\nhungry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive me a cup of tea; I want nothing else,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, and Tisha fixed\r\nhis tea just as she knew he liked it with plenty of rich cream and four\r\nlumps of sugar, for Darrell\xe2\x80\x99s teacup held a pint; she placed the tea on\r\na little table by the lounge and retired.\r\n\r\nThe tea seemed to refresh him in spite of himself, and he accepted the\r\nimprovement with an inward protest as if setting down an exception (as\r\nlawyers call it) by which he renounced all obligation to be grateful.\r\n\r\nEarly the settlers began to arrive at the \xe2\x80\x9ccolony\xe2\x80\x9d through the side door\r\nof the back hall. Everett joined the meeting, as Romeo came to request\r\nhis company. Darrell gave his son a withering look, but did not speak to\r\nhim. He kept his reclining position on the lounge and his satellites sat\r\nin a semi-circle around him. He soon told them he had nothing\r\nsatisfactory to say, as the Don had refused to make any explanation,\r\nalleging that he had promised Clarence to say nothing. When Clarence\r\nreturned he would clear the mystery. The settlers again recommenced\r\ntheir conjectures, and discussed the motives which must have actuated\r\nthe Don to make a false entry, to record having received money which he\r\nnever got. Land was the discussion, but there seemed no dissenting voice\r\nas to the Don\xe2\x80\x99s culpability, and the sinister motives which actuated him\r\nin acting in that underhand manner. When the altercation was at the\r\nhighest, and could be heard all over the house, Mrs. Darrell walked in\r\nand, bowing to the astonished squatters, came slowly forward and stood\r\nabout the middle of the semi-circle, though outside of it. Darrell sat\r\nup and all the others stood on their feet and stared as if they had seen\r\nsome Banquo spectre or other terrible ghostly apparition.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe seated, gentlemen, I beg of you. I have but a few words to say.\r\nPlease sit down,\xe2\x80\x9d she reiterated, seeing that every one remained\r\nstanding.\r\n\r\nSlowly all one by one dropped into their seats and all the faces were\r\nturned towards her. No one thought of offering her a chair, and she did\r\nnot want one either. When all had resumed their seats, she said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll those amongst you, gentlemen, who think that Don Mariano Alamar\r\ninduced my son Clarence to purchase land from him are much mistaken; and\r\nall those who think Don Mariano made a false entry of a land sale, do\r\nhim an injustice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho made the entry then?\xe2\x80\x9d Darrell asked, sharply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is what I came to say. The land was bought and paid for at _my_\r\nrequest. If there is any blame, or crime, or guilt in the matter, _I_ am\r\nthe criminal\xe2\x80\x94_I_ am the guilty one. I told my son, Clarence Darrell,\r\nthat if he did not pay for the land which his father had located, I\r\nwould never, _never_ come to live upon it. Moreover, I told my son not\r\nto mention the fact of having paid for the land, because his father\r\nwould think we were interfering in his business, and I did not wish him\r\nto know that the land was paid for until the question of the Don\xe2\x80\x99s title\r\nwas settled. Then we would have avoided painful discussions, and the\r\neloquence of facts (I trusted) would clearly show to my husband that his\r\nwife and son had acted right, when we had paid the legitimate owner for\r\nhis property.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now, gentlemen, let me add this, only this, that I do not mean to\r\ncriticise anybody\xe2\x80\x99s actions or opinions, but, from my point of view, I\r\nsay, those laws which authorize you to locate homesteads upon lands\r\nclaimed as Mexican grants, those laws are wrong, and good, just, moral\r\ncitizens should not be guided by them. Settlers should wait until the\r\ntitles are finally approved or rejected. See! look back and see all the\r\nmiseries that so many innocent families have suffered by locating in\r\ngood faith, their humble homes upon lands that they were forced to\r\nabandon. Our law-givers doubtless mean well, but they have\xe2\x80\x94through lack\r\nof matured reflection, I think, or lack of unbiased thought\xe2\x80\x94legislated\r\ncurses upon this land of God\xe2\x80\x99s blessings. I love my country, as every\r\ntrue-hearted American woman should, but, with shame and sorrow, I\r\nacknowledge that we have treated the conquered Spaniards most cruelly,\r\nand our law-givers have been most unjust to them. Those poor,\r\ndefenseless ones whom our Government pledged its faith to protect, have\r\nbeen sadly despoiled and reduced to poverty.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have only expressed my opinion, gentlemen; I mean no slur upon yours.\r\nI hope you see now that I alone, _I am_ the one to blame for the\r\npurchase of the land which has given so much offence. Good night,\r\ngentlemen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo profound was the silence following Mrs. Darrell\xe2\x80\x99s exit, that a pin\r\ncould have been heard drop. Romeo Hancock was the first to find\r\nutterance to his amazement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy George,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cbut ain\xe2\x80\x99t she superb! I see now where Clarence\r\ngets his good sense and correct ideas.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt any other time, Darrell would have been proud of this tribute paid to\r\nthe wife he adored, with passionate, secret, unrevealed tenderness, but\r\nnow he was too angry. He even felt angry at the longing to take to his\r\nheart that darling so resolute and yet so gentle. This longing, when his\r\npride clamored that she was wrong and should be reproved, was an\r\nadditional torture to him. He remained silent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I suppose that\xe2\x80\x94in the language of the poets\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98this settles our\r\nhash,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d Gasbang said, and laughed at his witticism, as it was his habit\r\nto do.\r\n\r\nHughes and Miller laughed with him, but no one else. All were deeply\r\nimpressed with Mrs. Darrell\xe2\x80\x99s words.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish she had told me this before,\xe2\x80\x9d Darrell said, and resumed his\r\nrecumbent position.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, why didn\xe2\x80\x99t she?\xe2\x80\x9d Gasbang asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause women are bound to do mischief,\xe2\x80\x9d Mathews replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe stated her reasons very clearly,\xe2\x80\x9d Romeo said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat were they?\xe2\x80\x9d Mathews asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan your memory be failing you already, Mr. Mathews, that you forget\r\nwhat you just heard, or are you getting hard of hearing?\xe2\x80\x9d Romeo\r\nanswered.\r\n\r\nMathews snorted and turned his back on Romeo. Everett answered him,\r\nsaying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy mother said that she wished the purchase to be kept quiet until the\r\nDon should have his title. Then the fact of the land being his, would\r\nprove the correctness of having paid for what we took, and thus all\r\ndiscussions would have been avoided. Unfortunately some busybody went to\r\nsee the entry, and came to herald his glorious discovery.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow did she know that the Don\xe2\x80\x99s title would not be rejected?\xe2\x80\x9d Mathews\r\ninquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHer good sense told her,\xe2\x80\x9d Romeo answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wasn\xe2\x80\x99t talking to you,\xe2\x80\x9d Mathews retorted, making all laugh\xe2\x80\x94and even\r\nDarrell smiled\xe2\x80\x94but he looked very pale, and Everett began to feel\r\nanxious, to see his pallor.\r\n\r\nThe conversation had now drifted to the subject of the coming survey of\r\nthe rancho.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI heard that the surveyor will be on the ground by the first of\r\nOctober,\xe2\x80\x9d Miller said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right; that will give us plenty of time,\xe2\x80\x9d Gasbang observed.\r\n\r\nEverett said something to Romeo, who then went and whispered to his\r\nfather, whereupon Old Hancock nodded an assent and in a few moments\r\nsaid:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, my friends, let us go home. For the present I don\xe2\x80\x99t see that\r\nanything can be done. Mr. Darrell looks fatigued, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t wonder at\r\nit, for we have bored him nearly to death. Let him go to bed and rest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEvidently Mathews, Gasbang and others had no idea of going home so\r\nearly, but as Darrell said nothing, they reluctantly arose and took\r\ntheir departure.\r\n\r\nIf Darrell had obeyed the impulse of his heart when he went up-stairs to\r\nhis bed-chamber, he would have taken his wife in his arms and, with a\r\nkiss, made his peace with her; for he knew her to be true, and always\r\nacting from the best motives. But there was that streak of perversity\r\nwithin, which impelled him to do or say the wrong thing, when at the\r\nsame time an inner voice was admonishing him to do the opposite.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry, William, that I kept that matter of the land purchase from\r\nyou. Believe me, my husband, I did so out of a desire to avoid\r\ndiscussions always painful to me. You seemed so happy here, that I hated\r\nto bring up for argument any disagreeable subject. It was a mistake; I\r\nregret it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, wise women generally put their foot in it,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, turning his\r\nback on her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan you forgive me? I am very sorry. And now I want you to take a nice\r\nwarm bath; after so much excitement it will soothe you, and you will\r\nsleep sweetly. After all, it is better that you know the whole thing\r\nnow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo thanks to you, though.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is true, but you know my maxim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich one? Wise women have so many.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo accept blessings thankfully, even when they come in disguise,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nreplied, taking no notice of his sarcasm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have yet to see the blessing in this.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will to-morrow if you will only take care now of your physical\r\ncomfort\xe2\x80\x94your health. Come, take a bath; it will prevent your having a\r\nfever.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want a bath; I feel badly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is why you should have it. I know your constitution well\xe2\x80\x94nothing\r\nwould be better for you than warm bathing. Be reasonable, please. I feel\r\ntired, too; I would like to go to bed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy don\xe2\x80\x99t you, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I wanted first to see you resting for the night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know that I\xe2\x80\x99ll go to bed. I think I\xe2\x80\x99ll sleep in this chair.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, then, I shall go into Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s room and sleep there! It\r\nwould keep me awake to know that you were sitting up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo as you please.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan it be possible, William, that you refuse to go to bed because you\r\nare too angry with me to have me lie by your side?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe said nothing, but looked very pale. She waited; he never said a word.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, William, I am dismissed I suppose. If you are sick or\r\nrequire anything, knock at Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s door. I shall be there. Good\r\nnight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe went quietly into Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s room and lit a lamp. She went to a hall\r\ncloset and took a soft merino wrapper, came back, locked her door,\r\nundressed herself, put the wrapper on, and sat by the window to think.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat fools men are? Such small vanity guides them. To think that\r\nWilliam should fling away happiness at the instigation of a reptile like\r\nGasbang! And you, my sweet boy, my darling Clarence, how will this\r\naffect your happiness?\xe2\x80\x9d This thought gave her the keenest pain.\r\n\r\nWhile Mrs. Darrell was thus sadly meditating, her angry lord was nearly\r\nchoking with smothered rage\xe2\x80\x94intensified a hundred fold by his\r\ndisappointment at being left alone without his adored, worshipped Mary.\r\nMrs. Darrell knew that her husband loved her, but she had never guessed\r\nthat torrent of passion and devotion which rushed through that rugged\r\nnature like a river plunging from Yosemite hights into unknown abysmal\r\ndepths.\r\n\r\nWhy would he not yield to her sweet entreaties to bathe and take his\r\ncomfort? Was it all perverse obstinacy? Partly, yes. He had refused a\r\nwarm bath and her sweet society, for the very reason that those two were\r\nthe things he most desired on earth\xe2\x80\x94he felt as if even his bones\r\nclamored for them. But there was yet another equally strong motive in\r\nthat very complex nature\xe2\x80\x94a motive stronger than obstinacy\xe2\x80\x94compelling him\r\nin spite of himself, and this was _his bashfulness_. He feared that his\r\nwife might see the bruises on his arms and the heavy welt that he knew\r\nthere must be around his body, made by the coil of the _reata_. He felt\r\nvery sore, and his bruises became more painful, but he would rather die\r\nthan let any one see his pitiful plight. And thus he sat up all night\r\nand would not undress, or go to bed, or be comforted.\r\n\r\nTowards morning he walked to the window and looked into the valley, then\r\nhis gaze wandered towards the Alamar house. All the windows had the\r\nshutters closed and no light was seen from them excepting one. He did\r\nnot know what room that was or who occupied it, but unconsciously he\r\nwatched it\xe2\x80\x94watched the light he could see through the lace curtains. The\r\nlight became intercepted at regular intervals; so he concluded that some\r\none must be going and coming before that light. He smiled, hoping that\r\nthe Don might be as miserable as he was\xe2\x80\x94unable to sleep.\r\n\r\nBut the Don was sleeping. She who was awake, walking in her solitary\r\nvigil, was Mercedes. Those beautiful blue eyes had never closed in sleep\r\nall night.\r\n\r\nShe had been embroidering a _mouchoir_ case for Clarence that\r\nunfortunate afternoon of Darrell\xe2\x80\x99s performance, when she heard loud\r\ntalking in the piazza. At first she paid no attention to it and went on\r\nwith her work, hoping that Clarence would return early, because her\r\ndream troubled her. The talking becoming louder, and more voices being\r\nheard, she felt alarmed, imagining that Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s horses had run away\r\nand he had been hurt. She went out to inquire.\r\n\r\nThe entire Alamar family, as well as Mrs. Mechlin, George and Lizzie,\r\nwere in the veranda. All had seen Darrell\xe2\x80\x99s attempt and subsequent\r\nsteeple-chase. Now Gabriel and Victoriano had returned and related what\r\nhad passed in the hollow. Victoriano was again overcome with laughter,\r\nwhich, being so hearty and uncontrollable, became contagious. Even\r\nGabriel and Mr. Mechlin, who were less disposed to indulge in hilarity,\r\nlaughed a little. Mercedes was the only one who not even smiled. She did\r\nnot understand a word of what was said. Gradually she began to\r\ncomprehend, and she stood motionless, listening, her pale lips firmly\r\ncompressed, her eyes only showing her agitation and how grieved she was;\r\ntheir dark-blue was almost black, and they glowed like stars.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCheer up, little pussy. When Clarence comes he will undeceive the old\r\nman, and all will be right,\xe2\x80\x9d said Don Mariano, putting his arms around\r\nher yielding form and drawing her to his heart.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Palabra suelta, no tiene vuelta_,\xe2\x80\x9d Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa said. \xe2\x80\x9cDarrell can\r\nnever recall his insulting words.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut he can apologize for them,\xe2\x80\x9d Don Mariano said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd would that satisfy you?\xe2\x80\x9d Carlota asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would have to,\xe2\x80\x9d was the Don\xe2\x80\x99s answer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! papa!\xe2\x80\x9d Rosario exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat then? Shall I go and shoot the old fool?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe he would enjoy that, he is so full of fight,\xe2\x80\x9d Victoriano\r\nsaid, recommencing his laughing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI fear his anger will not abate as long as the bruises of the _reata_\r\nremain painful,\xe2\x80\x9d Gabriel said, thoughtfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you draw the _lazo_ very tight?\xe2\x80\x9d Don Mariano asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot intentionally, but he himself did so by stooping forward as his\r\nhorse galloped. Every time he did so the noose became more closely drawn\r\nuntil he could scarcely breathe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is a bad business, George,\xe2\x80\x9d the Don said to his son-in-law, who\r\nhad remained a silent listener to all.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir; but let us hope that between Clarence and Mrs. Darrell they\r\nwill pacify the old man. The thing now is to give him time to cool off\r\nhis anger,\xe2\x80\x9d George replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf those squatters could be kept away, Darrell would come to his senses\r\nmuch sooner,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Mechlin said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s it exactly,\xe2\x80\x9d Gabriel added; \xe2\x80\x9cthey make the mischief.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why does he allow it?\xe2\x80\x9d Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause he loves the smell of gunpowder, and they are full of it,\xe2\x80\x9d Tano\r\nexplained.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think Mrs. Darrell ought to prevent those horrible creatures from\r\ninvading her house,\xe2\x80\x9d Carlota said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey only go to the \xe2\x80\x98colony.\xe2\x80\x99 The old buster wants them there. He would\r\nsmash the furniture if his pets were not allowed to come to lick his\r\nboots,\xe2\x80\x9d Victoriano asserted, positively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t speak very respectfully of your future father-in-law,\xe2\x80\x9d George\r\nsaid to Victoriano, laughing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at present. Not when I have just seen him running away like a\r\nchicken thief, just caught with a turkey under each arm,\xe2\x80\x9d Tano replied,\r\nlapsing into another fit of laughter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Tano! if you care for Alice, how can you so ridicule her father?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nMercedes exclaimed, speaking for the first time. And without waiting for\r\na reply, she turned away and went to her room.\r\n\r\nThere she remained inconsolable, her lovely face often bathed in tears.\r\nShe did not go to bed; she hoped that Clarence might possibly have\r\nfinished his business in town and hurried back. She watched for the\r\nfaintest sound all night.\r\n\r\nIn the morning Madame Halier came to see her, and immediately went to\r\nreport to Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa the state of Mercedes\xe2\x80\x99 eyes. Don Mariano came in\r\nat once and took his pet in his arms.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPapa, you said you were going to-day. Please don\xe2\x80\x99t go,\xe2\x80\x9d she begged.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not, my pet? I shall go only a little ways with those stupid\r\nIndians who keep letting the cattle turn back. I shall return before\r\ndark,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, smoothing her golden hair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPapa, please don\xe2\x80\x99t go. I want you to be here when Clarence returns. Let\r\nthe cattle be. I want you here. You may never see Clarence again in this\r\nworld if you go.\xe2\x80\x9d And she put her pale cheek against her father\xe2\x80\x99s and\r\nsobbed convulsively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat an idea! Why shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t I see Clarence again if I ride one or two\r\nmiles? My baby darling, you are too nervous. You have cried all night,\r\nand now your mind is in a whirl of sad visions. Do not exaggerate the\r\nmischief that Darrell might do. He will probably say very insulting\r\nthings to Clarence, but Clarence is as true as steel, and has a very\r\nclear head.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know that. I am sure of him. He is so true. But, papa, can I marry\r\nhim after what his father said to you, and when he tried to strike you?\r\nCan I marry him after that, papa?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not, pray? What he said is an infamous lie, and because Darrell\r\nchooses to indulge in mean thoughts and atrocious language, is that a\r\nreason why you and Clarence should be made wretched for life? If Darrell\r\ndid not permit men like Gasbang, and others influenced by Peter Roper,\r\nto come near him, his ears would not hear such low, vulgar suggestions.\r\nAs long as we know that Clarence is a gentleman, and he behaves as such,\r\nI shall not permit that you two be separated by anything that Darrell\r\nmay do or say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, papa, you will keep out of Mr. Darrell\xe2\x80\x99s way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly, my poor little darling. Don\xe2\x80\x99t be afraid; Darrell will not\r\nattack me again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Don talked in this consoling and reassuring way to his favorite\r\nchild until he saw that he had quieted her. She promised to eat\r\nbreakfast and then try to sleep.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt won\xe2\x80\x99t do to look at Clarence through such swollen orbs. You had\r\nbetter let Tano give you one of his graphic accounts of the battle of\r\nAlamar, as he calls Darrell\xe2\x80\x99s performance, and make you laugh.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t laugh. I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t if I could.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well. To sleep is the best for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe kissed her and soon after he and Gabriel went on their way. They\r\nquickly overtook the herders, who were driving the lot of cattle which\r\nhad started at daylight. The Don was confident of returning at sundown,\r\nand glad to leave Mercedes more contented and hopeful, he rode away\r\ncheerfully.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVII.\xe2\x80\x94_Darrell Astonishes Himself._\r\n\r\n\r\nMercedes felt so comforted by what her father had said, that in less\r\nthan ten minutes after he left she was sleeping like the good child that\r\nshe was. Madam Halier watched her slumbers, coming to the door every few\r\nminutes. And when she had slept and felt refreshed, she had a bath and a\r\nluncheon of tea, cold chicken, fresh peaches with cream, and fresh\r\ngrapes just cut from the vines; then she was ready to dress herself and\r\ntake up her embroidery. She was afraid her eyes would yet be too swollen\r\nfor her to go into the parlor or veranda, and perhaps meet George or Mr.\r\nMechlin. So she stayed in her room.\r\n\r\nBut she was missed, and George came to knock at her door, and being\r\nasked to come in, he did so, making a profound bow. Then counting on his\r\nfingers as he spoke, began:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo\xc3\xb1a Josefa, Do\xc3\xb1a Beatrice, Do\xc3\xb1a Carlota, Do\xc3\xb1a Rosario, Do\xc3\xb1a Elvira,\r\nDo\xc3\xb1a Carolina, Do\xc3\xb1a Elizabeth, all request the pleasure of your company\r\nat a canning performance to take place this afternoon in the kitchen of\r\nDo\xc3\xb1a Beatrice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMercedes laughed, asking: \xe2\x80\x9cAre they really going to do the canning? Who\r\nknows about it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey all know, theoretically, but as to practice, that \xe2\x80\x98_qui\xc3\xa9n sabe_.\xe2\x80\x99\r\nHowever, they are going to peal peaches by the bushel this evening, so\r\nthey will all dine there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoesn\xe2\x80\x99t mamma expect papa to dinner?\xe2\x80\x9d asked she, alarmed; \xe2\x80\x9cI hope so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll go and inquire,\xe2\x80\x9d George said, going; but she followed him,\r\ntrembling\xe2\x80\x94she did not know why. She took George\xe2\x80\x99s arm, and both went to\r\nthe piazza, where Carlota, Rosario and Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa were waiting for\r\nGeorge to go with them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMamma, don\xe2\x80\x99t you expect papa to dinner?\xe2\x80\x9d asked she.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but he might be late; so we will dine at Mrs. Mechlin\xe2\x80\x99s, and he\r\nand Gabriel will take supper here on their return.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will wait for them here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you not go to Mrs. Mechlin\xe2\x80\x99s?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, please. I\xe2\x80\x99ll stay home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake my advice, and don\xe2\x80\x99t see Clarence yet,\xe2\x80\x9d Carlota said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not, pray?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause, after what his father did and said, the least you have to do\r\nwith the Darrells the more it will be to your honor,\xe2\x80\x9d Rosario said,\r\nsententiously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd must I give up Clarence because\xe2\x80\x94because his father gets mad,\r\nand\xe2\x80\x94and\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd insults your father, and insults you,\xe2\x80\x9d Carlota said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut that would be awful,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, looking at George, who full of\r\nsympathy for his favorite sister-in-law, said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo not worry about that now\xe2\x80\x94you have suffered enough. No doubt,\r\nClarence will make it all right, if we only give him time. All will be\r\nexplained.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI doubt that,\xe2\x80\x9d Carlota said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think Mercedes knows all that Darrell said. I think Clarence\r\nhimself will see the impossibility of his marrying Mercedes as things\r\nare now,\xe2\x80\x9d Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are we to do?\xe2\x80\x9d Mercedes exclaimed, in low, tremulous tones, that\r\nrevealed all the desolation she felt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTry to be courageous, little sister,\xe2\x80\x9d Carlota said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat to do? Clarence himself ought to know\xe2\x80\x94to separate for the present.\r\nWill you marry the son of a man who said of you and your father such\r\nhorrible things?\xe2\x80\x9d Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut Clarence is innocent, and so am I,\xe2\x80\x9d pleaded Mercedes, with white\r\nlips.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy daughter, do you not see that I _must_ withdraw my permission to\r\nyour marriage now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you tell that to Clarence?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mercedes, frightened.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly, as soon as I see him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd break our engagement?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, with a voice scarcely audible.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly. What else, my daughter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want to go to my room,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, slowly turning to go back, walking\r\nas if in a dream.\r\n\r\nGeorge put his arm around her shoulder, and walked with her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t be discouraged, my dear _humanita_. Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa is justly\r\nindignant now, but her anger will pass off, and she will see how absurd\r\nit will be to punish you and Clarence for the sins of his ill-tempered,\r\nfoolish father. The only thing now is to drop the matter. \xe2\x80\x98Least said,\r\nsooner mended,\xe2\x80\x99 applies to this case exactly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish papa were here. He don\xe2\x80\x99t think as mamma does. If mamma sees\r\nClarence first, she will send him away. Oh! that will be awful to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will keep your mamma at our house until Don Mariano returns. Tano\r\nwill see Clarence first.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen George left, Mercedes hurried to her bedside to pray. In all the\r\nsad tribulations of her mind, her heart turned to her Redeemer and the\r\nBlessed Virgin Mary. To them she told all her grief, all her trials, and\r\nafter begging to be strengthened, she always arose from her bended knees\r\ncomforted.\r\n\r\nThis time, however, her convulsive sobs only became more uncontrollable,\r\nas she poured out her great sorrow and terrible fears before the pitying\r\nMother of suffering humanity.\r\n\r\nWhen her sobs were almost a paroxysm, Madame Halier, who had come to the\r\ndoor to listen, went, and much excited, told Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa that Mercita\r\nwould certainly be ill if some one didn\xe2\x80\x99t show a little humanity to her.\r\n\r\nDo\xc3\xb1a Josefa hurried to Mercedes\xe2\x80\x99 room, and found her still at her\r\nbedside sobbing and praying. Gently the mother lifted her child and\r\npressed her to her heart.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMercedes, darling, have courage. Your father and Clarence will talk\r\nthis matter over, and determine what is best to do. Perhaps it might all\r\nbe arranged.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will not tell Clarence to\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x94to go away?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly not. But there must be some other arrangement about the\r\nwedding. It will be postponed, perhaps. Darrell could not be expected to\r\nbe present, or he might wish the engagement broken off.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCarlota and Rosario came in to see how Mercedes felt, as Madam Halier\r\nseemed to be so anxious and indignant with everybody for their cruelty\r\nto Mercedes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf old Darrell wants the engagement broken off, then my dear sister you\r\nmust break it\xe2\x80\x94else he will have a good reason to say that papa wants to\r\nsell you, or to entrap Clarence, for his money, into marrying you,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nRosario said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid Mr. Darrell say that?\xe2\x80\x9d Mercedes asked, blushing, so that her pale\r\nface became suffused to the roots of her hair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe said worse\xe2\x80\x94but you had better hear no more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is awful!\xe2\x80\x9d the poor child exclaimed, clasping her hands in\r\neloquent protestation; then adding: \xe2\x80\x9cMamma, I will try to have courage.\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what I am to do. But if my father has been so grossly\r\ninsulted, I must feel for him. I must not be selfish. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll do,\xe2\x80\x9d and the unhappy girl pressed her hands to her forehead, as if\r\nto keep together her distracted thoughts.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think the best thing for you to do is to go to bed. To-morrow your\r\nfather will see Clarence. That is George\xe2\x80\x99s advice, and I think it is\r\ngood,\xe2\x80\x9d said her mother, as she kissed and embraced her, adding: \xe2\x80\x9cthe\r\nsweet, blued-eyed baby is too young to get married, any way, and can\r\nwell wait four years, and then be only twenty-two years old.\xe2\x80\x9d But seeing\r\nthe blank despair in those expressive eyes, Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa hastened to add:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t say that you will wait that long, but that you are young enough\r\nto do so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen Mercedes was again alone, she tried to think it was her duty to her\r\nfather to break her engagement. Her mind utterly refused to see the\r\nmatter in that light, but as her older sisters had said her engagement\r\nought to be broken off, and her mother spoke of the wedding being\r\npostponed, it was clear that she could not be married on the 16th. Would\r\nClarence be willing to wait? and these thoughts revolved around her mind\r\nin a circle of coils, worse than the one which so enraged and hurt\r\nDarrell.\r\n\r\nMadam Halier and Victoriano ate their dinner alone\xe2\x80\x94with Milord for sole\r\ncompany. Poor Tano, though he had laughed heartily at Darrell\xe2\x80\x99s plight,\r\nwas scarcely less distressed than Mercedes, and anxiously looked for\r\nClarence\xe2\x80\x99s return.\r\n\r\nIn the meantime this young gentleman was traveling at the rate of twelve\r\nmiles per hour, and would have come faster had the road been better. He\r\nhad been obliged to delay, because Hubert had telegraphed that if he\r\nwaited two hours he would give him a definite answer about Gabriel\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbusiness. The answer came, and it was all that could be desired. Gabriel\r\ncould go at any time, or wait until the first of October to take his\r\nplace at the bank. Clarence was delighted to have this good news to\r\ncarry to Mercedes, with the addition that Fred said that the mines\r\ndeveloped richer ores every day. He had an offer of two million dollars\r\nfor his mines\xe2\x80\x94but both Hubert and Fred advised him not to sell.\r\n\r\nWith these cheerful thoughts, he was getting into his ph\xc3\xa6ton, when the\r\nnotary, who had made the entry of Don Mariano\xe2\x80\x99s conveyance, came close\r\nto him, and said in a low voice, and looking mysteriously around:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here, it may be nothing, but those two fellows are so tricky and\r\nslippery that I always imagine they are up to something, and both have\r\nbeen twice to look in my books at the entry of the land conveyance which\r\nSe\xc3\xb1or Alamar made to you. They might mean mischief, though I don\xe2\x80\x99t see\r\nhow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf whom are you speaking?\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf Roper and Gasbang. Why should they wish to know about that\r\nconveyance?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know; but I am sure it is for no good. When did they look at\r\nthe entry?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout two days ago, the last time. When they first looked at it I was\r\nnot at home. My wife was at my office when Roper came and asked\r\npermission to see the date of a conveyance which he himself had made.\r\nThis was only a ruse. Two days after he came and told me that one of his\r\nclients wanted to buy land from Darrell, and wished to see what sort of\r\na title he had. I, of course, let him see it. Gasbang came after, and\r\nthat made me suspicious.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClarence thanked the notary, and drove home as fast as the uneven road\r\npermitted. He felt that he must at last disclose to his father all about\r\nthat land transaction, and feared that he would be angry. His fears, he\r\nsaw, were only too well founded as soon as he arrived home.\r\n\r\nThe family were at supper when he drove up to the door. On hearing the\r\nsound of wheels, Everett left the table and hastened to meet him. All\r\nhis brothers and sisters would gladly have done the same, but a look\r\nfrom their mother kept them in their chairs.\r\n\r\nIn a few words Everett condensed the unfortunate occurrences of the\r\nprevious day and evening, ending his hurried statement by saying that\r\nthe entire family hoped that Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s influence might appease their\r\nfather\xe2\x80\x99s irritation when nothing else would.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; I am sure that if mother has failed, I shall have no effect at\r\nall,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut are you sure that there is nothing else to\r\nanger him? The fact alone of my having paid for the land, and at my\r\nmother\xe2\x80\x99s request, would not so infuriate him while in his normal state\r\nof mind. There must be some _other_ irritating circumstance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone that we know of.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad he did not strike the Don.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo am I, though I have a big bump to testify that he struck _me_, and I\r\nsuppose Tano has another to speak for him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClarence told the servant who came to take the horses to the stable to\r\nleave them where they were, only throwing a blanket on, as he had driven\r\nthem very fast. He and Everett then walked into the hall, carrying some\r\nsmall parcels which he (as usual) had brought home\xe2\x80\x94one of those parcels\r\nbeing a beautiful pipe, for which he had paid forty dollars, and a lot\r\nof fine tobacco, for his father.\r\n\r\nPlacing them on the hall table, he said to Everett: \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose father\r\nwould rather throw this tobacco into my eyes than put it in his pipe and\r\nsmoke it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEverett laughed at this, thinking it rather a witticism under the\r\ncircumstances, and was still laughing when both went into the\r\ndining-room.\r\n\r\nClarence said good evening to all, kissing his mother as he took his\r\nseat beside her. Darrell never lifted his eyes, paying no attention to\r\nhis son.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat made you laugh just now, Retty?\xe2\x80\x9d Willie asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSomething that Clary said,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Everett.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas it anything funny?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt must have been; but you needn\xe2\x80\x99t hear it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I want to hear it,\xe2\x80\x9d he insisted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt must have been about your father, he is the funny man now\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nlaughing stock,\xe2\x80\x9d said Darrell to Willie; then to Clarence: \xe2\x80\x9cWe have had\r\ncircus performances. Your father distinguished himself by performing\r\n_in_ the tight rope, with Don Gabriel\xe2\x80\x94a very tight rope,\xe2\x80\x9d he said,\r\nmaking a semi-circular sign around his body with both hands, and nodding\r\nhis head at Clarence by way of emphasis, or as if he challenged him to\r\ncontradict his statement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, father! I am very sorry,\xe2\x80\x9d was all that Clarence could answer.\r\n\r\nThe entire family were almost choking with suppressed laughter, but none\r\ndare give vent to it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy don\xe2\x80\x99t you laugh\xe2\x80\x94all of you?\xe2\x80\x9d asked he, looking around fiercely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause you frighten their laughter away,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Darrell replied. \xe2\x80\x9cThey\r\nfear to offend you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOffend me? _Me?_ And since when such consideration? Since when, I say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSince they were old enough to know you as their father,\xe2\x80\x9d calmly replied\r\nMrs. Darrell.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! I am glad to hear it. Well, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, addressing Clarence\r\nagain, to the terror of all the family, \xe2\x80\x9cI have at last learned that you\r\nhave been making clandestine bargains with your future father-in-law,\r\nplacing me in a most ridiculous position, for which I don\xe2\x80\x99t thank you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry, father. My intention was most kind,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence answered,\r\nrespectfully, but very calmly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou only thought that as I was a fool, you would be my sense-bearer,\r\nand act for me\xe2\x80\x94you, the man of brains.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir. All I thought was, that as you seem to love my mother, you\r\nwould prefer to give her the kind of home that she desires. I thought\r\nthat when you came to know all, you would approve of my having obeyed my\r\nmother\xe2\x80\x99s wishes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you were so sure of my approval, why didn\xe2\x80\x99t you tell me the whole\r\nthing before?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I was pledged to my mother not to do so. I was bound to be\r\nsilent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy George!\xe2\x80\x9d said Darrell, striking the table with his fist, making all\r\nthe glasses and cups dance; \xe2\x80\x9cand for all that nonsense I have been made\r\na laughing stock, a ridiculous, trusting fool\xe2\x80\x94an ass!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo one will think that but yourself,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Darrell said; \xe2\x80\x9cand you will\r\nchange your mind, I hope.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how do you know that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was supposing that people reason in the way that in all my life I\r\nhave believed to be correct.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, what _you_ believe to be correct no one else has any right to\r\nthink differently.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhether they have or not, I shall not interfere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, you only wanted to interfere with me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly. As my life is united to yours, I am obliged to try and\r\nprevent such of your actions as will make me unhappy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn excellent doctrine for wives\xe2\x80\x94for mothers to teach their children\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nwe see the result now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Darrell was pleased that his attacks seemed directed to herself\r\ninstead of Clarence, but she felt prematurely relieved, for now he came\r\ndown upon Clarence. He said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, sir, since yourself and your mother have bought this land, and\r\nsince I am an unreclaimed _squatter_, I suppose I had better leave this\r\nplace, and go back to Alameda again. I suppose I can have that place\r\nagain?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will not have to lease it, father; you can have it rent free, as\r\nlong as you live, if you prefer to reside there,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarence replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow is that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI bought the place, and if you wish you can live in it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou? _You_ bought the place! Then, by George! _you_ have managed to\r\ncoop me up,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Darrell, drawing down the corners of his mouth and\r\nelevating his shoulders deprecatingly, as if he thought Clarence was a\r\nvoracious land-grabber, who wanted to appropriate to himself all the\r\nvacant land in the United States.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t say that, please. The place was for sale, Hubert telegraphed me,\r\nand I telegraphed back to buy it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t know you were so rich,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered, sneeringly.\r\n\r\nClarence made no reply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I must admit you have cornered me completely; but as I don\xe2\x80\x99t want\r\nto live on the bounty of my rich son, I must get out of this place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can refund me the price of one hundred and sixty acres, father, if\r\nyou are too proud to accept that from me, which is little enough,\r\nconsidering your generosity to me all my life. The other two claims, you\r\nknow, you said would be one for Retty and the other for myself. This\r\nhouse and the orchards are all on your claim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have taken a dislike to the whole thing,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, waiving his hand,\r\nas if to shift the position of the land in question. \xe2\x80\x9cYou can have it\r\nall, together with the Alameda farm. There are other lands in\r\nCalifornia.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Darrell and Clarence looked at each other. The case seemed\r\nhopeless. All were silent.\r\n\r\nMr. Darrell continued: \xe2\x80\x9cAll I want before I leave here is to give your\r\ngreaser father-in-law a sound thrashing and another to that puppy,\r\nGabriel, who is so airy and proud, and such an exquisite, that it will\r\nbe delightful to spoil his beauty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why should you wish to do that? What has Don Mariano done to you?\r\nand if Don Gabriel threw his _lazo_ on you, it was to protect his\r\nfather.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat has the old greaser done? He inveigled you into that land\r\nbusiness, and you together have made me ridiculous. That is what the\r\nmatter is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you don\xe2\x80\x99t believe me?\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Darrell said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you take so much credit to yourself, and throw yourself into the\r\nbreach like a heroine. If the Don hadn\xe2\x80\x99t had that pretty daughter,\r\nClarence would not have been so obedient to his mother, perhaps.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClarence rose to his feet, very pale, but he sat down again, and\r\ncontrolling himself, said as calmly as possible:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had never seen one, not one of Don Mariano\xe2\x80\x99s daughters when I went to\r\noffer to pay for this land.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean that you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have done so if your mother hadn\xe2\x80\x99t wished\r\nit?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo sir, not that. I think I would, for I felt great sympathy with the\r\nDon for the contemptible manner in which the squatters received the\r\npropositions he made them. I was convinced then that the land belonged\r\nto him, and nobody had a right to take it without paying for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAha! I knew we would come to that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Darrell, sternly, glaring at\r\nhis son. \xe2\x80\x9cI was a thieving squatter, of course, and that is what you\r\nsaid to your greaser father-in-law, who to reward your high sense of\r\nhonor, took you to the bosom of his family. The cowardly dog, who will\r\ntake insults and not resent them, but has puppies at his heels to throw\r\n_lasooing_ at people.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPshaw! I never thought you capable of\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf what? Insulting those greasers?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are gentlemen, no matter how much you may wish to besmear them\r\nwith low epithets.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGentlemen that won\xe2\x80\x99t fight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey told you they would fight _like gentlemen_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho told you that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did, father. I heard Don Mariano and Don Gabriel both tell you that,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nEverett said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf they are so ready to fight, why didn\xe2\x80\x99t they do it when I told the\r\nold dog that the bait to catch you was his daughter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat! Did you say that?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Clarence, reddening to the roots of his\r\nhair, his face quickly blanching again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did\xe2\x80\x94in clear language.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn dirty, low, nasty language, and it is you who are the coward, to\r\ninsult _me_ under the shelter of your paternal privileges,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nClarence, rising. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have been taunting me until I can bear it no\r\nlonger. I suppose you wish to drive me from your house. Be it so. I\r\nleave now\xe2\x80\x94never to enter it again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat suits me. You are too _greasy_ for both of us to live under the\r\nsame roof,\xe2\x80\x9d said Darrell, contemptuously, with a gesture of disgust.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-by, mother; good-by, my sisters; good-by, boys\xe2\x80\x94take care of mother\r\nand the girls. God bless you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith a piercing cry, that rang through the house, Alice ran to Clarence,\r\nand throwing her arms around his neck, said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKiss me, my darling, for if you leave us I shall be wretched until you\r\nreturn. Oh! I can\xe2\x80\x99t let you go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTenderly Clarence pressed his sister to his heart. He felt her arms\r\nrelaxing, her head fell back, and she closed her eyes. Lovingly he then\r\nlifted her, and placing her upon a lounge, said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlice has fainted, mother. My sweet sister, how dearly I love her, God\r\nonly knows.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe covered her face with kisses, while his own was bathed in tears.\r\nWithout lifting his eyes or saying another word, he walked out into the\r\ndarkness.\r\n\r\nThe delicious, fragrant air, loaded with the perfume of roses and\r\nhoneysuckle and heliotrope, seemed to breathe a farewell caress over his\r\nheated brow, and the recollection of the loving care he had bestowed\r\nupon these flowers when he planted them to welcome his mother, flashed\r\nthrough his memory with a pang. He sighed and passed into the gloom,\r\noverpowered with a dread that made him feel chilled to the heart. It\r\nseemed to him as if an unseen voice was warning him of a dire misfortune\r\nhe could not perceive nor avert. What could it be? Was Mercedes to be\r\ntaken from him? Would her family object to him on account of his\r\nfather\xe2\x80\x99s ruffianly behavior? Could he claim to be a gentleman, being the\r\nson of that rough? These thoughts flashed through his mind, filling him\r\nwith sickening dismay and inexpressible disgust. Would he dare stand in\r\nthe presence of Mercedes now? Or, would he return to town at this late\r\nhour? Where could he go for a shelter that night?\r\n\r\nMechanically he walked to the ph\xc3\xa6ton, got into it and took the reins to\r\ndrive off.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVIII.\xe2\x80\x94_Shall it be Forever?_\r\n\r\n\r\nEverett followed Clarence and got into the ph\xc3\xa6ton with him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear brother,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence, in a hoarse voice that sounded\r\nunnatural, as if coming from a great depth, \xe2\x80\x9cI would like to have your\r\ncompany, but as I am not coming back, I can\xe2\x80\x99t take you with me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo matter; drive off. I\xe2\x80\x99ll go with you a little ways, and will walk\r\nback,\xe2\x80\x9d said Everett. Clarence turned his horses and drove away through\r\nthe middle drive in the front lawn, and was out of the gate before he\r\nfully realized that he himself was driven away from the paternal roof.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRetty, you did not tell me that my father had insulted my darling so\r\ngrossly. I wish you had, for I would not have gone inside the house,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nClarence said, with a sigh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was so horrible, I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t. Forgive me, dear Clary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly; I can\xe2\x80\x99t blame you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you going to Don Mariano\xe2\x80\x99s?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. I will ask Tano to give me a place to sleep; that is, if Do\xc3\xb1a\r\nJosefa is not too disgusted to tolerate a Darrell under her roof.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sure they feel nothing but kindness for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope so; but should she wish to break the engagement, I will not\r\nstay. I\xe2\x80\x99ll drive to town to-night and take the boat for San Francisco,\r\nwhich is not to leave until to-morrow at daylight. I\xe2\x80\x99ll have time, I\r\nthink.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t do that. Wait for the Don, if he is not in now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI may, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t know. I dread to see Mercedes. I feel so humiliated,\r\nso ashamed. What can I say to her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt the foot of the hill Clarence stopped his horses to send to his\r\nmother and sisters\xe2\x80\x94especially to Alice\xe2\x80\x94loving messages. He also said if\r\nhe should miss seeing Don Mariano, Everett would say that he would write\r\nfrom San Francisco, and would return at any moment, if Mercedes called\r\nhim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you will see her yourself,\xe2\x80\x9d Everett said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope so,\xe2\x80\x9d said the disheartened Clarence, driving up toward the house\r\nin which he felt his fate would be decided. Victoriano had heard the\r\nph\xc3\xa6ton\xe2\x80\x99s wheels and came out to meet it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am so glad to see you, old fellow,\xe2\x80\x9d said he to Clarence; \xe2\x80\x9cit seems an\r\nage since sundown.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was detained in town about that business of Don Gabriel, but it is\r\nall arranged. He can take his place at the bank now, whenever he wishes,\r\nor wait until the 1st of October; it will be kept for him. Then I had my\r\nown business about the mine. That is all right, too. I only wish that\r\nthings had gone on as well at home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo do I, but it has been awful. Retty told you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I know it all now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnfortunately I did not tell him father\xe2\x80\x99s insulting remarks about Miss\r\nMercedes,\xe2\x80\x9d sadly observed Everett.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, had I known that, I would not have gone into the house. But I\r\nwent, and father had the satisfaction of saying it to me himself; and on\r\nmy telling him what I thought about it, he expressed himself willing\r\nthat I should take myself off. So here I am, driven from home, and I\r\ncame to ask you for a bed to-night, as I am very tired.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd hungry, too. Father spoiled his supper with his courteous remarks,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nadded Everett.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, my dear boy; no one is more welcome to this whole house,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nVictoriano said, with true Spanish hospitality, much intensified by\r\npresent circumstances. \xe2\x80\x9cCome; father will soon be here. At present,\r\nMercedes, Madame Halier Milord and myself only are at home. Mother and\r\nthe rest are at the Mechlins. Come in; come, Retty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. I\xe2\x80\x99ll say good-by to Clary now and walk home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut this is awful,\xe2\x80\x9d Victoriano said, as if beginning to realize the\r\nsituation. \xe2\x80\x9cFor Heaven\xe2\x80\x99s sake, where are you going? And why must you\r\ngo?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will not if Mercedes does not send me away. If she does, I shall go\r\nfirst to San Francisco, and thence God only knows where,\xe2\x80\x9d was Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nreply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe won\xe2\x80\x99t send you away; she shan\xe2\x80\x99t. If you only knew how the poor\r\nlittle thing cried, so that this morning literally she could not see out\r\nof her eyes, you would then know how she feels. She told me that if she\r\nlost all hope of being your wife she would lie down and die. She felt\r\nbetter this morning when father left, as he told her he would arrange\r\neverything with you so that the wedding should not be postponed. Then\r\nshe was comforted and went to sleep. But\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d And Victoriano stopped.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what? Better tell me all, dear Tano,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I was going to say that she is again unhappy because Lotte and\r\nRosy told her what your father said. She had not heard that part of the\r\ntrouble before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClarence stood silent with one foot upon the first step. He was\r\ncalculating the chances against him. He turned to Victoriano, and, with\r\na sickly smile that was truly painful to see, said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy heart misgives me, dear Tano; I cannot blame her if she considers my\r\nfather\xe2\x80\x99s words unpardonable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut they were not _your_ words,\xe2\x80\x9d Everett interposed. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are not to\r\nblame if your father forgets _himself_ and makes a brute of _himself_. I\r\nalmost hate him. Courage, dear Clary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, remember, \xe2\x80\x98Faint heart never won fair lady,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d Victoriano added,\r\nand the quotation brought such sweet recollections to poor Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ntroubled mind, that he staggered as he went up the steps. But, with a\r\nrenewed effort over himself, he managed to stand firmly, and to say to\r\nEverett:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose we must part now, dear brother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEverett threw his arms around him, and for a few moments both brothers\r\nheld each other in close, silent embrace.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCheer up, boys. Don\xe2\x80\x99t think you are to part,\xe2\x80\x9d said Victoriano, with\r\nassumed cheerfulness. \xe2\x80\x9cYou must come to breakfast with us to-morrow\r\nRetty. When father comes he and Clary will concoct some plan so as not\r\nto postpone the wedding. Come, I\xe2\x80\x99ll take you home. I\xe2\x80\x99ll let Mercedes\r\nknow first that Clarence is here.\xe2\x80\x9d So saying he walked into the house.\r\nReturning in a few moments, he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWalk in, Clary. Mercedes will be in the parlor in a minute. Now, Retty,\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll take you home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhile both drove to the Darrells, Clarence went in the parlor to wait\r\nwith beating heart Mercedes\xe2\x80\x99 coming. He walked about the room looking at\r\nevery object in it without seeing anything. When he heard the rustle of\r\nher dress, he stood by the piano with his arms crossed over his breast\r\nas if trying to compress the wild throbbing of his heart. He was pale to\r\nthe lips and his eyes had an expression of longing, of beseeching\r\ntenderness, that was far more sad and eloquent than tears would have\r\nbeen. Mercedes came in, followed by her faithful Milord, who, seeing\r\nthat Clarence paid no attention to him, turned up his nose in mild\r\nresentment and went to lie down upon the rug in front of the fire-place.\r\nShe offered to Clarence her hand in silence. In silence he took it,\r\nkissed it and led her to a sofa, sitting down by her side. She was the\r\nfirst to speak. Looking into his eyes, she said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClarence, must we part? I have such, faith in your truth that I believe\r\nyou will candidly tell me your opinion, even if it kills both of us. Am\r\nI right?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy darling, what is it? Do not put me to a test that may be too hard,\r\nfor I tell you frankly I can give up my life, but not my love. Not you!\r\nmy own! Oh, no; anything but that. Not that.\xe2\x80\x9d So saying, he took both\r\nher hands\xe2\x80\x94the beauty of which he so loved\xe2\x80\x94and kissed them warmly, all\r\nthe time fearing that if she said to him that she must break off their\r\nengagement, he must submit, as he could not blame her if she considered\r\nhim beneath her love. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it you wish to ask me? Oh, my angel! be\r\nmerciful!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish to ask you what must I do when your father has said such\r\nfrightful things to my papa? Am I obliged and in duty bound to decline a\r\ntie which will create any relationship with him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClarence was silent, still holding the dear little hands. His face\r\nflushed with shame, but became pale again as he replied:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would have been more difficult to solve that problem if my father\r\nhimself had not done so by driving me off. I am exiled now\xe2\x80\x94driven away\r\nfrom home. I doubt whether he would consider you related to him by being\r\nmy wife now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad of that,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, quickly, but then checking herself, and a\r\nlittle abashed by what she thought the hasty expression of a selfish\r\nfeeling, she said: \xe2\x80\x9cForgive me; I don\xe2\x80\x99t mean I am glad he should drive\r\nyou away, but that since he has cut you off\xe2\x80\x94and yet\xe2\x80\x94he cannot do that.\r\nHow can he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has done so. That proves he can, doesn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Clarence. No matter what he does he is still your father.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClarence leaned his head back on the sofa and looked at the chandelier\r\nin silence for some moments, then said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, he is my father, but not the father he used to be. There are\r\ndifferent kinds of fathers. Some are kind and good, others are most\r\nunnatural and cruel. Are they entitled to the same love and respect?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut was he ever cruel to you before?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever. He has been always most kind and indulgent to all his children,\r\nbut especially so to Alice and myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, Clarence, for this one fault, all his life of kindness and\r\ndevotion must not be forgotten.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, my darling! are you going to plead for him and forget my misery? My\r\nheart is bleeding yet with the pain of leaving home, and if your\r\nindulgence to him means that I must bear the burden of his fault, _I\r\nthen\xe2\x80\x94I must suffer alone_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not wish you to suffer at all. If there is to be any suffering, I\r\nshall share it with you. No. All I say is that if Mr. Darrell is so\r\nangry at my papa and myself, we had better postpone our wedding until\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClarence sprang to his feet, and with hands pressed to his forehead,\r\nbegan pacing the room, greatly agitated, but without speaking a word.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClarence, hear me. It will only be for a little while.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe shook his head, and continued his walk\xe2\x80\x94his mind a prey to the wildest\r\ndespair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWould it not be very unbecoming for us to marry now, and your family\r\nnot be present at the wedding?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t they be present? All would be but father, and in the\r\nfurious state of his feelings he had better be away\xe2\x80\x94a great deal\r\nbetter\xe2\x80\x94far, far away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSince he is so furious, I don\xe2\x80\x99t think he would like his wife and\r\nchildren to be at our wedding.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMercedes, tell me frankly,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, resuming his place at her side:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9ctell me, has my father\xe2\x80\x99s outrageous conduct made me lose caste in your\r\nestimation? If so, I shall not blame you, because when a man acts so\r\nungentlemanly, so ruffianly, it is fair to suppose that his sons might\r\ndo the same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever! Such an idea never entered my mind. How could it?\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMercedes, with great earnestness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf it did not, it is because you are good and generous. Still, perhaps,\r\nit is selfish in me to keep you to your engagement with the son of such\r\na rough. I release you, Mercedes. You are free,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, and he closed\r\nhis eyes and leaned his head again on the back of the sofa. A sensation\r\nof icy coldness came over him, and he thought that death must come like\r\nthat. But for all that mental agony, he still thought Mercedes would be\r\nright in rejecting him.\r\n\r\nThe whole scene as described to him by Everett, when his father was\r\nuttering those low insults to Don Mariano, came vividly before him, and\r\nhe thought it would be impossible for Mercedes not to feel a sense of\r\nhumiliation in uniting herself to him\xe2\x80\x94he, the son of that brutish\r\nfellow\xe2\x80\x94that rough. What do you mean? Will you not wait for papa?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. I had better go now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou misunderstood me, I think, else how could you think of going?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you not say that our wedding had better be postponed? And does that\r\nnot mean that it may never, _never_ be?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy should it mean that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause, how can we measure the duration of an anger so senseless? It\r\nmight last years. No, Mercedes, I feel that you have the right to reject\r\nme. I shall be so very wretched without you, that I would beg and\r\nentreat, but\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClarence, I do not reject you, and I have no right, no wish, to do so.\r\nPlease do not say that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you be mine\xe2\x80\x94my wife\xe2\x80\x94after all the ruffianly words my father has\r\nsaid?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly. Will you call me when you think you can\r\nconsent to our wedding?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean by asking if I will _call_ you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean that if our marriage is to be postponed, I shall leave you, but\r\nshall be ready to obey your call, and I pray I may not wait for it a\r\nlong time. Why should you go\r\naway?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause it is best, as long as our marriage is to be postponed. My\r\npresence here will be a cause of irritation to my father, and goodness\r\nknows what he might not do in his angry mood. If you would not feel\r\nhumiliated by marrying me, the best thing would be to have a quiet\r\nwedding immediately, with only the members of your family present, and\r\nnot invite guests at all, and then we would take the steamer to San\r\nFrancisco, and go to our home there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think mamma would consent to that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, my darling, I must leave you now. Make known your wishes, and I shall obey.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are offended, Clarence, and I do not know how I have incurred your\r\ndispleasure,\xe2\x80\x9d she said in those tones of her voice which were the most\r\nthrilling to him\xe2\x80\x94most sure of going straight to his heart.\r\n\r\nSilently he approached her, and kneeling at her feet, he put his arms\r\naround the slender and graceful form he idolized so fervently. Say, will you marry me, as I _beg_ of\r\nyou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Clarence, why do you ask me? How can I tell? You will have to ask\r\npapa and mamma.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill they consent?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPapa, perhaps; but I fear mamma will not approve of such a hasty\r\nmarriage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is so. Perhaps I am unreasonable. Good-by, my beloved. Will you\r\ncall me back soon?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClarence, you are not going? How can you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must. Do not ask me to remain, under the circumstances, unless it is\r\nto make you my wife. I cannot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe pressed her to his heart in a long, tender embrace. He arose, and\r\ngazed at her sweet face so sadly, that she felt a pang of keen distress\r\nand apprehension.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClarence, do not look at me so sadly. Please remain until papa comes.\r\nDo not go. You might never see him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must, or I will lose the steamer. Farewell, my own sweet love.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe clasped her to his heart, and wildly covered her face with kisses.\r\nThen, without daring to look back, hurried out of the room into the\r\nhall, across the piazza and down the garden-path to the gate, where his\r\nph\xc3\xa6ton had been left by Victoriano, after having taken Everett home.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe must naturally hesitate to marry the son of a man who can act and\r\nhas acted as my father did. I cannot blame her. I ought to respect her\r\nfor it. Oh, pitying God! how wretched I am! Farewell, happiness for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMuttering this short soliloquy, Clarence drove quickly down the incline\r\nleading to the main road.\r\n\r\nWhen the last sound of his footsteps died away, a feeling of utter\r\ndesolation rushed upon Mercedes. The silence of the house was appalling.\r\nIn that silence it seemed to her as if a life of lonely misery was\r\nsuddenly revealed. To lose Clarence, was to lose happiness forevermore.\r\nShocked and terrified at her loneliness, with no hope of seeing him\r\nagain, she rushed out and ran to the gate, calling him. She saw that he\r\nwas driving fast, and would soon be crossing the dry bed of the brook to\r\ntake the main road. Once there he would be too far to hear her voice.\r\nShe ran out of the gate and turned to the right into a narrow path that\r\nalso led to the main road, going across the hill through the low bushes\r\nand a few elder trees near the house, thus cutting off more than half\r\nthe distance. Loudly she called his name, again and again, running in\r\nthe narrow path as fast as her strength allowed. She heard the sound of\r\nthe ph\xc3\xa6ton\xe2\x80\x99s wheels as they grated harshly on the pebbles of the brook,\r\nand then all was silent again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, my darling is gone,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, and the ground swelled and moved\r\nunder her feet, and the trees went round in mad circles, and she knew no\r\nmore. She had fallen down fainting, with no one near her but her\r\nfaithful Milord, who had followed her, and now nestled by her side.\r\n\r\nClarence had heard her voice call to him, and tried to turn his horses\r\nimmediately, but they were going down the hill too fast to turn without\r\ndanger of upsetting; he saw he must first get to the foot of the hill,\r\nand turn when he reached the brook. He did so, and with heart-throbs of\r\nrenewed hope, he re-ascended the hill and hurried to the house. At the\r\ndoor he met Madam Halier, who was blinking at the hall lamp as if just\r\nawakened from a sound sleep. Clarence asked for Miss Mercedes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think madamoiselle has just gone down to Madame Mechlin\xe2\x80\x99s. I heard\r\nher calling Tano, and that woke me up. I had just dropped off into a\r\nshort nap of five minutes\xe2\x80\x94_just_ five minutes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought I heard her voice in this direction,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarence, pointing\r\nto the opposite side.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no. I think she was afraid to go to Mrs. Mechlin\xe2\x80\x99s alone, and she\r\ncalled her brother. But she has been anxious to see you all day. I will\r\nsend a servant to say you have come. Walk in. Had you a pleasant drive\r\nfrom town?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMadam, I have seen Miss Mercedes since my return from town. I had said\r\nfarewell, and was driving away, when I thought I heard her voice calling\r\nme. Perhaps I was mistaken, but I think not. Where has she gone, I\r\nwonder?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo Madam Mechlin\xe2\x80\x99s, monsieur.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe it so. Good-by, madam,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, extending his hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut will you not wait for madamoiselle?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, madam; if she did not call me, I need not wait.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis time Clarence drove slowly down the hill, looking at both sides of\r\nthe road, peering under the trees and bushes, still impressed with the\r\nidea that he might see her form or hear her voice. The moon was just\r\nrising, casting long shadows as it arose, but the shadow of that\r\nbeloved, graceful form was nowhere to be seen. This added disappointment\r\nwas added bitterness to his cup of misery, and he began to feel sick in\r\nbody and mind, and he saw in himself a most wretched outcast.\r\n\r\nTano and Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa now came and saw the ph\xc3\xa6ton ascending the hill on\r\nthe other side of the brook.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIX.\xe2\x80\x94_Hasty Decisions Repented Leisurely._\r\n\r\n\r\nWhen Victoriano had left Everett at his front door, exacting the promise\r\nthat he would come to breakfast with Clarence next morning, he merely\r\ndelayed long enough to learn that Alice was quiet, and Mrs. Darrell\r\nthought that with a night\xe2\x80\x99s rest she would be well next day. He then\r\ndrove back home, and thinking that Clarence was going to stay, left the\r\nph\xc3\xa6ton at the front gate to run down through the side gate to Mrs.\r\nMechlin\xe2\x80\x99s, to call his mother and say to her that Clarence had been sent\r\noff by his father, and had come to their house to pass the night. But as\r\nhe hurried through the front garden, Victoriano remembered that the\r\nhorses had to be put in the stable and taken care of, so he went in the\r\nkitchen to tell a servant he must attend to the horses immediately.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, _patroncito_, I\xe2\x80\x99ll do it right away,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lazy Indian, who\r\nfirst had to stretch himself and yawn several times, then hunt up\r\ntobacco and cigarette paper, and smoke his cigarette. This done, he,\r\nhaving had a heavy supper, shuffled lazily to the front of the house, as\r\nClarence was driving down the hill for the second time, and Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa\r\nand Victoriano returning from Mrs. Mechlin, came in through the garden\r\nside gate.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is going in that carriage?\xe2\x80\x9d was the first question put by\r\nVictoriano to Madame Halier.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is Monsieur Clarence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd where is Mercedes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe called you to go to Madame Mechlin\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo such thing,\xe2\x80\x9d said Victoriano, going to look in the parlor; returning\r\nimmediately to renew his questions.\r\n\r\nBut the madame could do no more than repeat all she knew, which was\r\nlittle enough, and that little thoroughly mixed in her mind.\r\n\r\nAll that Victoriano and Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa could ascertain, with some\r\nclearness, was that Clarence was going, and had come back, thinking that\r\nMercedes had called him, but that on being told that Mercedes had called\r\nTano to accompany her to Mrs. Mechlin\xe2\x80\x99s, he had gone away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must overtake Clarence. There is some misunderstanding here, that is\r\nplain,\xe2\x80\x9d said Victoriano, going to the back piazza to call a servant.\r\n\r\nThis time Chapo came a little quicker, not knowing whether he would be\r\nto blame, because the _Americano_ went off with his horses before he had\r\ntime to put them in the stable.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBring me my bay horse, saddled, in two minutes, do you hear? Two\r\nminutes\xe2\x80\x94not two hours\xe2\x80\x94go quick.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe cannot find Mercita. She is not in the house,\xe2\x80\x9d said Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa to\r\nher son, much alarmed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe must be, mother. Call the other girls. Look again for her. I must\r\nrun after Clarence, and learn why he is going, instead of passing the\r\nnight here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFifteen minutes after Clarence had left, Victoriano was galloping behind\r\nhim, wondering why he could not see him anywhere on the road.\r\n\r\nMadame Halier and Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa continued looking for Mercedes most\r\nanxiously, but in vain. George now came up, and joined in the search for\r\nthe missing girl.\r\n\r\nAs Victoriano crossed the brook and ascended the hill beyond it, Don\r\nMariano and Gabriel came up into the court-yard. They immediately\r\nhurried into the house, Don Mariano knowing that Mercedes would be\r\nanxious for him to talk with Clarence.\r\n\r\nDo\xc3\xb1a Josefa and the madame met them at the door, and related as well as\r\nthey knew all that had occurred. They all agreed that the matter had\r\nbetter be kept from the servants, if possible, and they all went out by\r\nthe front gate again, since it was useless to search in the direction of\r\nMrs. Mechlin\xe2\x80\x99s house. Don Mariano and Gabriel saw George follow the path\r\nto the right and disappear. They followed him. George had heard the\r\nbarking of a dog in the distance, and at first paid no attention to it,\r\nbut when the barking would be followed by most piteous howls, he\r\nlistened, and thought he recognized the plaintive whining of Milord. He\r\nfollowed the path, and as he did so, came nearer to the barking, and\r\nsoon after Milord himself met him, with demonstrations of great\r\nsatisfaction.\r\n\r\nGeorge had no doubt now of finding Mercedes. He let Milord be the guide,\r\nand run ahead, he following. In a few minutes he saw something white on\r\nthe ground, and immediately after recognized Mercedes\xe2\x80\x99 form lying\r\nmotionless across the path, as she had fallen. In a moment George had\r\nlifted her insensible form in his arms, calling out he had found her.\r\n\r\nDon Mariano ran to him, but Gabriel, being more active, passed him, and\r\nwas quickly at George\xe2\x80\x99s side, gazing anxiously at his sister\xe2\x80\x99s face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive her to me, George,\xe2\x80\x9d said Don Mariano, in a hoarse whisper, for he\r\nwas so agitated he could scarcely speak. \xe2\x80\x9cGive my baby to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait a little while. I\xe2\x80\x99ll carry her a little longer,\xe2\x80\x9d said George,\r\nholding the unconscious girl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFather is too agitated to be steady enough just now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll carry her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me see her face, for God\xe2\x80\x99s sake! Has she no life?\xe2\x80\x9d Don Mariano\r\nexclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes. She has fainted only. We will soon restore her to\r\nconsciousness. Don\xe2\x80\x99t be alarmed. I think the parting with Clarence has\r\nnearly killed her\xe2\x80\x94but she is alive,\xe2\x80\x9d George said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why did they part?
