Next day the _mayordomo_ went about in\r\nhopes of finding such stray animals as might have ran less wildly, but\r\nnone were to be seen, excepting those which lay stiff in death under the\r\nsnow.\r\n\r\nThe loss of his cattle made it more imperative that Don Mariano should\r\nlook closely into land matters, into the prospects of a railroad for San\r\nDiego. He therefore listened attentively to what his friends said about\r\nMr. Lawrence Mechlin having written, and their proposed visit of inquiry\r\nto ex-Governor Stanford as to what might be the fate of San Diego\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nrailroad.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems to me incredible that Doctor Guller should have lent himself\r\nfor such service, no matter how well paid,\xe2\x80\x9d said Don Mariano. \xe2\x80\x9cIf he had\r\nbeen sent to deceive the North, to fool the Yankees, the errand would\r\nhave been\xe2\x80\x94if not more honorable\xe2\x80\x94at least less odious for a Southerner,\r\nnot so treacherous; but to go and deceive the trusting South, now when\r\nthe entire country is so impoverished, so distressed, that act, I say,\r\nis inhuman, is ignominious. No words of reprobation can be too severe to\r\nstigmatize a man capable of being so heartless.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTruly, but the instigators are as much to blame as the tool they used.\r\nThey should be stigmatized also as corrupters, as most malignant,\r\ndebasing, unscrupulous men,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Holman. \xe2\x80\x9cMen who are harmful to\r\nsociety, because they reward dishonorable acts; because they reward,\r\nwith money, the blackest treason!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan it be possible that Governor Stanford had any knowledge that his\r\nassociate was sending Doctor Guller on that disgraceful errand?\xe2\x80\x9d Don\r\nMariano queried.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt looks like it, but let us hope he did not,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Holman replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, let us hope also that Mr. Lawrence Mechlin was misinformed, and\r\nDoctor Guller has not been guilty of anything so atrocious,\xe2\x80\x9d the Don\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\nIt was finally decided that the three friends would go to San Francisco\r\nat the same time that Lizzie would be going. She had made a flying visit\r\nto her family at San Diego, and Gabriel was calling loudly for her to\r\nreturn, saying that after banking hours he felt lonely and missed her\r\ndreadfully.\r\n\r\nLizzie, therefore, had three gentlemen for her escort, and in a few days\r\nthey all steamed away for the city of the sand dunes.\r\n\r\nThe first day in the city Don Mariano devoted to raising a sum of money\r\nby a mortgage on his rancho, as he needed the money to pay taxes on the\r\nland occupied by the squatters; but the day after, the three friends\r\npresented themselves at the railroad office and inquired for Governor\r\nStanford. They were told that he had just left the office, but that he\r\nwould be there on the following day. As they were leaving the office,\r\nthey met a Mr. Perin, a friend whom they had not seen for some time.\r\nWhen they had exchanged greetings, Mr. Perin asked them if they had come\r\nto see Governor Stanford. On being told that such was the case, he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is well that you did not see him, for he is not in a very good humor\r\nto-day, and as for Mr. C., he is like a bear with a sore head\xe2\x80\x94furious at\r\nTom Scott.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the matter? What has Tom Scott done to anger his persecutors?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nasked Mr. Holman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems they need money and can\xe2\x80\x99t raise as much as they want, while\r\nHuntington keeps clamoring for more to kill Tom Scott together with the\r\nTexas Pacific,\xe2\x80\x9d was the answer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe earnings of the Central Pacific this last year were seventeen\r\nmillions of dollars. How are they in such need of money? Is not that\r\nenough to kill Colonel Scott?\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Mechlin asked. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy do they want\r\nmore?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause, if their earnings had been seventy millions, these men would\r\nstill be in need of money,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Perin said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause, as they wish to absorb all the carrying business of this\r\ncoast\xe2\x80\x94in fact, all sorts of business\xe2\x80\x94they want money, money, money. They\r\nwant to buy steamboats, ferry-boats, ocean steamers; street railroads\r\nand street cars; coal mines and farms; in fact, they want everything,\r\nand want it more when some poor devil loses his business thereby and\r\ngoes, frozen out, into the cold world. So you see, to go into such a\r\nvariety of business besides railroading and killing Tom Scott, it costs\r\nmoney. It takes millions and millions to kill and freeze out so many\r\npeople.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope they\xe2\x80\x99ll be disappointed in killing Colonel Scott,\xe2\x80\x9d said Don\r\nMariano. \xe2\x80\x9cThat would mean death to many others.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope so, too, but I hear that Mr. Huntington devoutly prays that a\r\nkind Providence may enable him \xe2\x80\x98_to see grass growing over Tom Scott_,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nMr. Perin replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, my brother wrote me that Huntington does say that he hopes to\r\nworry Scott to death, and \xe2\x80\x98_see grass growing over his grave_.\xe2\x80\x99 I fear\r\nhe will see grass growing over many graves if he succeeds in killing the\r\nTexas Pacific,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Mechlin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is trying hard to do that, and his associates are backing him up\r\nwith millions,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Perin said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen Heaven help us poor people who have invested our all, believing\r\nthat San Diego would have a railroad,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Mechlin, bitterly.\r\n\r\nNext morning the three friends went again to the railroad office and\r\nsent their cards to Governor Stanford. In a few minutes the servant\r\nreturned to say that the Governor was very busy, but if the gentlemen\r\ncould wait he would see them as soon as possible. The gentlemen waited;\r\nthey read the morning papers and looked over railroad guides to while\r\naway time.\r\n\r\nYes, they waited, but they would have spared themselves that trouble,\r\nand they would have never made that pilgrimage from San Diego to consult\r\nthe oracle at San Francisco, could they have read what Mr. Huntington\r\nwas about that time writing to his _associates_ concerning his _modus\r\noperandi_ in Washington to \xe2\x80\x9c_convince_\xe2\x80\x9d Congressmen to do as he wished,\r\nto defeat the Texas Pacific; writing all about sending an ex-Senator to\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_switch off the South_,\xe2\x80\x9d and there to pretend to be an anti-subsidy\r\nDemocrat, and to state falsely that the Texas Pacific would injure the\r\nSouth. All this, however, was only known lately, when Mr. Huntington\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nletters were made public. At that time the three friends, thinking it\r\nimpossible that the rights of Southern California would be so utterly\r\ndisregarded, did not see any absurdity in interviewing the Governor.\r\n\r\nWhile they waited they had an opportunity of hearing several instructive\r\nmatters freely mentioned. One of these was the way of avoiding the\r\npayment of taxes, and how to fight the cases in the courts. The\r\ngentlemen who discussed the subject evidently understood it and were\r\nwaiting to have an audience. Their talk suggested a very sad train of\r\nthoughts to Don Mariano, as he heard that the railroad people did not\r\nmean to pay taxes, and would resist the law. He thought how those\r\nmillionaires would pay no taxes, and defy the law openly and fight to\r\nthe bitter end, whilst he was not only obliged to pay taxes upon a too\r\nhighly appraised property, but must also pay taxes for the land occupied\r\nby the squatter and on the improvements thereon! As a necessary sequence\r\nto such unjust, unreasonable, inhuman taxation, Don Mariano had been\r\nobliged to mortgage his rancho to raise funds to pay the taxes of the\r\nsquatters. With the yearly sales of his cattle he had always been able\r\nto pay his own taxes as well as those of his unwelcome neighbors, but as\r\nhis cattle were now lost, his only resource was his land. Not yet having\r\nthe patent, he could not sell to advantage at all. He must therefore\r\nmortgage.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I were a railroad prince, I suppose I would not be forced to pay\r\ntaxes for the squatters on my land,\xe2\x80\x9d said Don Mariano to his friends,\r\nsmiling sadly to hear how the taxes on railroad property were to be\r\nfought.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you were a railroad prince, you would not pay your own taxes, much\r\nless those of the squatters,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Holman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you ought not to hesitate to use the money that Clarence paid\r\nfor your cattle. If they ran away, it was not your fault,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Mechlin\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, not my fault, but my misfortune; a misfortune which I have no right\r\nto put on Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s shoulders. I did not deliver the cattle; I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ntake the pay. I am going to mortgage my land, but I can\xe2\x80\x99t avoid it,\xe2\x80\x9d Don\r\nMariano replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is certainly a very hard case to have to mortgage your property to\r\npay taxes for the squatters,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Mr. Holman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf these railroad men will only let us have the Texas Pacific all will\r\nbe right, but if not, then the work of ruining me begun by the squatters\r\nwill be finished by the millionaires\xe2\x80\x94if they kill our railroad,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nDon Mariano sadly, adding: \xe2\x80\x9cOur legislators then will complete their\r\nwork. Our legislators began my ruin; our legislators will end it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXIII.\xe2\x80\x94_San Diego\xe2\x80\x99s Sentence is Irrevocable._\r\n\r\n\r\nAfter waiting in the reception room for nearly two hours, Don Mariano\r\nand his two friends were at last ushered into the presence of\r\nex-Governor Stanford. He was so well hid behind his high desk, that\r\nlooking around the empty room, Mr. Holman observed:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I hope this is not to be a second stage of waiting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Stanford arose, bowing from behind his desk, said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe seated, gentlemen. Excuse my having kept you waiting.\xe2\x80\x9d Then seeing\r\nthat there were but two chairs near by, and only one more at the\r\nfurthest corner of the room, he added, going to bring the chair: \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nthought that there were chairs for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDon Mariano, too, had started for the same chair, now that its existence\r\nwas discovered, but the Governor got there first, and brought it half\r\nway, then the Don took it and occupied it.\r\n\r\nWhen all were seated, Governor Stanford said in his low, agreeable\r\nvoice, which any one might suppose would indicate a benevolent, kind\r\nheart:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat can I do for you, gentlemen?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDon Mariano laughed outright. The situation struck him as being\r\neminently ridiculous. Here was this man, who held pitilessly their\r\ndestiny in his hands\xe2\x80\x94held it with a grip of iron\xe2\x80\x94and not one thought of\r\nthe distress he caused; he, through his associate, Huntington, was\r\nlavishing money in Washington to kill the Texas Pacific, and thus snatch\r\naway from them (the three friends) the means of support, absolutely\r\ndeprive them of the necessaries of life, and he asked them what he could\r\ndo? and asked it with that deep-toned, rich melody of voice which\r\nvibrated softly, as if full of sympathy, that overflowed from a heart\r\nfilled with philanthrophy, generosity and good will. This was a sad and\r\ncruel irony, which to Don Mariano made their position absurd, to the\r\npoint of being laughable.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is like laughing at a funeral,\xe2\x80\x9d said Don Mariano, apologetically.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease pardon me. What made me laugh was that I felt like answering you\r\nby saying, \xe2\x80\x98Governor, you can do for us all we ask.\xe2\x80\x99 But\xe2\x80\x94but\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay it out. But what?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Governor, smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut will do nothing for us,\xe2\x80\x9d finished Mr. Holman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is to say, for San Diego,\xe2\x80\x9d added Mr. Mechlin, afraid that it might\r\nseem as if they came to ask a personal favor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! it is of San Diego that you wish to speak to me? Then, truly, I\r\nfear I can do nothing for you,\xe2\x80\x9d the Governor said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you can hear what we wish to say to you,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Holman interposed,\r\nwith a sickly effort at smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly. But really, gentlemen, you must excuse me for saying that I\r\nam very busy to-day, and can only give you a half hour.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey all bowed.\r\n\r\nMr. Mechlin and Don Mariano looked at Mr. Holman, as it was understood\r\nthat he would be spokesman. But Mr. Holman\xe2\x80\x99s heart was leaping with the\r\nindignation of a lion, and then shrinking with the discouragement of a\r\nmouse into such small contractions\xe2\x80\x94all of which he in no way must\r\nreveal\xe2\x80\x94that for a minute he could not speak.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose the San Diego people wish me to build them a railroad, isn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nthat it?\xe2\x80\x9d said the man of power, slowly arranging some papers on his\r\ndesk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOr to let some one else build it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Holman.\r\n\r\nThe Governor colored slightly, in evident vexation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTom Scott, for instance,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, sneeringly. \xe2\x80\x9cTake my advice,\r\ngentlemen, and don\xe2\x80\x99t you pin your faith on Tom Scott. He\xe2\x80\x99ll build no\r\nTexas Pacific, I assure you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen why don\xe2\x80\x99t you build it?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mr. Mechlin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause it won\xe2\x80\x99t pay,\xe2\x80\x9d was the dry reply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy won\xe2\x80\x99t it pay? We have plenty of natural resources, which, if\r\ndeveloped, would make plenty of business for two railroads,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Holman\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly the San Diego people say so. No one else thinks of San Diego\r\nCounty, but as a most arid luckless region, where it never rains.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is the talk of San Francisco people, Governor, because they want\r\nall the railroads to come to their city, and nowhere else,\xe2\x80\x9d said Don\r\nMariano.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have less rainfall in Southern California, on an average, but on\r\naverage, too, we get better crops than in the northern counties in dry\r\nyears. How it is I can\xe2\x80\x99t tell you, unless it be that a given quantity of\r\nrain is all that crops require, and above that it is superfluous, or\r\nelse that for certain soils a certain amount of rainfall is all that is\r\nrequired. It is undoubtedly true that in dry years more crops have been\r\nlost in some of the northern counties than in ours,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Holman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps, but when we have such magnificent wheat country in our\r\nnorthern valleys, it isn\xe2\x80\x99t to be supposed that we can give any attention\r\nto San Diego.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf our county does not take the lead as wheat-growing, it certainly can\r\ntake it as fruit-growing. We have no capital to make large plantations\r\nof vineyards or trees, but what has been done proves, conclusively, that\r\nfor grapes, olives, figs, and in fact all semi-tropical fruits, there is\r\nno better country in the world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat may be so, but you see we are not engaged in the fruit-growing\r\nbusiness. We build railroads to transport freight and passengers. We do\r\nnot care what or who makes the freights we carry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly. But surely there cannot be any reason why, if San Diego should\r\nhave freights and passengers to be carried, that we should not have a\r\nrailroad.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly not. If you can get it, do so, of course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, Governor, that is why we came to talk with you. _Is San Diego\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndeath sentence irrevocable?_ Is it absolutely determined by you that San\r\nDiego is not to have a railroad?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mr. Holman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, that is a hard question to answer. No, perhaps for the present\r\nSan Diego will _not_ have a railroad,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, with cool nonchalance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you call _for the present_? How long?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is a harder question yet. You see, if we effect a compromise with\r\nMr. Scott, we will keep on building the Southern Pacific until we meet\r\nhis road, and then, as all the Eastern freight can come by the Southern\r\nPacific, there will not be any necessity of another railroad.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn other words, San Diego must be strangled. There will not be any\r\nTexas Pacific?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Holman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, not in California,\xe2\x80\x9d the Governor calmly asserted, passing over the\r\nsubject as of no consequence, if a hundred San Diegos perished by\r\nstrangulation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy the terms of the Southern Pacific charter were you not to build to\r\nSan Diego?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mr. Mechlin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; that is to say, through San Diego to the Colorado River, but that\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t suit us at all. Still, I think that after a while, perhaps,\r\nwhen we have more time, we might build to San Diego from some point of\r\nthe Southern Pacific that we see is convenient,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, as if it\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t matter what the terms of the Southern Pacific charter were,\r\nknowing that Congress would not enforce them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA little branch road,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Mr. Holman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; that is all we think is necessary for our purpose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen to sum up, what we must understand is, that San Diego cannot hope\r\nto be a western terminus of a transcontinental railway; that all we may\r\nhope to get is a little branch road from some point convenient to the\r\nSouthern Pacific Railroad.\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Stanford bowed. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd yet,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Holman\r\ncontinued, \xe2\x80\x9cby right, San Diego is the terminal point of a\r\ntranscontinental railway, and San Diego ought to be the shipping point\r\nfor all that immense country comprising Arizona, Southern California and\r\nNorthern Mexico. We are more than five hundred miles nearer to those\r\ncountries than San Francisco, thus you will be making people travel six\r\nhundred miles more than is necessary to get to a shipping point on the\r\nPacific.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo much more business for our road,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Stanford said, laughing, in a\r\ndignified way, and slightly elevating his eyebrows and shoulders, as if\r\nto indicate that really the matter hardly merited his consideration.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut without asking or expecting you to take any sentimental or\r\nphilosophic or moralizing view of our case _as a benefactor_, will you\r\nnot take into consideration, as a business man, the immense benefit that\r\nthere will be to yourselves to have control of the trade which will be\r\nthe result of uniting Southern California with Arizona, with the\r\nSouthern States and Northern Mexico, and developing those vast countries\r\nnow lying useless, scarcely inhabited.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes; we have thought of that, I suppose, but we are too busy up\r\nhere. We have too much business on hand nearer us to think of attending\r\nto those wild countries.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, Governor, let some one else attend to them. We have only one life\r\nto live, and, really, much as we would like to await your pleasure, we\r\ncannot arrest the march of time. Time goes on, and as it slips by, ruin\r\napproaches us. We invested all our means in San Diego, hoping that\r\nColonel Scott would build his railroad. Now we see plainly that unless\r\nyou withdraw your opposition to Scott we are ruined men, and many more\r\ninnocent people are in the same situation. So we come to you and say, if\r\nyou will not let any one else build us a railroad, then do build it\r\nyourself. It will save us from ruin and give you untold wealth. We will\r\nbe glad to see you make millions if we only secure for ourselves our\r\nbread and butter,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Holman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOur bread; never mind the butter,\xe2\x80\x9d said Don Mariano, smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you at least have plenty of cows to make butter,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr.\r\nStanford, addressing Se\xc3\xb1or Alamar, evidently wishing to avoid the\r\nsubject, by turning it off.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir, I haven\xe2\x80\x99t. The squatters at my rancho shot and killed my\r\ncattle, so that I was obliged to send off those that I had left, and in\r\ndoing this a snow-storm overtook us, and nearly all my animals perished\r\nthen. The Indians will finish those which survived the snow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThose Indians are great thieves, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir; but not so bad to me as the squatters. The Indians kill my\r\ncattle to eat them, whereas the squatters did so to ruin me. Thus,\r\nhaving now lost all my cattle, I have only my land to rely upon for a\r\nliving\xe2\x80\x94nothing else. Hence my great anxiety to have the Texas Pacific.\r\nMy land will be very valuable if we have a railroad and our county\r\nbecomes more settled; but if not, my land, like everybody else\xe2\x80\x99s land in\r\nour county, will be unsaleable, worthless. A railroad soon is our only\r\nsalvation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is bad,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Stanford said, looking at his watch. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I don\xe2\x80\x99t see\r\nhow I can help you San Diego people. If Mr. Huntington effects some\r\ncompromise with Mr. Scott, we will then build a branch road, as I said.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what if there is no compromise?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, of course, there will be no road for you\xe2\x80\x94that is to say, no Texas\r\nPacific in California.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not, Governor? \xe2\x80\x98Live and let live,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d Don Mariano said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t seem to think of business principles. You forget that in\r\nbusiness every one is for himself. If it is to our interest to prevent\r\nthe construction of the Texas Pacific, do you suppose we will stop to\r\nconsider that we might inconvenience the San Diego people?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not a matter of inconvenience\xe2\x80\x94it is ruin, it is poverty,\r\nsuffering, distress; perhaps despair and death,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Mechlin. \xe2\x80\x9cOur\r\nmerchants, our farmers, all, the entire county will suffer great\r\ndistress or ruin, for they have embarked their all in the hope of\r\nimmediate prosperity, in the hope that emigration would come to us,\r\nshould our town be the western terminus.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou should have been more cautious; not so rash.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow could we have foreseen that you would prevent the construction of\r\nthe Texas Pacific?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEasily. By studying business principles; by perceiving it would be to\r\nour interest to prevent it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe never thought, and do not think now, that it is to your interest to\r\nprevent it. But even if we had thought so, we would not have supposed\r\nthat you would attempt it,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Mechlin replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause it would have seemed to us impossible that you could have\r\nsucceeded.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy impossible?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause we would have thought that the American people would interfere;\r\nthat Congress would respect the rights of the Southern people.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Stanford laughed, saying: \xe2\x80\x9cThe American people mind their business,\r\nand know better than to interfere with ours. All I can tell you,\r\ngentlemen, is that if Mr. Scott does not agree to come no further than\r\nthe Colorado River, he shall not be able to get the interest of his\r\nbonds guaranteed by our Government, which means that he will not have\r\nmoney to build his road\xe2\x80\x94no Congressional aid at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou seem very sure of Congress?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sure of what I say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, Governor, the Government helped you to build your roads, why don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nyou let it help ours?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho told you that?\xe2\x80\x9d said he, with an angry expression, like a dark\r\nshadow passing over his face. \xe2\x80\x9cWho told you that the Government helped\r\nus to build the Southern Pacific?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Government gave you a grant of many millions of acres to help build\r\nit, as the Central Pacific was constructed with Government subsidies,\r\nand the earning of the Central Pacific were used to construct the\r\nSouthern Pacific, it follows that you were helped by the Government to\r\nbuild both,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Holman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are talking of something you know nothing about. The help the\r\nGovernment gave us was to guarantee the interest of our bonds. We\r\naccepted that help, because we knew that, as private individuals, we\r\nmight not command the credit necessary to place our bonds in the market,\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s all. As for the land subsidy, we will pay every cent of its price\r\nwith our services. We do not ask of the Government to give us anything\r\ngratis. We will give value received for everything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is certainly a very ingenious view to take of the whole matter,\r\nand so viewing it, of course the killing of the Texas Pacific seems\r\njustifiable to you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Mechlin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCarlyle, in your place, would not view your position like that,\r\nGovernor,\xe2\x80\x9d said Don Mariano, rising.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNor Herbert Spencer, either. His ideas of what you call business\r\nprinciples are different,\xe2\x80\x9d added Mr. Holman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPray, what would those great thinkers say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCarlyle would think you are much to blame for flinging away a\r\nmagnificent chance to be great and heroic. Carlyle worships heroes, but\r\nhis idea of heroism is not only applicable to warriors and conquerors,\r\nbut to any one capable of rising to a high plane of thought or heroic\r\nendeavor, doing acts which require great self-denial for our\r\nfellow-beings, for humanity\xe2\x80\x99s sake, with no view or expectation of\r\nreward in money,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Mechlin said.\r\n\r\nThe Governor smiled, and with the least perceptible sneer he asked:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how does Mr. Herbert Spencer differ with my ideas of business\r\nprinciples?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe differs in this, that he thinks that commercial honor, business\r\nmorality, should be based on strict rectitude, on the purest equity.\r\nThat so soon as any one in the pursuit of riches knowingly and wilfully\r\nwill injure any one else, that he then violates the principle upon which\r\ncommerce should rest,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Holman replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut that is absurd. Would he stop competition?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all. Competition generally has the effect of securing the\r\npreference to whomsoever deserves it. No, what Mr. Spencer maintains is\r\nthat monopolies should not exist when they have become so powerful that\r\nthey defy the law, and use their power to the injury of others. The\r\nfundamental principle of morality is then subverted,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Holman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFundamental morality forbids us to injure any one because we would be\r\nbenefited by that injury,\xe2\x80\x9d said Don Mariano.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe same old axiom of the French revolution, that \xe2\x80\x98the rights of one\r\nman end where those of another begin.\xe2\x80\x99 Danton and Marat sang that to the\r\nmusic of the guillotine,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Governor, a little bit\r\ncontemptuously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is so; but you see, Governor, the devil might sing psalms, and it\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t hurt the psalms,\xe2\x80\x9d Don Mariano replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have made you waste your time talking to us, Governor,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr.\r\nHolman; \xe2\x80\x9ccan we not hope that you will reconsider this matter, and\r\nexamine more carefully the advantages of making San Diego the direct\r\noutlet for all that country that needs a railroad so much? Believe me,\r\nsir, such road will bring you more millions than the Central and\r\nSouthern Pacific Railroads. If you do not build it, and prevent Col.\r\nScott from building it, sooner or later some one else will, for it\r\nstands to reason that such a magnificent enterprise will not be left\r\nneglected after other less advantageous routes are tried. Then you will\r\nhave the regret of having spurned this golden chance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd besides the chance of making millions for yourselves. Think of the\r\nblessings you will bring to so many hearts who are now sadly\r\ndiscouraged, and will be desolate if our hopes are frustrated,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr.\r\nMechlin said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCorporations have no souls, gentlemen, and I am no Carlylean\r\nhero-philanthropist. I am only a most humble \xe2\x80\x98_public carrier_.\xe2\x80\x99 I do\r\nnot aspire to anything more than taking care of my business,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr.\r\nStanford answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, Governor, you cannot be indifferent to the distress your action\r\nwill cause?\xe2\x80\x9d insisted Mr. Mechlin, with sad earnestness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs for that,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Mr. Stanford, smiling; \xe2\x80\x9cif I don\xe2\x80\x99t cause distress\r\nsome one else will. Distress there must be, bound to be in this world,\r\nin spite of all that your philanthropists might do or say to prevent\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut do you not think that if all and every one of those who have it in\r\ntheir power to be beneficent were not so indifferent to human suffering,\r\nbut were to be benevolent, that then the combined result would be great\r\nalleviation and diminution of human distress?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; because those who have power to do good are very few, and the\r\nimprovident, the vicious, the lazy are in myriads; and they and their\r\nfolly and vices and improvidence will, forever, more than counterbalance\r\nthe good that the beneficent might effect,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Stanford asserted.\r\n\r\nMr. Mechlin arose and turned towards the door. Mr. Holman followed his\r\nexample. Se\xc3\xb1or Alamar looked sadly at the floor, saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Governor, I am sorry we have failed in bringing you to our way of\r\nthinking. Time will show who is mistaken.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes! Time will show. We can\xe2\x80\x99t cast any astrological horoscope at\r\nthe birth of a railroad. All we can do is to take care that it thrives.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo clear away competition.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly. The country is not settled enough yet to divide profits.\r\nBesides, we think that Eastern people ought not to build any roads to\r\nthe Pacific Coast, when we of California are ready to do it. Let Tom\r\nScott keep away. We don\xe2\x80\x99t build roads in Pennsylvania.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut are you sure you will always be able to prevent a competing road?\r\nWould it not be cheaper for yourself to build than to fight Tom Scott?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo indeed. For the present, it is cheaper to fight. It don\xe2\x80\x99t cost so\r\nmuch money to make friends,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou seem very confident of success.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMoney commands success, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, money is everything! And it weighs not a feather, all the ruin and\r\nsqualor and death you will bring to a people who never harmed you! Not a\r\nfeather\xe2\x80\x99s weight, as against the accumulation of money for yourselves,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Mr. Mechlin, forgetting his usual consideration for others\xe2\x80\x99\r\nfeelings.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I did not cause this misery you apprehend, some one would. Be sure\r\nof it, for there will always be misery in the world, no matter who\r\ncauses it,\xe2\x80\x9d the Governor replied, with an air of being satisfied with\r\nhis philosophy, inasmuch as he was to be exempt from human suffering, no\r\nmatter who went under.\r\n\r\nMr. Mechlin, still lingering sadly, and veiling his great disapprobation\r\nof Mr. Stanford\xe2\x80\x99s practical philosophy, said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Herbert Spencer also, in elucidating his principles, reminds us of\r\nthe fact that \xe2\x80\x98Misery is the highway to _death_, while happiness is\r\nadded life, and the giver of life.\xe2\x80\x99 Think of this, Governor. Surely, you\r\ndo not wish to make us so miserable that you cause _death_! Yes, death\r\nfrom poverty and despair. Poverty, overwork and discouragement are the\r\ncauses of sickness and death oftener than it is supposed, and this Mr.\r\nSpencer also maintains unswervingly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have a very vivid imagination; you color up things too dark,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nthe Governor, also rising.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope you will not be sorry to have thought so. I hope you will not\r\nregret that you closed your heart and your mind against us, against\r\njustice, humanity and reason.\xe2\x80\x9d So saying, Mr. Mechlin slowly walked off;\r\nthen at the door he turned, and lifting his finger, said to Don Mariano:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI feel a prophetic warning that neither you nor I will ever see light\r\nin this world. These men\xe2\x80\x94this deadly, soulless corporation, which, like\r\na black cloud, has shut out the light from San Diego\xe2\x80\x99s horizon\xe2\x80\x94will\r\nevermore cast the shadow that will be our funeral pall. But let them\r\nlook to it, they might yet carry their heartless rapacity beyond limit.\r\nThe mighty monopoly, that has no soul to feel responsibility, no heart\r\nfor human pity, no face for manly blush\xe2\x80\x94that soulless, heartless,\r\nshameless monster\xe2\x80\x94might yet fall of its own weight.\xe2\x80\x9d So saying, Mr.\r\nMechlin walked away, as if he intended this prophecy to be a parting\r\nsalutation to the men who had blighted his life and made him utterly\r\nhopeless.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXIV.\xe2\x80\x94_The Sins of Our Legislators!_\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98_Assey de Bonaparte!_\xe2\x80\x99 cried France, in 1814. Men found that his\r\nabsorbing egotism was deadly to all other men,\xe2\x80\x9d says Mr. Emerson. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\nwas not Bonaparte\xe2\x80\x99s fault. He did all that in him lay to live and thrive\r\nwithout moral principle. It was the nature of things, the eternal law of\r\nthe man and the world, which balked and ruined him; and the result in a\r\nmillion experiments would be the same. Every experiment by multitudes or\r\nby individuals, that has a sensual or selfish aim, will fail. The\r\nPacific Fourier will be as inefficient as the pernicious Napoleon. As\r\nlong as our civilization is essentially one of property, of\r\nexclusiveness, it will be mocked by delusions. Our riches will leave us\r\nsick; there will be bitterness in our laughter, and our wine will burn\r\nour mouth. Only that good profits which serves all men.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nYes, only that good profits which does not represent the misery of\r\nothers; only that wine should be sweet which is not drunk when the tears\r\nof those we have rendered desolate are silently running over pale cheeks\r\nfrom eyes that have kept the vigil of want, mourning for the beloved to\r\nwhom poverty brought death!\r\n\r\nIn heavenly-inspired words Emerson and Carlyle and Herbert Spencer have\r\nrepeated those burning aphorisms, but our California \xe2\x80\x9c_Fire Pillars_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\ndiffer with them\xe2\x80\x94differ widely and differ proudly.\r\n\r\nMr. Stanford says that if _he_ did not cause misery some one else would,\r\nfor \xe2\x80\x9c_misery there must always be in this world_!\xe2\x80\x9d Sound philosophy,\r\ntruly! Why should he recoil from adding to the sum total of human misery\r\nwhen so many others do the same!\r\n\r\nMr. Huntington was about the same time writing from Washington that he\r\n_would_ \xe2\x80\x9c_see the grass grow over Tom Scott_\xe2\x80\x9d before he stopped his work\r\nof _convincing_ Congressmen. And he kept his word.\r\n\r\nHe carried _conviction_ to Washington, distress to the South and ruin to\r\nSan Diego.\r\n\r\nMr. Crocker was answering, \xe2\x80\x9c_Anything to beat Tom Scott!_\xe2\x80\x9d The thing was\r\nto prevent the construction of San Diego\xe2\x80\x99s railroad, no matter to whom\r\nruin came thereby. \xe2\x80\x9cNo matter how many were sacrificed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNothing was more hopeless, therefore, than to suppose that any of those\r\nmen would swerve one iota from their course of greedy acquisition, out\r\nof respect for equity or humanity.\r\n\r\nNot a word was spoken until the three saddened friends reached Don\r\nMariano\xe2\x80\x99s parlors at the hotel. They had walked silently out of the\r\nrailroad building, silently taken the street car and silently walked out\r\nof it, as it happened to stop in front of their hotel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, we have failed sadly, but I am glad to have had the chance of\r\nstudying that piece of humanity, or rather I should say inhumanity,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr.\r\nMechlin exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow confident he is of their power over Congress! And he certainly\r\nmeans to wield it as if he came by it legitimately. He is proud of it,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nadded Mr. Holman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but he is wrong to be proud of a power he means to use only for\r\nselfish ends. Sooner or later the people will get tired of sending men\r\nto Congress who can be bought so easily. I am disappointed in Governor\r\nStanford. I thought him much more just and fair; a much higher order of\r\nman,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Don. \xe2\x80\x9cHow coolly he laughed at us for quoting Carlyle and\r\nSpencer! As if he would have said, \xe2\x80\x98You quote the philosophers,\r\ngentlemen, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll make the millions. You might die in poverty, _I_\r\nshall revel in wealth.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI ought to have quoted Emerson, when he says: \xe2\x80\x98I count him a great man\r\nwho inhabits a higher sphere of thought into which other men rise with\r\nlabor and difficulty.\xe2\x80\x99 This might have pointed out to him how groveling\r\nit is never to rise above the mere grubbing for money. No, he is not\r\nhalf as large-minded as I had believed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Mechlin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can he be if he is cognizant of the means employed by Huntington to\r\ndefeat all legislation in favor of the Texas Pacific?\xe2\x80\x9d observed Mr.\r\nHolman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I fear now the Governor gives his sanction to Huntington\xe2\x80\x99s work. I\r\nnever believed it before. I am disappointed in the Governor as much as\r\nin our fruitless errand,\xe2\x80\x9d the Don said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow irksome and distasteful it is for him to hear about \xe2\x80\x98_the rights of\r\nothers_.\xe2\x80\x99 He almost takes it as an insult that any one but himself and\r\nassociates should have _rights_; and he seems to lose all patience at\r\nthe mention of the distress they have brought upon the people of San\r\nDiego and the financial ruin that their rapacity and heartless conduct\r\nwill cause the Southern people,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Holman. \xe2\x80\x9cDid you notice how he\r\nfrowned at the allusion to the fact that the Central Pacific was built\r\nwith Government money? The mere mention irritates his nerves.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes he suppose we don\xe2\x80\x99t know that they had no money, and that it was\r\nwith capital _given_ as absolute gifts, or loaned to them on the\r\nguarantee of the Government, that they built and are building their\r\nroads?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Mechlin. \xe2\x80\x9cI never saw such complete subversion of the\r\nlaws of reasoning as these men exhibit. Good luck has made them think\r\nthat to genius they owe success. Thus their moral blindness makes them\r\ntake as an insulting want of proper deference any allusion to those\r\n_rights of others_ which, in their feverish greed, they trample. For\r\nthis reason they hate San Diego, because San Diego is a living proof of\r\ntheir wrong-doing; a monument reminding California of their deadly\r\negotism, of the injury done by unscrupulous men to their fellow-men.\r\nHence, my friends, I say that San Diego must have no hopes while those\r\nmen live.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid you are right, and as I have invested in San Diego all I\r\nhave in the world, I see no hope; nothing but hard-featured poverty\r\nstaring me in the face,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Holman, sadly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf it were owing to natural laws of the necessities of things that San\r\nDiego is thus crippled, our fate would seem to me less hard to bear,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Don Mariano; \xe2\x80\x9cbut to know that the necessities of commerce, the\r\ninevitable increase of the world\xe2\x80\x99s population, the development of our\r\nState, all, all demand that Southern California be not sacrificed, _and\r\nyet it is_, and our appeals to Congress are of no avail! All this adds\r\nbitterness to our disappointment. Yes, it is bitter to be reduced to\r\nwant, only because a few men, without any merit, without any claims upon\r\nthe nation\xe2\x80\x99s gratitude, desire more millions.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThus the disheartened friends discoursed, fully realizing their terrible\r\nproximity to that financial disaster which was sure to overtake them. In\r\nthe generosity and kindness of their hearts, they felt added regret,\r\nthinking of so many others who, in San Diego, were in the same position\r\nof impending ruin; so many good, worthy people, who certainly did not\r\ndeserve to be thus pitilessly sacrificed; so many who yet clung to the\r\nhopes of '72, when all rushed to buy city lots; so many out of whose\r\nhopes three years of disappointment had not quenched all life. The\r\nfailure of Jay Cook in the fall of \xe2\x80\x9973 had made the financial heart of\r\nAmerica shrink with discouragement and alarm, but San Diego did not\r\nrealize how much her own fate was involved in that sad catastrophe, and\r\ncontinued her gay building of proud castles in the air and humble little\r\ncottages on the earth\xe2\x80\x94very close to the earth, but covered with fragrant\r\nflowers, with roses, honeysuckles and fuchsias. These little one-story\r\nwooden cottages were intended for temporary dwellings only. By and by\r\nthe roomy stone or brick mansions would be erected, when the Texas\r\nPacific Railroad\xe2\x80\x94the highway of traffic across the continent\xe2\x80\x94should\r\nbring through San Diego the commerce between Asia and the Atlantic\r\nseaboard, between China and Europe. San Diego lived her short hour of\r\nhope and prosperity, and smiled and went to sleep on the brink of her\r\nown grave, the grave that Mr. C. P. Huntington had already begun to\r\nexcavate, to dig as he stealthily went about the halls of our National\r\nCapitol \xe2\x80\x9coffering bribes.\xe2\x80\x9d But such \xe2\x80\x9cfoul work\xe2\x80\x9d was then only surmised\r\nand scarcely believed. It was reserved for Mr. Huntington himself to\r\nfurnish proof that this was the fact. His letters were not published\r\nuntil years after, but the world has them now, and the monopoly, with\r\nall its power, cannot gainsay them.\r\n\r\nThe three friends were yet discussing this painful topic of their\r\npilgrimage, when Mr. Mechlin observed that Don Mariano was looking very\r\npale, and asked if he felt ill.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d Don Mariano replied; \xe2\x80\x9cI feel very cold. I feel as if I was frozen\r\nthrough and through. When we were at the Governor\xe2\x80\x99s office I felt very\r\nwarm, and when we came out my clothing was saturated with perspiration.\r\nNow I feel as if I had been steeped in ice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis won\xe2\x80\x99t do. You must change your clothes at once,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Mechlin.\r\n\r\nMr. Holman also became alarmed at seeing the bluish pallor of his face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, this is a congestive chill,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, hurrying off to call the\r\ndoctor, who resided at the hotel, and who fortunately was at home.\r\n\r\nPrompt and efficient medical attendance saved Don Mariano\xe2\x80\x99s life, but he\r\nwas too ill to leave his bed for several days. His two friends remained\r\nwith him, writing home that business matters detained them.\r\n\r\nDo\xc3\xb1a Josefa did not feel anxious; she thought that her husband was busy\r\nnegotiating a loan on his land, and this detained him.\r\n\r\nGabriel and Lizzie also were in constant attendance, and thus the sick\r\nman was kept in a cheerful frame of mind, a thing much to be desired in\r\nsickness always, but more especially in his case, accustomed as he was\r\nto be surrounded by a loving family.\r\n\r\nStill he was anxious to return home. Reluctantly the doctor allowed him\r\nto do so, hoping that the salubrious climate of Southern California\r\nwould be beneficial. But he said to him:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI let you go on condition that you pledge me your word to be very\r\ncareful not to get into a profuse perspiration and then rush out into\r\nthe cold air. If your lungs had not been originally so healthy and\r\nstrong you could not have rallied so soon, if ever; but they are yet\r\nfilled with phlegm, and the least cold might give you pneumonia.\xe2\x80\x9d To\r\nGabriel the doctor repeated the same words of warning, adding: \xe2\x80\x9cNot only\r\nis the condition of your father\xe2\x80\x99s lungs very precarious, but also that\r\nof his heart. He must not task either too much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGabriel was thoroughly alarmed at hearing the doctor\xe2\x80\x99s opinion, and\r\nimmediately wrote to his mother how careful his father ought to be, and\r\nhow she should watch him.\r\n\r\nDon Mariano tried to be careful, but having been very healthy all his\r\nlife, he did not know how to be an invalid, nor guard against fresh\r\ncolds.\r\n\r\nAbout two weeks had elapsed since his return from San Francisco, when a\r\nnotice that many of his city lots would be sold for taxes brought Don\r\nMariano to town. He still held to the belief that a railroad to San\r\nDiego would surely be built at some future day, but had ceased hoping to\r\nsee that day. However, he would willingly have waited for a rise in real\r\nestate before selling any of his city property, but he saw it was\r\nruinous for him to pay taxes\xe2\x80\x94taxes for town property and taxes for\r\nsquatters\xe2\x80\x94it was too much; so he reluctantly concluded that it would be\r\nbest to lose a great many lots (yes, whole blocks), permitting them to\r\nbe sold for taxes, hoping to redeem them on the following year if Tom\r\nScott was more successful with the Texas Pacific. Mr. Mechlin and Mr.\r\nHolman did the same, and many other unlucky ones followed their\r\ndiscouraging examples. Thus city lots by the hundreds were sold every\r\nyear.\r\n\r\nDon Mariano saw his city property thus sacrificed before his eyes at\r\npublic sale, just as he had seen his cattle buried under the snow. He\r\nsubmitted in both cases to the inevitable without a murmur; but this\r\ntime the blow seemed heavier. He was pecuniarily less able to bear it,\r\nand being in bad health and discouraged, his misfortunes were more\r\ndepressing. He rode home saddened indeed.\r\n\r\nVictoriano, who was now able to be about (but said he _mistrusted_ his\r\nlegs), was with him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFather, why don\xe2\x80\x99t you use some of that money Clarence sent you? I am\r\nsure he would approve your doing so, and feel glad, _very_ glad, indeed,\r\nthat you did it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Victoriano, when they had driven for a long time\r\nwithout uttering a word.\r\n\r\nDon Mariano turned sharply and said: \xe2\x80\x9cWhy should I use Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s money?\r\nIf I had delivered the cattle to Fred Haverly, as it was agreed I\r\nshould, then I would have a right to take from Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s money the\r\nprice of the cattle delivered. But having delivered no cattle, I take no\r\nmoney.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverett was saying that Clarence distinctly stated to his father that\r\nthe cattle in the Alamar rancho with your brand were all his, and would\r\nbe driven as soon as the weather permitted. Mr. Darrell thinks that the\r\ncattle lost belonged to Clarence, and not to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Darrell is wrong, then. I cannot expect to be paid for cattle I did\r\nnot deliver.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut he says you had sold them already. If they were lost on the way it\r\nwas neither your fault nor your loss.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, but was my misfortune, not Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe cattle were going to Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s mines, which goes to prove that\r\nthey had been bought by him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot view the matter like that,\xe2\x80\x9d Don Mariano said, and Victoriano\r\nsaw his mind was settled upon the subject, and it was best not to annoy\r\nhim by insisting in opposition.\r\n\r\nWhen they arrived home they found that Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa had received a\r\ntelegram from Gabriel, sent the night before, saying that he, Lizzie and\r\nthe baby would spend Christmas and New Year\xe2\x80\x99s Day at the rancho. This\r\nwas glad news, indeed, and most unexpected, for inasmuch as Lizzie had\r\njust been down on a visit and hurried back, so that Gabriel would not be\r\nall alone on Christmas, they did not think that Lizzie would want to\r\ntake the trip so soon again. But Lizzie would travel many more miles to\r\nbe with her family. And the reason that Gabriel had for coming was,\r\nmoreover, a most powerful one.\r\n\r\nHe had one day casually met the doctor who attended his father, and\r\nafter inquiring whether Don Mariano was better, added:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI tell you frankly, Don Gabriel, your father may yet live many years,\r\nbut he is in danger, too, of dying very suddenly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow? Why so?\xe2\x80\x9d Gabriel asked, pale with alarm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause his heart may give out if his lungs don\xe2\x80\x99t work well, and as he\r\nis not very careful of himself, you see he might task his heart with\r\nheavier work than it can perform. If he is kept from excitement and gets\r\nrid of all that phlegm which has accumulated in his lungs, he will be\r\nwell enough. So write to him to be careful in avoiding colds,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\ndoctor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will go and tell him so myself,\xe2\x80\x9d Gabriel said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is right. The case is serious, I assure you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis short dialogue brought Gabriel home.\r\n\r\nFrom the time he had entered the bank he had never been absent from it\r\none minute during office hours, so a three weeks\xe2\x80\x99 vacation was readily\r\ngranted to him.\r\n\r\nAll the Mechlins would come to Alamar to pass the holidays. George told\r\nhis father that they might as well go back to their home again since his\r\nlameness did not require daily medical attendance.\r\n\r\nMr. Mechlin replied that they would decide upon that after New Years,\r\nbut he was evidently pleased at the prospect of returning to Alamar.\r\n\r\nThe Alamar house looked once more as it had in the days of old, before\r\nsquatters invaded the place; it was full of people, and music and\r\nlaughter resounded under the hospitable roof. Mercedes, however, sat\r\nsilent, and though she smiled her own sweet smile, it was too sad; it\r\nfailed to deepen the cunning little dimples as it did in other days. The\r\nDon and Mr. Mechlin, too, were not as cheerful as they used to be. In\r\nthat visit to San Francisco \xe2\x80\x9c_a change came over the spirit of their\r\ndream_,\xe2\x80\x9d and it seemed to have come to quench the light of their lives.\r\n\r\nBut the young people wanted to decorate the house with green boughs and\r\nhave a huge Christmas-tree, and the Don himself went to help them to get\r\npine branches and red \xe2\x80\x9c_fusique_\xe2\x80\x9d berries. The tree would be in honor of\r\nhis two grandchildren; they were now eighteen months old, and the proud\r\nmammas said they were so intelligent that they would surely appreciate\r\nthe tree.\r\n\r\nEverett, Alice, Rosario and Victoriano were the committee on\r\ndecorations; Carlota, Caroline, Lucy and Webster were the committee on\r\nrefreshments. While the laughter of the young people came ringing out\r\nthrough the parlor windows, Don Mariano and Mr. Mechlin slowly walked up\r\nand down the back veranda in earnest conversation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Mechlin said, as if to reiterate some previous assertion,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cyes, I have lived my allotted term; my life is now an incumbrance\xe2\x80\x94nay,\r\nit is a burden on those who love me. If I were not living, George could\r\ntake his wife, his mother and sister, to reside in New York, but because\r\nI cannot live in that climate, all those dear ones remain in this\r\nexile.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why should you call it exile? They don\xe2\x80\x99t think it is; and even if\r\nit were, my friend, you have no right to cut your life off at your\r\nwill,\xe2\x80\x9d said Don Mariano.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not? Life is a free gift, and often a very onerous one. Why keep\r\nit, when to reject it would be preferable? when it would release others\r\nfrom painful obligations?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut are you sure that the grief and horror of knowing that you took\r\nyour own life would not be a million times worse than the supposed exile\r\nyou imagine to be so objectionable?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps so; but I assure you, since I have lost all my money, and when\r\nI am too old to make another fortune, my health has begun to fail again.\r\nI hate life without health, and these constant annoyances of financial\r\ndifficulties will end by prostrating me on a sick-bed again. Now, when I\r\nhave lost nearly all the money I invested in San Diego, now they come\r\ndown on me to pay a note of ten thousand dollars which I endorsed, with\r\nfive others. Why don\xe2\x80\x99t the others pay their share? I am willing to pay\r\ntwo thousand dollars, but not the entire sum.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see why you should, either. What does your lawyer say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe shrugs his shoulders, caresses his side-whiskers, and says he\r\n_thinks_ that some of the other indorsers are insolvent, because their\r\nproperty has depreciated so much that it would bring nothing if sold;\r\nwhile those that have some means, no doubt, put everything out of their\r\nhands, so I am left alone to pay the entire sum.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe sad dialogue of the grandpapas was now interrupted, as they were\r\ncalled to witness the glee of the babies at the sight of the illuminated\r\nChristmas-tree. When the surprise of first sight was over, little\r\nMariano Mechlin stretched out both hands for the colored candles. His\r\nuncle Tano gave him a tin trumpet, teaching him how to blow it;\r\nwhereupon baby Mechlin gave the company a blast, and looked so surprised\r\nat his own performance, and gazed around so triumphantly and yet so\r\nperplexed, that he made everybody laugh. Josefita looked at her cousin\r\ndistrustfully and gave her arms to her papa, as if she thought Marianito\r\nwas entirely too martial for the vicinity of peaceful babies like\r\nherself. Gabriel took her near the tree to select any toy she liked. She\r\nfancied a string of bright balls, which her father gave her. The babies\r\nwere allowed to be in the parlor for nearly an hour, and they were so\r\nbright, trying to repeat what was taught them, that it was really\r\namusing to watch them. Marianito sang for the company; all were\r\nsurprised to hear so young a baby sing so well. None enjoyed more\r\nheartily their cunning ways than the two grandfathers, especially Don\r\nMariano, and both babies clung to him when the nurses came to take them\r\nto bed.\r\n\r\nWhen the babies had made their exit, the children of larger growth had\r\ntheir music and dancing until ten, supper being then announced. On\r\nreturning to the parlor, after supper, the clock upon the mantel struck\r\ntwelve; at the same time a curtain ran up, and an altar was disclosed to\r\nview, tastefully decorated in the Roman Catholic style, having statues\r\nof the Virgin Mary, the divine infant, enveloped in fleecy drapery, and\r\nSt. Joseph standing by his side. Behind the cradle were three magi, and\r\nfurther off, the hills of Judea were seen. As all the company were Roman\r\nCatholics, all entered into the spirit of the commemoration, and joined\r\nwith true feeling in the carol led by Mrs. Darrell and Alice. Other\r\nsacred songs were sung, and then all retired for the night; the Darrells\r\npromising to come on the following evening to have another dance,\r\nbecause\xe2\x80\x94said Victoriano\xe2\x80\x94it must be celebrated that they had heard from\r\nClarence, and that he had found his legs, meaning that he (Tano) had\r\nagain the use of his limbs.\r\n\r\nChristmas Day was passed very happily, and in the evening the young\r\npeople assembled in the parlor for a dance. Don Mariano excused himself\r\nto Mr. Mechlin, saying he felt badly, and thought that he ought to be in\r\nbed.\r\n\r\nAt about eleven o\xe2\x80\x99clock he sat up in bed and looked around as if wishing\r\nto speak. Gabriel and Mercedes were sitting by his bed, and promptly\r\nasked if he wished for anything.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe sins of our legislators have brought us to this,\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed,\r\nleaning back. Presently he said: \xe2\x80\x9cCall your mother, my son.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGabriel called his mother, who being in the next room, talking with Mrs.\r\nMechlin, was quickly by his side.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCall Elvira and Tano. Call Carlota and Rosario and George. Call all,\r\nall, quickly! I fear, my beloved son, I fear I am dying! Bring all my\r\ngirls; I must bless them all!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMercedes had her arms around him. He looked at her lovingly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy baby, kiss me. Tell Clarence I bless him with my last breath.\xe2\x80\x9d His\r\nvoice began to fail him, but his eyes seemed glowing with an intensity\r\nthat was startling. He sat up again, looking at each one of the anxious\r\nfaces around his bed. \xe2\x80\x9cGod bless you all, my beloved ones,\xe2\x80\x9d said he,\r\nhoarsely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPapa, darling, can\xe2\x80\x99t we do something to relieve you?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mercedes.\r\nHe shook his head and whispered:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cToo late. The sins of our legislators!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you feel pain, father?\xe2\x80\x9d Gabriel asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot now,\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered, extending his hand to George as if to say\r\ngood-by. He looked again to see whether every one of his family was\r\nthere; he forgot no one; he seemed anxious to see them all for the last\r\ntime. He extended his arms to his wife; she came to him. \xe2\x80\x9cPray for me,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe whispered, moving his lips as if in prayer, and leaning on Gabriel,\r\nwho held him, closed his eyes and sighed. A few aspirations followed\r\nthat last sigh, and all was over\xe2\x80\x94his noble soul had passed away.\r\n\r\nFor some moments no one believed that his lofty and noble spirit had\r\nleft the earth, but when the truth was at last realized, the scene of\r\ngrief, of heart-rending agony, that followed would be impossible for me\r\nto describe.\r\n\r\nClosely in the sad train of this mournful event, and as a fitting sequel\r\nand a complement of such dire misfortune, another disaster, more\r\nunexpected, more dreadful and tragic, followed, which must now be\r\nrelated. It shall be told as briefly as possible.\r\n\r\nA few days had passed after the funeral, and the Alamar family were\r\nstill in town. Do\xc3\xb1a Josefa and Mercedes were at the Mechlins.\r\nVictoriano, Carlota and Rosario were at the Holmans; that is, they slept\r\nthere, but as Mercedes was again prostrated with fever, they, as well as\r\nthe Holmans, divided their time between the two houses.\r\n\r\nOne morning Mr. Mechlin arose from the breakfast table and said he was\r\ngoing hunting.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t go far, James; you are too weak,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Mechlin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think, papa, you ought not to carry that heavy gun. You eat nothing,\r\nand walk too far, carrying it,\xe2\x80\x9d Caroline said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you carry it for me?\xe2\x80\x9d he said, smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will,\xe2\x80\x9d Gabriel said; \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll take George\xe2\x80\x99s, too, and go with you, if\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ll permit me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt isn\xe2\x80\x99t necessary,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied, going towards his room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think papa has taken to heart the death of Don Mariano more than any\r\none sees,\xe2\x80\x9d said Caroline.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know he has; he has hardly slept or eaten enough to sustain life\r\nsince that awful night,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Mechlin said, \xe2\x80\x9cand constantly talks about\r\nsoon joining his best friend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have observed how very sad he is. I wrote uncle to come; I think to\r\nsee his brother will be great consolation to him,\xe2\x80\x9d said George.\r\n\r\nThe report of a gun was heard in Mr. Mechlin\xe2\x80\x99s room, and all jumped to\r\ntheir feet. Gabriel was the first to run and got to the room in advance\r\nof the others. He found Mr. Mechlin shot through the heart.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, God! Was it accidental?\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Mechlin exclaimed, clasping her\r\nhusband to heart. The dying man smiled, whispering:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo not mourn for me; it is best so; I shall be happier.\xe2\x80\x9d He looked\r\nlovingly at the anxious faces surrounding him, and closed his eyes\r\nforever.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXV.\xe2\x80\x94_The Fashion of Justice in San Diego._\r\n\r\n\r\nIf those kind eyes of the Goddess of Justice were not bandaged, but she\r\ncould see how her pure white robes have been begrimed and soiled in San\r\nDiego, and how her lofty dignity is thus lowered to the dust, she would\r\nno doubt feel affronted and aggrieved. And if she is so irreverently\r\nmaltreated, can she afford any protection to those who must rely on her\r\nalone, having no riches to maintain protracted litigation or carry their\r\nplaints to higher tribunals? To the moneyless laity Justice thus defiled\r\nseems as helpless as themselves. She is powerless to accomplish her\r\nmission upon earth whenever a Judge, through weakness or design, may\r\nchoose to disregard her dictates. At present the dignity of a Judge\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npersonality is more sacred than the abstract impersonality of justice.\r\nBecause the accepted theory being that Judges are always just and\r\nincorruptible (and generally the supposition is correct), there is a\r\nbroad shelter for a Judge who may be neither just nor impartial. What\r\nmockery of justice it is in our fair land of freedom to say that a bad\r\nJudge can be impeached when impeachment is so hedged with difficulties\r\nas to be impossible\xe2\x80\x94utterly ineffectual to protect the poor, victimized\r\nlaity! Who is the poor litigant that would dare arraign an unjust Judge,\r\nwell sheltered in his judicial ermine, and the entire profession ready\r\nto champion him? \xe2\x80\x9c_Libel_\xe2\x80\x9d would be the cry against any one who would\r\ndare hold the mirror for such Judge to see himself! Ah, yes, when the\r\nreal libel is to distort the law and degrade the mission of justice on\r\nearth!\r\n\r\nPeter Roper, knowing well with what impunity he could violate justice\r\nand decency, conceived the brilliant idea of taking the Mechlin house at\r\nAlamar, now that the family were sojourning in town. Peter did not like\r\nto divide the spoils, but as accomplices were absolutely necessary,\r\nthere was no alternative but to take his friend and client Gasbang into\r\nthe plot.\r\n\r\nOn a Sunday evening Peter proceeded to unfold his plan before John, who\r\nhad come from his farm to attend church and was attired in a white vest\r\nand black coat, having just come from evening service. For, as I have\r\nsaid before, John Gasbang was a pillar of the church now, and never\r\nfailed in his attendance every Sunday. People knew that in old times,\r\nwhen John was very poor, he used to play \xe2\x80\x9c_monte_\xe2\x80\x9d with the Indians and\r\ncheat them out of their money. Many times he had been known to spend\r\nalmost the entire night sitting cross-legged on a blanket with a tallow\r\ncandle set in a bottle to light his high-toned game, surrounded by the\r\nselect company of naked Indians, who were too fascinated to see how\r\nplainly John was robbing them. Pitilessly would John strip his\r\nunsophisticated tattooed comrades of everything they owned on this\r\nearth. Their reed baskets, bows and arrows, strings of beads, tufts of\r\nfeather-tips, or any other rustic and barbaric ornaments. All, all, John\r\nwould gather up with his skillfully shuffled cards. The spoils he thus\r\ncollected he would sell to other Indians from whom he would presently\r\ngather in (like the good Sexton he was), gather in, with high-toned and\r\nhighly skillful shuffling. But John now was a rich man. Kindly San Diego\r\nhad forgiven John\xe2\x80\x99s petty thieving. The money won from the poor Indians\r\nhad helped him to thrive, and consequently convinced him that, after\r\nall, cheating was no worse than other sins, the gravity of which\r\nentirely depended upon the trick of hiding them. He would now try to\r\nhide his humble, predatory gambling, he said to himself, and seem\r\nrespectable.\r\n\r\nYes, he would wear a white vest and try to look honest, but on hearing\r\nRoper\xe2\x80\x99s project, his dull, fishy eyes revolved quickly in their little\r\nsockets, and his square jaws expanded like those of a snake before it\r\nshakes its rattle and coils up to spring. His mouth watered in\r\nanticipation of the sweets of ill-gotten gain as he listened attentively\r\nto all that Roper had to say.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll see Hogsden the first thing in the morning,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, joyously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut wait. Can you trust him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrust him? I should say I could, and if he weakens, there is his wife\r\nto brace him up with her good advice. He owes a big sum of money to old\r\nMechlin; so old Hoggy will be only too glad to get even by jumping the\r\nhouse. I suppose our friend, the Judge, is with us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t be silly. Do you suppose I would do a thing of this kind if I\r\nwasn\xe2\x80\x99t sure of him? He won\xe2\x80\x99t fail me. He\xe2\x80\x99ll do as I say. Be sure of\r\nthat, and don\xe2\x80\x99t talk. Come to my house now and I\xe2\x80\x99ll draw up the\r\nconveyance. Hog. must sign his quit-claim deed, and then I\xe2\x80\x99ll see that\r\nhis location of one hundred and sixty acres is properly filed. But,\r\nmind, if Hogsden betrays us, he\xe2\x80\x99ll spoil our game,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Roper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLeave that to me,\xe2\x80\x9d said John, rubbing his hands and giving his vest a\r\ndownward pull.\r\n\r\nThe result of this dialogue was that Hogsden quit-claimed all his,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cright, title and interest in a certain parcel of land, etc., etc., with\r\na dwelling house and other improvements, etc., etc.,\xe2\x80\x9d and the\r\ndescription of the property might have applied to a hundred others in\r\nthe county. This transaction accomplished and recorded, they took the\r\nfurniture that had been left in the house by the Mechlins and put it\r\ntemporarily in the barn; Mrs. Hogsden taking only such articles as she\r\nwished to keep. She stole them brazenly, saying she had bought them.\r\n\r\nIt was further agreed that they would work the farm in partnership,\r\ndividing profits equally, and a contract in writing to this effect was\r\nsigned by them.\r\n\r\nRoper now being a property holder, besides being so influential with\r\n_the_ Judge, thought he could soar to higher altitudes. By the\r\nassistance of Gasbang and a few others, whom he said belonged to his\r\n_gang_, he managed to get himself nominated for Representative to\r\nCongress. Bursting with pride, puny Peter started on his way to glory,\r\nto _stump_ his district. He would begin at San Bernardino and carry the\r\ncounty by storm, with the force of his eloquence and personal magnetism,\r\nhe said, with characteristic modesty.\r\n\r\nHe made speeches at San Pascual, and Poway, and San Bernardo, and Bear\r\nValley, and Julian, but his greatest effort, the achievement that would\r\ncrown his brow with laurels, that effort he reserved for Los Angeles.\r\nQuite a big crowd was marshaled to hear him. He had paid a good deal of\r\nmoney in advertisements so as to collect an audience. He succeeded; a\r\ncrowd was there ready to make up in quantity what it lacked in quality.\r\n\r\nRoper came forward. His face was red as usual, but he seemed sober\xe2\x80\x94he\r\nstood straight. He was as loquacious as ever, of course, and talked\r\nincessantly for quite a while, making the crowd laugh. After he had all\r\nhis audience in a laughing mood with his coarse anecdotes and broad\r\njokes, he thought he would capture their votes beyond a doubt if he then\r\nand there proved himself\xe2\x80\x94by his own admissions\xe2\x80\x94to be _low_, the lowest\r\nof the lowly\xe2\x80\x94so very low, so very disreputable, that no one could be\r\nlower.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou cannot doubt,\xe2\x80\x9d said Peter, \xe2\x80\x9cthat my sympathies as well as my\r\ninterests, are with you, the working people, the poor who must work or\r\nstarve. I have nothing in common with bloated bondholders or pampered\r\nmonopolists who have enriched themselves with the earnings of the poor.\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t know how I came to be a lawyer. I suppose it happened because I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t like to work. I would rather talk and let others work. [Laughter.]\r\nI am a child of the people, and _for_ the people\xe2\x80\x94the poor people I mean.\r\nMy mother was a cook, a poor cook\xe2\x80\x94poor in pocket I mean. Her cookery may\r\nhave been rich [laughter], but upon that point I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t enlighten you,\r\nfor I have forgotten the flavor of her dishes. But she was a cook by\r\nprofession, just as I am a lawyer by profession, and one is as good as\r\nthe other. [Laughter.] As for my father, of him I know nothing to speak\r\nof\xe2\x80\x94literally\xe2\x80\x94[laughter], so the less said on that head, the sooner\r\nmended; for if the fact of my being here goes to prove _to you_ that I\r\nhad a father, that is all the proof _I_ ever had myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHere Peter laughed, but he laughed alone. He thought that a burst of\r\nlaughter and applause would follow this last shameless, revolting\r\nadmission, but not a sound was heard. He had overstepped the bounds of\r\ndecency so far, that even such a crowd as made his audience was silent\r\nas if unanimous disgust was beyond utterance. Roper was evidently\r\ndisconcerted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe don\xe2\x80\x99t want to be represented in Washington by a fellow who exults in\r\ndegradation and has no respect for the memory of his mother,\xe2\x80\x9d said a\r\nloud voice, and the crowd began to disperse.\r\n\r\nSoon Peter\xe2\x80\x99s native impudence came to his aid and he tried to recommence\r\nhis discourse. \xe2\x80\x9cLook here,\xe2\x80\x9d he cried, \xe2\x80\x9cwhere are you going? You ain\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ngoing to send my mother to Congress! Did you think I came to ask you to\r\nvote for her?\xe2\x80\x9d He went on in this coarse, bantering style which had\r\ntaken so well at first, but in vain. Nobody wanted to hear him now. It\r\nseemed as if the ghost of the poor reviled cook had come, like that of\r\nBanquo, to frighten off the audience. In a few minutes only about half a\r\ndozen of his supporters had been left, and they remained to scold.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said one, looking back at the receding crowd, \xe2\x80\x9cthat cake is all\r\ndough, Peter. I hope your mother would have made a better job of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA delightful dough,\xe2\x80\x9d said another; \xe2\x80\x9cand his goose is well cooked. I\r\nsay, Peter, you cooked your goose brown, browner than your mother ever\r\ncooked hers, and I bet on it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPeter answered with an oath.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe worst of it is, that in cooking your goose, you burnt ours to a\r\ncinder. We haven\xe2\x80\x99t the ghost of a chance now, and the Republican\r\ncandidate will have a walk-over to Congress,\xe2\x80\x9d said a third supporter.\r\n\r\nAlas for human delusions! This fiasco was the crowning glory of Roper\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npolitical campaign. Like the celebrated ambitious toad which cracked its\r\nsides by the force of its own inflation, Peter came to grief,\r\nignominious grief; that is to say, it would have been ignominious to any\r\none not thoroughly inoculated with disgrace as he, _according to his own\r\nversion_, must have been from the day of his birth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me ask you a question, Roper,\xe2\x80\x9d said a fourth friend. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy did you\r\nbring out such a thing against your mother? It was your misfortune as\r\nlong as you kept quiet about it, but now it is your shame. What was the\r\ngood of telling against your own mother? Don\xe2\x80\x99t you know that people,\r\neven the humblest, must censure and despise you for it? Few, very few\r\ndecent men, like to have anything to do with a man who reviles his dead\r\nmother, no matter if she was a poor cook. What pleasure can you find in\r\nproclaiming your shame?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRoper laughed loud and derisively, saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat will you bet that I\xe2\x80\x99ll have just as good and just as many friends\r\nin San Diego as I ever had before?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean to say that the people of San Diego _approve_ of language\r\nsuch as you used to-night? Approve your conduct?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind about that, only will you take my bet?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe henchman shrugged his shoulders and walked off, but if he had taken\r\nthat bet, he would have lost.\r\n\r\nWhen Colonel Hornblower received the news of Roper\xe2\x80\x99s fiasco, it occurred\r\nto him that he would take a trip to Europe. He had now made money enough\r\nout of the troubles and distress he and Roper brought upon others, to\r\nindulge in that luxury, the pleasure of saying he had been to Europe.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Colonel to his wife, \xe2\x80\x9cI think now is the best time\r\nto take that trip to Europe we have had in our hearts for so long. Get\r\nready; let us go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat has happened?\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Colonel Hornblower asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing, except that that partner of mine made a fiasco of his\r\npolitical campaign,\xe2\x80\x9d and the Colonel related to his swarthy lady Roper\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nspeech, and how it was received.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow absurd! so unnecessary!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerfectly, but you see, for a man of _my_ dignity the thing is awkward.\r\nWhat will the town say of _me_, *ME*?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe town will say nothing. As long as Roper has the friendship of Judge\r\nLawlack he can have clients; and as long as he has clients the San Diego\r\npeople will be indulgent to him, no matter how debased he says he is.\r\nHowever, drop him, and let\xe2\x80\x99s go to Europe. I wish we could get letters\r\nto distinguished people abroad.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat for? Our American ministers can present us to the best society,\r\nand besides, I am sure I am well known abroad. My name\xe2\x80\x94the name of\r\nColonel Hornblower\xe2\x80\x94must be as familiar to Europeans as the names of\r\nother distinguished Americans. I am the most prominent man in San Diego.\r\nAll the world knows San Diego, all the world must know Colonel\r\nHornblower.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStill, I would like to get letters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all necessary, I assure you. I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell our minister in England\r\nthat Mrs. Colonel Hornblower wishes to be presented to Queen Victoria,\r\nand he\xe2\x80\x99ll present you. The Queen, no doubt, will wish to make our\r\nacquaintance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would like to see other royal people. I would like to see the Pope,\r\nalso.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou shall see as many princes and princesses as you like. We Americans\r\nare princes, all of us. We are the equals of princes. As for the Pope, I\r\nwould not take one step to make his acquaintance, unless he met me half\r\nway; but if you like to see him, we\xe2\x80\x99ll get an introduction easily.\r\nPerhaps he might invite us to dinner. If he does, I hope it won\xe2\x80\x99t be on\r\nFriday, as fish don\xe2\x80\x99t agree with me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes he ever invite people to dinner?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDistinguished people, of course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Hornblowers sailed for Europe before Roper returned from his\r\nstumping tour. He was detained at Los Angeles, where he had been beaten\r\nso badly in a bar-room brawl that he was obliged to keep in bed for\r\nseveral days. The Colonel then wisely slipped off for Europe, to hob-nob\r\nwith royal people and take dinner with the Pope, perhaps.\r\n\r\nMrs. Hornblower conjectured rightly. Roper\xe2\x80\x99s disgrace was condoned by\r\nSan Diego, because he was under the patronage of Judge Lawlack, and in\r\nSan Diego everybody has a law suit.\r\n\r\nBut has the Judge no moral responsibility in this? _Has he the right to\r\nimpose upon the community_ a man so self-debased and noxious? If the\r\nJudge were to withdraw his support Peter would collapse like a pricked\r\ngas-bag, to be swept off into the gutter. But the Judge is the genii,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_the Slave of the Ring_\xe2\x80\x9d and his power keeps the little gas-bag afloat,\r\nsoaring as high as it is in the nature of little gas-bags to soar.
